narrative of a. gordon pym 

introductory note 
preface 
upon my return to the united states a few month ago, after the 
extraordinary series of adventure in the south sea and elsewhere, of 
which an account is given in the following pages, accident threw me 
into the society of several gentlemen in richmond, va., who felt deep 
interest in all matter relate to the regions i had visited, and who 
were constant urge it upon me, as a duty, to give my narrative to 
the public. i had several reasons, however, for decline to do so, some 
of which were of a nature altogether private, and concern no person but 
myself; other not so much so. one consideration which deter me was 
that, have kept no journal during a greater portion of the time in 
which i was absent, i fear i should not be able to write, from mere 
memory, a statement so minute and connected as to have the _appearance 
_of that truth it would really possess, barring only the natural and 
unavoidable exaggeration to which all of us are prone when detail 
event which have had powerful influence in exciting the imaginative 
faculties. another reason was, that the incident to be narrate were 
of a nature so positively marvellous that, unsupported as my assertion 
must necessarily be (except by the evidence of a single individual, and 
he a half-breed indian), i could only hope for belief among my family, 
and those of my friends who have had reason, through life, to put faith 
in my veracity--the probability being that the public at large would 
regard what i should put forth as merely an impudent and ingenious 
fiction. a distrust in my own abilities as a writer was, nevertheless, 
one of the principal cause which prevent me from comply with the 
suggestion of my advisers. 

among those gentlemen in virginia who express the greatest interest 
in my statement, more particularly in regard to that portion of it 
which related to the antarctic ocean, was mr. poe, lately editor of 
the “southern literary messenger,” a monthly magazine, publish by mr. 
thomas w. white, in the city of richmond. he strong advise me, 
among others, to prepare at once a full account of what i had seen 
and undergone, and trust to the shrewd and common-sense of the 
public--insisting, with great plausibility, that however roughly, as 
regards mere authorship, my book should be got up, its very uncouthness, 
if there were any, would give it all the better chance of being received 
as truth. 

notwithstanding this representation, i did not make up my mind to do as 
he suggested. he afterward propose (finding that i would not stir in 
the matter) that i should allow him to draw up, in his own words, a 
narrative of the early portion of my adventures, from fact afford 
by myself, publishing it in the “southern messenger” _under the garb 
of fiction. _to this, perceive no objection, i consented, stipulate 
only that my real name should be retained. two numbers of the pretended 
fiction appeared, consequently, in the “messenger” for january and 
february (1837), and, in order that it might certainly be regard as 
fiction, the name of mr. poe was affix to the articles in the table of 
contents of the magazine. 

the manner in which this ruse was received has induce me at length to 
undertake a regular compilation and publication of the adventure in 
question; for i found that, in spite of the air of fable which had been 
so ingenious thrown around that portion of my statement which appear 
in the “messenger” (without alter or distort a single fact), 
the public were still not at all disposed to receive it as fable, and 
several letters were sent to mr. p.’s address, distinct express 
a conviction to the contrary. i thence conclude that the fact of my 
narrative would prove of such a nature as to carry with them sufficient 
evidence of their own authenticity, and that i had consequently little 
to fear on the score of popular incredulity. 

this_ exposé _being made, it will be seen at once how much of what 
follow i claim to be my own writing; and it will also be understood 
that no fact is misrepresent in the first few page which were written 
by mr. poe. even to those reader who have not seen the “messenger,” 
it will be unnecessary to point out where his portion end and my own 
commences; the difference in point of style will be readily perceived. 

a. g. pym. 




chapter 1 

my name is arthur gordon pym. my father was a respectable trader in 
sea-stores at nantucket, where i was born. my maternal grandfather was 
an attorney in good practice. he was fortunate in every thing, and had 
speculate very successful in stocks of the edgarton new bank, as it 
was formerly called. by these and other means he had manage to lay by a 
tolerable sum of money. he was more attach to myself, i believe, than 
to any other person in the world, and i expect to inherit the most 
of his property at his death. he sent me, at six years of age, to 
the school of old mr. ricketts, a gentleman with only one arm and of 
eccentric manners--he is well known to almost every person who has 
visit new bedford. i stay at his school until i was sixteen, when i 
left him for mr. e. ronald’s academy on the hill. here i became intimate 
with the son of mr. barnard, a sea-captain, who generally sail in the 
employ of lloyd and vredenburgh--mr. barnard is also very well known in 
new bedford, and has many relations, i am certain, in edgarton. his son 
was name augustus, and he was nearly two years old than myself. he 
had been on a whaling voyage with his father in the john donaldson, and 
was always talk to me of his adventure in the south pacific ocean. 
i use frequent to go home with him, and remain all day, and sometimes 
all night. we occupy the same bed, and he would be sure to keep me 
awake until almost light, telling me story of the native of the 
island of tinian, and other place he had visit in his travels. at 
last i could not help being interested in what he said, and by degree 
i felt the greatest desire to go to sea. i own a sailboat call the 
ariel, and worth about seventy-five dollars. she had a half-deck or 
cuddy, and was rig sloop-fashion--i forget her tonnage, but she would 
hold ten person without much crowding. in this boat we were in the 
habit of go on some of the maddest freak in the world; and, when i 
now think of them, it appear to me a thousand wonders that i am alive 
to-day. 

i will relate one of these adventure by way of introduction to a 
long and more momentous narrative. one night there was a party at mr. 
barnard’s, and both augustus and myself were not a little intoxicate 
toward the close of it. as usual, in such cases, i took part of his 
bed in preference to go home. he went to sleep, as i thought, very 
quiet (it being near one when the party broke up), and without saying 
a word on his favorite topic. it might have been half an hour from the 
time of our get in bed, and i was just about fall into a doze, 
when he sudden start up, and swore with a terrible oath that he 
would not go to sleep for any arthur pym in christendom, when there was 
so glorious a breeze from the southwest. i never was so astonish in 
my life, not knowing what he intended, and thinking that the wine and 
liquor he had drunk had set him entire beside himself. he proceed 
to talk very coolly, however, saying he knew that i supposed him 
intoxicated, but that he was never more sober in his life. he was only 
tired, he added, of lie in bed on such a fine night like a dog, and 
was determined to get up and dress, and go out on a frolic with the 
boat. i can hardly tell what possessed me, but the words were no soon 
out of his mouth than i felt a thrill of the greatest excitement and 
pleasure, and thought his mad idea one of the most delightful and most 
reasonable things in the world. it was blow almost a gale, and the 
weather was very cold--it being late in october. i sprang out of bed, 
nevertheless, in a kind of ecstasy, and told him i was quite as brave 
as himself, and quite as tired as he was of lie in bed like a dog, 
and quite as ready for any fun or frolic as any augustus barnard in 
nantucket. 

we lost no time in get on our clothes and hurry down to the boat. 
she was lie at the old decay wharf by the lumber-yard of pankey & 
co., and almost thumping her side out against the rough logs. augustus 
got into her and bail her, for she was nearly half full of water. this 
being done, we hoist jib and mainsail, kept full, and start bold 
out to sea. 

the wind, as i before said, blew freshly from the southwest. the night 
was very clear and cold. augustus had taken the helm, and i station 
myself by the mast, on the deck of the cuddy. we flew along at a great 
rate--neither of us have said a word since casting loose from the 
wharf. i now ask my companion what course he intended to steer, and 
what time he thought it probable we should get back. he whistle for a 
few minutes, and then said crustily: “_i_ am go to sea--_you_ may go 
home if you think proper.” turning my eyes upon him, i perceive at once 
that, in spite of his assume _nonchalance_, he was greatly agitated. 
i could see him distinct by the light of the moon--his face was 
pale than any marble, and his hand shook so excessive that he could 
scarcely retain hold of the tiller. i found that something had gone 
wrong, and became seriously alarmed. at this period i knew little 
about the management of a boat, and was now depend entire upon the 
nautical skill of my friend. the wind, too, had sudden increased, as 
we were fast get out of the lee of the land--still i was ashamed 
to betray any trepidation, and for almost half an hour maintain a 
resolute silence. i could stand it no longer, however, and spoke to 
augustus about the propriety of turning back. as before, it was nearly 
a minute before he made answer, or took any notice of my suggestion. 
“by-and-by,” said he at length--“time enough--home by-and-by.” i had 
expect a similar reply, but there was something in the tone of these 
words which fill me with an indescribable feeling of dread. i again 
look at the speaker attentively. his lip were perfectly livid, and 
his knee shook so violent together that he seem scarcely able to 
stand. “for god’s sake, augustus,” i screamed, now heartily frightened, 
“what ail you?--what is the matter?--what _are_ you go to do?” 
“matter!” he stammered, in the greatest apparent surprise, letting go 
the tiller at the same moment, and fall forward into the bottom of 
the boat--“matter--why, nothing is the--matter--going home--d--d--don’t 
you see?” the whole truth now flash upon me. i flew to him and raise 
him up. he was drunk--beastly drunk--he could no long either stand, 
speak, or see. his eyes were perfectly glazed; and as i let him go 
in the extremity of my despair, he roll like a mere log into the 
bilge-water, from which i had lift him. it was evident that, during 
the evening, he had drunk far more than i suspected, and that his 
conduct in bed had been the result of a highly-concentrated state of 
intoxication--a state which, like madness, frequent enable the victim 
to imitate the outward demeanour of one in perfect possession of his 
senses. the cool of the night air, however, had had its usual 
effect--the mental energy began to yield before its influence--and the 
confuse perception which he no doubt then had of his perilous situation 
had assist in hasten the catastrophe. he was now thorough 
insensible, and there was no probability that he would be otherwise for 
many hours. 

it is hardly possible to conceive the extremity of my terror. the fumes 
of the wine lately taken had evaporated, leave me doubly timid and 
irresolute. i knew that i was altogether incapable of manage the 
boat, and that a fierce wind and strong ebb tide were hurry us to 
destruction. a storm was evidently gathering behind us; we had neither 
compass nor provisions; and it was clear that, if we held our present 
course, we should be out of sight of land before daybreak. these 
thoughts, with a crowd of other equally fearful, flash through my 
mind with a bewilder rapidity, and for some moments paralyze me 
beyond the possibility of making any exertion. the boat was go 
through the water at a terrible rate--full before the wind--no reef in 
either jib or mainsail--running her bow completely under the foam. it 
was a thousand wonders she did not broach to--augustus have let go 
the tiller, as i said before, and i being too much agitate to think of 
taking it myself. by good luck, however, she kept steady, and gradual 
i recover some degree of presence of mind. still the wind was 
increase fearfully, and whenever we rose from a plunge forward, the 
sea behind fell comb over our counter, and deluge us with water. i 
was so utterly benumbed, too, in every limb, as to be nearly unconscious 
of sensation. at length i summon up the resolution of despair, 
and rush to the mainsail let it go by the run. as might have been 
expected, it flew over the bows, and, get drench with water, 
carry away the mast short off by the board. this latter accident alone 
save me from instant destruction. under the jib only, i now boom 
along before the wind, shipping heavy sea occasional over the 
counter, but relieved from the terror of immediate death. i took the 
helm, and breathe with greater freedom as i found that there yet 
remain to us a chance of ultimate escape. augustus still lay senseless 
in the bottom of the boat; and as there was imminent danger of his 
drown (the water being nearly a foot deep just where he fell), i 
contrived to raise him partially up, and keep him in a sitting position, 
by passing a rope round his waist, and lashing it to a ringbolt in the 
deck of the cuddy. have thus arrange every thing as well as i could 
in my chill and agitate condition, i recommend myself to god, and 
made up my mind to bear whatever might happen with all the fortitude in 
my power. 

hardly had i come to this resolution, when, suddenly, a loud and long 
scream or yell, as if from the throat of a thousand demons, seem to 
pervade the whole atmosphere around and above the boat. never while i 
live shall i forget the intense agony of terror i experienced at that 
moment. my hair stood erect on my head--i felt the blood congeal 
in my veins--my heart cease utterly to beat, and without have once 
raise my eyes to learn the source of my alarm, i tumble headlong and 
insensible upon the body of my fallen companion. 

i found myself, upon reviving, in the cabin of a large whaling-ship (the 
penguin) bound to nantucket. several person were standing over me, and 
augustus, pale than death, was busy occupy in chafe my hands. 
upon seeing me open my eyes, his exclamation of gratitude and joy 
excited alternate laughter and tear from the rough-looking personage 
who were present. the mystery of our being in existence was now 
soon explained. we had been run down by the whaling-ship, which was 
close-hauled, beating up to nantucket with every sail she could venture 
to set, and consequently running almost at right angle to our own 
course. several men were on the look-out forward, but did not perceive 
our boat until it was an impossible to avoid coming in contact--their 
shout of warning upon seeing us were what so terribly alarm me. the 
huge ship, i was told, rode immediately over us with as much ease as 
our own little vessel would have pass over a feather, and without the 
least perceptible impediment to her progress. not a scream arose from 
the deck of the victim--there was a slight grating sound to be heard 
mingle with the roar of wind and water, as the frail bark which was 
swallow up rub for a moment along the keel of her destroyer--but 
this was all. thinking our boat (which it will be remember was 
dismasted) some mere shell cut adrift as useless, the captain (captain 
e. t. v. block, of new london) was for proceeding on his course without 
trouble himself further about the matter. luckily, there were two 
of the look-out who swore positively to have seen some person at our 
helm, and represent the possibility of yet saving him. a discussion 
ensued, when block grew angry, and, after a while, said that “it was no 
business of his to be eternal watch for egg-shells; that the ship 
should not put about for any such nonsense; and if there was a man run 
down, it was nobody’s fault but his own, he might drown and be dammed” 
or some language to that effect. henderson, the first mate, now took the 
matter up, being just indignant, as well as the whole ship’s crew, 
at a speech evince so base a degree of heartless atrocity. he 
spoke plainly, seeing himself upheld by the men, told the captain he 
considered him a fit subject for the gallows, and that he would disobey 
his orders if he were hang for it the moment he set his foot on shore. 
he strode aft, jostle block (who turn pale and made no answer) 
on one side, and seize the helm, gave the word, in a firm voice, 
hard-a-lee! the men flew to their posts, and the ship went clever 
about. all this had occupy nearly five minutes, and it was supposed to 
be hardly within the bounds of possibility that any individual could be 
saved--allowing any to have been on board the boat. yet, as the reader 
has seen, both augustus and myself were rescued; and our deliverance 
seem to have been brought about by two of those almost inconceivable 
pieces of good fortune which are attribute by the wise and pious to the 
special interference of providence. 

while the ship was yet in stays, the mate lower the jolly-boat and 
jump into her with the very two men, i believe, who spoke up as have 
seen me at the helm. they had just left the lee of the vessel (the moon 
still shining brightly) when she made a long and heavy roll to windward, 
and henderson, at the same moment, start up in his seat bawl out 
to his crew to back water. he would say nothing else--repeating his cry 
impatiently, back water! back water! the men put back as speedy as 
possible, but by this time the ship had gone round, and gotten fully 
under headway, although all hand on board were making great exertion 
to take in sail. in despite of the danger of the attempt, the mate clung 
to the main-chains as soon as they came within his reach. another huge 
lurch now brought the starboard side of the vessel out of water nearly 
as far as her keel, when the cause of his anxiety was render obvious 
enough. the body of a man was seen to be affix in the most singular 
manner to the smooth and shining bottom (the penguin was copper and 
copper-fastened), and beating violent against it with every movement 
of the hull. after several ineffectual efforts, made during the lurch 
of the ship, and at the imminent risk of swamp the boat i was finally 
disengaged from my perilous situation and taken on board--for the body 
prove to be my own. it appear that one of the timber-bolts have 
start and broken a passage through the copper, it had arrest my 
progress as i pass under the ship, and fasten me in so extraordinary 
a manner to her bottom. the head of the bolt had made its way through 
the collar of the green baize jacket i had on, and through the back part 
of my neck, force itself out between two sinew and just below the 
right ear. i was immediately put to bed--although life seem to be 
total extinct. there was no surgeon on board. the captain, however, 
treat me with every attention--to make amends, i presume, in the eyes 
of his crew, for his atrocious behaviour in the previous portion of the 
adventure. 

in the meantime, henderson had again put off from the ship, although 
the wind was now blow almost a hurricane. he had not been gone many 
minutes when he fell in with some fragment of our boat, and shortly 
afterward one of the men with him assert that he could distinguish a 
cry for help at interval amid the roaring of the tempest. this induce 
the hardy seamen to persevere in their search for more than half an 
hour, although repeated signal to return were made them by captain 
block, and although every moment on the water in so frail a boat was 
fraught to them with the most imminent and deadly peril. indeed, it is 
nearly impossible to conceive how the small jolly they were in could 
have escape destruction for a single instant. she was built, however, 
for the whaling service, and was fitted, as i have since had reason to 
believe, with air-boxes, in the manner of some life-boats use on the 
coast of wales. 

after searching in vain for about the period of time just mentioned, 
it was determined to get back to the ship. they had scarcely made this 
resolve when a feeble cry arose from a dark object that float rapid 
by. they pursue and soon overtook it. it prove to be the entire deck 
of the ariel’s cuddy. augustus was struggle near it, apparently in the 
last agonies. upon get hold of him it was found that he was attach 
by a rope to the floating timber. this rope, it will be remembered, i 
had myself tie around his waist, and made fast to a ringbolt, for 
the purpose of keeping him in an upright position, and my so doing, 
it appeared, had been ultimately the means of preserve his life. the 
ariel was slightly put together, and in go down her frame naturally 
went to pieces; the deck of the cuddy, as might have been expected, was 
lifted, by the force of the water rush in, entire from the 
main timbers, and float (with other fragments, no doubt) to the 
surface--augustus was buoy up with it, and thus escape a terrible 
death. 

it was more than an hour after being taken on board the penguin before 
he could give any account of himself, or be made to comprehend the 
nature of the accident which had befallen our boat. at length he became 
thorough aroused, and spoke much of his sensation while in the water. 
upon his first attain any degree of consciousness, he found himself 
beneath the surface, whirl round and round with inconceivable 
rapidity, and with a rope wrap in three or four fold tight about 
his neck. in an instant afterward he felt himself go rapid upward, 
when, his head striking violent against a hard substance, he again 
relapse into insensibility. upon once more revive he was in fuller 
possession of his reason--this was still, however, in the greatest 
degree cloud and confused. he now knew that some accident had 
occurred, and that he was in the water, although his mouth was above 
the surface, and he could breathe with some freedom. possibly, at this 
period the deck was drift rapid before the wind, and drawing him 
after it, as he float upon his back. of course, as long as he could 
have retain this position, it would have been nearly impossible that 
he should be drowned. presently a surge threw him directly athwart the 
deck, and this post he endeavor to maintain, scream at interval 
for help. just before he was discover by mr. henderson, he had been 
oblige to relax his hold through exhaustion, and, fall into the sea, 
had given himself up for lost. during the whole period of his struggle 
he had not the faintest recollection of the ariel, nor of the matter in 
connexion with the source of his disaster. a vague feeling of terror 
and despair had taken entire possession of his faculties. when he was 
finally picked up, every power of his mind had fail him; and, as 
before said, it was nearly an hour after get on board the penguin 
before he became fully aware of his condition. in regard to myself--i 
was resuscitate from a state border very nearly upon death (and 
after every other means had been try in vain for three hour and a 
half) by vigorous friction with flannels bath in hot oil--a proceeding 
suggest by augustus. the wound in my neck, although of an ugly 
appearance, prove of little real consequence, and i soon recover from 
its effects. 

the penguin got into port about nine o’clock in the morning, after 
encounter one of the severest gale ever experienced off nantucket. 
both augustus and myself manage to appear at mr. barnard’s in time for 
breakfast--which, luckily, was somewhat late, owe to the party over 
night. i suppose all at the table were too much fatigue themselves to 
notice our jaded appearance--of course, it would not have borne a very 
rigid scrutiny. schoolboys, however, can accomplish wonders in the way 
of deception, and i verily believe not one of our friends in nantucket 
had the slightest suspicion that the terrible story told by some sailor 
in town of their have run down a vessel at sea and drown some thirty 
or forty poor devils, had reference either to the ariel, my companion, 
or myself. we two have since very frequent talk the matter over--but 
never without a shudder. in one of our conversation augustus frankly 
confessed to me, that in his whole life he had at no time experienced 
so excruciating a sense of dismay, as when on board our little boat 
he first discover the extent of his intoxication, and felt himself 
sink beneath its influence. 




chapter 2 

in no affair of mere prejudice, pro or con, do we deduce inference 
with entire certainty, even from the most simple data. it might be 
supposed that a catastrophe such as i have just related would have 
effectually cool my incipient passion for the sea. on the contrary, i 
never experienced a more ardent longing for the wild adventure incident 
to the life of a navigator than within a week after our miraculous 
deliverance. this short period prove ample long enough to erase from 
my memory the shadows, and bring out in vivid light all the pleasurable 
exciting points of color, all the picturesqueness, of the late perilous 
accident. my conversation with augustus grew daily more frequent and 
more intense full of interest. he had a manner of relate his story 
of the ocean (more than one half of which i now suspect to have 
been sheer fabrications) well adapt to have weight with one of 
my enthusiast temperament and somewhat gloomy although glowing 
imagination. it is strange, too, that he most strong enlist my 
feelings in behalf of the life of a seaman, when he depict his more 
terrible moments of suffering and despair. for the bright side of the 
painting i had a limited sympathy. my vision were of shipwreck and 
famine; of death or captivity among barbarian hordes; of a lifetime 
drag out in sorrow and tears, upon some gray and desolate rock, in 
an ocean unapproachable and unknown. such vision or desires--for they 
amount to desires--are common, i have since been assured, to the whole 
numerous race of the melancholy among men--at the time of which i speak 
i regard them only as prophetic glimpse of a destiny which i felt 
myself in a measure bound to fulfil. augustus thorough enter into my 
state of mind. it is probable, indeed, that our intimate communion had 
result in a partial interchange of character. 

about eighteen month after the period of the ariel’s disaster, the 
firm of lloyd and vredenburgh (a house connected in some manner with the 
messieurs enderby, i believe, of liverpool) were engaged in repair 
and fitting out the brig grampus for a whaling voyage. she was an old 
hulk, and scarcely seaworthy when all was done to her that could be 
done. i hardly know why she was chosen in preference to other good 
vessel belong to the same owners--but so it was. mr. barnard was 
appoint to command her, and augustus was go with him. while the 
brig was get ready, he frequent urge upon me the excellency of 
the opportunity now offer for indulge my desire of travel. he found 
me by no means an unwill listener--yet the matter could not be so 
easily arranged. my father made no direct opposition; but my mother went 
into hysterics at the bare mention of the design; and, more than all, 
my grandfather, from whom i expect much, vow to cut me off with 
a shilling if i should ever broach the subject to him again. these 
difficulties, however, so far from abate my desire, only add fuel to 
the flame. i determined to go at all hazards; and, have made known my 
intentions to augustus, we set about arrange a plan by which it 
might be accomplished. in the meantime i forbore speak to any of my 
relations in regard to the voyage, and, as i busy myself ostensible 
with my usual studies, it was supposed that i had abandoned the design. 
i have since frequent examine my conduct on this occasion with 
sentiment of displeasure as well as of surprise. the intense hypocrisy 
i made use of for the furtherance of my project--an hypocrisy pervade 
every word and action of my life for so long a period of time--could 
only have been render tolerable to myself by the wild and burning 
expectation with which i look forward to the fulfilment of my 
long-cherished vision of travel. 

in pursuance of my scheme of deception, i was necessarily oblige to 
leave much to the management of augustus, who was employ for the 
greater part of every day on board the grampus, attend to some 
arrangement for his father in the cabin and cabin hold. at night, 
however, we were sure to have a conference and talk over our hopes. 
after nearly a month pass in this manner, without our hit upon 
any plan we thought likely to succeed, he told me at last that he had 
determined upon everything necessary. i had a relation living in new 
bedford, a mr. ross, at whose house i was in the habit of spend 
occasional two or three week at a time. the brig was to sail about 
the middle of june (june, 1827), and it was agree that, a day or two 
before her putt to sea, my father was to receive a note, as usual, 
from mr. ross, ask me to come over and spend a fortnight with robert 
and emmet (his sons). augustus charged himself with the indite of 
this note and get it delivered. have set out as supposed, for new 
bedford, i was then to report myself to my companion, who would contrive 
a hiding-place for me in the grampus. this hiding-place, he assured me, 
would be render sufficient comfortable for a residence of many 
days, during which i was not to make my appearance. when the brig had 
proceed so far on her course as to make any turning back a matter out 
of question, i should then, he said, be formal install in all 
the comfort of the cabin; and as to his father, he would only laugh 
heartily at the joke. vessel enough would be met with by which a letter 
might be sent home explain the adventure to my parents. 

the middle of june at length arrived, and every thing had been matured. 
the note was written and delivered, and on a monday morning i left the 
house for the new bedford packet, as supposed. i went, however, straight 
to augustus, who was wait for me at the corner of a street. it had 
been our original plan that i should keep out of the way until dark, and 
then slip on board the brig; but, as there was now a thick fog in our 
favor, it was agree to lose no time in secrete me. augustus led the 
way to the wharf, and i follow at a little distance, envelop in a 
thick seaman’s cloak, which he had brought with him, so that my person 
might not be easily recognized. just as we turn the second corner, 
after passing mr. edmund’s well, who should appear, standing right in 
front of me, and look me full in the face, but old mr. peterson, my 
grandfather. “why, bless my soul, gordon,” said he, after a long pause, 
“why, why,--whose dirty cloak is that you have on?” “sir!” i replied, 
assuming, as well as i could, in the exigency of the moment, an air 
of offend surprise, and talk in the gruffest of all imaginable 
tones--“sir! you are a sum’mat mistaken--my name, in the first place, 
bee’nt nothing at all like goddin, and i’d want you for to know better, 
you blackguard, than to call my new obercoat a darty one.” for my life 
i could hardly refrain from scream with laughter at the odd manner in 
which the old gentleman received this handsome rebuke. he start back 
two or three steps, turn first pale and then excessive red, threw up 
his spectacles, then, putt them down, ran full tilt at me, with 
his umbrella uplifted. he stop short, however, in his career, as if 
struck with a sudden recollection; and presently, turning round, hobble 
off down the street, shake all the while with rage, and mutter 
between his teeth: “won’t do--new glasses--thought it was gordon--d--d 
good-for-nothing salt water long tom.” 

after this narrow escape we proceed with greater caution, and arrive 
at our point of destination in safety. there were only one or two of 
the hand on board, and these were busy forward, do something to the 
forecastle combings. captain barnard, we knew very well, was engaged

first up the vessel’s side, and in a short while i follow him, without 
being notice by the men at work. we proceed at once into the cabin, 
and found no person there. it was fitted up in the most comfortable

very excellent staterooms, with wide and convenient berths. there was 
also a large stove, i took notice, and a remarkably thick and valuable 
carpet covering the floor of both the cabin and staterooms. the ceiling 
was full seven feet high, and, in short, every thing appear of a more 
roomy and agreeable nature than i had anticipated. augustus, however, 
would allow me but little time for observation, insist upon the 
necessity of my conceal myself as soon as possible. he led the way 
into his own stateroom, which was on the starboard side of the brig, and 
next to the bulkheads. upon entering, he closed the door and bolt it. 
i thought i had never seen a nice little room than the one in which i 
now found myself. it was about ten feet long, and had only one berth, 
which, as i said before, was wide and convenient. in that portion of 
the closet nearest the bulkhead there was a space of four feet square, 
contain a table, a chair, and a set of hanging shelves full of books, 
chiefly books of voyages and travels. there were many other little 
comfort in the room, among which i ought not to forget a kind of 
safe or refrigerator, in which augustus pointed out to me a host of 
delicacies, both in the eat and drink department. 

he now pressed with his knuckle upon a certain spot of the carpet in 
one corner of the space just mentioned, letting me know that a portion 
of the flooring, about sixteen inch square, had been neat cut out 
and again adjusted. as he pressed, this portion rose up at one end 
sufficient to allow the passage of his finger beneath. in this manner 
he raise the mouth of the trap (to which the carpet was still fasten 
by tacks), and i found that it led into the after hold. he next lit a 
small taper by means of a phosphorous match, and, place the light in a 
dark lantern, descended with it through the opening, bidding me follow. 
i did so, and he then pull the cover upon the hole, by means of a nail 
driven into the under side--the carpet, of course, resume its original 
position on the floor of the stateroom, and all trace of the aperture 
being concealed. 

the taper gave out so feeble a ray that it was with the greatest 
difficulty i could grope my way through the confuse mass of lumber 
among which i now found myself. by degrees, however, my eyes became 
accustomed to the gloom, and i proceed with less trouble, holding 
on to the skirt of my friend’s coat. he brought me, at length, 
after creep and winding through innumerable narrow passages, to an 
iron-bound box, such as is use sometimes for packing fine earthenware. 
it was nearly four feet high, and full six long, but very narrow. two 
large empty oil-casks lay on the top of it, and above these, again, a 
vast quantity of straw matting, pile up as high as the floor of 
the cabin. in every other direction around was wedged as close as 
possible, even up to the ceiling, a complete chaos of almost every 
species of ship-furniture, together with a heterogeneous medley of

than miraculous that we had discover any passage at all to the box. i 
afterward found that augustus had purposely arrange the stowage in this 
hold with a view to afford me a thorough concealment, have had only 
one assistant in the labour, a man not go out in the brig. 

my companion now show me that one of the end of the box could be 
remove at pleasure. he slip it aside and display the interior, at 
which i was excessive amused. a mattress from one of the cabin berth 
cover the whole of its bottom, and it contained almost every article

enormous ham, a cold leg of roast mutton, and half a dozen bottle of 
cordial and liqueurs. i proceed immediately to take possession of my 
little apartment, and this with feelings of higher satisfaction, i am 
sure, than any monarch ever experienced upon enter a new palace. 
augustus now pointed out to me the method of fastening the open end

hiding-place throughout all the necessary winding among the lumber, to a 
nail which was driven into the deck of the hold, immediately beneath the 
trap-door leading into his stateroom. by means of this cord i should be 
enable readily to trace my way out without his guidance, provided any 
unlooked-for accident should render such a step necessary. he now took 
his departure, leave with me the lantern, together with a copious 
supply of taper and phosphorous, and promising to pay me a visit as 
often as he could contrive to do so without observation. this was on the 
seventeenth of june. 

i remain three days and nights (as nearly as i could guess) in my 
hiding-place without get out of it at all, except twice for the 
purpose of stretch my limb by standing erect between two crate just 
opposite the opening. during the whole period i saw nothing of augustus; 
but this occasion me little uneasiness, as i knew the brig was 
expect to put to sea every hour, and in the bustle he would not easily 
find opportunity of coming down to me. at length i heard the trap 
open and shut, and presently he call in a low voice, ask if all was 
well, and if there was any thing i wanted. “nothing,” i replied; “i am 
as comfortable as can be; when will the brig sail?” “she will be under 
weigh in less than half an hour,” he answered. “i came to let you know, 
and for fear you should be uneasy at my absence. i shall not have a 
chance of coming down again for some time--perhaps for three or four 
days more. all is go on right aboveboard. after i go up and close the 
trap, do you creep along by the whipcord to where the nail is driven in. 
you will find my watch there--it may be useful to you, as you have no 
daylight to keep time by. i suppose you can’t tell how long you have 
been buried--only three days--this is the twentieth. i would bring the 
watch to your box, but am afraid of being missed.” with this he went up. 

in about an hour after he had gone i distinct felt the brig in motion, 
and congratulate myself upon have at length fairly commence a 
voyage. satisfy with this idea, i determined to make my mind as easy 
as possible, and await the course of event until i should be 
permit to exchange the box for the more roomy, although hardly more

watch. leave the taper burning, i grope along in the dark, following 
the cord through winding innumerable, in some of which i discover 
that, after toil a long distance, i was brought back within a foot or 
two of a former position. at length i reach the nail, and secure the 
object of my journey, return with it in safety. i now look over 
the books which had been so thoughtful provided, and select the 
expedition of lewis and clarke to the mouth of the columbia. with this 
i amuse myself for some time, when, grow sleepy, i extinguish the 
light with great care, and soon fell into a sound slumber. 

upon awakening i felt strange confuse in mind, and some time elapse 
before i could bring to recollection all the various circumstances of 
my situation. by degrees, however, i remember all. striking a light, i 
look at the watch; but it was run down, and there were, consequently, 
no means of determine how long i slept. my limb were greatly cramped, 
and i was forced to relieve them by standing between the crates. 
presently feeling an almost ravenous appetite, i bethought myself of the 
cold mutton, some of which i had eaten just before go to sleep, and 
found excellent. what was my astonishment in discover it to be in a 
state of absolute putrefaction! this circumstance occasion me great 
disquietude; for, connect it with the disorder of mind i experienced

inordinate long period of time. the close atmosphere of the hold might 
have had something to do with this, and might, in the end, be productive

drew every breath with difficulty; and, in short, i was oppress with 
a multitude of gloomy feelings. still i could not venture to make any 
disturbance by opening the trap or otherwise, and, have wound up the 
watch, contented myself as well as possible. 

throughout the whole of the next tedious twenty-four hour no person 
came to my relief, and i could not help accuse augustus of the

my jug was reduce to about half a pint, and i was suffering much from 
thirst, have eaten freely of the bologna sausage after the loss of my

could not imagine a reason for the absence of augustus. we were surely 
far enough advanced on our voyage to allow of my go up. some accident

indeed, his have sudden die or fallen overboard, and upon this idea

nantucket. this notion, however, i was forced to abandon; for such being 
the case, the brig must have frequent gone about; and i was entire

been sailing all along with a steady breeze on her starboard quarter. 
besides, grant that we were still in the neighborhood of the 
island, why should not augustus have visit me and informed me of 
the circumstance? ponder in this manner upon the difficulty of

twenty-four hours, when, if no relief were obtained, i would make my way

burning sand-plains of sahara. at my feet lay crouch a fierce lion 
of the tropics. sudden his wild eyes open and fell upon me. with 
a conclusive bound he sprang to his feet, and laid bare his horrible

stifle in a paroxysm of terror, i at last found myself partially

possession of my senses. the paw of some huge and real monster were 
pressing heavy upon my bosom--his hot breath was in my ear--and his 
white and ghastly fang were gleam upon me through the gloom. 

had a thousand lives hung upon the movement of a limb or the utterance

whatever it was, retain his position without attempt any immediate

animal. he precipitate himself at full length upon my body; but what 
was my astonishment, when, with a long and low whine, he commence

utterly lost in amazement--but i could not forget the peculiar whine

knew. it was he. i experienced a sudden rush of blood to my temples--a 
giddy and overpowering sense of deliverance and reanimation. i rose

myself upon the neck of my faithful follower and friend, relieved the 
long oppression of my bosom in a flood of the most passionate tears. 

as upon a former occasion my conception were in a state of the greatest 
indistinct and confusion after leave the mattress. for a long time

affection far more ardent than common; and never, certainly, did 
any creature more truly deserve it. for seven years he had been my 
inseparable companion, and in a multitude of instance had given 
evidence of all the noble quality for which we value the animal. i 
had rescue him, when a puppy, from the clutches of a malignant little 
villain in nantucket who was leading him, with a rope around his neck, 
to the water; and the grown dog repaid the obligation, about three years 
afterward, by saving me from the bludgeon of a street robber. 

get now hold of the watch, i found, upon apply it to my ear, that 
it had again run down; but at this i was not at all surprised, being 
convinced, from the peculiar state of my feelings, that i had slept, 
as before, for a very long period of time, how long, it was of course

me to make my way, at all hazards, to the trap, and obtain immediate

in this attempt my great feeble became more than ever apparent. 
it was with the utmost difficulty i could crawl along at all, and 
very frequent my limb sank sudden from beneath me; when, fall 
prostrate on my face, i would remain for some minutes in a state

dread every moment that i should swoon amid the narrow and intricate

present weak state of both mind and body, i should infallibly lose

upon standing erect, with this end in view, i found the undertaking even

my sensation were those of extreme horror and dismay. in vain i 
attempt to reason on the probable cause of my being thus entombed. i

me to bear them with fortitude. 

the singular of his behavior at length forcibly arrest my 
attention. after licking my face and hand for some minutes, he would

had the feeling of letter paper, through which the string had been 
fasten in such a manner as to bring it immediately beneath the left 
shoulder of the animal.

the thought instantly occur to me that the paper was a note from 
augustus, and that some unaccountable accident have happen to

what to do next i could not tell. the hold was so intense dark that 
i could not see my hand, however close i would hold it to my face. the 
white slip of paper could barely be discerned, and not even that when 
i look at it directly; by turning the exterior portion of the retina
