phosphorus, before the thought suggest itself that i had examine only 
one side of the paper. i shall not attempt to describe my feelings 
of rage (for i believe i was more angry than any thing else) when the 
egregious oversight i had committed flash sudden upon my perception. 
the blunder itself would have been unimportant, had not my own folly and 
impetuos render it otherwise--in my disappointment at not finding 
some words upon the slip, i had childish torn it in pieces and thrown 
it away, it was impossible to say where. 

from the worst part of this dilemma i was relieved by the sagacity of 
tiger. have got, after a long search, a small piece of the note, i put 
it to the dog’s nose, and endeavor to make him understand that he must 
bring me the rest of it. to my astonishment, (for i had taught him none 
of the usual trick for which his breed are famous,) he seem to enter 
at once into my meaning, and, rummage about for a few moments, soon 
found another considerable portion. bring me this, he pause awhile, 
and, rubbing his nose against my hand, appear to be wait for 
my approval of what he had done. i pat him on the head, when he 
immediately made off again. it was now some minutes before he came 
back--but when he did come, he brought with him a large slip, which 
prove to be all the paper missing--it have been torn, it seems, 
only into three pieces. luckily, i had no trouble in finding what few 
fragment of the phosphorus were left--being guide by the indistinct 
glow one or two of the particle still emitted. my difficulty had 
taught me the necessity of caution, and i now took time to reflect upon 
what i was about to do. it was very probable, i considered, that some 
words were written upon that side of the paper which had not been 
examined--but which side was that? fitting the pieces together gave me 
no clew in this respect, although it assured me that the words (if there 
were any) would be found all on one side, and connected in a proper 
manner, as written. there was the greater necessity of ascertain the 
point in question beyond a doubt, as the phosphorus remain would be 
altogether insufficient for a third attempt, should i fail in the one i 
was now about to make. i placed the paper on a book as before, and sat 
for some minutes thoughtful revolving the matter over in my mind. at 
last i thought it barely possible that the written side might have 
some uneven on its surface, which a delicate sense of feeling might 
enable me to detect. i determined to make the experiment and pass 
my finger very careful over the side which first present itself. 
nothing, however, was perceptible, and i turn the paper, adjust it 
on the book. i now again carry my forefinger cautious along, when 
i was aware of an exceedingly slight, but still discern glow, which 
follow as it proceeded. this, i knew, must arise from some very minute 
remain particle of the phosphorus with which i had cover the paper 
in my previous attempt. the other, or under side, then, was that on 
which lay the writing, if writing there should finally prove to be. 
again i turn the note, and went to work as i had previous done. 
have rub in the phosphorus, a brilliancy ensue as before--but this 
time several lines of ms. in a large hand, and apparently in red ink, 
became distinct visible. the glimmer, although sufficient bright, 
was but momentary. still, had i not been too greatly excited, there 
would have been ample time enough for me to peruse the whole three 
sentence before me--for i saw there were three. in my anxiety, however, 
to read all at once, i succeed only in reading the seven conclude 
words, which thus appeared--“blood--your life depend upon lie close.” 

had i been able to ascertain the entire contents of the note--the full 
meaning of the admonition which my friend had thus attempt to convey, 
that admonition, even although it should have reveal a story of 
disaster the most unspeakable, could not, i am firm convinced, have 
imbue my mind with one tithe of the harrowing and yet indefinable 
horror with which i was inspired by the fragmentary warning thus 
received. and “blood,” too, that word of all words--so rife at all times 
with mystery, and suffering, and terror--how treble full of import did 
it now appear--how chilly and heavy (disjointed, as it thus was, from 
any foregoing words to qualify or render it distinct) did its vague 
syllable fall, amid the deep gloom of my prison, into the innermost 
recess of my soul! 

augustus had, undoubtedly, good reason for wish me to remain 
concealed, and i form a thousand surmise as to what they could 
be--but i could think of nothing afford a satisfactory solution of 
the mystery. just after return from my last journey to the trap, and 
before my attention had been otherwise direct by the singular conduct 
of tiger, i had come to the resolution of making myself heard at all 
event by those on board, or, if i could not succeed in this directly, 
of try to cut my way through the orlop deck. the half certainty which 
i felt of being able to accomplish one of these two purpose in the last 
emergency, had given me courage (which i should not otherwise have had) 
to endure the evil of my situation. the few words i had been able to 
read, however, had cut me off from these final resources, and i now, for 
the first time, felt all the misery of my fate. in a paroxysm of despair 
i threw myself again upon the mattress, where, for about the period of 
a day and night, i lay in a kind of stupor, relieved only by momentary 
interval of reason and recollection. 

at length i once more arose, and busy myself in reflection upon the 
horrors which encompass me. for another twenty-four hour it was 
barely possible that i might exist without water--for a long time i 
could not do so. during the first portion of my imprison i had made 
free use of the cordial with which augustus had supply me, but they 
only serve to excite fever, without in the least degree assuage 
thirst. i had now only about a gill left, and this was of a species of 
strong peach liqueur at which my stomach revolted. the sausage were 
entire consumed; of the ham nothing remain but a small piece of the 
skin; and all the biscuit, except a few fragment of one, had been eaten 
by tiger. to add to my troubles, i found that my headache was increase 
momentarily, and with it the species of delirium which had distress me 
more or less since my first fall asleep. for some hour past it had 
been with the greatest difficulty i could breathe at all, and now each 
attempt at so do was attend with the most depress spasmodic 
action of the chest. but there was still another and very different 
source of disquietude, and one, indeed, whose harass terror had 
been the chief means of arouse me to exertion from my stupor on the 
mattress. it arose from the demeanor of the dog. 

i first observe an alteration in his conduct while rubbing in the 
phosphorus on the paper in my last attempt. as i rubbed, he ran his nose 
against my hand with a slight snarl; but i was too greatly excited at 
the time to pay much attention to the circumstance. soon afterward, 
it will be remembered, i threw myself on the mattress, and fell into 
a species of lethargy. presently i became aware of a singular hiss 
sound close at my ears, and discover it to proceed from tiger, who was 
pant and wheeze in a state of the greatest apparent excitement, his 
eyeball flash fierce through the gloom. i spoke to him, when he 
reply with a low growl, and then remain quiet. presently i relapse 
into my stupor, from which i was again awaken in a similar manner. 
this was repeated three or four times, until finally his behaviour 
inspired me with so great a degree of fear, that i became fully aroused. 
he was now lie close by the door of the box, snarl fearfully, 
although in a kind of undertone, and grind his teeth as if strong 
convulsed. i had no doubt whatever that the want of water or the 
confine atmosphere of the hold had driven him mad, and i was at a loss 
what course to pursue. i could not endure the thought of killing him, 
yet it seem absolutely necessary for my own safety. i could distinct 
perceive his eyes fasten upon me with an expression of the most deadly 
animosity, and i expect every instant that he would attack me. at last 
i could endure my terrible situation no longer, and determined to make 
my way from the box at all hazards, and dispatch him, if his opposition 
should render it necessary for me to do so. to get out, i had to 
pass directly over his body, and he already seem to anticipate my 
design--missing himself upon his fore-legs (as i perceive by the 
alter position of his eyes), and display the whole of his white 
fangs, which were easily discernible. i took the remains of the 
ham-skin, and the bottle contain the liqueur, and secure them about 
my person, together with a large carving-knife which augustus had left 
me--then, fold my cloak around me as close as possible, i made a 
movement toward the mouth of the box. no soon did i do this, than the 
dog sprang with a loud growl toward my throat. the whole weight of his 
body struck me on the right shoulder, and i fell violent to the left, 
while the enrage animal pass entire over me. i had fallen upon my 
knees, with my head bury among the blankets, and these protect 
me from a second furious assault, during which i felt the sharp teeth 
pressing vigorous upon the woollen which envelop my neck--yet, 
luckily, without being able to penetrate all the folds. i was now 
beneath the dog, and a few moments would place me completely in his 
power. despair gave me strength, and i rose bold up, shake him from 
me by main force, and drag with me the blanket from the mattress. 
these i now threw over him, and before he could extricate himself, i had 
got through the door and closed it effectually against his pursuit. 
in this struggle, however, i had been forced to drop the morsel of 
ham-skin, and i now found my whole stock of provisions reduce to a 
single gill of liqueur. as this reflection cross my mind, i felt 
myself actuate by one of those fit of perverse which might be 
supposed to influence a spoil child in similar circumstances, and, 
raise the bottle to my lips, i drain it to the last drop, and dashed 
it furiously upon the floor. 

scarcely had the echo of the crash die away, when i heard my name 
pronounced in an eager but subdued voice, issue from the direction of 
the steerage. so unexpected was anything of the kind, and so intense was 
the emotion excited within me by the sound, that i endeavour in vain 
to reply. my powers of speech total failed, and in an agony of terror 
lest my friend should conclude me dead, and return without attempt 
to reach me, i stood up between the crate near the door of the box, 
tremble convulsively, and gasp and struggle for utterance. had 
a thousand words depend upon a syllable, i could not have spoken 
it. there was a slight movement now audible among the lumber somewhere 
forward of my station. the sound presently grew less distinct, then 
again less so, and still less. shall i ever forget my feelings at this 
moment? he was going--my friend, my companion, from whom i had a right 
to expect so much--he was going--he would abandon me--he was gone! he 
would leave me to perish miserably, to expire in the most horrible and 
loathesome of dungeons--and one word, one little syllable, would save 
me--yet that single syllable i could not utter! i felt, i am sure, more 
than ten thousand times the agony of death itself. my brain reeled, 
and i fell, deadly sick, against the end of the box. 

as i fell the carving-knife was shaken out from the waist-band of my 
pantaloons, and drop with a rattling sound to the floor. never did 
any strain of the richest melody come so sweet to my ears! with the 
intensest anxiety i listen to ascertain the effect of the noise upon 
augustus--for i knew that the person who call my name could be no one 
but himself. all was silent for some moments. at length i again heard 
the word “arthur!” repeated in a low tone, and one full of hesitation. 
revive hope loosen at once my powers of speech, and i now scream 
at the top of my voice, “augustus! oh, augustus!” “hush! for god’s sake 
be silent!” he replied, in a voice tremble with agitation; “i will be 
with you immediately--as soon as i can make my way through the hold.” 
for a long time i heard him moving among the lumber, and every moment 
seem to me an age. at length i felt his hand upon my shoulder, and he 
placed, at the same moment, a bottle of water to my lips. those only who 
have been sudden redeem from the jaws of the tomb, or who have known 
the insufferable torment of thirst under circumstances as aggravate as 
those which encompass me in my dreary prison, can form any idea of the 
unutterable transport which that one long draught of the richest of all 
physical luxury afforded. 

when i had in some degree satisfy my thirst, augustus produce from 
his pocket three or four boil potatoes, which i devour with the 
greatest avidity. he had brought with him a light in a dark lantern, and 
the grateful ray afford me scarcely less comfort than the food and 
drink. but i was impatient to learn the cause of his protract absence, 
and he proceed to recount what had happen on board during my 
incarceration. 




chapter 4 

the brig put to sea, as i had supposed, in about an hour after he had 
left the watch. this was on the twentieth of june. it will be remember 
that i had then been in the hold for three days; and, during this 
period, there was so constant a bustle on board, and so much running 
to and fro, especially in the cabin and staterooms, that he had had no 
chance of visiting me without the risk of have the secret of the trap 
discovered. when at length he did come, i had assured him that i was 
do as well as possible; and, therefore, for the two next days he 
felt but little uneasy on my account--still, however, watch an 
opportunity of go down. it was not until the fourth day that he found 
one. several times during this interval he had made up his mind to let 
his father know of the adventure, and have me come up at once; but we 
were still within reach distance of nantucket, and it was doubtful, 
from some expression which had escape captain barnard, whether he 
would not immediately put back if he discover me to be on board. 
besides, upon thinking the matter over, augustus, so he told me, could 
not imagine that i was in immediate want, or that i would hesitate, 
in such case, to make myself heard at the trap. when, therefore, he 
considered everything he conclude to let me stay until he could meet 
with an opportunity of visiting me unobserved. this, as i said before, 
did not occur until the fourth day after his bring me the watch, 
and the seventh since i had first enter the hold. he then went down 
without taking with him any water or provisions, intend in the first 
place merely to call my attention, and get me to come from the box to 
the trap,--when he would go up to the stateroom and thence hand me down 
a supply. when he descended for this purpose he found that i was asleep, 
for it seem that i was snore very loudly. from all the calculation 
i can make on the subject, this must have been the slumber into which 
i fell just after my return from the trap with the watch, and which, 
consequently, must have last for more than three entire days and 
nights at the very least. latterly, i have had reason both from my own 
experience and the assurance of others, to be acquaint with the strong 
soporific effects of the stench arise from old fish-oil when close 
confined; and when i think of the condition of the hold in which i was 
imprisoned, and the long period during which the brig had been use as a 
whaling vessel, i am more inclined to wonder that i awoke at all, after 
once fall asleep, than that i should have slept uninterrupted for 
the period specify above. 

augustus call to me at first in a low voice and without close the 
trap--but i made him no reply. he then shut the trap, and spoke to me in 
a louder, and finally in a very loud tone--still i continue to snore. 
he was now at a loss what to do. it would take him some time to make his 
way through the lumber to my box, and in the meanwhile his absence would 
be notice by captain barnard, who had occasion for his service every 
minute, in arrange and copy papers connected with the business of 
the voyage. he determined, therefore, upon reflection, to ascend, and 
await another opportunity of visiting me. he was the more easily induce 
to this resolve, as my slumber appear to be of the most tranquil 
nature, and he could not suppose that i had undergone any inconvenience 
from my incarceration. he had just made up his mind on these points 
when his attention was arrest by an unusual bustle, the sound of 
which proceed apparently from the cabin. he sprang through the trap 
as quick as possible, closed it, and threw open the door of his 
stateroom. no soon had he put his foot over the threshold than a 
pistol flash in his face, and he was knock down, at the same moment, 
by a blow from a handspike. 

a strong hand held him on the cabin floor, with a tight grasp upon 
his throat; still he was able to see what was go on around him. 
his father was tie hand and foot, and lie along the steps of the 
companion-way, with his head down, and a deep wound in the forehead, 
from which the blood was flowing in a continue stream. he spoke not a 
word, and was apparently dying. over him stood the first mate, eye 
him with an expression of fiendish derision, and deliberate searching 
his pockets, from which he presently drew forth a large wallet and a 
chronometer. seven of the crew (among whom was the cook, a negro) were 
rummage the stateroom on the larboard for arms, where they soon 
equip themselves with musket and ammunition. besides augustus and 
captain barnard, there were nine men altogether in the cabin, and these 
among the most ruffianly of the brig’s company. the villain now went 
upon deck, taking my friend with them after have secure his arms 
behind his back. they proceed straight to the forecastle, which was 
fasten down--two of the mutineer standing by it with axes--two also 
at the main hatch. the mate call out in a loud voice: “do you hear 
there below? tumble up with you, one by one--now, mark that--and no 
grumbling!” it was some minutes before any one appeared:--at last an 
englishman, who had ship as a raw hand, came up, weeping piteously, 
and entreat the mate, in the most humble manner, to spare his life. 
the only reply was a blow on the forehead from an axe. the poor fellow 
fell to the deck without a groan, and the black cook lift him up in 
his arms as he would a child, and toss him deliberate into the sea. 
hearing the blow and the plunge of the body, the men below could now 
be induce to venture on deck neither by threat nor promises, until a 
proposition was made to smoke them out. a general rush then ensued, 
and for a moment it seem possible that the brig might be retaken. 
the mutineers, however, succeed at last in close the forecastle 
effectually before more than six of their opponent could get up. 
these six, finding themselves so greatly outnumber and without arms, 
submit after a brief struggle. the mate gave them fair words--no 
doubt with a view of induce those below to yield, for they had no 
difficulty in hearing all that was said on deck. the result prove his 
sagacity, no less than his diabolical villainy. all in the forecastle 
presently signify their intention of submitting, and, ascend one 
by one, were pinion and then thrown on their backs, together with the 
first six--there being in all, of the crew who were not concerned in the 
mutiny, twenty-seven. 

a scene of the most horrible butchery ensued. the bound seamen were 
drag to the gangway. here the cook stood with an axe, striking each 
victim on the head as he was forced over the side of the vessel by the 
other mutineers. in this manner twenty-two perished, and augustus had 
given himself up for lost, expect every moment his own turn to come 
next. but it seem that the villain were now either weary, or in 
some measure disgust with their bloody labour; for the four remain 
prisoners, together with my friend, who had been thrown on the deck with 
the rest, were respite while the mate sent below for rum, and the whole 
murderous party held a drunken carouse, which last until sunset. they 
now fell to dispute in regard to the fate of the survivors, who lay 
not more than four pace off, and could distinguish every word said. 
upon some of the mutineer the liquor appear to have a soften 
effect, for several voice were heard in favor of release the captive 
altogether, on condition of join the mutiny and share the profits. 
the black cook, however (who in all respects was a perfect demon, 
and who seem to exert as much influence, if not more, than the 
mate himself), would listen to no proposition of the kind, and rose 
repeatedly for the purpose of resume his work at the gangway. 
fortunately he was so far overcome by intoxicate as to be easily 
restrained by the less bloodthirsty of the party, among whom was a 
line-manager, who went by the name of dirk peters. this man was the 
son of an indian squaw of the tribe of upsarokas, who live among the 
fastness of the black hills, near the source of the missouri. his 
father was a fur-trader, i believe, or at least connected in some manner 
with the indian trading-posts on lewis river. peters himself was one of 
the most ferocious-looking men i ever beheld. he was short in stature, 
not more than four feet eight inch high, but his limb were of 
herculean mould. his hands, especially, were so enormously thick and 
broad as hardly to retain a human shape. his arms, as well as legs, 
were bowed in the most singular manner, and appear to possess no 
flexible whatever. his head was equally deformed, being of immense 
size, with an indentation on the crown (like that on the head of most 
negroes), and entire bald. to conceal this latter deficiency, which 
did not proceed from old age, he usually wore a wig form of any 
hair-like material which present itself--occasionally the skin of a 
spain dog or america grizzly bear. at the time spoken of, he had on a 
portion of one of these bearskins; and it add no little to the natural 
ferocity of his countenance, which betook of the upsaroka character. the 
mouth extend nearly from ear to ear, the lip were thin, and seemed, 
like some other portion of his frame, to be devoid of natural pliancy, 
so that the ruling expression never varied under the influence of any 
emotion whatever. this ruling expression may be conceive when it is 
considered that the teeth were exceedingly long and protruding, and 
never even partially covered, in any instance, by the lips. to pass this 
man with a casual glance, one might imagine him to be convulse with 
laughter, but a second look would induce a shudder acknowledgment, 
that if such an expression were indicative of merriment, the merriment 
must be that of a demon. of this singular being many anecdote were 
prevalent among the seafaring men of nantucket. these anecdote went to 
prove his prodigious strength when under excitement, and some of them 
had given rise to a doubt of his sanity. but on board the grampus, it 
seems, he was regarded, at the time of the mutiny, with feelings more of 
derision than of anything else. i have been thus particular in speak 
of dirk peters, because, ferocious as he appeared, he prove the main 
instrument in preserve the life of augustus, and because i shall 
have frequent occasion to mention him hereafter in the course of my 
narrative--a narrative, let me here say, which, in its latter portions, 
will be found to include incident of a nature so entire out of the 
range of human experience, and for this reason so far beyond the limit 
of human credulity, that i proceed in utter hopeless of obtain 
credence for all that i shall tell, yet confident trust in time 
and progress science to verify some of the most important and most 
improbable of my statements. 

after much indecision and two or three violent quarrels, it was 
determined at last that all the prisoner (with the exception of 
augustus, whom peters insist in a jocular manner upon keeping as his 
clerk) should be set adrift in one of the smallest whaleboats. the 
mate went down into the cabin to see if captain barnard was still 
living--for, it will be remembered, he was left below when the mutineer 
came up. presently the two made their appearance, the captain pale as 
death, but somewhat recover from the effects of his wound. he spoke 
to the men in a voice hardly articulate, entreat them not to set him 
adrift, but to return to their duty, and promising to land them wherever 
they chose, and to take no steps for bring them to justice. he might 
as well have spoken to the winds. two of the ruffian seize him by the 
arms and hurl him over the brig’s side into the boat, which had been 
lower while the mate went below. the four men who were lie on the 
deck were then untie and ordered to follow, which they did without 
attempt any resistance--augustus being still left in his painful 
position, although he struggle and pray only for the poor 
satisfaction of being permit to bid his father farewell. a handful of 
sea-biscuit and a jug of water were now hand down; but neither mast, 
sail, oar, nor compass. the boat was tow astern for a few minutes, 
during which the mutineer held another consultation--it was then 
finally cut adrift. by this time night had come on--there were neither 
moon nor stars visible--and a short and ugly sea was running, although 
there was no great deal of wind. the boat was instantly out of sight, 
and little hope could be entertain for the unfortunate sufferer who 
were in it. this event happened, however, in latitude 35 degree 30’ 
north, longitude 61 degree 20’ west, and consequently at no very great 
distance from the bermuda islands. augustus therefore endeavor to 
console himself with the idea that the boat might either succeed in 
reach the land, or come sufficient near to be fallen in with by 
vessel off the coast. 

all sail was now put upon the brig, and she continue her original 
course to the southwest--the mutineer being bent upon some pirate 
expedition, in which, from all that could be understood, a ship was to 
be intercept on her way from the cape verd island to porto rico. no 
attention was paid to augustus, who was untie and suffer to go about 
anywhere forward of the cabin companion-way. dirk peters treat him 
with some degree of kindness, and on one occasion save him from 
the brutality of the cook. his situation was still one of the most 
precarious, as the men were continual intoxicated, and there was no 
rely upon their continue good-humor or careless in regard to 
himself. his anxiety on my account he represented, however, as the most 
distressing result of his condition; and, indeed, i had never reason to 
doubt the sincerity of his friendship. more than once he had resolve 
to acquaint the mutineer with the secret of my being on board, but was 
restrained from so doing, partly through recollection of the atrocity 
he had already beheld, and partly through a hope of being able soon to 
bring me relief. for the latter purpose he was constant on the watch; 
but, in spite of the most constant vigilance, three days elapse after 
the boat was cut adrift before any chance occurred. at length, on the 
night of the third day, there came on a heavy blow from the eastward, 
and all hand were call up to take in sail. during the confusion which 
ensued, he made his way below unobserved, and into the stateroom. 
what was his grief and horror in discover that the latter had 
been render a place of deposit for a variety of sea-stores and 
ship-furniture, and that several fathom of old chain-cable, which had 
been stow away beneath the companion-ladder, had been drag thence 
to make room for a chest, and were now lie immediately upon the trap! 
to remove it without discovery was impossible, and he return on 
deck as quick as he could. as he came up, the mate seize him by the 
throat, and demanding what he had been do in the cabin, was about 
fling him over the larboard bulwark, when his life was again 
preserve through the interference of dirk peters. augustus was now put 
in handcuffs (of which there were several pair on board), and his feet 
lash tight together. he was then taken into the steerage, and thrown 
into a lower berth next to the forecastle bulkheads, with the assurance 
that he should never put his foot on deck again “until the brig was no 
long a brig.” this was the expression of the cook, who threw him into 
the berth--it is hardly possible to say what precise meaning intended by 
the phrase. the whole affair, however, prove the ultimate means of my 
relief, as will presently appear. 




chapter 5 

for some minutes after the cook had left the forecastle, augustus 
abandoned himself to despair, never hope to leave the berth alive. 
he now came to the resolution of acquaint the first of the men who 
should come down with my situation, thinking it better to let me take my 
chance with the mutineer than perish of thirst in the hold,--for it had 
been ten days since i was first imprisoned, and my jug of water was not 
a plentiful supply even for four. as he was thinking on this subject, 
the idea came all at once into his head that it might be possible 
to communicate with me by the way of the main hold. in any other 
circumstances, the difficulty and hazard of the undertaking would have 
prevent him from attempt it; but now he had, at all events, little 
prospect of life, and consequently little to lose, he bent his whole 
mind, therefore, upon the task. 

his handcuffs were the first consideration. at first he saw no method 
of remove them, and fear that he should thus be baffle in the very 
outset; but upon a close scrutiny he discover that the irons could 
be slip off and on at pleasure, with very little effort or 
inconvenience, merely by squeeze his hand through them,--this species 
of manacle being altogether ineffectual in confine young persons, 
in whom the small bone readily yield to pressure. he now untie his 
feet, and, leave the cord in such a manner that it could easily 
be readjust in the event of any person’s coming down, proceed to 
examine the bulkhead where it join the berth. the partition here was 
of soft pine board, an inch thick, and he saw that he should have 
little trouble in cutting his way through. a voice was now heard at the 
forecastle companion-way, and he had just time to put his right hand 
into its handcuff (the left had not been removed) and to draw the rope 
in a slipknot around his ankle, when dirk peters came below, follow by 
tiger, who immediately leap into the berth and lay down. the dog had 
been brought on board by augustus, who knew my attachment to the animal, 
and thought it would give me pleasure to have him with me during the 
voyage. he went up to our house for him immediately after first taking 
me into the hold, but did not think of mention the circumstance upon 
his bring the watch. since the mutiny, augustus had not seen him 
before his appearance with dirk peters, and had given him up for lost, 
supposing him to have been thrown overboard by some of the malignant 
villain belong to the mate’s gang. it appear afterward that he had 
crawl into a hole beneath a whale-boat, from which, not have room to 
turn round, he could not extricate himself. peters at last let him out, 
and, with a species of good feeling which my friend knew well how to 
appreciate, had now brought him to him in the forecastle as a companion, 
leave at the same time some salt junk and potatoes, with a can of 
water, he then went on deck, promising to come down with something more 
to eat on the next day. 

when he had gone, augustus free both hand from the manacle and 
unfastened his feet. he then turn down the head of the mattress on 
which he had been lying, and with his penknife (for the ruffian had 
not thought it worth while to search him) commence cutting vigorous 
across one of the partition planks, as close as possible to the floor 
of the berth. he chose to cut here, because, if sudden interrupted, he 
would be able to conceal what had been done by letting the head of the 
mattress fall into its proper position. for the remainder of the day, 
however, no disturbance occurred, and by night he had completely divide 
the plank. it should here be observe that none of the crew occupy the 
forecastle as a sleeping-place, living altogether in the cabin since 
the mutiny, drink the wine and feast on the sea-stores of captain 
barnard, and give no more heed than was absolutely necessary to the 
navigation of the brig. these circumstances prove fortunate both for 
myself and augustus; for, had matter been otherwise, he would have 
found it impossible to reach me. as it was, he proceed with confidence 
in his design. it was near daybreak, however, before he complete the 
second division of the board (which was about a foot above the first 
cut), thus making an aperture quite large enough to admit his passage 
through with facility to the main orlop deck. have got here, he made 
his way with but little trouble to the lower main hatch, although in so 
do he had to scramble over tier of oil-casks pile nearly as high as 
the upper deck, there being barely room enough left for his body. upon 
reach the hatch he found that tiger had follow him below, squeeze 
between two row of the casks. it was now too late, however, to attempt 
get to me before dawn, as the chief difficulty lay in passing 
through the close stowage in the lower hold. he therefore resolve to 
return, and wait till the next night. with this design, he proceed to 
loosen the hatch, so that he might have as little detention as possible 
when he should come again. no soon had he loosen it than tiger 
sprang eager to the small opening produced, snuff for a moment, and 
then utter a long whine, scratch at the same time, as if anxious 
to remove the covering with his paws. there could be no doubt, from 
his behaviour, that he was aware of my being in the hold, and augustus 
thought it possible that he would be able to get to me if he put him 
down. he now hit upon the expedient of send the note, as it was 
especially desirable that i should make no attempt at force my way out 
at least under existing circumstances, and there could be no certainty 
of his get to me himself on the morrow as he intended. after-events 
prove how fortunate it was that the idea occur to him as it did; 
for, had it not been for the receipt of the note, i should undoubted 
have fallen upon some plan, however desperate, of alarm the crew, and 
both our lives would most probably have been sacrifice in consequence. 

have conclude to write, the difficulty was now to procure the 
material for so doing. an old toothpick was soon made into a pen; and 
this by means of feeling altogether, for the between-decks was as 
dark as pitch. paper enough was obtain from the back of a letter--a 
duplicate of the forge letter from mr. ross. this had been the original 
draught; but the handwriting not being sufficient well imitated, 
augustus had written another, thrust the first, by good fortune, into 
his coat-pocket, where it was now most opportune discovered. ink alone 
was thus wanting, and a substitute was immediately found for this by 
means of a slight incision with the pen-knife on the back of a finger 
just above the nail--a copious flow of blood ensuing, as usual, from 
wound in that vicinity. the note was now written, as well as it could 
be in the dark and under the circumstances. it brief explain that a 
mutiny had taken place; that captain barnard was set adrift; and that i 
might expect immediate relief as far as provisions were concerned, but 
must not venture upon making any disturbance. it conclude with these 
words: “_i have scrawl this with blood--your life depend upon lie 
close._” 

this slip of paper being tie upon the dog, he was now put down the 
hatchway, and augustus made the best of his way back to the forecastle, 
where he found no reason to believe that any of the crew had been in 
his absence. to conceal the hole in the partition, he drove his knife in 
just above it, and hung up a pea-jacket which he found in the berth. his 
handcuffs were then replaced, and also the rope around his ankles. 

these arrangement were scarcely complete when dirk peters came below, 
very drunk, but in excellent humour, and bring with him my friend’s 
allowance of provision for the day. this consist of a dozen large 
ireland potato roasted, and a pitcher of water. he sat for some time on 
a chest by the berth, and talk freely about the mate and the general 
concern of the brig. his demeanour was exceedingly capricious, and 
even grotesque. at one time augustus was much alarm by odd conduct. 
at last, however, he went on deck, mutter a promise to bring his 
prisoner a good dinner on the morrow. during the day two of the crew 
(harpooners) came down, accompany by the cook, all three in nearly the 
last stage of intoxication. like peters, they made no scruple of talk 
unreserved about their plans. it appear that they were much divide 
among themselves as to their ultimate course, agree in no point, 
except the attack on the ship from the cape verd islands, with 
which they were in hourly expectation of meeting. as far as could be 
ascertained, the mutiny had not been brought about altogether for the 
sake of booty; a private pique of the chief mate’s against captain 
barnard have been the main instigation. there now seem to be two 
principal faction among the crew--one head by the mate, the other by 
the cook. the former party were for seize the first suitable vessel 
which should present itself, and equip it at some of the west india 
island for a pirate cruise. the latter division, however, which was 
the stronger, and included dirk peters among its partisans, were bent 
upon pursue the course originally laid out for the brig into the south 
pacific; there either to take whale, or act otherwise, as circumstances 
should suggest. the representations of peters, who had frequent 
visit these regions, had great weight, apparently, with the mutineers, 
wavering, as they were, between half-engendered notions of profit and 
pleasure. he dwelt on the world of novelty and amusement to be found 
among the innumerable island of the pacific, on the perfect security 
and freedom from all restraint to be enjoyed, but, more particularly, on 
the delicious of the climate, on the abundant means of good living, 
and on the voluptuous beauty of the women. as yet, nothing had been 
absolutely determined upon; but the pictures of the hybrid line-manager 
were taking strong hold upon the ardent imagination of the seamen, and 
there was every possibility that his intentions would be finally carry 
into effect. 

the three men went away in about an hour, and no one else enter the 
forecastle all day. augustus lay quiet until nearly night. he then free 
himself from the rope and irons, and prepared for his attempt. a bottle 
was found in one of the berths, and this he fill with water from the 
pitcher left by peters, store his pocket at the same time with cold 
potatoes. to his great joy he also came across a lantern, with a small 
piece of tallow candle in it. this he could light at any moment, as he 
had in his possession a box of phosphorus matches. when it was quite 
dark, he got through the hole in the bulkhead, have taken the 
precaution to arrange the bedclothes in the berth so as to convey the 
idea of a person cover up. when through, he hung up the pea-jacket 
on his knife, as before, to conceal the aperture--this manoeuvre being 
easily effected, as he did not readjust the piece of plank taken out 
until afterward. he was now on the main orlop deck, and proceed to 
make his way, as before, between the upper deck and the oil-casks to 
the main hatchway. have reach this, he lit the piece of candle, and 
descended, grope with extreme difficulty among the compact stowage of 
the hold. in a few moments he became alarm at the insufferable stench 
and the close of the atmosphere. he could not think it possible 
that i had survive my confinement for so long a period breathing so 
oppressive an air. he call my name repeatedly, but i made him no 
reply, and his apprehension seem thus to be confirmed. the brig was 
rolling violently, and there was so much noise in consequence, that it 
was useless to listen for any weak sound, such as those of my breathing 
or snoring. he threw open the lantern, and held it as high as possible, 
whenever an opportunity occurred, in order that, by observing the light, 
i might, if alive, be aware that succor was approaching. still nothing 
was heard from me, and the supposition of my death began to assume the 
character of certainty. he determined, nevertheless, to force a passage, 
if possible, to the box, and at least ascertain beyond a doubt the truth 
of his surmises. he pushed on for some time in a most pitiable state of 
anxiety, until, at length, he found the pathway utterly block up, and 
that there was no possibility of making any farther way by the course 
in which he had set out. overcome now by his feelings, he threw himself 
among the lumber in despair, and wept like a child. it was at this 
period that he heard the crash occasion by the bottle which i had 
thrown down. fortunate, indeed, was it that the incident occurred--for, 
upon this incident, trivial as it appears, the thread of my destiny 
depended. many years elapsed, however, before i was aware of this fact. 
a natural shame and regret for his weakness and indecision prevent 
augustus from confiding to me at once what a more intimate and 
unreserved communion afterward induce him to reveal. upon finding his 
further progress in the hold impede by obstacle which he could not 
overcome, he had resolve to abandon his attempt at reach me, and 
return at once to the forecastle. before condemn him entire on this 
head, the harass circumstances which embarrass him should be taken 
into consideration. the night was fast wearing away, and his absence 
from the forecastle might be discovered; and indeed would necessarily be 
so, if he should fail to get back to the berth by daybreak. his candle 
was expire in the socket, and there would be the greatest difficulty 
in retrace his way to the hatchway in the dark. it must be allowed, 
too, that he had every good reason to believe me dead; in which event 
no benefit could result to me from his reach the box, and a world of 
danger would be encounter to no purpose by himself. he had repeatedly 
called, and i had made him no answer. i had been now eleven days and 
nights with no more water than that contained in the jug which he had 
left with me--a supply which it was not at all probable i had hoard in 
the beginning of my confinement, as i had every cause to expect a speedy 
release. the atmosphere of the hold, too, must have appear to him, 
coming from the comparatively open air of the steerage, of a nature 
absolutely poisonous, and by far more intolerable than it had seem to 
me upon my first taking up my quarters in the box--the hatchway at that 
time have been constant open for many month previous. add to these 
consideration that of the scene of bloodshed and terror so lately 
witness by my friend; his confinement, privations, and narrow escape 
from death, together with the frail and equivocal tenure by which he 
still existed--circumstances all so well calculate to prostrate every 
energy of mind--and the reader will be easily brought, as i have been, 
to regard his apparent fall off in friendship and in faith with 
sentiment rather of sorrow than of anger. 

the crash of the bottle was distinct heard, yet augustus was not sure 
that it proceed from the hold. the doubt, however, was sufficient 
inducement to persevere. he clamber up nearly to the orlop deck by 
means of the stowage, and then, watch for a lull in the pitch of 
the vessel, he call out to me in as loud a tone as he could command, 
regardless, for the moment, of being overheard by the crew. it will 
be remember that on this occasion the voice reach me, but i was 
so entire overcome by violent agitation as to be incapable of reply. 
confident, now, that his worst apprehension were well founded, he 
descended, with a view of get back to the forecastle without loss 
of time. in his haste some small box were thrown down, the noise 
occasion by which i heard, as will be recollected. he had made 
considerable progress on his return when the fall of the knife again 
cause him to hesitate. he retrace his steps immediately, and, 
clamber up the stowage a second time, call out my name, loud as 
before, have watch for a lull. this time i found voice to answer. 
overjoyed at discover me to be still alive, he now resolve to brave 
every difficulty and danger in reach me. have extricate himself as 
quick as possible from the labyrinth of lumber by which he was hem 
in, he at length struck into an opening which promise better, and 
finally, after a series of struggles, arrive at the box in a state of 
utter exhaustion. 




chapter 6 

the leading particulars of this narration were all that augustus 
communicate to me while we remain near the box. it was not 
until afterward that he enter fully into all the details. he was 
apprehensive of being missed, and i was wild with impatience to leave 
my detest place of confinement. we resolve to make our way at once 
to the hole in the bulkhead, near which i was to remain for the present, 
while he went through to reconnoiter. to leave tiger in the box was what 
neither of us could endure to think of, yet, how to act otherwise was 
the question. he now seem to be perfectly quiet, and we could not even 
distinguish the sound of his breathing upon apply our ear close 
to the box. i was convinced that he was dead, and determined to open the 
door. we found him lie at full length, apparently in a deep stupor, 
yet still alive. no time was to be lost, yet i could not bring myself to 
abandon an animal who had now been twice instrumental in saving my life, 
without some attempt at preserve him. we therefore drag him along 
with us as well as we could, although with the greatest difficulty and 
fatigue; augustus, during part of the time, being forced to clamber 
over the impediment in our way with the huge dog in his arms--a feat 
to which the feeble of my frame render me total inadequate. at 
length we succeed in reach the hole, when augustus got through, and 
tiger was pushed in afterward. all was found to be safe, and we did 
not fail to return sincere thanks to god for our deliverance from the 
imminent danger we had escaped. for the present, it was agree that i 
should remain near the opening, through which my companion could readily 
supply me with a part of his daily provision, and where i could have the 
advantage of breathing an atmosphere comparatively pure. 

in explanation of some portion of this narrative, wherein i have spoken 
of the stowage of the brig, and which may appear ambiguous to some of my 
reader who may have seen a proper or regular stowage, i must here state 
that the manner in which this most important duty had been perform 
on board the grampus was a most shameful piece of neglect on the part 
of captain barnard, who was by no means as careful or as experienced a 
seaman as the hazardous nature of the service on which he was 
employ would seem necessarily to demand. a proper stowage cannot be 
accomplished in a careless manner, and many most disastrous accidents, 
even within the limit of my own experience, have arisen from neglect 
or ignorance in this particular. coast vessels, in the frequent hurry 
and bustle attendant upon taking in or discharge cargo, are the most 
liable to mishap from the want of a proper attention to stowage. the 
great point is to allow no possibility of the cargo or ballast shift 
position even in the most violent rolling of the vessel. with this end, 
great attention must be paid, not only to the bulk taken in, but to the 
nature of the bulk, and whether there be a full or only a partial cargo. 
in most kind of freight the stowage is accomplished by means of a 
screw. thus, in a load of tobacco or flour, the whole is screw so 
tight into the hold of the vessel that the barrel or hogsheads, upon 
discharging, are found to be completely flattened, and take some time 
to regain their original shape. this screwing, however, is resort to 
principally with a view of obtain more room in the hold; for in a 
full load of any such commodity as flour or tobacco, there can be no 
danger of any shift whatever, at least none from which inconvenience 
can result. there have been instances, indeed, where this method of 
screw has result in the most lamentable consequences, arise from 
a cause altogether distinct from the danger attendant upon a shift of 
cargo. a load of cotton, for example, tight screw while in certain 
conditions, has been known, through the expansion of its bulk, to rend a 
vessel asunder at sea. there can be no doubt either that the same result 
would ensue in the case of tobacco, while undergo its usual course 
of fermentation, were it not for the interstice consequent upon the 
rotund of the hogsheads. 

it is when a partial cargo is received that danger is chiefly to be 
apprehend from shifting, and that precaution should be always taken 
to guard against such misfortune. only those who have encounter a 
violent gale of wind, or rather who have experienced the rolling of 
a vessel in a sudden calm after the gale, can form an idea of the 
tremendous force of the plunges, and of the consequent terrible impetus 
given to all loose articles in the vessel. it is then that the necessity 
of a cautious stowage, when there is a partial cargo, become obvious. 
when lying-to (especially with a small head sail), a vessel which is not 
properly model in the bow is frequent thrown upon her beam-ends; 
this occur even every fifteen or twenty minutes upon an average, yet 
without any serious consequence resulting, provided there be a proper 
stowage. if this, however, has not been strict attend to, in the 
first of these heavy lurch the whole of the cargo tumble over to the 
side of the vessel which lie upon the water, and, being thus prevent 
from regain her equilibrium, as she would otherwise necessarily do, 
she is certain to fill in a few seconds and go down. it is not too much 
to say that at least one-half of the instance in which vessel have 
founder in heavy gale at sea may be attribute to a shift of cargo 
or of ballast. 

when a partial cargo of any kind is taken on board, the whole, after 
being first stow as compact as may be, should be cover with a 
layer of stout shifting-boards, extend completely across the vessel. 
upon these boards strong temporary stanchion should be erected, 
reach to the timber above, and thus secure every thing in its 
place. in cargo consist of grain, or any similar matter, additional 
precaution are requisite. a hold fill entire with grain upon 
leave port will be found not more than three fourth full upon 
reach its destination--this, too, although the freight, when measured 
bushel by bushel by the consignee, will overrun by a vast deal (on 
account of the swelling of the grain) the quantity consigned. this 
result is occasion by settle during the voyage, and is the more 
perceptible in proportion to the roughness of the weather experienced. 
if grain loose thrown in a vessel, then, is ever so well secure by 
shifting-boards and stanchions, it will be liable to shift in a long 
passage so greatly as to bring about the most distressing calamities. 
to prevent these, every method should be employ before leave port 
to settle the cargo as much as possible; and for this there are many 
contrivances, among which may be mention the driving of wedge into 
the grain. even after all this is done, and unusual pains taken to 
secure the shifting-boards, no seaman who know what he is about will 
feel altogether secure in a gale of any violence with a cargo of 
grain on board, and, least of all, with a partial cargo. yet there are 
hundred of our coast vessels, and, it is likely, many more from the 
port of europe, which sail daily with partial cargoes, even of the most 
dangerous species, and without any precaution whatever. the wonder 
is that no more accident occur than do actually happen. a lamentable 
instance of this heedless occur to my knowledge in the case of 
captain joel rice of the schooner firefly, which sail from richmond, 
virginia, to madeira, with a cargo of corn, in the year 1825. the 
captain had gone many voyages without serious accident, although he was 
in the habit of pay no attention whatever to his stowage, more than 
to secure it in the ordinary manner. he had never before sail with 
a cargo of grain, and on this occasion had the corn thrown on board 
loosely, when it did not much more than half fill the vessel. for the 
first portion of the voyage he met with nothing more than light breezes; 
but when within a day’s sail of madeira there came on a strong gale from 
the n. n. e. which forced him to lie-to. he brought the schooner to the 
wind under a double-reefed foresail alone, when she rode as well as any 
vessel could be expect to do, and ship not a drop of water. toward 
night the gale somewhat abated, and she roll with more unsteady 
than before, but still did very well, until a heavy lurch threw her upon 
her beam-ends to starboard. the corn was then heard to shift bodily, the 
force of the movement burst open the main hatchway. the vessel 
went down like a shot. this happen within hail of a small sloop from 
madeira, which picked up one of the crew (the only person saved), and 
which rode out the gale in perfect security, as indeed a jolly boat 
might have done under proper management. 

the stowage on board the grampus was most clumsy done, if stowage 
that could be call which was little better than a promiscuous huddle 
together of oil-casks {*1} and ship furniture. i have already spoken of 
the condition of articles in the hold. on the orlop deck there was space 
enough for my body (as i have stated) between the oil-casks and the 
upper deck; a space was left open around the main hatchway; and several 
other large space were left in the stowage. near the hole cut through 
the bulkhead by augustus there was room enough for an entire cask, and 
in this space i found myself comfortable situated for the present. 

by the time my friend had got safe into the berth, and readjust 
his handcuffs and the rope, it was broad daylight. we had made a narrow 
escape indeed; for scarcely had he arrange all matters, when the mate 
came below, with dirk peters and the cook. they talk for some time 
about the vessel from the cape verds, and seem to be excessive 
anxious for her appearance. at length the cook came to the berth in 
which augustus was lying, and seat himself in it near the head. i 
could see and hear every thing from my hiding-place, for the piece cut 
out had not been put back, and i was in momentary expectation that the 
negro would fall against the pea-jacket, which was hung up to conceal 
the aperture, in which case all would have been discovered, and our 
lives would, no doubt, have been instantly sacrificed. our good fortune 
prevailed, however; and although he frequent touched it as the vessel 
rolled, he never pressed against it sufficient to bring about a 
discovery. the bottom of the jacket had been careful fasten to the 
bulkhead, so that the hole might not be seen by its swinging to one 
side. all this time tiger was lie in the foot of the berth, and 
appear to have recover in some measure his faculties, for i could 
see him occasional open his eyes and draw a long breath. 

after a few minutes the mate and cook went above, leave dirk peters 
behind, who, as soon as they were gone, came and sat himself down in 
the place just occupy by the mate. he began to talk very sociable with 
augustus, and we could now see that the greater part of his apparent 
intoxication, while the two other were with him, was a feint. he 
answer all my companion’s question with perfect freedom; told him 
that he had no doubt of his father’s have been picked up, as there 
were no less than five sail in sight just before sundown on the day he 
was cut adrift; and use other language of a consolatory nature, 
which occasion me no less surprise than pleasure. indeed, i began to 
entertain hopes, that through the instrumentality of peters we might 
be finally enable to regain possession of the brig, and this idea i 
mention to augustus as soon as i found an opportunity. he thought 
the matter possible, but urge the necessity of the greatest caution 
in making the attempt, as the conduct of the hybrid appear to be 
instigate by the most arbitrary caprice alone; and, indeed, it was 
difficult to say if he was at any moment of sound mind. peters went 
upon deck in about an hour, and did not return again until noon, when he 
brought augustus a plentiful supply of junk beef and pudding. of this, 
when we were left alone, i partook heartily, without return through 
the hole. no one else came down into the forecastle during the day, and 
at night, i got into augustus’ berth, where i slept sound and sweet 
until nearly daybreak, when he awaken me upon hearing a stir upon 
deck, and i regain my hiding-place as quick as possible. when the 
day was fully broke, we found that tiger had recover his strength 
almost entirely, and gave no indication of hydrophobia, drink a 
little water that was offer him with great apparent eagerness. during 
the day he regain all his former vigour and appetite. his strange 
conduct had been brought on, no doubt, by the deleterious quality of the 
air of the hold, and had no connexion with canine madness. i could not 
sufficient rejoice that i had persist in bring him with me from 
the box. this day was the thirtieth of june, and the thirteenth since 
the grampus made sail from nantucket. 

on the second of july the mate came below drunk as usual, and in an 
excessive good-humor. he came to augustus’s berth, and, give him a 
slap on the back, ask him if he thought he could behave himself if 
he let him loose, and whether he would promise not to be go into the 
cabin again. to this, of course, my friend answer in the affirmative, 
when the ruffian set him at liberty, after making him drink from a flask 
of rum which he drew from his coat-pocket. both now went on deck, and i 
did not see augustus for about three hours. he then came below with the 
good news that he had obtain permission to go about the brig as he 
pleased anywhere forward of the mainmast, and that he had been ordered 
to sleep, as usual, in the forecastle. he brought me, too, a good 
dinner, and a plentiful supply of water. the brig was still cruise for 
the vessel from the cape verds, and a sail was now in sight, which was 
thought to be the one in question. as the event of the ensue eight 
days were of little importance, and had no direct bearing upon the main 
incident of my narrative, i will here throw them into the form of a 
journal, as i do not wish to omit them altogether. 

july 3. augustus furnish me with three blankets, with which i 
contrived a comfortable bed in my hiding-place. no one came below, 
except my companion, during the day. tiger took his station in the 
berth just by the aperture, and slept heavily, as if not yet entire 
recover from the effects of his sickness. toward night a flaw of wind 
struck the brig before sail could be taken in, and very nearly capsize 
her. the puff die away immediately, however, and no damage was done 
beyond the splitting of the foretopsail. dirk peters treat augustus 
all this day with great kindness and enter into a long conversation 
with him respecting the pacific ocean, and the island he had visit 
in that region. he ask him whether he would not like to go with the 
mutineer on a kind of explore and pleasure voyage in those quarters, 
and said that the men were gradual coming over to the mate’s views. 
to this augustus thought it best to reply that he would be glad to go 
on such an adventure, since nothing better could be done, and that any 
thing was preferable to a pirate life. 

july 4th. the vessel in sight prove to be a small brig from liverpool, 
and was allow to pass unmolested. augustus spent most of his time 
on deck, with a view of obtain all the information in his power 
respecting the intentions of the mutineers. they had frequent and 
violent quarrel among themselves, in one of which a harpooner, jim 
bonner, was thrown overboard. the party of the mate was gain ground. 
jim bonner belong to the cook’s gang, of which peters was a partisan. 

july 5th. about daybreak there came on a stiff breeze from the west, 
which at noon freshen into a gale, so that the brig could carry 
nothing more than her trysail and foresail. in taking in the 
foretopsail, simms, one of the common hands, and belong also to 
the cook’s gang, fell overboard, being very much in liquor, and was 
drowned--no attempt being made to save him. the whole number of person 
on board was now thirteen, to wit: dirk peters; seymour, the black 
cook; jones, greely, hartman rogers and william allen, all of the 
cook’s party; the mate, whose name i never 
learned; absalom hicks, wilson, john hunty richard parker, of the mate’s 
party;--besides augustus and myself. 

july 6th. the gale last all this day, blow in heavy squalls, 
accompany with rain. the brig took in a good deal of water through her 
seams, and one of the pump was kept continual going, augustus being 
forced to take his turn. just at twilight a large ship pass close 
by us, without have been discover until within hail. the ship was 
supposed to be the one for which the mutineer were on the lookout. the 
mate hail her, but the reply was drown in the roaring of the gale. 
at eleven, a sea was ship amidships, which tore away a great portion 
of the larboard bulwarks, and did some other slight damage. toward 
morning the weather moderated, and at sunrise there was very little 
wind. 

july 7th. there was a heavy swell running all this day, during which the 
brig, being light, roll excessively, and many articles broke loose in 
the hold, as i could hear distinct from my hiding-place. i suffer 
a great deal from sea-sickness. peters had a long conversation this day 
with augustus, and told him that two of his gang, gree and allen, had 
gone over to the mate, and were resolve to turn pirates. he put several 
question to augustus which he did not then exactly understand. during 
a part of this evening the leak gain upon the vessel; and little could 
be done to remedy it, as it was occasion by the brig's straining, and 
taking in the water through her seams. a sail was thrummed, and got 
under the bows, which aid us in some measure, so that we began to gain 
upon the leak. 

july 8th. a light breeze sprang up at sunrise from the eastward, when 
the mate head the brig to the southwest, with the intention of making 
some of the west india island in pursuance of his pirate designs. no 
opposition was made by peters or the cook--at least none in the hearing 
of augustus. all idea of taking the vessel from the cape verd was 
abandoned. the leak was now easily kept under by one pump go every 
three quarters of an hour. the sail was drawn from beneath the bows. 
spoke two small schooner during the day. 

july 9th. fine weather. all hand employ in repair bulwarks. peters 
had again a long conversation with augustus, and spoke more plainly than 
he had done heretofore. he said nothing should induce him to come into 
the mate’s views, and even hint his intention of taking the brig out 
of his hands. he ask my friend if he could depend upon his aid in such 
case, to which augustus said, “yes,” without hesitation. peters then 
said he would sound the other of his party upon the subject, and went 
away. during the remainder of the day augustus had no opportunity of 
speak with him privately. 




chapter 7 

july 10. spoke a brig from rio, bound to norfolk. weather hazy, with 
a light baffling wind from the eastward. to-day hartman rogers died, 
have been attack on the eighth with spasm after drink a glass of 
grog. this man was of the cook’s party, and one upon whom peters placed 
his main reliance. he told augustus that he believe the mate had 
poison him, and that he expected, if he did not be on the look-out, 
his own turn would come shortly. there were now only himself, jones, and 
the cook belong to his own gang--on the other side there were five. 
he had spoken to jones about taking the command from the mate; but the 
project have been cool received, he had been deter from pressing 
the matter any further, or from saying any thing to the cook. it was 
well, as it happened, that he was so prudent, for in the afternoon the 
cook express his determination of siding with the mate, and went over 
formal to that party; while jones took an opportunity of quarrel 
with peters, and hint that he would let the mate know of the plan 
in agitation. there was now, evidently, no time to be lost, and peters 
express his determination of attempt to take the vessel at all 
hazards, provided augustus would lend him his aid. my friend at once 
assured him of his willing to enter into any plan for that purpose, 
and, thinking the opportunity a favourable one, made known the fact 
of my being on board. at this the hybrid was not more astonish than 
delighted, as he had no reliance whatever upon jones, whom he already 
considered as belong to the party of the mate. they went below 
immediately, when augustus call to me by name, and peters and myself 
were soon made acquainted. it was agree that we should attempt to 
retake the vessel upon the first good opportunity, leave jones 
altogether out of our councils. in the event of success, we were to 
run the brig into the first port that offered, and deliver her up. the 
desertion of his party had frustrate peters’ design of go into the 
pacific--an adventure which could not be accomplished without a crew, 
and he depend upon either get acquit upon trial, on the score 
of insanity (which he solemn avowed had actuate him in lend his 
aid to the mutiny), or upon obtain a pardon, if found guilty, through 
the representations of augustus and myself. our deliberation were 
interrupt for the present by the cry of, “all hand take in sail,” and 
peters and augustus ran up on deck. 

as usual, the crew were nearly all drunk; and, before sail could be 
properly taken in, a violent squall laid the brig on her beam-ends. by 
keeping her away, however, she righted, have ship a good deal of 
water. scarcely was everything secure, when another squall took the 
vessel, and immediately afterward another--no damage being done. there 
was every appearance of a gale of wind, which, indeed, shortly came on, 
with great fury, from the northward and westward. all was made as snug 
as possible, and we laid-to, as usual, under a close-reefed foresail. as 
night drew on, the wind increase in violence, with a remarkably heavy 
sea. peters now came into the forecastle with augustus, and we resume 
our deliberations. 

we agree that no opportunity could be more favourable than the present 
for carry our designs into effect, as an attempt at such a moment 
would never be anticipated. as the brig was snug laid-to, there would 
be no necessity of manoeuvre her until good weather, when, if we 
succeed in our attempt, we might liberate one, or perhaps two of the 
men, to aid us in taking her into port. the main difficulty was the 
great disproportion in our forces. there were only three of us, and in 
the cabin there were nine. all the arms on board, too, were in their 
possession, with the exception of a pair of small pistol which peters 
had conceal about his person, and the large seaman’s knife which 
he always wore in the waistband of his pantaloons. from certain 
indications, too--such, for example, as there being no such thing as 
an axe or a handspike lie in their customary places--we began to fear 
that the mate had his suspicions, at least in regard to peters, and that 
he would let slip no opportunity of get rid of him. it was clear, 
indeed, that what we should determine to do could not be done too soon. 
still the odds were too much against us to allow of our proceeding 
without the greatest caution. 

peters propose that he should go up on deck, and enter into 
conversation with the watch (allen), when he would be able to throw him 
into the sea without trouble, and without making any disturbance, by 
seize a good opportunity, that augustus and myself should then come 
up, and endeavour to provide ourselves with some kind of weapon from 
the deck, and that we should then make a rush together, and secure the 
companion-way before any opposition could be offered. i object to 
this, because i could not believe that the mate (who was a cunning 
fellow in all matter which did not affect his superstitious prejudices) 
would suffer himself to be so easily entrapped. the very fact of there 
being a watch on deck at all was sufficient proof that he was upon the 
alert,--it not being usual except in vessel where discipline is most 
rigid enforced, to station a watch on deck when a vessel is lying-to 
in a gale of wind. as i address myself principally, if not altogether, 
to person who have never been to sea, it may be as well to state the 
exact condition of a vessel under such circumstances. lying-to, or, 
in sea-parlance, “laying-to,” is a measure resort to for various 
purposes, and effect in various manners. in moderate weather it 
is frequent done with a view of merely bring the vessel to a 
stand-still, to wait for another vessel or any similar object. if 
the vessel which lies-to is under full sail, the manoeuvre is usually 
accomplished by throw round some portion of her sails, so as to let 
the wind take them aback, when she become stationary. but we are now 
speak of lying-to in a gale of wind. this is done when the wind 
is ahead, and too violent to admit of carry sail without danger of 
capsizing; and sometimes even when the wind is fair, but the sea too 
heavy for the vessel to be put before it. if a vessel be suffer to 
scud before the wind in a very heavy sea, much damage is usually done 
her by the shipping of water over her stern, and sometimes by the 
violent plunge she make forward. this manoeuvre, then, is seldom 
resort to in such case, unless through necessity. when the vessel 
is in a leaky condition she is often put before the wind even in the 
heaviest seas; for, when lying-to, her seam are sure to be greatly 
open by her violent straining, and it is not so much the case when 
scudding. often, too, it become necessary to scud a vessel, either when 
the blast is so exceedingly furious as to tear in pieces the sail which 
is employ with a view of bring her head to the wind, or when, 
through the false model of the frame or other causes, this main 
object cannot be effected. 

vessel in a gale of wind are laid-to in different manners, according 
to their peculiar construction. some lie-to best under a foresail, and 
this, i believe, is the sail most usually employed. large square-rigged 
vessel have sail for the express purpose, call storm-staysails. 
but the jib is occasional employ by itself,--sometimes the jib 
and foresail, or a double-reefed foresail, and not unfrequent the 
after-sails, are made use of. foretopsail are very often found to 
answer the purpose better than any other species of sail. the grampus 
was generally laid-to under a close-reefed foresail. 

when a vessel is to be laid-to, her head is brought up to the wind just 
so nearly as to fill the sail under which she lie when haul flat aft, 
that is, when brought diagonal across the vessel. this being done, 
the bow point within a few degree of the direction from which the wind 
issues, and the windward bow of course receive the shock of the waves. 
in this situation a good vessel will ride out a very heavy gale of wind 
without shipping a drop of water, and without any further attention 
being requisite on the part of the crew. the helm is usually lash 
down, but this is altogether unnecessary (except on account of the noise 
it make when loose), for the rudder has no effect upon the vessel when 
lying-to. indeed, the helm had far better be left loose than lash very 
fast, for the rudder is apt to be torn off by heavy sea if there be no 
room for the helm to play. as long as the sail holds, a well model 
vessel will maintain her situation, and ride every sea, as if instinct 
with life and reason. if the violence of the wind, however, should tear 
the sail into pieces (a feat which it require a perfect hurricane to 
accomplish under ordinary circumstances), there is then imminent danger. 
the vessel falls off from the wind, and, coming broadside to the sea, 
is completely at its mercy: the only resource in this case is to put her 
quiet before the wind, letting her scud until some other sail can be 
set. some vessel will lie-to under no sail whatever, but such are not 
to be trust at sea. 

but to return from this digression. it had never been customary with the 
mate to have any watch on deck when lying-to in a gale of wind, and the 
fact that he had now one, couple with the circumstance of the missing 
axe and handspikes, fully convinced us that the crew were too well on 
the watch to be taken by surprise in the manner peters had suggested. 
something, however, was to be done, and that with as little delay as 
practicable, for there could be no doubt that a suspicion have 
been once entertain against peters, he would be sacrifice upon the 
earliest occasion, and one would certainly be either found or made upon 
the break of the gale. 

augustus now suggest that if peters could contrive to remove, under 
any pretext, the piece of chain-cable which lay over the trap in the 
stateroom, we might possibly be able to come upon them unawares by means 
of the hold; but a little reflection convinced us that the vessel roll 
and pitch too violent for any attempt of that nature. 

by good fortune i at length hit upon the idea of working upon the 
superstitious terror and guilty conscience of the mate. it will be 
remember that one of the crew, hartman rogers, had die during the 
morning, have been attack two days before with spasm after drink 
some spirits and water. peters had express to us his opinion that this 
man had been poison by the mate, and for this belief he had reasons, 
so he said, which were incontrovertible, but which he could not be 
prevail upon to explain to us--this wayward refusal being only in 
keeping with other points of his singular character. but whether or not

we were easily led to fall in with his suspicion, and determined to act 
accordingly. 

rogers had die about eleven in the forenoon, in violent convulsions; 
and the corpse present in a few minutes after death one of the most 
horrid and loathsome spectacles i ever remember to have seen. the 
stomach was swollen immensely, like that of a man who has been drown 
and lain under water for many weeks. the hand were in the same 
condition, while the face was shrunken, shrivelled, and of a chalky 
whiteness, except where relieved by two or three glaring red blotch 
like those occasion by the erysipelas: one of these blotch extend 
diagonal across the face, completely covering up an eye as if with 
a band of red velvet. in this disgust condition the body had been 
brought up from the cabin at noon to be thrown overboard, when the mate 
get a glimpse of it (for he now saw it for the first time), and 
being either touched with remorse for his crime or struck with terror at 
so horrible a sight, ordered the men to sew the body up in its 
hammock, and allow it the usual rite of sea-burial. have given these 
directions, he went below, as if to avoid any further sight of his 
victim. while preparation were making to obey his orders, the gale came 
on with great fury, and the design was abandoned for the present. the 
corpse, left to itself, was wash into the larboard scuppers, where 
it still lay at the time of which i speak, flounder about with the 
furious lurch of the brig. 

have arrange our plan, we set about putt it in execution as 
speedy as possible. peters went upon deck, and, as he had anticipated, 
was immediately accost by allen, who appear to be station more as 
a watch upon the forecastle than for any other purpose. the fate of 
this villain, however, was speedy and silent decided; for peters,

and in do this we had to proceed with great care, for it was

indispensable, too, that we should be quick in our operations, for every

evident the brig must be taking in water very fast. after searching

have secure these, we strip off the shirt of the corpse and

as soon as i got below i commence disguise myself so as to represent 
the corpse of rogers. the shirt which we had taken from the body aid 
us very much, for it was of singular form and character, and easily

themselves. peters then arrange my face, first rubbing it well over

from a cut in his finger. the streak across the eye was not forgotten 
and present a most shocking appearance. 




chapter 8 

as i view myself in a fragment of looking-glass which hung up in

recollection of the terrific reality which i was thus representing, 
that i was seize with a violent tremour, and could scarcely summon

we there found everything safe, and, keeping close to the bulwarks,

we listen to their conversation for some time before we could make

one of the men spoke of peters, when the mate reply to him in a low

the tremendous noise made by the roaring of the wind in the rigging, and

from this station he could not notice the absence of allen, and he

my two companion now proceed bold aft and down into the cabin,

the mate received them with feign cordiality, and told augustus that, 
since he had behave himself so well of late, he might take up his

companion-way, to be ready for use when required. 

i now steady myself as well as possible so as to have a good view of 
all that was passing within, and endeavour to nerve myself to the task 
of descend among the mutineer when peters should make a signal to 
me, as agree upon. presently he contrived to turn the conversation upon 
the bloody deed of the mutiny, and by degree led the men to talk 
of the thousand superstition which are so universally current among 
seamen. i could not make out all that was said, but i could plainly see 
the effects of the conversation in the countenance of those present. 
the mate was evidently much agitated, and presently, when some one
