would be better to have the body thrown overboard at once as it was too 
horrible a sight to see it flounder about in the scuppers. at this 
the villain absolutely gasp for breath, and turn his head slow 
round upon his companions, as if implore some one to go up and perform 
the task. no one, however, stirred, and it was quite evident that the 
whole party were wound up to the highest pitch of nervous excitement. 
peters now made me the signal. i immediately threw open the door of the 
companion-way, and, descending, without utter a syllable, stood erect 
in the midst of the party. 

the intense effect produce by this sudden apparition is not at all 
to be wonder at when the various circumstances are taken into 
consideration. usually, in case of a similar nature, there is left in 
the mind of the spectator some glimmer of doubt as to the reality of 
the vision before his eyes; a degree of hope, however feeble, that he 
is the victim of chicanery, and that the apparition is not actually a 
visitant from the old world of shadows. it is not too much to say that 
such remnant of doubt have been at the bottom of almost every such 
visitation, and that the appalling horror which has sometimes been 
brought about, is to be attributed, even in the case most in point, 
and where most suffering has been experienced, more to a kind of 
anticipate horror, lest the apparition might possibly be real, than 
to an unwavere belief in its reality. but, in the present instance, it 
will be seen immediately, that in the mind of the mutineer there 
was not even the shadow of a basis upon which to rest a doubt that 
the apparition of rogers was indeed a revivify of his disgust 
corpse, or at least its spiritual image. the isolated situation of the 
brig, with its entire inaccessible on account of the gale, confine 
the apparently possible means of deception within such narrow and 
definite limits, that they must have thought themselves enable to 
survey them all at a glance. they had now been at sea twenty-four days, 
without holding more than a speak communication with any vessel 
whatever. the whole of the crew, too--at least all whom they had the 
most remote reason for suspect to be on board--were assemble in the 
cabin, with the exception of allen, the watch; and his gigantic stature 
(he was six feet six inch high) was too familiar in their eyes to 
permit the notion that he was the apparition before them to enter their 
mind even for an instant. add to these consideration the awe-inspiring 
nature of the tempest, and that of the conversation brought about by 
peters; the deep impression which the loathsome of the actual corpse 
had made in the morning upon the imagination of the men; the excellence 
of the imitation in my person, and the uncertain and waver light 
in which they beheld me, as the glare of the cabin lantern, swinging 
violent to and fro, fell dubious and fitful upon my figure, and 
there will be no reason to wonder that the deception had even more than 
the entire effect which we had anticipated. the mate sprang up from the 
mattress on which he was lying, and, without utter a syllable, fell 
back, stone dead, upon the cabin floor, and was hurl to the leeward 
like a log by a heavy roll of the brig. of the remain seven, there 
were but three who had at first any degree of presence of mind. the 
four other sat for some time rooted apparently to the floor, the most 
pitiable object of horror and utter despair my eyes ever encountered. 
the only opposition we experienced at all was from the cook, john hunt, 
and richard parker; but they made but a feeble and irresolute defence. 
the two former were shot instantly by peters, and i fell parker with 
a blow on the head from the pump-handle which i had brought with me. in 
the meantime, augustus seize one of the musket lie on the floor 
and shot another mutineer wilson through the breast. there were now but

lethargy, and perhaps began to see that a deception had been practised

jones had thrown augustus to the floor, stab him in several place

tiger. with a low growl, he bound into the cabin, at a most critical

render us any aid whatever, and i was so encumber with my disguise

than i have taken to tell it, we found ourselves master of the brig. 

the only person of our opponent who was left alive was richard parker.

the door of the shatter stateroom; but, upon peters touching him with

behind his back. the dog was still growl over jones; but, upon

it was now about one o’clock in the morning, and the wind was still 
blow tremendously. the brig evidently labour much more than usual, 
and it became absolutely necessary that something should be done with a

the creak and working of the mainmast, too, gave indication that it

practice, occasional resort to by ignorant ship-builders), so that

leave the body of the crew lie in the cabin, we got to work

in this manner we pass a night of terrible anxiety and fatigue, and,

contrary to parker’s advice, we now proceed to cut away the foremast, 
and at length accomplished it after much difficulty, owe to the 
position in which we lay. in go overboard the wreck took with it the 
bowsprit, and left us a complete hulk. 

so far we had had reason to rejoice in the escape of our longboat, 
which had received no damage from any of the huge sea which had come on 
board. but we had not long to congratulate ourselves; for the foremast 
have gone, and, of course, the foresail with it, by which the brig had 
been steadied, every sea now made a complete breach over us, and in 
five minutes our deck was swept from stem to stern, the longboat 
and starboard bulwark torn off, and even the windlass shatter into 
fragments. it was, indeed, hardly possible for us to be in a more 
pitiable condition. 

at noon there seem to be some slight appearance of the gale’s abating, 
but in this we were sadly disappointed, for it only lull for a few 
minutes to blow with redouble fury. about four in the afternoon it was 
utterly impossible to stand up against the violence of the blast; and, 
as the night closed in upon us, i had not a shadow of hope that the 
vessel would hold together until morning. 

by midnight we had settled very deep in the water, which was now up to 
the orlop deck. the rudder went soon afterward, the sea which tore it 
away lift the after portion of the brig entire from the water, 
against which she thump in her descent with such a concussion as would 
be occasion by go ashore. we had all calculate that the rudder 
would hold its own to the last, as it was unusually strong, being rig 
as i have never seen one rig either before or since. down its main 
timber there ran a succession of stout iron hooks, and other in the 
same manner down the stern-post. through these hook there extend 
a very thick wrought-iron rod, the rudder being thus held to the 
stern-post and swinging freely on the rod. the tremendous force of the 
sea which tore it off may be estimate by the fact, that the hook in 
the stern-post, which ran entire through it, being clinch on the 
inside, were drawn every one of them completely out of the solid wood. 

we had scarcely time to draw breath after the violence of this shock, 
when one of the most tremendous wave i had then ever known broke right 
on board of us, sweeping the companion-way clear off, burst in the 
hatchways, and filling every inch of the vessel with water. 




chapter 9 

luckily, just before night, all four of us had lash ourselves firm 
to the fragment of the windlass, lie in this manner as flat upon the 
deck as possible. this precaution alone save us from destruction. as 
it was, we were all more or less stun by the immense weight of water 
which tumble upon us, and which did not roll from above us until we 
were nearly exhausted. as soon as i could recover breath, i call aloud 
to my companions. augustus alone replied, saying: “it is all over with 
us, and may god have mercy upon our souls!” by-and-by both the other 
were enable to speak, when they exhort us to take courage, as there 
was still hope; it being impossible, from the nature of the cargo, that 
the brig could go down, and there being every chance that the gale would 
blow over by the morning. these words inspired me with new life; for, 
strange as it may seem, although it was obvious that a vessel with a 
cargo of empty oil-casks would not sink, i had been hitherto so confuse 
in mind as to have overlook this consideration altogether; and the 
danger which i had for some time regard as the most imminent was 
that of foundering. as hope revive within me, i made use of every 
opportunity to strengthen the lashings which held me to the remains 
of the windlass, and in this occupation i soon discover that my 
companion were also busy. the night was as dark as it could possibly 
be, and the horrible shriek din and confusion which surround us it 
is useless to attempt describing. our deck lay level with the sea, or 
rather we were encircle with a towering ridge of foam, a portion of 
which swept over us every instant. it is not too much to say that our 
head were not fairly out of the water more than one second in three. 
although we lay close together, no one of us could see the other, 
or, indeed, any portion of the brig itself, upon which we were so 
tempestuous hurl about. at interval we call one to the other, 
thus endeavour to keep alive hope, and render consolation and 
encouragement to such of us as stood most in need of it. the feeble 
condition of augustus made him an object of solicitude with us all; and 
as, from the lacerate condition of his right arm, it must have been 
impossible for him to secure his lashings with any degree of 
firmness, we were in momentary expectation of finding that he had gone 
overboard--yet to render him aid was a thing altogether out of the 
question. fortunately, his station was more secure than that of any 
of the rest of us; for the upper part of his body lie just beneath 
a portion of the shatter windlass, the seas, as they tumble in upon 
him, were greatly broken in their violence. in any other situation than 
this (into which he had been accidental thrown after have lash 
himself in a very expose spot) he must inevitable have perish before 
morning. owe to the brig’s lie so much along, we were all less 
liable to be wash off than otherwise would have been the case. the 
heel, as i have before stated, was to larboard, about one half of the 
deck being constant under water. the seas, therefore, which struck us 
to starboard were much broken, by the vessel’s side, only reach us 
in fragment as we lay flat on our faces; while those which came from 
larboard being what are call back-water seas, and obtain little 
hold upon us on account of our posture, had not sufficient force to drag 
us from our fastenings. 

in this frightful situation we lay until the day broke so as to show 
us more fully the horrors which surround us. the brig was a mere 
log, rolling about at the mercy of every wave; the gale was upon the 
increase, if any thing, blow indeed a complete hurricane, and there 
appear to us no earthly prospect of deliverance. for several hour 
we held on in silence, expect every moment that our lashings would 
either give way, that the remains of the windlass would go by the board, 
or that some of the huge seas, which roar in every direction around 
us and above us, would drive the hulk so far beneath the water that we 
should be drown before it could regain the surface. by the mercy of 
god, however, we were preserve from these imminent dangers, and about 
midday were cheer by the light of the blessed sun. shortly afterward 
we could perceive a sensible diminution in the force of the wind, when, 
now for the first time since the latter part of the evening before, 
augustus spoke, ask peters, who lay closest to him, if he thought 
there was any possibility of our being saved. as no reply was at first 
made to this question, we all conclude that the hybrid had been drown 
where he lay; but presently, to our great joy, he spoke, although very 
feebly, saying that he was in great pain, being so cut by the tight 
of his lashings across the stomach, that he must either find means of 
loosen them or perish, as it was impossible that he could endure 
his misery much longer. this occasion us great distress, as it was 
altogether useless to think of aid him in any manner while the 
sea continue washing over us as it did. we exhort him to bear his 
suffering with fortitude, and promise to seize the first opportunity 
which should offer itself to relieve him. he reply that it would soon 
be too late; that it would be all over with him before we could help 
him; and then, after moan for some minutes, lay silent, when we 
conclude that he had perished. 

as the evening drew on, the sea had fallen so much that scarcely more 
than one wave broke over the hulk from windward in the course of five 
minutes, and the wind had abate a great deal, although still blow a 
severe gale. i had not heard any of my companion speak for hours, and 
now call to augustus. he replied, although very feebly, so that 
i could not distinguish what he said. i then spoke to peters and to 
parker, neither of whom return any answer. 

shortly after this period i fell into a state of partial insensibility, 
during which the most pleasing image float in my imagination; such as 
green trees, wave meadow of ripe grain, procession of dance girls, 
troops of cavalry, and other phantasies. i now remember that, in all 
which pass before my mind’s eye, motion was a predominant idea. thus, 
i never fancy any stationary object, such as a house, a mountain, or 
any thing of that kind; but windmills, ships, large birds, balloons, 
people on horseback, carriage driving furiously, and similar moving 
objects, present themselves in endless succession. when i recover 
from this state, the sun was, as near as i could guess, an hour high. 
i had the greatest difficulty in bring to recollection the various 
circumstances connected with my situation, and for some time remain 
firm convinced that i was still in the hold of the brig, near the box, 
and that the body of parker was that of tiger. 

when i at length completely came to my senses, i found that the wind 
blew no more than a moderate breeze, and that the sea was comparatively 
calm; so much so that it only wash over the brig amidships. my left 
arm had broken loose from its lashings, and was much cut about the 
elbow; my right was entire benumbed, and the hand and wrist swollen 
prodigious by the pressure of the rope, which had work from the 
shoulder downward. i was also in great pain from another rope which 
went about my waist, and had been drawn to an insufferable degree of 
tightness. look round upon my companions, i saw that peters still 
lived, although a thick line was pull so forcibly around his loins as 
to give him the appearance of being cut nearly in two; as i stirred, he 
made a feeble motion to me with his hand, point to the rope. augustus 
gave no indication of life whatever, and was bent nearly double across a 
splinter of the windlass. parker spoke to me when he saw me moving, 
and ask me if i had not sufficient strength to release him from his 
situation, saying that if i would summon up what spirits i could, and 
contrive to untie him, we might yet save our lives; but that otherwise 
we must all perish. i told him to take courage, and i would endeavor to 
free him. feeling in my pantaloons’ pocket, i got hold of my penknife, 
and, after several ineffectual attempts, at length succeed in opening 
it. i then, with my left hand, manage to free my right from its 
fastenings, and afterward cut the other ropes which held me. upon 
attempting, however, to move from my position, i found that my leg 
fail me altogether, and that i could not get up; neither could i 
move my right arm in any direction. upon mention this to parker, he 
advise me to lie quiet for a few minutes, holding on to the windlass 
with my left hand, so as to allow time for the blood to circulate. do 
this, the numb presently began to die away so that i could move 
first one of my legs, and then the other, and, shortly afterward i 
regain the partial use of my right arm. i now crawl with great 
caution toward parker, without get on my legs, and soon cut loose 
all the lashings about him, when, after a short delay, he also recover 
the partial use of his limbs. we now lost no time in get loose the 
rope from peters. it had cut a deep gash through the waistband of his 
woollen pantaloons, and through two shirts, and made its way into his 
groin, from which the blood flow out copious as we remove the 
cordage. no soon had we remove it, however, than he spoke, and seem 
to experience instant relief--being able to move with much greater ease 
than either parker or myself--this was no doubt owe to the discharge 
of blood. 

we had little hope that augustus would recover, as he evince no sign 
of life; but, upon get to him, we discover that he had merely 
swoon from the loss of blood, the bandage we had placed around his 
wound arm have been torn off by the water; none of the ropes which 
held him to the windlass were drawn sufficient tight to occasion his 
death. have relieved him from the fastenings, and got him clear of 
the broken wood about the windlass, we secure him in a dry place to 
windward, with his head somewhat lower than his body, and all three of 
us busy ourselves in chafe his limbs. in about half an hour he came 
to himself, although it was not until the next morning that he gave 
sign of recognize any of us, or had sufficient strength to speak. by 
the time we had thus got clear of our lashings it was quite dark, and it 
began to cloud up, so that we were again in the greatest agony lest it 
should come on to blow hard, in which event nothing could have save us 
from perishing, exhaust as we were. by good fortune it continue very 
moderate during the night, the sea subside every minute, which gave 
us great hope of ultimate preservation. a gentle breeze still blew 
from the n. w., but the weather was not at all cold. augustus was lash 
careful to windward in such a manner as to prevent him from slip 
overboard with the rolls of the vessel, as he was still too weak to 
hold on at all. for ourselves there was no such necessity. we sat close 
together, support each other with the aid of the broken ropes 
about the windlass, and devise method of escape from our frightful 
situation. we derive much comfort from taking off our clothes and 
wring the water from them. when we put them on after this, they felt 
remarkably warm and pleasant, and serve to invigorate us in no little 
degree. we help augustus off with his, and wrung them for him, when he 
experienced the same comfort. 

our chief suffering were now those of hunger and thirst, and when we 
look forward to the means of relief in this respect, our heart sunk 
within us, and we were induce to regret that we had escape the 
less dreadful peril of the sea. we endeavoured, however, to console 
ourselves with the hope of being speedy picked up by some vessel 
and encourage each other to bear with fortitude the evil that might 
happen. 

the morning of the fourteenth at length dawned, and the weather still 
continue clear and pleasant, with a steady but very light breeze from 
the n. w. the sea was now quite smooth, and as, from some cause which we 
could not determine, the brig did not lie so much along as she had done 
before, the deck was comparatively dry, and we could move about with 
freedom. we had now been better than three entire days and nights 
without either food or drink, and it became absolutely necessary that we 
should make an attempt to get up something from below. as the brig was 
completely full of water, we went to this work despondently, and with 
but little expectation of being able to obtain anything. we made a kind 
of drag by driving some nail which we broke out from the remains of the 
companion-hatch into two pieces of wood. tie these across each other, 
and fastening them to the end of a rope, we threw them into the cabin, 
and drag them to and fro, in the faint hope of being thus able to 
entangle some article which might be of use to us for food, or which 
might at least render us assistance in get it. we spent the greater 
part of the morning in this labour without effect, fishing up nothing 
more than a few bedclothes, which were readily caught by the nails. 
indeed, our contrivance was so very clumsy that any greater success was 
hardly to be anticipated. 

we now try the forecastle, but equally in vain, and were upon the 
brink of despair, when peters propose that we should fasten a rope to 
his body, and let him make an attempt to get up something by dive 
into the cabin. this proposition we hail with all the delight which 
revive hope could inspire. he proceed immediately to strip off his 
clothes with the exception of his pantaloons; and a strong rope was 
then careful fasten around his middle, being brought up over 
his shoulder in such a manner that there was no possibility of its 
slipping. the undertaking was one of great difficulty and danger; for, 
as we could hardly expect to find much, if any, provision in the cabin 
itself, it was necessary that the diver, after letting himself down, 
should make a turn to the right, and proceed under water a distance of 
ten or twelve feet, in a narrow passage, to the storeroom, and return, 
without drawing breath. 

everything being ready, peters now descended in the cabin, go down 
the companion-ladder until the water reach his chin. he then plunge 
in, head first, turning to the right as he plunged, and endeavour to 
make his way to the storeroom. in this first attempt, however, he was 
altogether unsuccessful. in less than half a minute after his go down 
we felt the rope jerk violent (the signal we had agree upon when 
he desire to be drawn up). we accordingly drew him up instantly, but so 
incautious as to bruise him badly against the ladder. he had brought 
nothing with him, and had been unable to penetrate more than a very 
little way into the passage, owe to the constant exertion he found it 
necessary to make in order to keep himself from floating up against the 
deck. upon get out he was very much exhausted, and had to rest full 
fifteen minutes before he could again venture to descend. 

the second attempt met with even worse success; for he remain so long 
under water without give the signal, that, becoming alarm for his 
safety, we drew him out without it, and found that he was almost at the 
last gasp, having, as he said, repeatedly jerk at the rope without 
our feeling it. this was probably owe to a portion of it have become 
entangle in the balustrade at the foot of the ladder. this balustrade 
was, indeed, so much in the way, that we determined to remove it, if 
possible, before proceeding with our design. as we had no means of 
get it away except by main force, we all descended into the water 
as far as we could on the ladder, and give a pull against it with our 
united strength, succeed in break it down. 

the third attempt was equally unsuccessful with the two first, and it 
now became evident that nothing could be done in this manner without the 
aid of some weight with which the diver might steady himself, and keep 
to the floor of the cabin while making his search. for a long time we 
look about in vain for something which might answer this purpose; but 
at length, to our great joy, we discover one of the weather-forechains 
so loose that we had not the least difficulty in wrench it off. 
have fasten this secure to one of his ankles, peters now made his 
fourth descent into the cabin, and this time succeed in making his way 
to the door of the steward’s room. to his inexpressible grief, however, 
he found it locked, and was oblige to return without effect an 
entrance, as, with the greatest exertion, he could remain under water 
not more, at the utmost extent, than a single minute. our affair now 
look gloomy indeed, and neither augustus nor myself could refrain from 
burst into tears, as we thought of the host of difficulty which 
encompass us, and the slight probability which exist of our finally 
making an escape. but this weakness was not of long duration. throw 
ourselves on our knee to god, we implore his aid in the many danger 
which beset us; and arose with renew hope and vigor to think what 
could yet be done by mortal means toward accomplish our deliverance. 




chapter 10 

shortly afterward an incident occur which i am induce to look upon 
as more intense productive of emotion, as far more replete with the 
extreme first of delight and then of horror, than even any of the 
thousand chance which afterward befell me in nine long years, crowded 
with event of the most startle and, in many cases, of the most 
unconceived and unconceivable character. we were lie on the deck near 
the companion-way, and debate the possibility of yet making our way 
into the storeroom, when, look toward augustus, who lay front 
myself, i perceive that he had become all at once deadly pale, and that 
his lip were quiver in the most singular and unaccountable manner. 
greatly alarmed, i spoke to him, but he made me no reply, and i was 
beginning to think that he was sudden taken ill, when i took notice 
of his eyes, which were glaring apparently at some object behind me. i 
turn my head, and shall never forget the ecstatic joy which thrill 
through every particle of my frame, when i perceive a large brig 
bearing down upon us, and not more than a couple of mile off. i sprung 
to my feet as if a musket bullet had sudden struck me to the heart; 
and, stretch out my arms in the direction of the vessel, stood in 
this manner, motionless, and unable to articulate a syllable. peters 
and parker were equally affected, although in different ways. the former 
dance about the deck like a madman, utter the most extravagant 
rhodomontades, intermingle with howl and imprecations, while the 
latter burst into tears, and continue for many minutes weeping like a 
child. 

the vessel in sight was a large hermaphrodite brig, of a dutch build, 
and paint black, with a tawdry gilt figure-head. she had evidently 
seen a good deal of rough weather, and, we supposed, had suffer 
much in the gale which had prove so disastrous to ourselves; for her 
foretopmast was gone, and some of her starboard bulwarks. when we first 
saw her, she was, as i have already said, about two mile off and to 
windward, bearing down upon us. the breeze was very gentle, and what 
astonish us chiefly was, that she had no other sail set than her 
foremast and mainsail, with a flying jib--of course she came down but 
slowly, and our impatience amount nearly to phrensy. the awkward 
manner in which she steered, too, was remark by all of us, even 
excited as we were. she yaw about so considerably, that once or twice 
we thought it impossible she could see us, or imagine that, have seen 
us, and discover no person on board, she was about to tack and make 
off in another direction. upon each of these occasion we scream and 
shout at the top of our voices, when the stranger would appear to 
change for a moment her intention, and again hold on toward us--this 
singular conduct being repeated two or three times, so that at last we 
could think of no other manner of account for it than by supposing 
the helmsman to be in liquor. 

no person was seen upon her deck until she arrive within about a 
quarter of a mile of us. we then saw three seamen, whom by their dress 
we took to be hollanders. two of these were lie on some old sail near 
the forecastle, and the third, who appear to be look at us with 
great curiosity, was leaning over the starboard bow near the bowsprit. 
this last was a stout and tall man, with a very dark skin. he seem 
by his manner to be encourage us to have patience, nod to us in 
a cheerful although rather odd way, and smile constantly, so as to 
display a set of the most brilliant white teeth. as his vessel drew 
nearer, we saw a red flannel cap which he had on fall from his head into 
the water; but of this he took little or no notice, continue his 
odd smile and gesticulations. i relate these things and circumstances 
minutely, and i relate them, it must be understood, precisely as they 
_appeared _to us. 

the brig came on slowly, and now more steady than before, and--i 
cannot speak calm of this event--our heart leap up wildly within 
us, and we pour out our whole soul in shout and thanksgiving to 
god for the complete, unexpected, and glorious deliverance that was so 
palpable at hand. of a sudden, and all at once, there came waft over 
the ocean from the strange vessel (which was now close upon us) a 
smell, a stench, such as the whole world has no name for--no conception 
of--hellish--utterly suffocating--insufferable, inconceivable. i gasp 
for breath, and turning to my companions, perceive that they were pale 
than marble. but we had now no time left for question or surmise--the 
brig was within fifty feet of us, and it seem to be her intention to 
run under our counter, that we might board her without putt out a 
boat. we rush aft, when, suddenly, a wide yaw threw her off full five 
or six points from the course she had been running, and, as she pass 
under our stern at the distance of about twenty feet, we had a full view 
of her decks. shall i ever forget the triple horror of that spectacle? 
twenty-five or thirty human bodies, among whom were several females, lay 
scattered about between the counter and the galley in the last and most 
loathsome state of putrefaction. we plainly saw that not a soul live in 
that fated vessel! yet we could not help shouting to the dead for help! 
yes, long and loud did we beg, in the agony of the moment, that those 
silent and disgust image would stay for us, would not abandon us to 
become like them, would receive us among their goodly company! we were 
raving with horror and despair--thoroughly mad through the anguish of 
our grievous disappointment. 

as our first loud yell of terror broke forth, it was reply to by 
something, from near the bowsprit of the stranger, so close resemble 
the scream of a human voice that the nicest ear might have been startle 
and deceived. at this instant another sudden yaw brought the region of 
the forecastle for a moment into view, and we beheld at once the origin 
of the sound. we saw the tall stout figure still leaning on the bulwark, 
and still nod his head to and fro, but his face was now turn from 
us so that we could not behold it. his arms were extend over the rail, 
and the palm of his hand fell outward. his knee were lodge upon 
a stout rope, tight stretched, and reach from the heel of the 
bowsprit to a cathead. on his back, from which a portion of the shirt 
had been torn, leave it bare, there sat a huge sea-gull, busy 
gorge itself with the horrible flesh, its bill and talon deep buried, 
and its white plumage spatter all over with blood. as the brig move 
farther round so as to bring us close in view, the bird, with much 
apparent difficulty, drew out its crimson head, and, after eye us 
for a moment as if stupefied, arose lazy from the body upon which it 
had been feasting, and, flying directly above our deck, hover there 
a while with a portion of clot and liver-like substance in its beak. 
the horrid morsel drop at length with a sullen splash immediately 
at the feet of parker. may god forgive me, but now, for the first time, 
there flash through my mind a thought, a thought which i will not 
mention, and i felt myself making a step toward the ensanguined spot. 
i look upward, and the eyes of augustus met my own with a degree of 
intense and eager meaning which immediately brought me to my senses. i 
sprang forward quickly, and, with a deep shudder, threw the frightful 
thing into the sea. 

the body from which it had been taken, rest as it did upon the rope, 
had been easily sway to and fro by the exertion of the carnivorous 
bird, and it was this motion which had at first impress us with the 
belief of its being alive. as the gull relieved it of its weight, 
it swung round and fell partially over, so that the face was fully 
discovered. never, surely, was any object so terribly full of awe! the 
eyes were gone, and the whole flesh around the mouth, leave the teeth 
utterly naked. this, then, was the smile which had cheer us on to 
hope! this the--but i forbear. the brig, as i have already told, pass 
under our stern, and made its way slow but steady to leeward. with 
her and with her terrible crew went all our gay vision of deliverance 
and joy. deliberate as she went by, we might possibly have found means 
of boarding her, had not our sudden disappointment and the appalling 
nature of the discovery which accompany it laid entire prostrate 
every active faculty of mind and body. we had seen and felt, but 
we could neither think nor act, until, alas! too late. how much our 
intellect had been weaken by this incident may be estimate by the 
fact, that when the vessel had proceed so far that we could perceive 
no more than the half of her hull, the proposition was seriously 
entertain of attempt to overtake her by swimming! 

i have, since this period, vain endeavour to obtain some clew to the 
hideous uncertainty which envelop the fate of the stranger. her build 
and general appearance, as i have before stated, led us to the belief 
that she was a dutch trader, and the dress of the crew also sustain 
this opinion. we might have easily seen the name upon her stern, and, 
indeed, taken other observations, which would have guide us in making 
out her character; but the intense excitement of the moment blind us 
to every thing of that nature. from the saffron-like hue of such of the 
corpse as were not entire decayed, we conclude that the whole of her 
company had perish by the yellow fever, or some other virulent disease 
of the same fearful kind. if such were the case (and i know not what 
else to imagine), death, to judge from the position of the bodies, must 
have come upon them in a manner awfully sudden and overwhelming, in a 
way total distinct from that which generally characterize even 
the most deadly pestilence with which mankind are acquainted. it is 
possible, indeed, that poison, accidental introduce into some of 
their sea-stores, may have brought about the disaster, or that the 
eat of some unknown venomous species of fish, or other marine animal, 
or ocean bird, might have induce it--but it is utterly useless to 
form conjecture where all is involved, and will, no doubt, remain for 
ever involved, in the most appalling and unfathomable mystery. 




chapter 11 

we spent the remainder of the day in a condition of stupid lethargy, 
gaze after the retreat vessel until the darkness, hiding her from 
our sight, recall us in some measure to our senses. the pang 
of hunger and thirst then returned, absorbing all other care and 
considerations. nothing, however, could be done until the morning, 
and, secure ourselves as well as possible, we endeavour to snatch 
a little repose. in this i succeed beyond my expectations, sleep 
until my companions, who had not been so fortunate, arouse me at 
daybreak to renew our attempt at get up provisions from the hull. 

it was now a dead calm, with the sea as smooth as i have ever known 
it,--the weather warm and pleasant. the brig was out of sight. we 
commence our operation by wrench off, with some trouble, another of 
the forechains; and have fasten both to peters’ feet, he again made 
an endeavour to reach the door of the storeroom, thinking it possible 
that he might be able to force it open, provided he could get at it 
in sufficient time; and this he hope to do, as the hulk lay much more 
steady than before. 

he succeed very quick in reach the door, when, loosen one of 
the chain from his ankle, he made every exertion to force the passage 
with it, but in vain, the framework of the room being far strong than 
was anticipated. he was quite exhaust with his long stay under water, 
and it became absolutely necessary that some other one of us should take 
his place. for this service parker immediately volunteered; but, after 
making three ineffectual efforts, found that he could never even succeed 
in get near the door. the condition of augustus’s wound arm 
render it useless for him to attempt go down, as he would be 
unable to force the room open should he reach it, and it accordingly now 
devolve upon me to exert myself for our common deliverance. 

peters had left one of the chain in the passage, and i found, upon 
plunge in, that i had not sufficient balance to keep me firm down. 
i determined, therefore, to attempt no more, in my first effort, than 
merely to recover the other chain. in grope along the floor of the 
passage for this, i felt a hard substance, which i immediately grasped, 
not have time to ascertain what it was, but return and ascend 
instantly to the surface. the prize prove to be a bottle, and our joy 
may be conceive when i say that it was found to be full of port 
wine. give thanks to god for this timely and cheering assistance, we 
immediately drew the cork with my penknife, and, each taking a moderate 
sup, felt the most indescribable comfort from the warmth, strength, 
and spirits with which it inspired us. we then careful recorke the 
bottle, and, by means of a handkerchief, swung it in such a manner that 
there was no possibility of its get broken. 

have rest a while after this fortunate discovery, i again descended, 
and now recover the chain, with which i instantly came up. i then 
fasten it on and went down for the third time, when i became fully 
satisfy that no exertion whatever, in that situation, would enable 
me to force open the door of the storeroom. i therefore return in 
despair. 

there seem now to be no long any room for hope, and i could perceive 
in the countenance of my companion that they had made up their 
mind to perish. the wine had evidently produce in them a species 
of delirium, which, perhaps, i had been prevent from feeling by the 
immersion i had undergone since drink it. they talk incoherently, 
and about matter unconnected with our condition, peters repeatedly 
ask me question about nantucket. augustus, too, i remember, 
approach me with a serious air, and request me to lend him a 
pocket-comb, as his hair was full of fish-scales, and he wish to get 
them out before go on shore. parker appear somewhat less affected, 
and urge me to dive at random into the cabin, and bring up any article 
which might come to hand. to this i consented, and, in the first 
attempt, after stay under a full minute, brought up a small leather 
trunk belong to captain barnard. this was immediately open in the 
faint hope that it might contain something to eat or drink. we found 
nothing, however, except a box of razor and two linen shirts. i now 
went down again, and return without any success. as my head came 
above water i heard a crash on deck, and, upon get up, saw that my 
companion had ungrateful taken advantage of my absence to drink the 
remainder of the wine, have let the bottle fall in the endeavour 
to replace it before i saw them. i remonstrate with them on the 
heartless of their conduct, when augustus burst into tears. the 
other two endeavour to laugh the matter off as a joke, but i hope 
never again to behold laughter of such a species: the distortion of 
countenance was absolutely frightful. indeed, it was apparent that the 
stimulus, in the empty state of their stomachs, had taken instant and 
violent effect, and that they were all exceedingly intoxicated. with 
great difficulty i prevail upon them to lie down, when they fell very 
soon into a heavy slumber, accompany with loud stertorous breathing. 

i now found myself, as it were, alone in the brig, and my reflections, 
to be sure, were of the most fearful and gloomy nature. no prospect 
offer itself to my view but a lingering death by famine, or, at the 
best, by being overwhelm in the first gale which should spring up, 
for in our present exhaust condition we could have no hope of living 
through another. 

the gnawing hunger which i now experienced was nearly insupportable, and 
i felt myself capable of go to any length in order to appease 
it. with my knife i cut off a small portion of the leather trunk, and 
endeavour to eat it, but found it utterly impossible to swallow a 
single morsel, although i fancy that some little alleviate of my 
suffering was obtain by chew small pieces of it and spit 
them out. toward night my companion awoke, one by one, each in an 
indescribable state of weakness and horror, brought on by the wine, 
whose fumes had now evaporated. they shook as if with a violent ague, 
and utter the most lamentable cries for water. their condition 
affected me in the most lively degree, at the same time cause me to 
rejoice in the fortunate train of circumstances which had prevent 
me from indulge in the wine, and consequently from share their 
melancholy and most distressing sensations. their conduct, however, 
gave me great uneasy and alarm; for it was evident that, unless 
some favourable change took place, they could afford me no assistance in 
providing for our common safety. i had not yet abandoned all idea of being 
able to get up something from below; but the attempt could not possibly 
be resume until some one of them was sufficient master of himself to 
aid me by holding the end of the rope while i went down. parker appear 
to be somewhat more in possession of his senses than the others, and i 
endeavoured, by every means in my power, to rouse him. thinking that a 
plunge in the sea-water might have a beneficial effect, i contrived to 
fasten the end of a rope around his body, and then, leading him to the 
companion-way (he remain quite passive all the while), pushed him in, 
and immediately drew him out. i had good reason to congratulate myself 
upon have made this experiment; for he appear much revive and 
invigorated, and, upon get out, ask me, in a rational manner, why 
i had so serve him. have explain my object, he express himself 
indebted to me, and said that he felt greatly better from the immersion, 
afterward converse sensible upon our situation. we then resolve to 
treat augustus and peters in the same way, which we immediately did, 
when they both experienced much benefit from the shock. this idea of 
sudden immersion had been suggest to me by reading in some medical 
work the good effect of the shower-bath in a case where the patient was 
suffering from _mania a potu_. 

finding that i could now trust my companion to hold the end of the 
rope, i again made three or four plunge into the cabin, although it was 
now quite dark, and a gentle but long swell from the northward render 
the hulk somewhat unsteady. in the course of these attempt i succeed 
in bring up two case-knives, a three-gallon jug, empty, and a 
blanket, but nothing which could serve us for food. i continue my 
efforts, after get these articles, until i was completely exhausted, 
but brought up nothing else. during the night parker and peters occupy 
themselves by turn in the same manner; but nothing coming to hand, we 
now gave up this attempt in despair, conclude that we were exhaust 
ourselves in vain. 

we pass the remainder of this night in a state of the most intense 
mental and bodily anguish that can possibly be imagined. the morning of 
the sixteenth at length dawned, and we look eager around the horizon 
for relief, but to no purpose. the sea was still smooth, with only a 
long swell from the northward, as on yesterday. this was the sixth day 
since we had taste either food or drink, with the exception of the 
bottle of port wine, and it was clear that we could hold out but a very 
little while long unless something could be obtained. i never saw 
before, nor wish to see again, human being so utterly emaciate as 
peters and augustus. had i met them on shore in their present condition 
i should not have had the slightest suspicion that i had ever beheld 
them. their countenance were total change in character, so that i 
could not bring myself to believe them really the same individual with 
whom i had been in company but a few days before. parker, although sadly 
reduced, and so feeble that he could not raise his head from his bosom, 
was not so far gone as the other two. he suffer with great patience, 
making no complaint, and endeavour to inspire us with hope in every 
manner he could devise. for myself, although at the commencement of 
the voyage i had been in bad health, and was at all times of a delicate 
constitution, i suffer less than any of us, being much less reduce in 
frame, and retain my powers of mind in a surprising degree, while the 
rest were completely prostrate in intellect, and seem to be 
brought to a species of second childhood, generally simper in 
their expressions, with idiot smiles, and utter the most absurd 
platitudes. at intervals, however, they would appear to revive suddenly, 
as if inspired all at once with a consciousness of their condition, when 
they would spring upon their feet in a momentary flash of vigour, and 
speak, for a short period, of their prospects, in a manner altogether 
rational, although full of the most intense despair. it is possible, 
however, that my companion may have entertain the same opinion of 
their own condition as i did of mine, and that i may have 
unwitting been guilty of the same extravagance and imbecility as 
themselves--this is a matter which cannot be determined. 

about noon parker declared that he saw land off the larboard quarter, 
and it was with the utmost difficulty i could restrain him from plunge 
into the sea with the view of swimming toward it. peters and augustus 
took little notice of what he said, being apparently wrap up in moody 
contemplation. upon look in the direction pointed out, i could not 
perceive the faintest appearance of the shore--indeed, i was too well 
aware that we were far from any land to indulge in a hope of that 
nature. it was a long time, nevertheless, before i could convince parker 
of his mistake. he then burst into a flood of tears, weeping like a 
child, with loud cries and sobs, for two or three hours, when becoming 
exhausted, he fell asleep. 

peters and augustus now made several ineffectual effort to swallow 
portion of the leather. i advise them to chew it and spit it out; but 
they were too excessive debilitate to be able to follow my advice. i 
continue to chew pieces of it at intervals, and found some relief from 
so doing; my chief distress was for water, and i was only prevent from 
taking a draught from the sea by remember the horrible consequence 
which thus have result to other who were similarly situated with 
ourselves. 

the day wore on in this manner, when i sudden discover a sail to the 
eastward, and on our larboard bow. she appear to be a large ship, and 
was coming nearly athwart us, being probably twelve or fifteen mile 
distant. none of my companion had as yet discover her, and i forbore 
to tell them of her for the present, lest we might again be disappointed 
of relief. at length upon her get nearer, i saw distinct that she 
was heading immediately for us, with her light sail filled. i could now 
contain myself no longer, and pointed her out to my fellow-sufferers. 
they immediately sprang to their feet, again indulge in the most 
extravagant demonstration of joy, weeping, laugh in an idiot 
manner, jumping, stamp upon the deck, tear their hair, and pray 
and curse by turns. i was so affected by their conduct, as well as 
by what i considered a sure prospect of deliverance, that i could not 
refrain from join in with their madness, and gave way to the impulse 
of my gratitude and ecstasy by lie and rolling on the deck, clap 
my hands, shouting, and other similar acts, until i was sudden call 
to my recollection, and once more to the extreme human misery and 
despair, by perceive the ship all at once with her stern fully 
present toward us, and steer in a direction nearly opposite to that 
in which i had at first perceive her. 

it was some time before i could induce my poor companion to believe 
that this sad reverse in our prospects had actually taken place. they 
reply to all my assertion with a stare and a gesture imply that 
they were not to be deceive by such misrepresentations. the conduct of 
augustus most sensible affected me. in spite of all i could say or do to 
the contrary, he persist in saying that the ship was rapid near 
us, and in making preparation to go on board of her. some seaweed 
floating by the brig, he maintain that it was the ship’s boat, and 
endeavour to throw himself upon it, howling and shriek in the most 
heartrending manner, when i forcibly restrained him from thus casting 
himself into the sea. 

have become in some degree pacified, we continue to watch the ship 
until we finally lost sight of her, the weather becoming hazy, with 
a light breeze spring up. as soon as she was entire gone, parker 
turn sudden toward me with an expression of countenance which made 
me shudder. there was about him an air of self-possession which i had 
not notice in him until now, and before he open his lip my heart 
told me what he would say. he proposed, in a few words, that one of us 
should die to preserve the existence of the others. 




chapter 12 

i had for some time past, dwelt upon the prospect of our being reduce 
to this last horrible extremity, and had secret made up my mind to 
suffer death in any shape or under any circumstances rather than resort 
to such a course. nor was this resolution in any degree weaken by the 
present intensity of hunger under which i laboured. the proposition had 
not been heard by either peters or augustus. i therefore took parker 
aside; and mental pray to god for power to dissuade him from the 
horrible purpose he entertained, i expostulate with him for a long 
time, and in the most supplicate manner, beg him in the name of 
every thing which he held sacred, and urge him by every species of 
argument which the extremity of the case suggested, to abandon the idea, 
and not to mention it to either of the other two. 

he heard all i said without attempt to controvert any of my 
arguments, and i had begun to hope that he would be prevail upon to do 
as i desired. but when i had cease speaking, he said that he knew very 
well all i had said was true, and that to resort to such a course was 
the most horrible alternative which could enter into the mind of man; 
but that he had now held out as long as human nature could be sustained; 
that it was unnecessary for all to perish, when, by the death of one, 
it was possible, and even probable, that the rest might be finally 
preserved; add that i might save myself the trouble of try to turn 
him from his purpose, his mind have been thorough made up on the 
subject even before the appearance of the ship, and that only her 
heave in sight had prevent him from mention his intention at an 
early period. 

i now beg him, if he would not be prevail upon to abandon his 
design, at least to defer it for another day, when some vessel might 
come to our relief; again reiterate every argument i could devise, and 
which i thought likely to have influence with one of his rough nature. 
he said, in reply, that he had not spoken until the very last possible 
moment, that he could exist no long without sustenance of some kind, 
and that therefore in another day his suggestion would be too late, as 
regard himself at least. 

finding that he was not to be move by anything i could say in a mild 
tone, i now assume a different demeanor, and told him that he must be 
aware i had suffer less than any of us from our calamities; that my 
health and strength, consequently, were at that moment far better than 
his own, or than that either of peters or augustus; in short, that i was 
in a condition to have my own way by force if i found it necessary; 
and that if he attempt in any manner to acquaint the other with his 
bloody and cannibal designs, i would not hesitate to throw him into the 
sea. upon this he immediately seize me by the throat, and drawing a 
knife, made several ineffectual effort to stab me in the stomach; 
an atrocity which his excessive debility alone prevent him from 
accomplishing. in the meantime, being rouse to a high pitch of anger, i 
forced him to the vessel’s side, with the full intention of throw him 
overboard. he was save from his fate, however, by the interference of 
peters, who now approach and separate us, ask the cause of the 
disturbance. this parker told before i could find means in any manner to 
prevent him. 

the effect of his words was even more terrible than what i had 
anticipated. both augustus and peters, who, it seems, had long secret 
entertain the same fearful idea which parker had been merely the 
first to broach, join with him in his design and insist upon its 
immediately being carry into effect. i had calculate that one at 
least of the two former would be found still possessed of sufficient 
strength of mind to side with myself in resist any attempt to execute 
so dreadful a purpose, and, with the aid of either one of them, i had no 
fear of being able to prevent its accomplishment. being disappointed in 
this expectation, it became absolutely necessary that i should attend 
to my own safety, as a further resistance on my part might possibly be 
considered by men in their frightful condition a sufficient excuse 
for refuse me fair play in the tragedy that i knew would speedy be 
enacted. 

i now told them i was willing to submit to the proposal, merely 
request a delay of about one hour, in order that the fog which had 
gather around us might have an opportunity of lifting, when it was 
possible that the ship we had seen might be again in sight. after great 
difficulty i obtain from them a promise to wait thus long; and, as i 
had anticipate (a breeze rapid coming in), the fog lift before the 
hour had expired, when, no vessel appear in sight, we prepared to 
draw lots. 

it is with extreme reluctance that i dwell upon the appalling scene 
which ensued; a scene which, with its minutest details, no after event 
have been able to efface in the slightest degree from my memory, 
and whose stern recollection will embitter every future moment of my 
existence. let me run over this portion of my narrative with as much 
haste as the nature of the event to be spoken of will permit. the only 
method we could devise for the terrific lottery, in which we were to 
take each a chance, was that of drawing straws. small splinter of wood 
were made to answer our purpose, and it was agree that i should be 
the holder. i retired to one end of the hulk, while my poor companion 
silent took up their station in the other with their back turn 
toward me. the bitterest anxiety which i endure at any period of this 
fearful drama was while i occupy myself in the arrangement of the 
lots. there are few conditions into which man can possibly fall where he 
will not feel a deep interest in the preservation of his existence; 
an interest momentarily increase with the frail of the tenure by 
which that existence may be held. but now that the silent, definite, and 
stern nature of the business in which i was engaged (so different from 
the tumultuous danger of the storm or the gradual approach horrors 
of famine) allow me to reflect on the few chance i had of escape 
the most appalling of deaths--a death for the most appalling of 
purposes--every particle of that energy which had so long buoy me up 
departed like feather before the wind, leave me a helpless prey to 
the most abject and pitiable terror. i could not, at first, even summon 
up sufficient strength to tear and fit together the small splinter of 
wood, my finger absolutely refuse their office, and my knee knock 
violent against each other. my mind ran over rapid a thousand absurd 
project by which to avoid becoming a partner in the awful speculation. 
i thought of fall on my knee to my companions, and entreat them 
to let me escape this necessity; of sudden rush upon them, and, 
by putt one of them to death, of rendering the decision by lot 
useless--in short, of every thing but of go through with the matter 
i had in hand. at last, after waste a long time in this imbecile 
conduct, i was recall to my senses by the voice of parker, who urge 
me to relieve them at once from the terrible anxiety they were enduring. 
even then i could not bring myself to arrange the splinter upon the 
spot, but thought over every species of finesse by which i could trick 
some one of my fellow-sufferers to draw the short straw, as it had been 
agree that whoever drew the shortest of four splinter from my hand was 
to die for the preservation of the rest. before any one condemn me for 
this apparent heartlessness, let him be placed in a situation precisely 
similar to my own. 

at length delay was no long possible, and, with a heart almost 
burst from my bosom, i advanced to the region of the forecastle, 
where my companion were await me. i held out my hand with the 
splinters, and peters immediately drew. he was free--his, at least, was 
not the shortest; and there was now another chance against my escape. 
i summon up all my strength, and pass the lot to augustus. he also 
drew immediately, and he also was free; and now, whether i should live 
or die, the chance were no more than precisely even. at this moment 
all the fierce of the tiger possessed my bosom, and i felt toward 
my poor fellow-creature, parker, the most intense, the most diabolical 
hatred. but the feeling did not last; and, at length, with a convulsive 
shudder and closed eyes, i held out the two remain splinter toward 
him. it was fully five minutes before he could summon resolution to 
draw, during which period of heartrending suspense i never once open 
my eyes. presently one of the two lot was quick drawn from my hand. 
the decision was then over, yet i knew not whether it was for me or 
against me. no one spoke, and still i dare not satisfy myself by 
look at the splinter i held. peters at length took me by the 
hand, and i forced myself to look up, when i immediately saw by the 
countenance of parker that i was safe, and that he it was who had been 
doom to suffer. gasp for breath, i fell senseless to the deck. 

i recover from my swoon in time to behold the consummation of the 
tragedy in the death of him who had been chiefly instrumental in 
bring it about. he made no resistance whatever, and was stab in 
the back by peters, when he fell instantly dead. i must not dwell 
upon the fearful repast which immediately ensued. such things may be 
imagined, but words have no power to impress the mind with the exquisite 
horror of their reality. let it suffice to say that, have in some 
measure appease the rage thirst which consume us by the blood of 
the victim, and have by common consent taken off the hands, feet, 
and head, throw them together with the entrails, into the sea, we 
devour the rest of the body, piecemeal, during the four ever memorable 
days of the seventeenth, eighteenth, nineteenth, and twentieth of the 
month. 

on the nineteenth, there coming on a smart shower which last fifteen 
or twenty minutes, we contrived to catch some water by means of a sheet 
which had been fish up from the cabin by our drag just after the gale. 
the quantity we took in all did not amount to more than half a gallon; 
but even this scanty allowance supply us with comparative strength and 
hope. 

on the twenty-first we were again reduce to the last necessity. the 
weather still remain warm and pleasant, with occasional fog and light 
breezes, most usually from n. to w. 

on the twenty-second, as we were sitting close huddle together, 
gloomy revolving over our lamentable condition, there flash through 
my mind all at once an idea which inspired me with a bright gleam of 
hope. i remember that, when the foremast had been cut away, peters, 
being in the windward chains, pass one of the axe into my hand, 
request me to put it, if possible, in a place of security, and that 
a few minutes before the last heavy sea struck the brig and fill her 
i had taken this axe into the forecastle and laid it in one of the 
larboard berths. i now thought it possible that, by get at this 
axe, we might cut through the deck over the storeroom, and thus readily 
supply ourselves with provisions. 

when i communicate this object to my companions, they utter a feeble 
shout of joy, and we all proceed forthwith to the forecastle. the 
difficulty of descend here was greater than that of go down in the 
cabin, the opening being much smaller, for it will be remember that 
the whole framework about the cabin companion-hatch had been carry 
away, whereas the forecastle-way, being a simple hatch of only about 
three feet square, had remain uninjured. i did not hesitate, however, 
to attempt the descent; and a rope being fasten round my body as 
before, i plunge bold in, feet foremost, made my way quick to the 
berth, and at the first attempt brought up the axe. it was hail with 
the most ecstatic joy and triumph, and the ease with which it had been 
obtain was regard as an omen of our ultimate preservation. 

we now commence cutting at the deck with all the energy of rekindle 
hope, peters and myself taking the axe by turns, augustus’s wound arm 
not permit him to aid us in any degree. as we were still so feeble 
as to be scarcely able to stand unsupported, and could consequently work 
but a minute or two without resting, it soon became evident that many 
long hour would be necessary to accomplish our task--that is, to cut an 
opening sufficient large to admit of a free access to the storeroom. 
this consideration, however, did not discourage us; and, working all 
night by the light of the moon, we succeed in effect our purpose by 
daybreak on the morning of the twenty-third. 

peters now volunteer to go down; and, have made all arrangement 
as before, he descended, and soon return bring up with him a small 
jar, which, to our great joy, prove to be full of olives. have 
share these among us, and devour them with the greatest avidity, 
we proceed to let him down again. this time he succeed beyond our 
utmost expectations, return instantly with a large ham and a bottle 
of madeira wine. of the latter we each took a moderate sup, have 
learned by experience the pernicious consequence of indulge too 
freely. the ham, except about two pound near the bone, was not in a 
condition to be eaten, have been entire spoil by the salt water. 
the sound part was divide among us. peters and augustus, not being able 
to restrain their appetite, swallow theirs upon the instant; but i was 
more cautious, and ate but a small portion of mine, dread the thirst 
which i knew would ensue. we now rest a while from our labors, which 
had been intolerable severe. 

by noon, feeling somewhat strengthen and refreshed, we again renew 
our attempt at get up provisions, peters and myself go down 
alternately, and always with more or less success, until sundown. during 
this interval we had the good fortune to bring up, altogether, four 
more small jar of olives, another ham, a carboy contain nearly three 
gallon of excellent cape madeira wine, and, what gave us still more 
delight, a small tortoise of the gallipago breed, several of which had 
been taken on board by captain barnard, as the _grampus_ was leave 
port, from the schooner _mary pitts_, just return from a sealing 
voyage in the pacific. 

in a subsequent portion of this narrative i shall have frequent occasion 
to mention this species of tortoise. it is found principally, as most 
of my reader may know, in the group of island call the gallipagos, 
which, indeed, derive their name from the animal--the spain word 
gallipago meaning a fresh-water terrapin. from the peculiarity of their 
shape and action they have been sometimes call the elephant tortoise. 
they are frequent found of an enormous size. i have myself seen 
several which would weigh from twelve to fifteen hundred pounds, 
although i do not remember that any navigator speak of have seen them 
weigh more than eight hundred. their appearance is singular, and even 
disgusting. their steps are very slow, measured, and heavy, their body 
being carry about a foot from the ground. their neck is long, and 
exceedingly slender, from eighteen inch to two feet is a very common 
length, and i kill one, where the distance from the shoulder to the 
extremity of the head was no less than three feet ten inches. the head 
has a striking resemblance to that of a serpent. they can exist without 
food for an almost incredible length of time, instance have been 
known where they have been thrown into the hold of a vessel and lain 
two years without nourishment of any kind--being as fat, and, in every 
respect, in as good order at the expiration of the time as when they 
were first put in. in one particular these extraordinary animal bear 
a resemblance to the dromedary, or camel of the desert. in a bag at the 
root of the neck they carry with them a constant supply of water. in 
some instances, upon killing them after a full year’s deprivation of all 
nourishment, as much as three gallon of perfectly sweet and fresh water 
have been found in their bags. their food is chiefly wild parsley and 
celery, with purslain, sea-kelp, and prickly pears, upon which latter 
vegetable they thrive wonderfully, a great quantity of it being usually 
found on the hillside near the shore wherever the animal itself is 
discovered. they are excellent and highly nutritious food, and have, 
no doubt, been the means of preserve the lives of thousand of seamen 
employ in the whale-fishery and other pursuit in the pacific. 

the one which we had the good fortune to bring up from the storeroom was 
not of a large size, weigh probably sixty-five or seventy pounds. 
it was a female, and in excellent condition, being exceedingly fat, and 
have more than a quart of limpid and sweet water in its bag. this 
was indeed a treasure; and, fall on our knee with one accord, we 
return fervent thanks to god for so seasonable a relief. 

we had great difficulty in get the animal up through the opening, as 
its struggle were fierce and its strength prodigious. it was upon the 
point of making its escape from peter’s grasp, and slip back into 
the water, when augustus, throw a rope with a slipknot around its 
throat, held it up in this manner until i jump into the hole by the 
side of peters, and assist him in lift it out. 

the water we drew careful from the bag into the jug; which, it will be 
remembered, had been brought up before from the cabin. have done this, 
we broke off the neck of a bottle so as to form, with the cork, a kind 
of glass, holding not quite half a gill. we then each drank one of these 
measure full, and resolve to limit ourselves to this quantity per day 
as long as it should hold out. 

during the last two or three days, the weather have been dry and 
pleasant, the bedding we had obtain from the cabin, as well as our 
clothing, had become thorough dry, so that we pass this night (that 
of the twenty-third) in comparative comfort, enjoy a tranquil 
repose, after have sup plentiful on olive and ham, with a 
small allowance of the wine. being afraid of lose some of our stores 
overboard during the night, in the event of a breeze spring up, we 
secure them as well as possible with cordage to the fragment of the 
windlass. our tortoise, which we were anxious to preserve alive as long 
as we could, we threw on its back, and otherwise careful fastened. 




chapter 13 

july 24. this morning saw us wonderful recruit in spirits and 
strength. notwithstanding the perilous situation in which we were still 
placed, ignorant of our position, although certainly at a great distance 
from land, without more food than would last us for a fortnight even 
with great care, almost entire without water, and floating about at 
the mercy of every wind and wave on the merest wreck in the world, still 
the infinite more terrible distress and danger from which we had so 
lately and so providential been deliver cause us to regard what 
we now endure as but little more than an ordinary evil--so strict 
comparative is either good or ill. 

at sunrise we were prepare to renew our attempt at get up 
something from the storeroom, when, a smart shower coming on, with some 
lightning, we turn our attention to the catching of water by means of 
the sheet we had use before for this purpose. we had no other means of 
collect the rain than by holding the sheet spread out with one of the 
forechain-plates in the middle of it. the water, thus conduct to the 
centre, was drain through into our jug. we had nearly fill it in 
this manner, when, a heavy squall coming on from the northward, oblige 
us to desist, as the hulk began once more to roll so violent that 
we could no long keep our feet. we now went forward, and, lashing 
ourselves secure to the remnant of the windlass as before, await the 
event with far more calm than could have been anticipate or would 
have been imagine possible under the circumstances. at noon the wind 
had freshen into a two-reef breeze, and by night into a stiff gale, 
accompany with a tremendous heavy swell. experience have taught 
us, however, the best method of arrange our lashings, we weather 
this dreary night in tolerable security, although thorough drench at 
almost every instant by the sea, and in momentary dread of being wash 
off. fortunately, the weather was so warm as to render the water rather 
grateful than otherwise. 

july 25. this morning the gale had diminish to a mere ten-knot breeze, 
and the sea had gone down with it so considerably that we were able to 
keep ourselves dry upon the deck. to our great grief, however, we found 
that two jar of our olives, as well as the whole of our ham, had been 
wash overboard, in spite of the careful manner in which they had been 
fastened. we determined not to kill the tortoise as yet, and contented 
ourselves for the present with a breakfast on a few of the olives, and 
a measure of water each, which latter we mixed half and half, with 
wine, finding great relief and strength from the mixture, without the 
distressing intoxicate which had ensue upon drink the port. the 
sea was still far too rough for the renewal of our effort at get up 
provision from the storeroom. several articles, of no importance to us 
in our present situation, float up through the opening during the day, 
and were immediately wash overboard. we also now observe that the 
hulk lay more along than ever, so that we could not stand an instant 
without lashing ourselves. on this account we pass a gloomy and 
uncomfortable day. at noon the sun appear to be nearly vertical, and 
we had no doubt that we had been driven down by the long succession of 
northward and northwesterly wind into the near vicinity of the equator. 
toward evening we saw several sharks, and were somewhat alarm by the 
audacious manner in which an enormously large one approach us. at one 
time, a lurch throw the deck very far beneath the water, the monster 
actually swam in upon us, flounder for some moments just over the 
companion-hatch, and striking peters violent with his tail. a heavy 
sea at length hurl him overboard, much to our relief. in moderate 
weather we might have easily capture him. 

july 26. this morning, the wind have greatly abated, and the sea not 
being very rough, we determined to renew our exertion in the storeroom. 
after a great deal of hard labor during the whole day, we found that 
nothing further was to be expect from this quarter, the partition of 
the room have been stove during the night, and its contents swept into 
the hold. this discovery, as may be supposed, fill us with despair. 

july 27. the sea nearly smooth, with a light wind, and still from the 
northward and westward. the sun coming out hotly in the afternoon, 
we occupy ourselves in dry our clothes. found great relief from 
thirst, and much comfort otherwise, by bathing in the sea; in this, 
however, we were forced to use great caution, being afraid of sharks, 
several of which were seen swimming around the brig during the day. 

july 28. good weather still. the brig now began to lie along so 
alarming that we fear she would eventually roll bottom up. prepared 
ourselves as well as we could for this emergency, lashing our tortoise, 
waterjug, and two remain jar of olive as far as possible over to 
the windward, place them outside the hull below the main-chains. the 
sea very smooth all day, with little or no wind. 

july 29. a continuance of the same weather. augustus’s wound arm began 
to evince symptom of mortification. he complain of drowsy and 
excessive thirst, but no acute pain. nothing could be done for his 
relief beyond rubbing his wound with a little of the vinegar from the 
olives, and from this no benefit seem to be experienced. we did every 
thing in our power for his comfort, and treble his allowance of water. 

july 30. an excessive hot day, with no wind. an enormous shark kept 
close by the hulk during the whole of the forenoon. we made several 
unsuccessful attempt to capture him by means of a noose. augustus much 
worse, and evidently sink as much from want of proper nourishment as 
from the effect of his wounds. he constant pray to be relieved from 
his sufferings, wish for nothing but death. this evening we ate the 
last of our olives, and found the water in our jug so putrid that we 
could not swallow it at all without the addition of wine. determined to 
kill our tortoise in the morning. 

july 31. after a night of excessive anxiety and fatigue, owe to the 
position of the hulk, we set about killing and cutting up our tortoise. 
he prove to be much small than we had supposed, although in good 
condition,--the whole meat about him not amount to more than ten 
pounds. with a view of preserve a portion of this as long as possible, 
we cut it into fine pieces, and fill with them our three remain 
olive jar and the wine-bottle (all of which had been kept), pour in 
afterward the vinegar from the olives. in this manner we put away about 
three pound of the tortoise, intend not to touch it until we had 
consume the rest. we conclude to restrict ourselves to about four 
ounce of the meat per day; the whole would thus last us thirteen days. 
a brisk shower, with severe thunder and lightning, came on about dusk, 
but last so short a time that we only succeed in catching about 
half a pint of water. the whole of this, by common consent, was given 
to augustus, who now appear to be in the last extremity. he drank the 
water from the sheet as we caught it (we holding it above him as he lay 
so as to let it run into his mouth), for we had now nothing left capable 
of holding water, unless we had chosen to empty out our wine from the 
carboy, or the stale water from the jug. either of these expedient 
would have been resort to had the shower lasted. 

the sufferer seem to derive but little benefit from the draught. his 
arm was completely black from the wrist to the shoulder, and his feet 
were like ice. we expect every moment to see him breathe his last. 
he was frightfully emaciated; so much so that, although he weigh a 
hundred and twenty-seven pound upon his leave nantucket, he now did 
not weigh more than forty or fifty at the farthest. his eyes were sunk 
far in his head, being scarcely perceptible, and the skin of his 
cheek hung so loose as to prevent his masticate any food, or even 
swallow any liquid, without great difficulty. 

august 1. a continuance of the same calm weather, with an oppressive 
hot sun. suffer exceedingly from thirst, the water in the jug being 
absolutely putrid and swarm with vermin. we contrived, nevertheless, 
to swallow a portion of it by mix it with wine; our thirst, however, 
was but little abated. we found more relief by bathing in the sea, but 
could not avail ourselves of this expedient except at long intervals, 
on account of the continual presence of sharks. we now saw clearly that 
augustus could not be saved; that he was evidently dying. we could do 
nothing to relieve his sufferings, which appear to be great. about 
twelve o’clock he expire in strong convulsions, and without have 
spoken for several hours. his death fill us with the most gloomy 
forebodings, and had so great an effect upon our spirits that we sat 
motionless by the corpse during the whole day, and never address each 
other except in a whisper. it was not until some time after dark that 
we took courage to get up and throw the body overboard. it was then 
loathsome beyond expression, and so far decay that, as peters 
attempt to lift it, an entire leg came off in his grasp. as the mass 
of putrefaction slip over the vessel’s side into the water, the glare 
of phosphoric light with which it was surround plainly discover to 
us seven or eight large sharks, the clash of whose horrible teeth, as 
their prey was torn to pieces among them, might have been heard at 
the distance of a mile. we shrunk within ourselves in the extremity of 
horror at the sound. 

august 2. the same fearful calm and hot weather. the dawn found us in 
a state of pitiable dejection as well as bodily exhaustion. the water 
in the jug was now absolutely useless, being a thick gelatinous mass; 
nothing but frightful-looking worm mingle with slime. we threw it out, 
and wash the jug well in the sea, afterward pour a little vinegar 
in it from our bottle of pickled tortoise. our thirst could now 
scarcely be endured, and we try in vain to relieve it by wine, which 
seem only to add fuel to the flame, and excited us to a high degree 
of intoxication. we afterward endeavour to relieve our suffering by 
mix the wine with seawater; but this instantly brought about the most 
violent retchings, so that we never again attempt it. during the whole 
day we anxious sought an opportunity of bathing, but to no purpose; 
for the hulk was now entire besiege on all side with sharks--no 
doubt the identical monster who had devour our poor companion on the 
evening before, and who were in momentary expectation of another similar 
feast. this circumstance occasion us the most bitter regret and 
fill us with the most depress and melancholy forebodings. we had 
experienced indescribable relief in bathing, and to have this resource 
cut off in so frightful a manner was more than we could bear. nor, 
indeed, were we altogether free from the apprehension of immediate 
danger, for the least slip or false movement would have thrown us 
at once within reach of those voracious fish, who frequent thrust 
themselves directly upon us, swimming up to leeward. no shout or 
exertion on our part seem to alarm them. even when one of the largest 
was struck with an axe by peters and much wounded, he persist in his 
attempt to push in where we were. a cloud came up at dusk, but, to our 
extreme anguish, pass over without discharge itself. it is quite 
impossible to conceive our suffering from thirst at this period. we 
pass a sleepless night, both on this account and through dread of the 
sharks. 

august 3. no prospect of relief, and the brig lie still more and more 
along, so that now we could not maintain a footing upon deck at all. 
busy ourselves in secure our wine and tortoise-meat, so that we 
might not lose them in the event of our rolling over. got out two stout 
spike from the forechains, and, by means of the axe, drove them into
