the hull to windward within a couple of feet of the water, this not 
being very far from the keel, as we were nearly upon our beam-ends. to 
these spike we now lash our provisions, as being more secure than 
their former position beneath the chains. suffer great agony from 
thirst during the whole day--no chance of bathing on account of the 
sharks, which never left us for a moment. found it impossible to sleep. 

august 4. a little before daybreak we perceive that the hulk was 
heel over, and arouse ourselves to prevent being thrown off by the 
movement. at first the roll was slow and gradual, and we contrived to 
clamber over to windward very well, have taken the precaution to leave 
ropes hanging from the spike we had driven in for the provision. but 
we had not calculate sufficient upon the acceleration of the impetus; 
for, presently the heel became too violent to allow of our keeping pace 
with it; and, before either of us knew what was to happen, we found 
ourselves hurl furiously into the sea, and struggle several fathom 
beneath the surface, with the huge hull immediately above us. 

in go under the water i had been oblige to let go my hold upon 
the rope; and finding that i was completely beneath the vessel, and 
my strength nearly exhausted, i scarcely made a struggle for life, 
and resigned myself, in a few seconds, to die. but here again i was 
deceived, not have taken into consideration the natural rebound of 
the hull to windward. the whirl of the water upward, which the vessel 
occasion in rolling partially back, brought me to the surface still 
more violent than i had been plunge beneath. upon coming up i found 
myself about twenty yard from the hulk, as near as i could judge. she 
was lie keel up, rock furiously from side to side, and the sea in 
all directions around was much agitated, and full of strong whirlpools. 
i could see nothing of peters. an oil-cask was floating within a few 
feet of me, and various other articles from the brig were scattered 
about. 

my principal terror was now on account of the sharks, which i knew to be 
in my vicinity. in order to deter these, if possible, from approach 
me, i splash the water vigorous with both hand and feet as i swam 
towards the hulk, create a body of foam. i have no doubt that to this 
expedient, simple as it was, i was indebted for my preservation; for 
the sea all round the brig, just before her rolling over, was so crowded 
with these monsters, that i must have been, and really was, in actual 
contact with some of them during my progress. by great good fortune, 
however, i reach the side of the vessel in safety, although so utterly 
weaken by the violent exertion i had use that i should never have 
been able to get upon it but for the timely assistance of peters, who, 
now, to my great joy, made his appearance (having scramble up to the 
keel from the opposite side of the hull), and threw me the end of a 
rope--one of those which had been attach to the spikes. 

have barely escape this danger, our attention was now direct to the 
dreadful imminent of another--that of absolute starvation. our whole 
stock of provision had been swept overboard in spite of all our care in 
secure it; and seeing no long the remotest possibility of obtain 
more, we gave way both of us to despair, weeping aloud like children, 
and neither of us attempt to offer consolation to the other. such 
weakness can scarcely be conceived, and to those who have never been 
similarly situated will, no doubt, appear unnatural; but it must be 
remember that our intellect were so entire disorder by the long 
course of privation and terror to which we had been subjected, that we 
could not just be considered, at that period, in the light of rational 
beings. in subsequent perils, nearly as great, if not greater, i bore 
up with fortitude against all the evil of my situation, and peters, it 
will be seen, evince a stoical philosophy nearly as incredible as his 
present childlike supine and imbecility--the mental condition made 
the difference. 

the overturn of the brig, even with the consequent loss of the 
wine and turtle, would not, in fact, have render our situation more 
deplorable than before, except for the disappearance of the bedclothes 
by which we had been hitherto enable to catch rainwater, and of the jug 
in which we had kept it when caught; for we found the whole bottom, from 
within two or three feet of the bends as far as the keel, together with 
the keel itself, thick cover with large barnacles, which prove 
to be excellent and highly nutritious food. thus, in two important 
respects, the accident we had so greatly dread prove to be a benefit 
rather than an injury; it had open to us a supply of provisions which 
we could not have exhausted, use it moderately, in a month; and it had 
greatly contribute to our comfort as regards position, we being much 
more at ease, and in infinite less danger, than before. 

the difficulty, however, of now obtain water blind us to all the 
benefit of the change in our condition. that we might be ready to avail 
ourselves, as far as possible, of any shower which might fall we took 
off our shirts, to make use of them as we had of the sheets--not hoping, 
of course, to get more in this way, even under the most favorable 
circumstances, than half a gill at a time. no sign of a cloud appear 
during the day, and the agony of our thirst were nearly intolerable. 
at night, peters obtain about an hour’s disturbed sleep, but my 
intense suffering would not permit me to close my eyes for a single 
moment. 

august 5. to-day, a gentle breeze spring up carry us through a vast 
quantity of seaweed, among which we were so fortunate as to find eleven 
small crabs, which afford us several delicious meals. their shell 
being quite soft, we ate them entire, and found that they irritate our 
thirst far less than the barnacles. seeing no trace of shark among the 
seaweed, we also venture to bathe, and remain in the water for four 
or five hours, during which we experienced a very sensible diminution 
of our thirst. were greatly refreshed, and spent the night somewhat more 
comfortable than before, both of us snatch a little sleep. 

august 6. this day we were blessed by a brisk and continual rain, 
lasting from about noon until after dark. bitter did we now regret the 
loss of our jug and carboy; for, in spite of the little means we had of 
catching the water, we might have fill one, if not both of them. as 
it was, we contrived to satisfy the craving of thirst by suffering 
the shirt to become saturated, and then wring them so as to let the 
grateful fluid trickle into our mouths. in this occupation we pass the 
entire day. 

august 7. just at daybreak we both at the same instant descry a 
sail to the eastward, and _evidently coming towards us!_ we hail the 
glorious sight with a long, although feeble shout of rapture; and began 
instantly to make every signal in our power, by flare the shirt in 
the air, leap as high as our weak condition would permit, and even by 
hallooe with all the strength of our lungs, although the vessel 
could not have been less than fifteen mile distant. however, she 
still continue to near our hulk, and we felt that, if she but held her 
present course, she must eventually come so close as to perceive us. in 
about an hour after we first discover her, we could clearly see the 
people on her decks. she was a long, low, and rakish-looking topsail 
schooner, with a black ball in her foretopsail, and had, apparently, 
a full crew. we now became alarmed, for we could hardly imagine it 
possible that she did not observe us, and were apprehensive that she 
meant to leave us to perish as we were--an act of fiendish barbarity, 
which, however incredible it may appear, has been repeatedly perpetuate 
at sea, under circumstances very nearly similar, and by being who 
were regard as belong to the human species. {*2} in this instance, 
however, by the mercy of god, we were destined to be most happily 
deceived; for, presently we were aware of a sudden commotion on the deck 
of the stranger, who immediately afterward ran up a britain flag, and, 
haul her wind, bore up directly upon us. in half an hour more 
we found ourselves in her cabin. she prove to be the jane guy, of 
liverpool, captain guy, bound on a sealing and trade voyage to the 
south sea and pacific. 




chapter 14 

the _jane guy_ was a fine-looking topsail schooner of a hundred and 
eighty ton burden. she was unusually sharp in the bows, and on a wind, 
in moderate weather, the fastest sail i have ever seen. her qualities, 
however, as a rough sea-boat, were not so good, and her draught of water 
was by far too great for the trade to which she was destined. for this 
peculiar service, a large vessel, and one of a light proportionate 
draught, is desirable--say a vessel of from three hundred to three 
hundred and fifty tons. she should be bark-rigged, and in other respects 
of a different construction from the usual south sea ships. it is 
absolutely necessary that she should be well armed. she should have, say 
ten or twelve twelve-pound carronades, and two or three long twelves, 
with brass blunderbusses, and water-tight arm-chests for each top. her 
anchor and cable should be of far greater strength than is require 
for any other species of trade, and, above all, her crew should be 
numerous and efficient--not less, for such a vessel as i have described, 
than fifty or sixty able-bodied men. the jane guy had a crew of 
thirty-five, all able seamen, besides the captain and mate, but she 
was not altogether as well armed or otherwise equipped, as a navigator 
acquaint with the difficulty and danger of the trade could have 
desired. 

captain guy was a gentleman of great urban of manner, and of 
considerable experience in the southern traffic, to which he had devoted 
a great portion of his life. he was deficient, however, in energy, and, 
consequently, in that spirit of enterprise which is here so absolutely 
requisite. he was part owner of the vessel in which he sailed, and was 
invest with discretionary powers to cruise in the south sea for any 
cargo which might come most readily to hand. he had on board, as usual 
in such voyages, beads, looking-glasses, tinder-works, axes, hatchets, 
saws, adzes, planes, chisels, gouges, gimlets, files, spokeshaves, 
rasps, hammers, nails, knives, scissors, razors, needles, thread, 
crockery-ware, calico, trinkets, and other similar articles. 

the schooner sail from liverpool on the tenth of july, cross the 
tropic of cancer on the twenty-fifth, in longitude twenty degree west, 
and reach sal, one of the cape verd islands, on the twenty-ninth, 
where she took in salt and other necessaries for the voyage. on 
the third of august, she left the cape verd and steer southwest, 
stretch over toward the coast of brazil, so as to cross the equator 
between the meridian of twenty-eight and thirty degree west longitude. 
this is the course usually taken by vessel bound from europe to the 
cape of good hope, or by that route to the east indies. by proceeding 
thus they avoid the calm and strong contrary current which continual 
prevail on the coast of guinea, while, in the end, it is found to be the 
shortest track, as westerly wind are never wanting afterward by which 
to reach the cape. it was captain guy’s intention to make his first 
stoppage at kerguelen’s land--i hardly know for what reason. on the 
day we were picked up the schooner was off cape st. roque, in longitude 
thirty-one degree west; so that, when found, we had drift probably, 
from north to south, _not less than five-and-twenty degrees!_ 

on board the jane guy we were treat with all the kindness our 
distress situation demanded. in about a fortnight, during which time 
we continue steer to the southeast, with gentle breeze and fine 
weather, both peters and myself recover entire from the effects of 
our late privation and dreadful sufferings, and we began to remember 
what had pass rather as a frightful dream from which we had been 
happily awakened, than as event which had taken place in sober and 
naked reality. i have since found that this species of partial oblivion 
is usually brought about by sudden transition, whether from joy 
to sorrow or from sorrow to joy--the degree of forgetful being 
proportion to the degree of difference in the exchange. thus, in my 
own case, i now feel it impossible to realize the full extent of 
the misery which i endure during the days spent upon the hulk. the 
incident are remembered, but not the feelings which the incident 
elicit at the time of their occurrence. i only know, that when they 
did occur, i then thought human nature could sustain nothing more of 
agony. 

we continue our voyage for some week without any incident of 
greater moment than the occasional meeting with whaling-ships, and more 
frequent with the black or right whale, so call in contradistinction 
to the spermaceti. these, however, were chiefly found south of the 
twenty-fifth parallel. on the sixteenth of september, being in the 
vicinity of the cape of good hope, the schooner encounter her first 
gale of any violence since leave liverpool. in this neighborhood, but 
more frequent to the south and east of the promontory (we were to 
the westward), navigator have often to contend with storm from the 
northward, which rage with great fury. they always bring with them a 
heavy sea, and one of their most dangerous features is the instantaneous 
chop round of the wind, an occurrence almost certain to take place 
during the greatest force of the gale. a perfect hurricane will be 
blow at one moment from the northward or northeast, and in the next 
not a breath of wind will be felt in that direction, while from 
the southwest it will come out all at once with a violence almost 
inconceivable. a bright spot to the southward is the sure forerunner of 
the change, and vessel are thus enable to take the proper precautions. 

it was about six in the morning when the blow came on with a white 
squall, and, as usual, from the northward. by eight it had increase 
very much, and brought down upon us one of the most tremendous sea i 
had then ever beheld. every thing had been made as snug as possible, 
but the schooner labour excessively, and gave evidence of her bad 
quality as a seaboat, pitch her forecastle under at every plunge 
and with the greatest difficulty struggle up from one wave before she 
was bury in another. just before sunset the bright spot for which we 
had been on the look-out made its appearance in the southwest, and in 
an hour afterward we perceive the little headsail we carry flap 
listless against the mast. in two minutes more, in spite of every 
preparation, we were hurl on our beam-ends, as if by magic, and a 
perfect wilderness of foam made a clear breach over us as we lay. the 
blow from the southwest, however, lucky prove to be nothing more than 
a squall, and we had the good fortune to right the vessel without the 
loss of a spar. a heavy cross sea gave us great trouble for a few hour 
after this, but toward morning we found ourselves in nearly as good 
condition as before the gale. captain guy considered that he had made an 
escape little less than miraculous. 

on the thirteenth of october we came in sight of prince edward’s island, 
in latitude 46 degree 53’ s., longitude 37 degree 46’ e. two days 
afterward we found ourselves near possession island, and presently 
pass the island of crozet, in latitude 42 degree 59’ s., longitude 
48 degree e. on the eighteenth we made kerguelen’s or desolate 
island, in the southern indian ocean, and came to anchor in christmas 
harbour, have four fathom of water. 

this island, or rather group of islands, bear southeast from the cape 
of good hope, and is distant therefrom nearly eight hundred leagues. it 
was first discover in 1772, by the baron de kergulen, or kerguelen, 
a frenchman, who, thinking the land to form a portion of an extensive 
southern continent carry home information to that effect, which 
produce much excitement at the time. the government, taking the matter 
up, sent the baron back in the following year for the purpose of 
give his new discovery a critical examination, when the mistake was 
discovered. in 1777, captain cook fell in with the same group, and gave 
to the principal one the name of desolate island, a title which 
it certainly well deserves. upon approach the land, however, the 
navigator might be induce to suppose otherwise, as the side of most 
of the hills, from september to march, are clothe with very brilliant 
verdure. this deceitful appearance is cause by a small plant resemble 
saxifrage, which is abundant, grow in large patch on a species 
of crumble moss. besides this plant there is scarcely a sign of 
vegetation on the island, if we except some coarse rank grass near the 
harbor, some lichen, and a shrub which bear resemblance to a cabbage 
shoot into seed, and which has a bitter and acrid taste. 

the face of the country is hilly, although none of the hill can be 
call lofty. their tops are perpetual cover with snow. there are 
several harbors, of which christmas harbour is the most convenient. it 
is the first to be met with on the northeast side of the island after 
passing cape francois, which form the northern shore, and, by its 
peculiar shape, serve to distinguish the harbour. its project point 
terminate in a high rock, through which is a large hole, form a 
natural arch. the entrance is in latitude 48 degree 40’ s., longitude 
69 degree 6’ e. passing in here, good anchorage may be found under the 
shelter of several small islands, which form a sufficient protection 
from all easterly winds. proceeding on eastward from this anchorage 
you come to wasp bay, at the head of the harbour. this is a small basin, 
completely landlocked, into which you can go with four fathoms, and find 
anchorage in from ten to three, hard clay bottom. a ship might lie 
here with her best bower ahead all the year round without risk. to the 
westward, at the head of wasp bay, is a small stream of excellent water, 
easily procured. 

some seal of the fur and hair species are still to be found on 
kerguelen’s island, and sea elephant abound. the feather tribe are 
discover in great numbers. penguin are very plenty, and of these 
there are four different kinds. the royal penguin, so call from its 
size and beautiful plumage, is the largest. the upper part of the body 
is usually gray, sometimes of a lilac tint; the under portion of the 
purest white imaginable. the head is of a glossy and most brilliant 
black, the feet also. the chief beauty of plumage, however, consist in 
two broad stripe of a gold color, which pass along from the head to the 
breast. the bill is long, and either pink or bright scarlet. these bird 
walk erect; with a stately carriage. they carry their head high with 
their wings droop like two arms, and, as their tails project from 
their body in a line with the legs, the resemblance to a human figure 
is very striking, and would be apt to deceive the spectator at a casual 
glance or in the gloom of the evening. the royal penguin which we met 
with on kerguelen’s land were rather large than a goose. the other 
kind are the macaroni, the jackass, and the rookery penguin. these 
are much smaller, less beautiful in plumage, and different in other 
respects. 

besides the penguin many other bird are here to be found, among which 
may be mention sea-hens, blue peterels, teal, ducks, port egmont hens, 
shags, cape pigeons, the nelly, sea swallows, terns, sea gulls, mother 
carey’s chickens, mother carey’s geese, or the great peterel, and, 
lastly, the albatross. 

the great peterel is as large as the common albatross, and is 
carnivorous. it is frequent call the break-bones, or osprey peterel. 
they are not at all shy, and, when properly cooked, are palatable food. 
in flying they sometimes sail very close to the surface of the water, 
with the wings expanded, without appear to move them in the least 
degree, or make any exertion with them whatever. 

the albatross is one of the largest and fiercest of the south sea birds. 
it is of the gull species, and take its prey on the wing, never coming 
on land except for the purpose of breeding. between this bird and the 
penguin the most singular friendship exists. their nest are 
construct with great uniform upon a plan concerted between the two 
species--that of the albatross being placed in the centre of a little 
square form by the nest of four penguins. navigator have agree in 
calling an assemblage of such encampment a rookery. these rookery 
have been often described, but as my reader may not all have seen these 
descriptions, and as i shall have occasion hereafter to speak of the 
penguin and albatross, it will not be amiss to say something here of 
their mode of building and living. 

when the season for incubation arrives, the bird assemble in vast 
numbers, and for some days appear to be deliberate upon the proper 
course to be pursued. at length they proceed to action. a level piece of 
ground is selected, of suitable extent, usually comprise three or four 
acres, and situated as near the sea as possible, being still beyond its 
reach. the spot is chosen with reference to its even of surface, and 
that is prefer which is the least encumber with stones. this 
matter being arranged, the bird proceed, with one accord, and actuate 
apparently by one mind, to trace out, with mathematics accuracy, either 
a square or other parallelogram, as may best suit the nature of the 
ground, and of just sufficient size to accommodate easily all the bird 
assembled, and no more--in this particular seeming determined upon 
prevent the access of future straggle who have not participate in 
the labor of the encampment. one side of the place thus marked out runs 
parallel with the water’s edge, and is left open for ingress or egress. 

have define the limit of the rookery, the colony now begin to clear 
it of every species of rubbish, pick up stone by stone, and carry 
them outside of the lines, and close by them, so as to form a wall on 
the three inland sides. just within this wall a perfectly level and 
smooth walk is formed, from six to eight feet wide, and extend around 
the encampment--thus serving the purpose of a general promenade. 

the next process is to partition out the whole area into small square 
exactly equal in size. this is done by form narrow paths, very 
smooth, and crossing each other at right angle throughout the entire 
extent of the rookery. at each intersection of these path the nest of 
an albatross is constructed, and a penguin’s nest in the centre of each 
square--thus every penguin is surround by four albatrosses, and each 
albatross by a like number of penguins. the penguin’s nest consist of a 
hole in the earth, very shallow, being only just of sufficient depth to 
keep her single egg from rolling. the albatross is somewhat less simple 
in her arrangements, erect a hillock about a foot high and two in 
diameter. this is made of earth, seaweed, and shells. on its summit she 
build her nest. 

the bird take especial care never to leave their nest unoccupy for 
an instant during the period of incubation, or, indeed, until the young 
progeny are sufficient strong to take care of themselves. while the 
male is absent at sea in search of food, the female remains on duty, and 
it is only upon the return of her partner that she venture abroad. the 
egg are never left uncover at all--while one bird leaves the nest the 
other nestling in by its side. this precaution is render necessary 
by the thieving propensity prevalent in the rookery, the inhabitant 
making no scruple to purloin each other’s egg at every good 
opportunity. 

although there are some rookery in which the penguin and albatross are 
the sole population, yet in most of them a variety of ocean bird 
are to be met with, enjoy all the privilege of citizenship, and 
scatter their nest here and there, wherever they can find room, 
never interfering, however, with the station of the large species. 
the appearance of such encampments, when seen from a distance, is 
exceedingly singular. the whole atmosphere just above the settlement 
is darken with the immense number of the albatross (mingled with the 
small tribes) which are continual hover over it, either go to 
the ocean or return home. at the same time a crowd of penguin are 
to be observed, some passing to and fro in the narrow alleys, and some 
march with the military strut so peculiar to them, around the general 
promenade ground which encircle the rookery. in short, survey it as 
we will, nothing can be more astonish than the spirit of reflection 
evince by these feather beings, and nothing surely can be better 
calculate to elicit reflection in every well-regulated human intellect. 

on the morning after our arrival in christmas harbour the chief mate, 
mr. patterson, took the boats, and (although it was somewhat early in 
the season) went in search of seal, leave the captain and a young 
relation of his on a point of barren land to the westward, they have 
some business, whose nature i could not ascertain, to transact in the 
interior of the island. captain guy took with him a bottle, in which was 
a seal letter, and made his way from the point on which he was set on 
shore toward one of the highest peak in the place. it is probable that 
his design was to leave the letter on that height for some vessel 
which he expect to come after him. as soon as we lost sight of him 
we proceed (peters and myself being in the mate’s boat) on our cruise 
around the coast, look for seal. in this business we were occupy 
about three weeks, examine with great care every nook and corner, 
not only of kerguelen’s land, but of the several small island in the 
vicinity. our labours, however, were not crown with any important 
success. we saw a great many fur seal, but they were exceedingly shy, 
and with the greatest exertions, we could only procure three hundred 
and fifty skin in all. sea elephant were abundant, especially on the 
western coast of the mainland, but of these we kill only twenty, and 
this with great difficulty. on the small island we discover a 
good many of the hair seal, but did not molest them. we return to the 
schooner on the eleventh, where we found captain guy and his nephew, 
who gave a very bad account of the interior, represent it as one 
of the most dreary and utterly barren country in the world. they had 
remain two nights on the island, owe to some misunderstanding, on 
the part of the second mate, in regard to the send a jollyboat from 
the schooner to take them off. 




chapter 15 

on the twelfth we made sail from christmas harbour retrace our way to 
the westward, and leave marion’s island, one of crozet’s group, on the 
larboard. we afterward pass prince edward’s island, leave it also on 
our left, then, steer more to the northward, made, in fifteen days, 
the island of tristan d’acunha, in latitude 37 degree 8’ s, longitude 
12 degree 8’ w. 

this group, now so well known, and which consist of three circular 
islands, was first discover by the portuguese, and was visit 
afterward by the dutch in 1643, and by the france in 1767. the three 
island together form a triangle, and are distant from each other about 
ten miles, there being fine open passage between. the land in all of 
them is very high, especially in tristan d’acunha, properly so called. 
this is the largest of the group, being fifteen mile in circumference, 
and so elevated that it can be seen in clear weather at the distance of 
eighty or ninety miles. a part of the land toward the north rise more 
than a thousand feet perpendicular from the sea. a tableland at this 
height extend back nearly to the centre of the island, and from this 
tableland arise a lofty cone like that of teneriffe. the lower half of 
this cone is clothe with tree of good size, but the upper region is 
barren rock, usually hidden among the clouds, and cover with snow 
during the greater part of the year. there are no shoal or other 
danger about the island, the shore being remarkably bold and the water 
deep. on the northwestern coast is a bay, with a beach of black sand 
where a landing with boat can be easily effected, provided there be a 
southerly wind. plenty of excellent water may here be readily procured; 
also cod and other fish may be taken with hook and line. 

the next island in point of size, and the most westward of the group, 
is that call the inaccessible. its precise situation is 37 degree 17’ 
s. latitude, longitude 12 degree 24’ w. it is seven or eight mile in 
circumference, and on all side presents a forbidding and precipitous 
aspect. its top is perfectly flat, and the whole region is sterile, 
nothing grow upon it except a few stunt shrubs. 

nightingale island, the smallest and most southerly, is in latitude 37 
degree 26’ s., longitude 12 degree 12’ w. off its southern extremity 
is a high ledge of rocky islets; a few also of a similar appearance are 
seen to the northeast. the ground is irregular and sterile, and a deep 
valley partially separate it. 

the shore of these island abound, in the proper season, with sea 
lions, sea elephants, the hair and fur seal, together with a great 
variety of ocean birds. whale are also plenty in their vicinity. 
owe to the ease with which these various animal were here formerly 
taken, the group has been much visit since its discovery. the dutch 
and france frequent it at a very early period. in 1790, captain 
patten, of the ship industry, of philadelphia, made tristan d’acunha, 
where he remain seven month (from august, 1790, to april, 1791) for 
the purpose of collect sealskins. in this time he gather no less 
than five thousand six hundred, and say that he would have had no 
difficulty in load a large ship with oil in three weeks. upon his 
arrival he found no quadrupeds, with the exception of a few wild goats; 
the island now abound with all our most valuable domestic animals, 
which have been introduce by subsequent navigators. 

i believe it was not long after captain patten’s visit that captain 
colquhoun, of the america brig betsey, touched at the largest of the 
island for the purpose of refreshment. he plant onions, potatoes, 
cabbages, and a great many other vegetables, an abundance of all which 
is now to be met with. 

in 1811, a captain haywood, in the nereus, visit tristan. he found 
there three americans, who were reside upon the island to prepare 
sealskin and oil. one of these men was name jonathan lambert, and 
he call himself the sovereign of the country. he had clear and 
cultivated about sixty acre of land, and turn his attention to 
raise the coffee-plant and sugar-cane, with which he had been 
furnish by the america minister at rio janeiro. this settlement, 
however, was finally abandoned, and in 1817 the island were taken 
possession of by the britain government, who sent a detachment for that 
purpose from the cape of good hope. they did not, however, retain them 
long; but, upon the evacuate of the country as a britain possession, 
two or three english family took up their residence there 
independent of the government. on the twenty-fifth of march, 1824, the 
berwick, captain jeffrey, from london to van diemen’s land, arrive at 
the place, where they found an englishman of the name of glass, formerly 
a corporal in the britain artillery. he claim to be supreme governor 
of the islands, and had under his control twenty-one men and three 
women. he gave a very favourable account of the salubr of the 
climate and of the productive of the soil. the population occupy 
themselves chiefly in collect sealskin and sea elephant oil, 
with which they trade to the cape of good hope, glass own a 
small schooner. at the period of our arrival the governor was still a 
resident, but his little community had multiplied, there being 
fifty-six person upon tristan, besides a small settlement of seven on 
nightingale island. we had no difficulty in procure almost every 
kind of refreshment which we required--sheep, hogs, bullocks, rabbits, 
poultry, goats, fish in great variety, and vegetable were abundant. 
have come to anchor close in with the large island, in eighteen 
fathoms, we took all we want on board very conveniently. captain 
guy also purchase of glass five hundred sealskin and some ivory. we 
remain here a week, during which the prevailing wind were from the 
northward and westward, and the weather somewhat hazy. on the fifth of 
november we made sail to the southward and westward, with the intention 
of have a thorough search for a group of island call the auroras, 
respecting whose existence a great diversity of opinion has existed. 

these island are said to have been discover as early as 1762, by the 
commander of the ship aurora. in 1790, captain manuel de oyarvido, in 
the ship princess, belong to the royal philippine company, sailed, as 
he asserts, directly among them. in 1794, the spain corvette atrevida 
went with the determination of ascertain their precise situation, 
and, in a paper publish by the royal hydrographic society of 
madrid in the year 1809, the following language is use respecting 
this expedition: “the corvette atrevida practised, in their immediate 
vicinity, from the twenty-first to the twenty-seventh of january, all 
the necessary observations, and measured by chronometer the difference 
of longitude between these island and the port of soledad in the 
manillas. the island are three, they are very nearly in the same 
meridian; the centre one is rather low, and the other two may be seen 
at nine leagues’ distance.” the observations made on board the atrevida 
give the following result as the precise situation of each island. 
the most northern is in latitude 52 degree 37’ 24” s., longitude 47 
degrees, 43’ 15” w.; the middle one in latitude 53 degree 2’ 40” s., 
longitude 47 degree 55’ 15” w.; and the most southern in latitude 53 
degree 15’ 22” s., longitude 47 degree 57’ 15” w. 

on the twenty-seventh of january, 1820, captain james weddel, of the 
britain navy, sail from staten land also in search of the auroras. he 
report that, have made the most diligent search and pass not only 
immediately over the spot indicate by the commander of the atrevida, 
but in every direction throughout the vicinity of these spots, he 
could discover no indication of land. these conflict statement have 
induce other navigator to look out for the islands; and, strange to 
say, while some have sail through every inch of sea where they are 
supposed to lie without finding them, there have been not a few who 
declare positively that they have seen them; and even been close in 
with their shores. it was captain guy’s intention to make every exertion 
within his power to settle the question so oddly in dispute. {*3} 

we kept on our course, between the south and west, with variable 
weather, until the twentieth of the month, when we found ourselves on 
the debate ground, being in latitude 53 degree 15’ s., longitude 47 
degree 58’ w.--that is to say, very nearly upon the spot indicate as 
the situation of the most southern of the group. not perceive any sign 
of land, we continue to the westward of the parallel of fifty-three 
degree south, as far as the meridian of fifty degree west. we then 
stood to the north as far as the parallel of fifty-two degree south, 
when we turn to the eastward, and kept our parallel by double 
altitudes, morning and evening, and meridian altitude of the planet 
and moon. have thus gone eastward to the meridian of the western 
coast of georgia, we kept that meridian until we were in the latitude 
from which we set out. we then took diagonal course throughout the 
entire extent of sea circumscribed, keeping a lookout constant at the 
masthead, and repeating our examination with the greatest care for a 
period of three weeks, during which the weather was remarkably pleasant 
and fair, with no haze whatsoever. of course we were thorough 
satisfy that, whatever island might have exist in this vicinity at 
any former period, no vestige of them remain at the present day. since 
my return home i find that the same ground was trace over, with equal 
care, in 1822, by captain johnson, of the america schooner henry, and 
by captain morrell in the america schooner wasp--in both case with the 
same result as in our own. 




chapter 16 

it had been captain guy’s original intention, after satisfying himself 
about the auroras, to proceed through the strait of magellan, and 
up along the western coast of patagonia; but information received at 
tristan d’acunha induce him to steer to the southward, in the hope of 
fall in with some small island said to lie about the parallel of 
60 degree s., longitude 41 degree 20’ w. in the event of his 
not discover these lands, he designed, should the season prove 
favourable, to push on toward the pole. accordingly, on the twelfth of 
december, we made sail in that direction. on the eighteenth we found 
ourselves about the station indicate by glass, and cruise for three 
days in that neighborhood without finding any trace of the island 
he had mentioned. on the twenty-first, the weather being unusually 
pleasant, we again made sail to the southward, with the resolution of 
penetrating in that course as far as possible. before enter upon this 
portion of my narrative, it may be as well, for the information of those 
reader who have paid little attention to the progress of discovery in 
these regions, to give some brief account of the very few attempt at 
reach the southern pole which have hitherto been made. 

that of captain cook was the first of which we have any distinct 
account. in 1772 he sail to the south in the resolution, accompany 
by lieutenant furneaux in the adventure. in december he found himself as 
far as the fifty-eighth parallel of south latitude, and in longitude 26 
degree 57’ e. here he met with narrow field of ice, about eight or ten 
inch thick, and running northwest and southeast. this ice was in large 
cakes, and usually it was packed so close that the vessel had great 
difficulty in force a passage. at this period captain cook supposed, 
from the vast number of bird to be seen, and from other indications, 
that he was in the near vicinity of land. he kept on to the southward, 
the weather being exceedingly cold, until he reach the sixty-fourth 
parallel, in longitude 38 degree 14’ e. here he had mild weather, with 
gentle breezes, for five days, the thermometer being at thirty-six. in 
january, 1773, the vessel cross the antarctic circle, but did not 
succeed in penetrating much farther; for upon reach latitude 67 
degree 15’ they found all farther progress impede by an immense body 
of ice, extend all along the southern horizon as far as the eye could 
reach. this ice was of every variety--and some large floe of it, mile 
in extent, form a compact mass, rising eighteen or twenty feet above 
the water. it being late in the season, and no hope entertain of 
round these obstructions, captain cook now reluctantly turn to the 
northward. 

in the november following he renew his search in the antarctic. in 
latitude 59 degree 40’ he met with a strong current setting to the 
southward. in december, when the vessel were in latitude 67 degree 
31’, longitude 142 degree 54’ w., the cold was excessive, with heavy 
gale and fog. here also bird were abundant; the albatross, the 
penguin, and the peterel especially. in latitude 70 degree 23’ some 
large island of ice were encountered, and shortly afterward the cloud 
to the southward were observe to be of a snowy whiteness, indicate 
the vicinity of field ice. in latitude 71 degree 10’, longitude 106 
degree 54’ w., the navigator were stopped, as before, by an immense 
frozen expanse, which fill the whole area of the southern horizon. the 
northern edge of this expanse was ragged and broken, so firm wedged 
together as to be utterly impassible, and extend about a mile to the 
southward. behind it the frozen surface was comparatively smooth for 
some distance, until terminate in the extreme background by gigantic 
range of ice mountains, the one towering above the other. captain cook 
conclude that this vast field reach the southern pole or was 
join to a continent. mr. j. n. reynolds, whose great exertion and 
perseverance have at length succeed in get set on foot a national 
expedition, partly for the purpose of explore these regions, thus 
speak of the attempt of the resolution. “we are not surprise that 
captain cook was unable to go beyond 71 degree 10’, but we are 
astonish that he did attain that point on the meridian of 106 degree 
54’ west longitude. palmer’s land lie south of the shetland, latitude 
sixty-four degrees, and tend to the southward and westward farther than 
any navigator has yet penetrated. cook was standing for this land when 
his progress was arrest by the ice; which, we apprehend, must always 
be the case in that point, and so early in the season as the sixth 
of january--and we should not be surprise if a portion of the icy 
mountain describe was attach to the main body of palmer’s land, 
or to some other portion of land lie farther to the southward and 
westward.” 

in 1803, captain kreutzenstern and lisiausky were dispatch by 
alexander of russia for the purpose of circumnavigate the globe. in 
endeavour to get south, they made no farther than 59 degree 58’, in 
longitude 70 degree 15’ w. they here met with strong current setting 
eastwardly. whale were abundant, but they saw no ice. in regard to this 
voyage, mr. reynolds observe that, if kreutzenstern had arrive where 
he did early in the season, he must have encounter ice--it was march 
when he reach the latitude specified. the winds, prevailing, as they 
do, from the southward and westward, had carry the floes, aid by 
currents, into that icy region bound on the north by georgia, east 
by sandwich land and the south orkneys, and west by the south shetland 
islands. 

in 1822, captain james weddell, of the britain navy, with two very small 
vessels, penetrate farther to the south than any previous navigator, 
and this, too, without encounter extraordinary difficulties. he 
states that although he was frequent hem in by ice before reach 
the seventy-second parallel, yet, upon attain it, not a particle was 
to be discovered, and that, upon arrive at the latitude of 74 degree 
15’, no fields, and only three island of ice were visible. it is 
somewhat remarkable that, although vast flock of bird were seen, and 
other usual indication of land, and although, south of the shetlands, 
unknown coast were observe from the masthead tend southwardly, 
weddell discourage the idea of land existing in the polar regions of 
the south. 

on the 11th of january, 1823, captain benjamin morrell, of the america 
schooner wasp, sail from kerguelen’s land with a view of penetrating 
as far south as possible. on the first of february he found himself in 
latitude 64 degree 52’ s., longitude 118 degree 27’ e. the following 
passage is extract from his journal of that date. “the wind soon 
freshen to an eleven-knot breeze, and we embrace this opportunity 
of making to the west; being however convinced that the farther we 
went south beyond latitude sixty-four degrees, the less ice was to be 
apprehended, we steer a little to the southward, until we cross 
the antarctic circle, and were in latitude 69 degree 15’ e. in this 
latitude there was no field ice, and very few ice island in sight.” 

under the date of march fourteenth i find also this entry. “the sea was 
now entire free of field ice, and there were not more than a dozen ice 
island in sight. at the same time the temperature of the air and water 
was at least thirteen degree higher (more mild) than we had ever found 
it between the parallel of sixty and sixty-two south. we were now 
in latitude 70 degree 14’ s., and the temperature of the air was 
forty-seven, and that of the water forty-four. in this situation i found 
the variation to be 14 degree 27’ easterly, per azimuth.... i 
have several times pass within the antarctic circle, on different 
meridians, and have uniform found the temperature, both of the air and 
the water, to become more and more mild the farther i advanced beyond 
the sixty-fifth degree of south latitude, and that the variation 
decrease in the same proportion. while north of this latitude, say 
between sixty and sixty-five south, we frequent had great difficulty 
in finding a passage for the vessel between the immense and almost 
innumerable ice islands, some of which were from one to two mile in 
circumference, and more than five hundred feet above the surface of the 
water.” 

being nearly destitute of fuel and water, and without proper 
instruments, it being also late in the season, captain morrell was now 
oblige to put back, without attempt any further progress to the 
westward, although an entire open, sea lay before him. he express 
the opinion that, had not these overrule consideration oblige him to 
retreat, he could have penetrated, if not to the pole itself, at least 
to the eighty-fifth parallel. i have given his idea respecting these 
matter somewhat at length, that the reader may have an opportunity of 
seeing how far they were borne out by my own subsequent experience. 

in 1831, captain briscoe, in the employ of the messieurs enderby, 
whale-ship owner of london, sail in the brig lively for the south 
seas, accompany by the cutter tula. on the twenty-eighth of february, 
being in latitude 66 degree 30’ s., longitude 47 degree 31’ e., he 
descry land, and “clearly discover through the snow the black 
peak of a range of mountain running e. s. e.” he remain in this 
neighbourhood during the whole of the following month, but was unable 
to approach the coast near than within ten leagues, owe to the 
boisterous state of the weather. finding it impossible to make further 
discovery during this season, he return northward to winter in van 
diemen’s land. 

in the beginning of 1832 he again proceed southwardly, and on the 
fourth of february land was seen to the southeast in latitude 67 degree 15’ 
longitude 69 degree 29’ w. this was soon found to be an island near the 
headland of the country he had first discovered. on the twenty-first of 
the month he succeed in landing on the latter, and took possession of 
it in the name of william iv, calling it adelaide’s island, in honour 
of the english queen. these particulars being made known to the royal 
geography society of london, the conclusion was drawn by that body 
“that there is a continuous tract of land extend from 47 degree 
30’ e. to 69 degree 29’ w. longitude, running the parallel of from 
sixty-six to sixty-seven degree south latitude.” in respect to this 
conclusion mr. reynolds observes: “in the correct of it we by 
no means concur; nor do the discovery of briscoe warrant any such 
indifference. it was within these limit that weddel proceed south on 
a meridian to the east of georgia, sandwich land, and the south orkney 
and shetland islands.” my own experience will be found to testify most 
directly to the falsity of the conclusion arrive at by the society. 

these are the principal attempt which have been made at penetrating to 
a high southern latitude, and it will now be seen that there remained, 
previous to the voyage of the jane, nearly three hundred degree of 
longitude in which the antarctic circle had not been cross at all. 
of course a wide field lay before us for discovery, and it was with 
feelings of most intense interest that i heard captain guy express his 
resolution of push bold to the southward. 




chapter 17 

we kept our course southward for four days after give up the 
search for glass’s islands, without meeting with any ice at all. on the 
twenty-sixth, at noon, we were in latitude 63 degree 23’ s., longitude 
41 degree 25’ w. we now saw several large ice islands, and a floe of 
field ice, not, however, of any great extent. the wind generally blew 
from the southeast, or the northeast, but were very light. whenever we 
had a westerly wind, which was seldom, it was invariable attend with a 
rain squall. every day we had more or less snow. the thermometer, on the 
twenty-seventh stood at thirty-five. 

january 1, 1828.--this day we found ourselves completely hem in by 
the ice, and our prospects look cheerless indeed. a strong gale blew, 
during the whole forenoon, from the northeast, and drove large cake of 
the drift against the rudder and counter with such violence that we all 
tremble for the consequences. toward evening, the gale still blow 
with fury, a large field in front separated, and we were enabled, by 
carry a press of sail to force a passage through the small flake 
into some open water beyond. as we approach this space we took in sail 
by degrees, and have at length got clear, lay-to under a single reef 
foresail. 

january 2.--we had now tolerably pleasant weather. at noon we found 
ourselves in latitude 69 degree 10’ s, longitude 42 degree 20’ w, 
have cross the antarctic circle. very little ice was to be seen to 
the southward, although large field of it lay behind us. this day we 
rig some sound gear, use a large iron pot capable of holding 
twenty gallons, and a line of two hundred fathoms. we found the 
current setting to the north, about a quarter of a mile per hour. the 
temperature of the air was now about thirty-three. here we found the 
variation to be 14 degree 28’ easterly, per azimuth. 

january 5.--we had still held on to the southward without any very great 
impediments. on this morning, however, being in latitude 73 degree 15’ 
e., longitude 42 degree 10’ w, we were again brought to a stand by an 
immense expanse of firm ice. we saw, nevertheless, much open water to 
the southward, and felt no doubt of being able to reach it eventually. 
standing to the eastward along the edge of the floe, we at length came 
to a passage of about a mile in width, through which we warp our way 
by sundown. the sea in which we now were was thick cover with ice 
islands, but had no field ice, and we pushed on bold as before. the 
cold did not seem to increase, although we had snow very frequently, 
and now and then hail squall of great violence. immense flock of 
the albatross flew over the schooner this day, go from southeast to 
northwest. 

january 7.--the sea still remain pretty well open, so that we had 
no difficulty in holding on our course. to the westward we saw some 
iceberg of incredible size, and in the afternoon pass very near one 
whose summit could not have been less than four hundred fathom from 
the surface of the ocean. its girth was probably, at the base, 
three-quarters of a league, and several stream of water were running 
from crevice in its sides. we remain in sight of this island two 
days, and then only lost it in a fog. 

january 10.--early this morning we had the misfortune to lose a man 
overboard. he was an america name peter vredenburgh, a native of new 
york, and was one of the most valuable hand on board the schooner. in 
go over the bow his foot slipped, and he fell between two cake 
of ice, never rising again. at noon of this day we were in latitude 78 
degree 30’, longitude 40 degree 15’ w. the cold was now excessive, and 
we had hail squall continual from the northward and eastward. in 
this direction also we saw several more immense icebergs, and the whole 
horizon to the eastward appear to be block up with field ice, rising 
in tiers, one mass above the other. some driftwood float by during 
the evening, and a great quantity of bird flew over, among which were 
nellies, peterels, albatrosses, and a large bird of a brilliant blue 
plumage. the variation here, per azimuth, was less than it had been 
previous to our passing the antarctic circle. 

january 12.--our passage to the south again look doubtful, as nothing 
was to be seen in the direction of the pole but one apparently limitless 
floe, back by absolute mountain of ragged ice, one precipice of which 
arose frowning above the other. we stood to the westward until the 
fourteenth, in the hope of finding an entrance. 

january 14.--this morning we reach the western extremity of the field 
which had impede us, and, weather it, came to an open sea, without a 
particle of ice. upon sound with two hundred fathoms, we here found 
a current setting southward at the rate of half a mile per hour. the 
temperature of the air was forty-seven, that of the water thirty-four. we 
now sail to the southward without meeting any interrupt of moment 
until the sixteenth, when, at noon, we were in latitude 81 degree 
21’, longitude 42 degree w. we here again sounded, and found a current 
setting still southwardly, and at the rate of three quarters of a mile 
per hour. the variation per azimuth had diminished, and the temperature 
of the air was mild and pleasant, the thermometer being as high as 
fifty-one. at this period not a particle of ice was to be discovered. 
all hand on board now felt certain of attain the pole. 

january 17.--this day was full of incident. innumerable flight of bird 
flew over us from the southward, and several were shot from the deck, 
one of them, a species of pelican, prove to be excellent eating. about 
midday a small floe of ice was seen from the masthead off the larboard 
bow, and upon it there appear to be some large animal. as the weather 
was good and nearly calm, captain guy ordered out two of the boat to 
see what it was. dirk peters and myself accompany the mate in the 
large boat. upon coming up with the floe, we perceive that it was in 
the possession of a gigantic creature of the race of the arctic bear, 
but far exceed in size the largest of these animals. being well 
armed, we made no scruple of attack it at once. several shot were 
fire in quick succession, the most of which took effect, apparently, 
in the head and body. nothing discouraged, however, the monster threw 
himself from the ice, and swam with open jaws, to the boat in which were 
peters and myself. owe to the confusion which ensue among us at this 
unexpected turn of the adventure, no person was ready immediately with 
a second shot, and the bear had actually succeed in get half his 
vast bulk across our gunwale, and seize one of the men by the small 
of his back, before any efficient means were taken to repel him. in this 
extremity nothing but the promptness and agile of peters save us from 
destruction. leap upon the back of the huge beast, he plunge the 
blade of a knife behind the neck, reach the spinal marrow at a blow. 
the brute tumble into the sea lifeless, and without a struggle, rolling 
over peters as he fell. the latter soon recover himself, and a rope 
being thrown him, he secure the carcass before enter the boat. we 
then return in triumph to the schooner, tow our trophy behind us. 
this bear, upon admeasurement, prove to be full fifteen feet in his 
greatest length. his wool was perfectly white, and very coarse, curling 
tightly. the eyes were of a blood red, and large than those of the 
arctic bear, the snout also more rounded, rather resemble the snout 
of the bulldog. the meat was tender, but excessive rank and fishy, 
although the men devour it with avidity, and declared it excellent 
eating. 

scarcely had we got our prize alongside, when the man at the masthead 
gave the joyful shout of “land on the starboard bow!” all hand were 
now upon the alert, and, a breeze spring up very opportune from the 
northward and eastward, we were soon close in with the coast. it 
prove to be a low rocky islet, of about a league in circumference, and 
altogether destitute of vegetation, if we except a species of prickly 
pear. in approach it from the northward, a singular ledge of rock is 
seen project into the sea, and bearing a strong resemblance to cord 
bale of cotton. around this ledge to the westward is a small bay, at 
the bottom of which our boat effect a convenient landing. 

it did not take us long to explore every portion of the island, but, 
with one exception, we found nothing worthy of our observation. in the 
southern extremity, we picked up near the shore, half bury in a pile 
of loose stones, a piece of wood, which seem to have form the prow 
of a canoe. there had been evidently some attempt at carving upon it, 
and captain guy fancy that he made out the figure of a tortoise, but 
the resemblance did not strike me very forcibly. besides this prow, if 
such it were, we found no other token that any living creature had ever 
been here before. around the coast we discover occasional small floe 
of ice--but these were very few. the exact situation of the islet (to 
which captain guy gave the name of bennet’s islet, in honour of his 
partner in the ownership of the schooner) is 82 degree 50’ s. latitude, 
42 degree 20’ w. longitude. 

we had now advanced to the southward more than eight degree farther 
than any previous navigators, and the sea still lay perfectly open 
before us. we found, too, that the variation uniform decrease as we 
proceeded, and, what was still more surprising, that the temperature 
of the air, and latterly of the water, became milder. the weather might 
even be call pleasant, and we had a steady but very gentle breeze 
always from some northern point of the compass. the sky was usually 
clear, with now and then a slight appearance of thin vapour in the 
southern horizon--this, however, was invariable of brief duration. two 
difficulty alone present themselves to our view; we were get 
short of fuel, and symptom of scurvy had occur among several of 
the crew. these consideration began to impress upon captain guy the 
necessity of returning, and he spoke of it frequently. for my own part, 
confident as i was of soon arrive at land of some description upon 
the course we were pursuing, and have every reason to believe, from 
present appearances, that we should not find it the sterile soil met 
with in the higher arctic latitudes, i warm pressed upon him the 
expediency of persevering, at least for a few days longer, in the 
direction we were now holding. so tempt an opportunity of solve 
the great problem in regard to an antarctic continent had never yet 
been afford to man, and i confess that i felt myself burst with 
indignation at the timid and ill-timed suggestion of our commander. 
i believe, indeed, that what i could not refrain from saying to him on 
this head had the effect of induce him to push on. while, therefore, 
i cannot but lament the most unfortunate and bloody event which 
immediately arose from my advice, i must still be allow to feel some 
degree of gratification at have been instrumental, however remotely, 
in opening to the eye of science one of the most intense exciting 
secret which has ever engross its attention. 




chapter 18 

january 18.--this morning {*4} we continue to the southward, with the 
same pleasant weather as before. the sea was entire smooth, the air 
tolerably warm and from the northeast, the temperature of the water 
fifty-three. we now again got our sounding-gear in order, and, with a 
hundred and fifty fathom of line, found the current setting toward 
the pole at the rate of a mile an hour. this constant tendency to 
the southward, both in the wind and current, cause some degree of 
speculation, and even of alarm, in different quarters of the schooner, 
and i saw distinct that no little impression had been made upon the 
mind of captain guy. he was exceedingly sensitive to ridicule, however, 
and i finally succeed in laugh him out of his apprehensions. the 
variation was now very trivial. in the course of the day we saw several 
large whale of the right species, and innumerable flight of the 
albatross pass over the vessel. we also picked up a bush, full of 
red berries, like those of the hawthorn, and the carcass of a 
singular-looking land-animal. it was three feet in length, and but six 
inch in height, with four very short legs, the feet armed with long 
claw of a brilliant scarlet, and resemble coral in substance. the 
body was cover with a straight silky hair, perfectly white. the tail 
was peaked like that of a rat, and about a foot and a half long. the 
head resemble a cat’s, with the exception of the ears--these were 
flop like the ear of a dog. the teeth were of the same brilliant 
scarlet as the claws. 

january 19.--to-day, being in latitude 83 degree 20’, longitude 43 
degree 5’ w. (the sea being of an extraordinarily dark colour), we 
again saw land from the masthead, and, upon a close scrutiny, found it 
to be one of a group of very large islands. the shore was precipitous, 
and the interior seem to be well wooded, a circumstance which 
occasion us great joy. in about four hour from our first discover 
the land we came to anchor in ten fathoms, sandy bottom, a league from 
the coast, as a high surf, with strong ripple here and there, render 
a near approach of doubtful expediency. the two largest boat were 
now ordered out, and a party, well armed (among whom were peters and 
myself), proceed to look for an opening in the reef which appear to 
encircle the island. after searching about for some time, we discover 
an inlet, which we were entering, when we saw four large canoe put off 
from the shore, fill with men who seem to be well armed. we wait 
for them to come up, and, as they move with great rapidity, they were 
soon within hail. captain guy now held up a white handkerchief on the 
blade of an oar, when the stranger made a full stop, and commence 
a loud jabber all at once, intermingle with occasional shouts, in 
which we could distinguish the words anamoo-moo! and lama-lama! they 
continue this for at least half an hour, during which we had a good 
opportunity of observing their appearance. 

in the four canoes, which might have been fifty feet long and five 
broad, there were a hundred and ten savage in all. they were about the 
ordinary stature of europeans, but of a more muscular and brawny frame. 
their complexion a jet black, with thick and long woolly hair. they were 
clothe in skin of an unknown black animal, shaggy and silky, and made 
to fit the body with some degree of skill, the hair being inside, 
except where turn out about the neck, wrists, and ankles. their arms 
consist principally of clubs, of a dark, and apparently very heavy 
wood. some spears, however, were observe among them, head with flint, 
and a few slings. the bottom of the canoe were full of black stone 
about the size of a large egg. 

when they had conclude their harangue (for it was clear they intended 
their jabber for such), one of them who seem to be the chief stood 
up in the prow of his canoe, and made sign for us to bring our boat 
alongside of him. this hint we pretended not to understand, thinking 
it the wise plan to maintain, if possible, the interval between us, as 
their number more than quadruple our own. finding this to be the case, 
the chief ordered the three other canoe to hold back, while he advanced 
toward us with his own. as soon as he came up with us he leap on board 
the largest of our boats, and seat himself by the side of captain 
guy, point at the same time to the schooner, and repeating the word 
anamoo-moo! and lama-lama! we now put back to the vessel, the four 
canoe following at a little distance. 

upon get alongside, the chief evince symptom of extreme surprise 
and delight, clap his hands, slap his thigh and breast, and 
laugh obstreperously. his follower behind join in his merriment, 
and for some minutes the din was so excessive as to be absolutely 
deafening. quiet being at length restored, captain guy ordered the boat 
to be hoist up, as a necessary precaution, and gave the chief (whose 
name we soon found to be too-wit) to understand that we could admit no 
more than twenty of his men on deck at one time. with this arrangement 
he appear perfectly satisfied, and gave some directions to the canoes, 
when one of them approached, the rest remain about fifty yard off. 
twenty of the savage now got on board, and proceed to ramble over 
every part of the deck, and scramble about among the rigging, making 
themselves much at home, and examine every article with great 
inquisitiveness. 

it was quite evident that they had never before seen any of the white 
race--from whose complexion, indeed, they appear to recoil. they 
believe the jane to be a living creature, and seem to be afraid of 
hurt it with the points of their spears, careful turning them up. 
our crew were much amuse with the conduct of too-wit in one instance. 
the cook was splitting some wood near the galley, and, by accident, 
struck his axe into the deck, making a gash of considerable depth. 
the chief immediately ran up, and push the cook on one side rather 
roughly, commence a half whine, half howl, strong indicative of 
sympathy in what he considered the suffering of the schooner, pat 
and smooth the gash with his hand, and washing it from a bucket of 
seawater which stood by. this was a degree of ignorance for which we 
were not prepared, and for my part i could not help thinking some of it 
affected. 

when the visitor had satisfied, as well as they could, their curiosity 
in regard to our upper works, they were admitted below, when their 
amaze exceed all bounds. their astonishment now appear to be far 
too deep for words, for they roam about in silence, broken only by low 
ejaculations. the arms afford them much food for speculation, and they 
were suffer to handle and examine them at leisure. i do not believe 
that they had the least suspicion of their actual use, but rather took 
them for idols, seeing the care we had of them, and the attention with 
which we watch their movements while handle them. at the great gun 
their wonder was redoubled. they approach them with every mark of the 
profoundest reverence and awe, but forbore to examine them minutely. 
there were two large mirror in the cabin, and here was the acme of 
their amazement. too-wit was the first to approach them, and he had got 
in the middle of the cabin, with his face to one and his back to the 
other, before he fairly perceive them. upon raise his eyes and seeing 
his reflect self in the glass, i thought the savage would go mad; 
but, upon turning short round to make a retreat, and behold himself a 
second time in the opposite direction, i was afraid he would expire upon 
the spot. no persuasion could prevail upon him to take another look; 
throw himself upon the floor, with his face bury in his hands, he 
remain thus until we were oblige to drag him upon deck. 

the whole of the savage were admitted on board in this manner, twenty 
at a time, too-wit being suffer to remain during the entire period. 
we saw no disposition to thievery among them, nor did we miss a single 
article after their departure. throughout the whole of their visit they 
evince the most friendly manner. there were, however, some points in 
their demeanour which we found it impossible to understand; for example, 
we could not get them to approach several very harmless objects--such 
as the schooner’s sails, an egg, an open book, or a pan of flour. we 
endeavour to ascertain if they had among them any articles which might 
be turn to account in the way of traffic, but found great difficulty 
in being comprehended. we made out, nevertheless, what greatly 
astonish us, that the island abound in the large tortoise of the 
gallipagos, one of which we saw in the canoe of too-wit. we saw also 
some biche de mer in the hand of one of the savages, who was greedy 
devour it in its natural state. these anomalies--for they were such 
when considered in regard to the latitude--induced captain guy to wish 
for a thorough investigate of the country, in the hope of making a 
profitable speculation in his discovery. for my own part, anxious as i 
was to know something more of these islands, i was still more earnest 
bent on prosecute the voyage to the southward without delay. we had 
now fine weather, but there was no telling how long it would last; and 
being already in the eighty-fourth parallel, with an open sea before us, 
a current setting strong to the southward, and the wind fair, i could 
not listen with any patience to a proposition of stopping long than 
was absolutely necessary for the health of the crew and the taking on 
board a proper supply of fuel and fresh provisions. i represent to the 
captain that we might easily make this group on our return, and winter 
here in the event of being block up by the ice. he at length came into 
my view (for in some way, hardly known to myself, i had acquire much 
influence over him), and it was finally resolve that, even in the event 
of our finding biche de mer, we should only stay here a week to recruit, 
and then push on to the southward while we might. accordingly we made 
every necessary preparation, and, under the guidance of too-wit, got the 
jane through the reef in safety, coming to anchor about a mile from the 
shore, in an excellent bay, completely landlocked, on the southeastern 
coast of the main island, and in ten fathom of water, black sandy 
bottom. at the head of this bay there were three fine spring (we were 
told) of good water, and we saw abundance of wood in the vicinity. the 
four canoe follow us in, keeping, however, at a respectful distance. 
too-wit himself remain on board, and, upon our drop anchor, 
invite us to accompany him on shore, and visit his village in the 
interior. to this captain guy consented; and ten savage being left on 
board as hostages, a party of us, twelve in all, got in readiness to 
attend the chief. we took care to be well armed, yet without evince 
any distrust. the schooner had her gun run out, her boarding-nettings 
up, and every other proper precaution was taken to guard against 
surprise. directions were left with the chief mate to admit no person 
on board during our absence, and, in the event of our not appear in 
twelve hours, to send the cutter, with a swivel, around the island in 
search of us. 

at every step we took inland the conviction forced itself upon us that 
we were in a country differ essentially from any hitherto visit 
by civilize men. we saw nothing with which we had been formerly 
conversant. the tree resemble no growth of either the torrid, the 
temperate, or the northern frigid zones, and were altogether unlike 
those of the lower southern latitudes we had already traversed. the very 
rocks were novel in their mass, their color, and their stratification; 
and the stream themselves, utterly incredible as it may appear, had so 
little in common with those of other climates, that we were scrupulous 
of taste them, and, indeed, had difficulty in bring ourselves to 
believe that their quality were purely those of nature. at a small 
brook which cross our path (the first we had reached) too-wit and his 
attendant halt to drink. on account of the singular character of the 
water, we refuse to taste it, supposing it to be polluted; and it was 
not until some time afterward we came to understand that such was the 
appearance of the stream throughout the whole group. i am at a loss 
to give a distinct idea of the nature of this liquid, and cannot do so 
without many words. although it flow with rapid in all declivity 
where common water would do so, yet never, except when fall in 
a cascade, had it the customary appearance of limpidity. it was, 
nevertheless, in point of fact, as perfectly limpid as any limestone 
water in existence, the difference being only in appearance. at first 
sight, and especially in case where little declivity was found, it bore 
resemblance, as regards consistency, to a thick infusion of gum 
arabic in common water. but this was only the least remarkable of its 
extraordinary qualities. it was not colourless, nor was it of any one 
uniform colour--presenting to the eye, as it flowed, every possible 
shade of purple; like the hue of a changeable silk. this variation in 
shade was produce in a manner which excited as profound astonishment 
in the mind of our party as the mirror had done in the case of too-wit. 
upon collect a basinful, and allow it to settle thoroughly, we 
perceive that the whole mass of liquid was made up of a number of 
distinct veins, each of a distinct hue; that these vein did not 
commingle; and that their cohesion was perfect in regard to their own 
particle among themselves, and imperfect in regard to neighbouring 
veins. upon passing the blade of a knife athwart the veins, the water 
closed over it immediately, as with us, and also, in withdraw it, 
all trace of the passage of the knife were instantly obliterated. if, 
however, the blade was pass down accurate between the two veins, 
a perfect separation was effected, which the power of cohesion did 
not immediately rectify. the phenomena of this water form the first 
definite link in that vast chain of apparent miracle with which i was 
destined to be at length encircled. 




chapter 19 

we were nearly three hour in reach the village, it being more than 
nine mile in the interior, and the path lie through a rugged country. 
as we pass along, the party of too-wit (the whole hundred and 
ten savage of the canoes) was momentarily strengthen by small 
detachments, of from two to six or seven, which join us, as if by 
accident, at different turn of the road. there appear so much of 
system in this that i could not help feeling distrust, and i spoke 
to captain guy of my apprehensions. it was now too late, however, 
to recede, and we conclude that our best security lay in evince a 
perfect confidence in the good faith of too-wit. we accordingly went 
on, keeping a wary eye upon the manoeuvre of the savages, and not 
permit them to divide our numbers by push in between. in this 
way, passing through a precipitous ravine, we at length reach what we 
were told was the only collection of habitation upon the island. as we 
came in sight of them, the chief set up a shout, and frequent repeated 
the word klock-klock, which we supposed to be the name of the village, 
or perhaps the generic name for villages. 

the dwelling were of the most miserable description imaginable, and, 
unlike those of even the lowest of the savage races with which mankind 
are acquainted, were of no uniform plan. some of them (and these we 
found belong to the wampoo or yampoos, the great men of the land) 
consist of a tree cut down at about four feet from the root, with a 
large black skin thrown over it, and hanging in loose fold upon the 
ground. under this the savage nestled. other were form by means 
of rough limb of trees, with the wither foliage upon them, made to 
recline, at an angle of forty-five degrees, against a bank of clay, 
heap up, without regular form, to the height of five or six feet. 
others, again, were mere hole dug in the earth perpendicularly, and 
cover over with similar branches, these being remove when the tenant 
was about to enter, and pull on again when he had entered. a few were 
built among the forked limb of tree as they stood, the upper limb 
being partially cut through, so as to bend over upon the lower, thus 
form thick shelter from the weather. the greater number, however, 
consist of small shallow caverns, apparently scratch in the face of 
a precipitous ledge of dark stone, resemble fuller’s earth, with which 
three side of the village were bounded. at the door of each of these 
primitive cavern was a small rock, which the tenant careful placed 
before the entrance upon leave his residence, for what purpose i could 
not ascertain, as the stone itself was never of sufficient size to close 
up more than a third of the opening. 

this village, if it were worthy of the name, lay in a valley of some 
depth, and could only be approach from the southward, the precipitous 
ledge of which i have already spoken cutting off all access in other 
directions. through the middle of the valley ran a brawl stream of 
the same magical-looking water which has been described. we saw several 
strange animal about the dwellings, all appear to be thorough 
domesticated. the largest of these creature resemble our common hog in 
the structure of the body and snout; the tail, however, was bushy, and 
the leg slender as those of the antelope. its motion was exceedingly 
awkward and indecisive, and we never saw it attempt to run. we notice 
also several animal very similar in appearance, but of a greater length 
of body, and cover with a black wool. there were a great variety of 
tame fowl running about, and these seem to constitute the chief food 
of the natives. to our astonishment we saw black albatross among these 
bird in a state of entire domestication, go to sea periodical 
for food, but always return to the village as a home, and use the 
southern shore in the vicinity as a place of incubation. there they were 
join by their friends the pelican as usual, but these latter never 
follow them to the dwelling of the savages. among the other kind of 
tame fowl were ducks, differ very little from the canvass-back of 
our own country, black gannets, and a large bird not unlike the buzzard 
in appearance, but not carnivorous. of fish there seem to be a great 
abundance. we saw, during our visit, a quantity of dry salmon, 
rock cod, blue dolphins, mackerel, blackfish, skate, conger eels, 
elephantfish, mullets, soles, parrotfish, leather-jackets, gurnards, 
hake, flounders, paracutas, and innumerable other varieties. we noticed, 
too, that most of them were similar to the fish about the group of lord 
auckland islands, in a latitude as low as fifty-one degree south. the 
gallipago tortoise was also very plentiful. we saw but few wild animals, 
and none of a large size, or of a species with which we were familiar. 
one or two serpent of a formidable aspect cross our path, but the 
native paid them little attention, and we conclude that they were not 
venomous. 

as we approach the village with too-wit and his party, a vast crowd of 
the people rush out to meet us, with loud shouts, among which we could 
only distinguish the everlasting anamoo-moo! and lama-lama! we were 
much surprise at perceive that, with one or two exceptions, these new 
comer were entire naked, and skin being use only by the men of 
the canoes. all the weapon of the country seem also to be in the 
possession of the latter, for there was no appearance of any among the 
villagers. there were a great many women and children, the former not 
altogether wanting in what might be term personal beauty. they were 
straight, tall, and well formed, with a grace and freedom of carriage 
not to be found in civilize society. their lips, however, like those of 
the men, were thick and clumsy, so that, even when laughing, the teeth 
were never disclosed. their hair was of a fine texture than that of the 
males. among these naked villager there might have been ten or twelve 
who were clothed, like the party of too-wit, in dress of black skin, 
and armed with lance and heavy clubs. these appear to have great 
influence among the rest, and were always address by the title wampoo. 
these, too, were the tenant of the black skin palaces. that of too-wit 
was situated in the centre of the village, and was much large and 
somewhat better construct than other of its kind. the tree which 
form its support was cut off at a distance of twelve feet or 
thereabouts from the root, and there were several branch left just 
below the cut, these serving to extend the covering, and in this way 
prevent its flap about the trunk. the covering, too, which consist
