of four very large skin fasten together with wooden skewers, was 
secure at the bottom with peg driven through it and into the ground. 
the floor was strew with a quantity of dry leaves by way of carpet. 

to this hut we were conduct with great solemnity, and as many of the 
native crowded in after us as possible. too-wit seat himself on the 
leaves, and made sign that we should follow his example. this we did, 
and presently found ourselves in a situation peculiarly uncomfortable, 
if not indeed critical. we were on the ground, twelve in number, with 
the savages, as many as forty, sitting on their ham so close 
around us that, if any disturbance had arisen, we should have found it 
impossible to make use of our arms, or indeed to have risen to our feet. 
the pressure was not only inside the tent, but outside, where probably 
was every individual on the whole island, the crowd being prevent from 
trample us to death only by the incessant exertion and vociferation 
of too-wit. our chief security lay, however, in the presence of too-wit 
himself among us, and we resolve to stick by him closely, as the 
best chance of extricate ourselves from the dilemma, sacrifice him 
immediately upon the first appearance of hostile design. 

after some trouble a certain degree of quiet was restored, when 
the chief address us in a speech of great length, and very nearly 
resemble the one deliver in the canoes, with the exception that the 
anamoo-moos! were now somewhat more strenuous insist upon than the 
lama-lamas! we listen in profound silence until the conclusion of this 
harangue, when captain guy reply by assure the chief of his eternal 
friendship and goodwill, conclude what he had to say by a present of 
several strings of blue bead and a knife. at the former the monarch, 
much to our surprise, turn up his nose with some expression of 
contempt, but the knife gave him the most unlimited satisfaction, and he 
immediately ordered dinner. this was hand into the tent over the 
head of the attendants, and consist of the palpitate entrails of a 
speciali of unknown animal, probably one of the slim-legged hog which 
we had observe in our approach to the village. seeing us at a loss how 
to proceed, he began, by way of setting us an example, to devour yard 
after yard of the entice food, until we could positively stand it no 
longer, and evince such manifest symptom of rebellion of stomach as 
inspired his majesty with a degree of astonishment only inferior to that 
brought about by the looking-glasses. we declined, however, partake of 
the delicacy before us, and endeavour to make him understand that we 
had no appetite whatever, have just finished a hearty dejeuner. 

when the monarch had made an end of his meal, we commence a series of 
cross-questioning in every ingenious manner we could devise, with a 
view of discover what were the chief production of the country, and 
whether any of them might be turn to profit. at length he seem to 
have some idea of our meaning, and offer to accompany us to a part of 
coast where he assured us the biche de mer (pointing to a specimen of 
that animal) was to be found in great abundance. we were glad of this 
early opportunity of escape from the oppression of the crowd, 
and signify our eager to proceed. we now left the tent, and, 
accompany by the whole population of the village, follow the chief 
to the southeastern extremity of the island, nor far from the bay where 
our vessel lay at anchor. we wait here for about an hour, until the 
four canoe were brought around by some of the savage to our station. 
the whole of our party then get into one of them, we were paddle 
along the edge of the reef before mentioned, and of another still 
farther out, where we saw a far greater quantity of biche de mer than 
the oldest seamen among us had ever seen in those group of the lower 
latitudes most celebrated for this article of commerce. we stay near 
these reef only long enough to satisfy ourselves that we could easily 
load a dozen vessel with the animal if necessary, when we were taken 
alongside the schooner, and part with too-wit, after obtain from 
him a promise that he would bring us, in the course of twenty-four 
hours, as many of the canvass-back ducks and gallipago tortoise as his 
canoe would hold. in the whole of this adventure we saw nothing in the 
demeanour of the native calculate to create suspicion, with the single 
exception of the systematic manner in which their party was strengthen 
during our route from the schooner to the village. 




chapter 20 

the chief was as good as his word, and we were soon plentiful supply 
with fresh provisions. we found the tortoise as fine as we had ever 
seen, and the ducks surpass our best species of wild fowl, being 
exceedingly tender, juicy, and well-flavoured. besides these, the 
savage brought us, upon our making them comprehend our wishes, a vast 
quantity of brown celery and scurvy grass, with a canoe-load of fresh 
fish and some dried. the celery was a treat indeed, and the scurvy grass 
prove of incalculable benefit in restore those of our men who had 
shown symptom of disease. in a very short time we had not a single 
person on the sick-list. we had also plenty of other kind of fresh 
provisions, among which may be mention a species of shellfish 
resemble the mussel in shape, but with the taste of an oyster. 
shrimps, too, and prawn were abundant, and albatross and other birds’ 
egg with dark shells. we took in, too, a plentiful stock of the flesh 
of the hog which i have mention before. most of the men found it a 
palatable food, but i thought it fishy and otherwise disagreeable. in 
return for these good things we present the native with blue beads, 
brass trinkets, nails, knives, and pieces of red cloth, they being fully 
delight in the exchange. we establish a regular market on shore, 
just under the gun of the schooner, where our barter were carry 
on with every appearance of good faith, and a degree of order which 
their conduct at the village of _klock-klock_ had not led us to expect 
from the savages. 

matter went on thus very amicable for several days, during which 
party of the native were frequent on board the schooner, and 
party of our men frequent on shore, making long excursion into the 
interior, and receiving no molest whatever. finding the ease with 
which the vessel might be loaded with _biche de mer_, owe to the 
friendly disposition of the islanders, and the readiness with which they 
would render us assistance in collect it, captain guy resolve to 
enter into negotiation with too-wit for the erection of suitable house 
in which to cure the article, and for the service of himself and tribe 
in gathering as much as possible, while he himself took advantage of the 
fine weather to prosecute his voyage to the southward. upon mention 
this project to the chief he seem very willing to enter into an 
agreement. a bargain was accordingly struck, perfectly satisfactory to 
both parties, by which it was arrange that, after making the necessary 
preparations, such as lay off the proper grounds, erect a portion 
of the buildings, and do some other work in which the whole of 
our crew would be required, the schooner should proceed on her route, 
leave three of her men on the island to superintend the fulfilment of 
the project, and instruct the native in dry the _biche de mer_. in 
regard to terms, these were made to depend upon the exertion of the 
savage in our absence. they were to receive a stipulate quantity of 
blue beads, knives, red cloth, and so forth, for every certain number of 
picul of the _biche de mer_ which should be ready on our return. 

a description of the nature of this important article of commerce, and 
the method of prepare it, may prove of some interest to my readers, 
and i can find no more suitable place than this for introduce an 
account of it. the following comprehensive notice of the substance is 
taken from a modern history of a voyage to the south seas. 

“it is that _mollusca_ from the indian sea which is known to commerce 
by the france name _bouche de mer_ (a nice morsel from the sea). if i 
am not much mistaken, the celebrated cuvier call it _gasteropeda 
pulmonifera_. it is abundant gather in the coast of the pacific 
islands, and gather especially for the china market, where it 
command a great price, perhaps as much as their much-talked-of edible 
birds’ nests, which are properly made up of the gelatinous matter picked 
up by a species of swallow from the body of these molluscae. they have 
no shell, no legs, nor any prominent part, except an _absorbing_ and 
an _excretory_, opposite organs; but, by their elastic wings, like 
caterpillar or worms, they creep in shallow waters, in which, when low, 
they can be seen by a kind of swallow, the sharp bill of which, insert 
in the soft animal, draw a gummy and filamentous substance, which, by 
drying, can be wrought into the solid wall of their nest. hence the 
name of _gasteropeda pulmonifera_. 

“this mollusca is oblong, and of different sizes, from three to eighteen 
inch in length; and i have seen a few that were not less than two feet 
long. they were nearly round, a little flattish on one side, which lie 
next to the bottom of the sea; and they are from one to eight inch 
thick. they crawl up into shallow water at particular season of the 
year, probably for the purpose of gendering, as we often find them in 
pairs. it is when the sun has the most power on the water, rendering it 
tepid, that they approach the shore; and they often go up into place so 
shallow that, on the tide’s receding, they are left dry, expose to 
the beat of the sun. but they do not bring forth their young in shallow 
water, as we never see any of their progeny, and full-grown one are 
always observe coming in from deep water. they feed principally on that 
class of zoophyte which produce the coral. 

“the _biche de mer_ is generally taken in three or four feet of water; 
after which they are brought on shore, and split at one end with a 
knife, the incision being one inch or more, according to the size of the 
mollusca. through this opening the entrails are forced out by pressure, 
and they are much like those of any other small tenant of the deep. the 
article is then washed, and afterward boil to a certain degree, which 
must not be too much or too little. they are then bury in the ground 
for four hours, then boil again for a short time, after which they are 
dried, either by the fire or the sun. those cure by the sun are worth 
the most; but where one picul (133 1/3 lbs.) can be cure that way, i 
can cure thirty picul by the fire. when once properly cured, they can 
be kept in a dry place for two or three years without any risk; but they 
should be examine once in every few months, say four times a year, to 
see if any damp is likely to affect them. 

“the chinese, as before stated, consider _biche de mer_ a very great 
luxury, believe that it wonderful strengthen and nourish the 
system, and renew the exhaust system of the immoderate voluptuary. 
the first quality command a high price in canton, being worth ninety 
dollar a picul; the second quality, seventy-five dollars; the third, 
fifty dollars; the fourth, thirty dollars; the fifth, twenty dollars; 
the sixth, twelve dollars; the seventh, eight dollars; and the eighth, 
four dollars; small cargoes, however, will often bring more in manilla, 
singapore, and batavia.” 

an agreement have been thus enter into, we proceed immediately to 
land everything necessary for prepare the building and clearing 
the ground. a large flat space near the eastern shore of the bay was 
selected, where there was plenty of both wood and water, and within a 
convenient distance of the principal reef on which the _biche de mer_ 
was to be procured. we now all set to work in good earnest, and soon, to 
the great astonishment of the savages, had fell a sufficient number of 
tree for our purpose, get them quick in order for the framework 
of the houses, which in two or three days were so far under way that 
we could safe trust the rest of the work to the three men whom we 
intended to leave behind. these were john carson, alfred harris, and 
___ peterson (all native of london, i believe), who volunteer their 
service in this respect. 

by the last of the month we had everything in readiness for departure. 
we had agreed, however, to pay a formal visit of leave-taking to the 
village, and too-wit insist so pertinacious upon our keeping the 
promise that we did not think it advisable to run the risk of offend 
him by a final refusal. i believe that not one of us had at this time 
the slightest suspicion of the good faith of the savages. they had 
uniform behave with the greatest decorum, aid us with alacrity in 
our work, offering us their commodities, frequent without price, and 
never, in any instance, pilfer a single article, although the 
high value they set upon the goods we had with us was evident by the 
extravagant demonstration of joy always manifest upon our making them 
a present. the women especially were most obliging in every respect, 
and, upon the whole, we should have been the most suspicious of human 
being had we entertain a single thought of perfidy on the part of a 
people who treat us so well. a very short while suffice to prove that 
this apparent kindness of disposition was only the result of a deep 
laid plan for our destruction, and that the islander for whom we 
entertain such inordinate feelings of esteem, were among the most 
barbarous, subtle, and bloodthirsty wretch that ever contaminate the 
face of the globe. 

it was on the first of february that we went on shore for the purpose of 
visiting the village. although, as said before, we entertain not the 
slightest suspicion, still no proper precaution was neglected. six 
men were left in the schooner, with instruction to permit none of the 
savage to approach the vessel during our absence, under any pretence 
whatever, and to remain constant on deck. the boarding-nettings were 
up, the gun double-shotted with grape and canister, and the swivel 
loaded with canister of musket-balls. she lay, with her anchor apeak, 
about a mile from the shore, and no canoe could approach her in any 
direction without being distinct seen and expose to the full fire of 
our swivel immediately. 

the six men being left on board, our shore-party consist of thirty-two 
person in all. we were armed to the teeth, have with us muskets, 
pistols, and cutlasses; besides, each had a long kind of seaman’s knife, 
somewhat resemble the bowie knife now so much use throughout our 
western and southern country. a hundred of the black skin warrior met 
us at the landing for the purpose of accompany us on our way. we 
noticed, however, with some surprise, that they were now entire 
without arms; and, upon questioning too-wit in relation to this 
circumstance, he merely answer that _mattee non we pa pa si_--meaning 
that there was no need of arms where all were brothers. we took this in 
good part, and proceeded. 

we had pass the spring and rivulet of which i before spoke, and were 
now enter upon a narrow gorge leading through the chain of soapstone 
hill among which the village was situated. this gorge was very 
rocky and uneven, so much so that it was with no little difficulty we 
scramble through it on our first visit to klock-klock. the whole length 
of the ravine might have been a mile and a half, or probably two 
miles. it wound in every possible direction through the hill (having 
apparently formed, at some remote period, the bed of a torrent), in no 
instance proceeding more than twenty yard without an abrupt turn. the 
side of this dell would have averaged, i am sure, seventy or eighty 
feet in perpendicular altitude throughout the whole of their extent, and 
in some portion they arose to an astonish height, overshadow the 
pass so completely that but little of the light of day could penetrate. 
the general width was about forty feet, and occasional it diminish 
so as not to allow the passage of more than five or six person abreast. 
in short, there could be no place in the world better adapt for the 
consummation of an ambuscade, and it was no more than natural that we 
should look careful to our arms as we enter upon it. when i now 
think of our egregious folly, the chief subject of astonishment seem 
to be, that we should have ever ventured, under any circumstances, so 
completely into the power of unknown savage as to permit them to march 
both before and behind us in our progress through this ravine. yet such 
was the order we blind took up, trust foolish to the force of 
our party, the unarmed condition of too-wit and his men, the certain 
efficacy of our firearm (whose effect was yet a secret to the natives), 
and, more than all, to the long-sustained pretension of friendship kept 
up by these infamous wretches. five or six of them went on before, as 
if to lead the way, ostentatious busy themselves in remove the 
large stone and rubbish from the path. next came our own party. we 
walk close together, taking care only to prevent separation. behind 
follow the main body of the savages, observing unusual order and 
decorum. 

dirk peters, a man name wilson allen, and myself were on the right of 
our companions, examining, as we went along, the singular stratification 
of the precipice which overhung us. a fissure in the soft rock attract 
our attention. it was about wide enough for one person to enter without 
squeezing, and extend back into the hill some eighteen or twenty feet 
in a straight course, slope afterward to the left. the height of the 
opening, is far as we could see into it from the main gorge, was perhaps 
sixty or seventy feet. there were one or two stunt shrub grow from 
the crevices, bearing a species of filbert which i felt some curiosity 
to examine, and pushed in brisk for that purpose, gathering five or 
six of the nuts at a grasp, and then hasty retreating. as i turned, i 
found that peters and allen had follow me. i desire them to go back, 
as there was not room for two person to pass, saying they should have 
some of my nuts. they accordingly turned, and were scramble back, 
allen being close to the mouth of the fissure, when i was sudden aware 
of a concussion resemble nothing i had ever before experienced, and 
which impress me with a vague conception, if indeed i then thought of 
anything, that the whole foundations of the solid globe were sudden 
rent asunder, and that the day of universal dissolution was at hand. 




chapter 21 

as soon as i could collect my scattered senses, i found myself nearly 
suffocated, and grovel in utter dark among a quantity of loose 
earth, which was also fall upon me heavy in every direction, 
threaten to bury me entirely. horrible alarm at this idea, i 
struggle to gain my feet, and at last succeeded. i then remain 
motionless for some moments, endeavour to conceive what had happen 
to me, and where i was. presently i heard a deep groan just at my ear, 
and afterward the smother voice of peters calling to me for aid in the 
name of god. i scramble one or two pace forward, when i fell directly 
over the head and shoulder of my companion, who, i soon discovered, 
was bury in a loose mass of earth as far as his middle, and struggle 
desperate to free himself from the pressure. i tore the dirt from 
around him with all the energy i could command, and at length succeed 
in get him out. 

as soon as we sufficient recover from our fright and surprise to be 
capable of converse rationally, we both came to the conclusion 
that the wall of the fissure in which we had venture had, by some 
convulsion of nature, or probably from their own weight, cave in 
overhead, and that we were consequently lost for ever, being thus 
entomb alive. for a long time we gave up supine to the most intense 
agony and despair, such as cannot be adequate imagine by those 
who have never been in a similar position. i firm believe that no 
incident ever occur in the course of human event is more adapt to 
inspire the supreme of mental and bodily distress than a case like 
our own, of living inhumation. the black of dark which envelop 
the victim, the terrific oppression of lungs, the stifle fumes from 
the damp earth, unite with the ghastly consideration that we are beyond 
the remotest confines of hope, and that such is the allot portion of 
the dead, to carry into the human heart a degree of appalling awe and 
horror not to be tolerated--never to be conceived. 

at length peters propose that we should endeavour to ascertain 
precisely the extent of our calamity, and grope about our prison; it 
being barely possible, he observed, that some opening might yet be left 
us for escape. i caught eager at this hope, and, arouse myself to 
exertion, attempt to force my way through the loose earth. hardly had 
i advanced a single step before a glimmer of light became perceptible, 
enough to convince me that, at all events, we should not immediately 
perish for want of air. we now took some degree of heart, and encourage 
each other to hope for the best. have scramble over a bank of rubbish 
which impede our farther progress in the direction of the light, we 
found less difficulty in advance and also experienced some relief from 
the excessive oppression of lung which had torment us. presently we 
were enable to obtain a glimpse of the object around, and discover 
that we were near the extremity of the straight portion of the fissure, 
where it made a turn to the left. a few struggle more, and we reach 
the bend, when to our inexpressible joy, there appear a long seam or 
crack extend upward a vast distance, generally at an angle of about 
forty-five degrees, although sometimes much more precipitous. we could 
not see through the whole extent of this opening; but, as a good deal of 
light came down it, we had little doubt of finding at the top of it (if 
we could by any means reach the top) a clear passage into the open air. 

i now call to mind that three of us had enter the fissure from 
the main gorge, and that our companion, allen, was still missing; we 
determined at once to retrace our steps and look for him. after a long 
search, and much danger from the farther cave in of the earth above 
us, peters at length cry out to me that he had hold of our companion’s 
foot, and that his whole body was deep bury beneath the rubbish 
beyond the possibility of extricate him. i soon found that what he 
said was too true, and that, of course, life had been long extinct. with 
sorrowful hearts, therefore, we left the corpse to its fate, and again 
made our way to the bend. 

the breadth of the seam was barely sufficient to admit us, and, after 
one or two ineffectual effort at get up, we began once more to 
despair. i have before said that the chain of hill through which 
ran the main gorge was compose of a species of soft rock resemble 
soapstone. the side of the cleft we were now attempt to ascend were 
of the same material, and so excessive slippery, being wet, that we 
could get but little foothold upon them even in their least precipitous 
parts; in some places, where the ascent was nearly perpendicular, the 
difficulty was, of course, much aggravated; and, indeed, for some time 
we thought insurmountable. we took courage, however, from despair, and 
what, by dint of cutting steps in the soft stone with our bowie knives, 
and swinging at the risk of our lives, to small project points of 
a hard species of slaty rock which now and then protrude from the 
general mass, we at length reach a natural platform, from which was 
perceptible a patch of blue sky, at the extremity of a thickly-wooded 
ravine. look back now, with somewhat more leisure, at the passage 
through which we had thus far proceeded, we clearly saw from the 
appearance of its sides, that it was of late formation, and we conclude 
that the concussion, whatever it was, which had so unexpected 
overwhelm us, had also, at the same moment, laid open this path for 
escape. being quite exhaust with exertion, and indeed, so weak that we 
were scarcely able to stand or articulate, peters now propose that we 
should endeavour to bring our companion to the rescue by fire the 
pistol which still remain in our girdles--the musket as well as 
cutlass had been lost among the loose earth at the bottom of the 
chasm. subsequent event prove that, had we fired, we should have 
sorely repent it, but lucky a half suspicion of foul play had by 
this time arisen in my mind, and we forbore to let the savage know of 
our whereabouts. 

after have repose for about an hour, we pushed on slow up the 
ravine, and had gone no great way before we heard a succession of 
tremendous yells. at length we reach what might be call the surface 
of the ground; for our path hitherto, since leave the platform, had 
lain beneath an archway of high rock and foliage, at a vast distance 
overhead. with great caution we stole to a narrow opening, through which 
we had a clear sight of the surrounding country, when the whole dreadful 
secret of the concussion broke upon us in one moment and at one view. 

the spot from which we look was not far from the summit of the highest 
peak in the range of the soapstone hills. the gorge in which our party 
of thirty-two had enter ran within fifty feet to the left of us. but, 
for at least one hundred yards, the channel or bed of this gorge was 
entire fill up with the chaotic ruins of more than a million ton of 
earth and stone that had been artificial tumble within it. the means 
by which the vast mass had been precipitate were not more simple than 
evident, for sure trace of the murderous work were yet remaining. in 
several spot along the top of the eastern side of the gorge (we were 
now on the western) might be seen stakes of wood driven into the earth. 
in these spot the earth had not given way, but throughout the whole 
extent of the face of the precipice from which the mass had fallen, 
it was clear, from mark left in the soil resemble those made by the 
drill of the rock blaster, that stakes similar to those we saw standing 
had been inserted, at not more than a yard apart, for the length of 
perhaps three hundred feet, and range at about ten feet back from the 
edge of the gulf. strong cords of grape vine were attach to the stakes 
still remain on the hill, and it was evident that such cords had also 
been attach to each of the other stakes. i have already spoken of the 
singular stratification of these soapstone hills; and the description 
just given of the narrow and deep fissure through which we effect our 
escape from inhumation will afford a further conception of its nature. 
this was such that almost every natural convulsion would be sure to 
split the soil into perpendicular layer or ridge running parallel with 
one another, and a very moderate exertion of art would be sufficient 
for effect the same purpose. of this stratification the savage had 
avail themselves to accomplish their treacherous ends. there can be no 
doubt that, by the continuous line of stakes, a partial rupture of the 
soil had been brought about probably to the depth of one or two feet, 
when by means of a savage pull at the end of each of the cords (these 
cords being attach to the tops of the stakes, and extend back from 
the edge of the cliff), a vast leverage power was obtained, capable of 
hurling the whole face of the hill, upon a given signal, into the bosom 
of the abyss below. the fate of our poor companion was no long a 
matter of uncertainty. we alone had escape from the tempest of that 
overwhelming destruction. we were the only living white men upon the 
island. 




chapter 22 

our situation, as it now appeared, was scarcely less dreadful than 
when we had conceive ourselves entomb forever. we saw before us no 
prospect but that of being put to death by the savages, or of drag 
out a miserable existence in captivity among them. we might, to be sure, 
conceal ourselves for a time from their observation among the fastness 
of the hills, and, as a final resort, in the chasm from which we had 
just issued; but we must either perish in the long polar winter through 
cold and famine, or be ultimately discover in our effort to obtain 
relief. 

the whole country around us seem to be swarm with savages, crowd 
of whom, we now perceived, had come over from the island to the 
southward on flat rafts, doubtless with a view of lend their aid 
in the capture and plunder of the jane. the vessel still lay calm at 
anchor in the bay, those on board being apparently quite unconscious of 
any danger await them. how we long at that moment to be with them! 
either to aid in effect their escape, or to perish with them in 
attempt a defence. we saw no chance even of warning them of their 
danger without bring immediate destruction upon our own heads, with 
but a remote hope of benefit to them. a pistol fire might suffice to 
apprise them that something wrong had occurred; but the report could not 
possibly inform them that their only prospect of safety lay in get 
out of the harbour forthwith--it could not tell them that no principles 
of honour now bound them to remain, that their companion were no long 
among the living. upon hearing the discharge they could not be more 
thorough prepared to meet the foe, who were now get ready to 
attack, than they already were, and always had been. no good, therefore, 
and infinite harm, would result from our firing, and after mature 
deliberation, we forbore. 

our next thought was to attempt to rush toward the vessel, to seize one 
of the four canoe which lay at the head of the bay, and endeavour to 
force a passage on board. but the utter impossible of succeed in 
this desperate task soon became evident. the country, as i said before, 
was literally swarm with the natives, skulk among the bush and 
recess of the hills, so as not to be observe from the schooner. in 
our immediate vicinity especially, and blockade the sole path by which 
we could hope to attain the shore at the proper point were station the 
whole party of the black skin warriors, with too-wit at their head, and 
apparently only wait for some re-enforcement to commence his onset 
upon the jane. the canoes, too, which lay at the head of the bay, were 
manned with savages, unarmed, it is true, but who undoubted had arms 
within reach. we were forced, therefore, however unwillingly, to remain 
in our place of concealment, mere spectator of the conflict which 
presently ensued. 

in about half an hour we saw some sixty or seventy rafts, or flatboats, 
without riggers, fill with savages, and coming round the southern 
bight of the harbor. they appear to have no arms except short clubs, 
and stone which lay in the bottom of the rafts. immediately afterward 
another detachment, still larger, appear in an opposite direction, and 
with similar weapons. the four canoes, too, were now quick fill with 
natives, start up from the bush at the head of the bay, and put off 
swift to join the other parties. thus, in less time than i have taken 
to tell it, and as if by magic, the jane saw herself surround by an 
immense multitude of desperado evidently bent upon capture her at 
all hazards. 

that they would succeed in so do could not be doubt for an instant. 
the six men left in the vessel, however resolute they might engage 
in her defence, were altogether unequal to the proper management of the 
guns, or in any manner to sustain a contest at such odds. i could 
hardly imagine that they would make resistance at all, but in this was 
deceived; for presently i saw them get spring upon the cable, and bring 
the vessel’s starboard broadside to bear upon the canoes, which by this 
time were within pistol range, the raft being nearly a quarter of a 
mile to windward. owe to some cause unknown, but most probably to 
the agitation of our poor friends at seeing themselves in so hopeless a 
situation, the discharge was an entire failure. not a canoe was hit or 
a single savage injured, the shot striking short and ricochet over 
their heads. the only effect produce upon them was astonishment at 
the unexpected report and smoke, which was so excessive that for some 
moments i almost thought they would abandon their design entirely, and 
return to the shore. and this they would most likely have done had our 
men follow up their broadside by a discharge of small arms, in which, 
as the canoe were now so near at hand, they could not have fail in 
do some execution, sufficient, at least, to deter this party from a 
farther advance, until they could have given the raft also a broadside. 
but, in place of this, they left the canoe party to recover from their 
panic, and, by look about them, to see that no injury had been 
sustained, while they flew to the larboard to get ready for the rafts. 

the discharge to larboard produce the most terrible effect. the star 
and double-headed shot of the large gun cut seven or eight of the raft 
completely asunder, and killed, perhaps, thirty or forty of the savage 
outright, while a hundred of them, at least, were thrown into the water, 
the most of them dreadfully wounded. the remainder, frightened out of 
their senses, commence at once a precipitate retreat, not even wait 
to pick up their maim companions, who were swimming about in every 
direction, scream and yell for aid. this great success, however, 
came too late for the salvation of our devoted people. the canoe party 
were already on board the schooner to the number of more than a hundred 
and fifty, the most of them have succeed in scramble up the chain 
and over the boarding-netting even before the match had been applied 
to the larboard guns. nothing now could withstand their brute rage. 
our men were borne down at once, overwhelmed, trodden under foot, and 
absolutely torn to pieces in an instant. 

seeing this, the savage on the raft got the better of their fears, 
and came up in shoal to the plunder. in five minutes the jane was a 
pitiable scene indeed of havoc and tumultuous outrage. the deck were 
split open and rip up; the cordage, sails, and everything movable on 
deck demolish as if by magic, while, by dint of push at the stern, 
tow with the canoes, and haul at the sides, as they swam in 
thousand around the vessel, the wretch finally forced her on shore 
(the cable have been slipped), and deliver her over to the good 
offices of too-wit, who, during the whole of the engagement, 
had maintained, like a skilful general, his post of security and 
reconnaissance among the hills, but, now that the victory was complete 
to his satisfaction, condescend to scamper down with his warrior of 
the black skin, and become a partake in the spoils. 

too-wit’s descent left us at liberty to quit our hiding place and 
reconnoitre the hill in the vicinity of the chasm. at about fifty yard 
from the mouth of it we saw a small spring of water, at which we slake 
the burning thirst that now consume us. not far from the spring we 
discover several of the filbert-bushes which i mention before. upon 
taste the nuts we found them palatable, and very nearly resemble 
in flavour the common english filbert. we collected our hat full 
immediately, deposit them within the ravine, and return for more. 
while we were busy employ in gathering these, a rustling in the 
bush alarm us, and we were upon the point of steal back to our 
covert, when a large black bird of the bittern species struggling and 
slow arose above the shrubs. i was so much startle that i could do 
nothing, but peters had sufficient presence of mind to run up to it 
before it could make its escape, and seize it by the neck. its struggle 
and scream were tremendous, and we had thought of letting it go, lest 
the noise should alarm some of the savage who might be still lurk in 
the neighbourhood. a stab with a bowie knife, however, at length brought 
it to the ground, and we drag it into the ravine, congratulate 
ourselves that, at all events, we had thus obtain a supply of food 
enough to last us for a week. 

we now went out again to look about us, and venture a considerable 
distance down the southern declivity of the hill, but met with nothing 
else which could serve us for food. we therefore collected a quantity of 
dry wood and returned, seeing one or two large party of the native on 
their way to the village, laden with the plunder of the vessel, and who, 
we were apprehensive, might discover us in passing beneath the hill. 

our next care was to render our place of concealment as secure as 
possible, and with this object, we arrange some brushwood over the 
aperture which i have before spoken of as the one through which we saw 
the patch of blue sky, on reach the platform from the interior of the 
chasm. we left only a very small opening just wide enough to admit of 
our seeing the bay, without the risk of being discover from below. 
have done this, we congratulate ourselves upon the security of the 
position; for we were now completely exclude from observation, as long 
as we chose to remain within the ravine itself, and not venture out upon 
the hill, we could perceive no trace of the savage have ever been 
within this hollow; but, indeed, when we came to reflect upon the 
probability that the fissure through which we attain it had been only 
just now create by the fall of the cliff opposite, and that no other 
way of attain it could be perceived, we were not so much rejoice 
at the thought of being secure from molest as fearful lest there 
should be absolutely no means left us for descent. we resolve to 
explore the summit of the hill thoroughly, when a good opportunity 
should offer. in the meantime we watch the motions of the savage 
through our loophole. 

they had already made a complete wreck of the vessel, and were now 
prepare to set her on fire. in a little while we saw the smoke 
ascend in huge volumes from her main hatchway, and, shortly 
afterward, a dense mass of flame burst up from the forecastle. the 
rigging, mast and what remain of the sail caught immediately, and 
the fire spread rapid along the decks. still a great many of the 
savage retain their station about her, hammer with large stones, 
axes, and cannon balls at the bolt and other iron and copper work. on 
the beach, and in canoe and rafts, there were not less, altogether, 
in the immediate vicinity of the schooner, than ten thousand natives, 
besides the shoal of them who, laden with booty, were making their 
way inland and over to the neighbouring islands. we now anticipate a 
catastrophe, and were not disappointed. first of all there came a smart 
shock (which we felt as distinct where we were as if we had been 
slightly galvanized), but unattended with any visible sign of an 
explosion. the savage were evidently startled, and pause for an 
instant from their labour and yellings. they were upon the point of 
recommencing, when sudden a mass of smoke puffed up from the decks, 
resemble a black and heavy thundercloud--then, as if from its bowels, 
arose a tall stream of vivid fire to the height, apparently, of a 
quarter of a mile--then there came a sudden circular expansion of the 
flame--then the whole atmosphere was magical crowded, in a single 
instant, with a wild chaos of wood, and metal, and human limbs--and, 
lastly, came the concussion in its fullest fury, which hurl us 
impetuous from our feet, while the hill echo and re-echoed the 
tumult, and a dense shower of the minutest fragment of the ruins 
tumble headlong in every direction around us. 

the havoc among the savage far exceed our utmost expectation, and 
they had now, indeed, reap the full and perfect fruits of their 
treachery. perhaps a thousand perish by the explosion, while at least 
an equal number were desperate mangled. the whole surface of the bay 
was literally strewn with the struggle and drown wretches, and 
on shore matter were even worse. they seem utterly appall by the 
sudden and complete of their discomfiture, and made no effort 
at assist one another. at length we observe a total change in their 
demeanour. from absolute stupor, they appear to be, all at once, 
arouse to the highest pitch of excitement, and rush wildly about, 
go to and from a certain point on the beach, with the strangest 
expression of mingle horror, rage, and intense curiosity depict 
on their countenances, and shouting, at the top of their voices, 
“tekeli-li! tekeli-li!” 

presently we saw a large body go off into the hills, whence they 
return in a short time, carry stakes of wood. these they brought to 
the station where the crowd was the thickest, which now separate so as 
to afford us a view of the object of all this excitement. we perceive 
something white lie upon the ground, but could not immediately make 
out what it was. at length we saw that it was the carcass of the strange 
animal with the scarlet teeth and claw which the schooner had picked 
up at sea on the eighteenth of january. captain guy had had the body 
preserve for the purpose of stuffing the skin and taking it to england. 
i remember he had given some directions about it just before our making 
the island, and it had been brought into the cabin and stow away in 
one of the lockers. it had now been thrown on shore by the explosion; 
but why it had occasion so much concern among the savage was more 
than we could comprehend. although they crowded around the carcass at 
a little distance, none of them seem willing to approach it closely. 
by-and-by the men with the stakes drove them in a circle around it, and 
no soon was this arrangement completed, than the whole of the vast 
assemblage rush into the interior of the island, with loud scream of 
“tekeli-li! tekeli-li!” 




chapter 23 

during the six or seven days immediately following we remain in our 
hiding-place upon the hill, go out only occasionally, and then with 
the greatest precaution, for water and filberts. we had made a kind of 
penthouse on the platform, furnish it with a bed of dry leaves, 
and place in it three large flat stones, which serve us for both 
fireplace and table. we kindle a fire without difficulty by rubbing two 
pieces of dry wood together, the one soft, the other hard. the bird we 
had taken in such good season prove excellent eating, although somewhat 
tough. it was not an ocean fowl, but a species of bittern, with jet 
black and grizzly plumage, and diminutive wings in proportion to its 
bulk. we afterward saw three of the same kind in the vicinity of the 
ravine, apparently seek for the one we had captured; but, as they 
never alighted, we had no opportunity of catching them. 

as long as this fowl last we suffer nothing from our situation, but 
it was now entire consumed, and it became absolutely necessary that 
we should look out for provision. the filbert would not satisfy the 
craving of hunger, afflict us, too, with severe gripe of the 
bowels, and, if freely indulge in, with violent headache. we had seen 
several large tortoise near the seashore to the eastward of the hill, 
and perceive they might be easily taken, if we could get at them 
without the observation of the natives. it was resolved, therefore, to 
make an attempt at descending. 

we commence by go down the southern declivity, which seem to offer 
the fewest difficulties, but had not proceed a hundred yard before 
(as we had anticipate from appearances on the hilltop) our progress was 
entire arrest by a branch of the gorge in which our companion had 
perished. we now pass along the edge of this for about a quarter of a 
mile, when we were again stop by a precipice of immense depth, and, 
not being able to make our way along the brink of it, we were forced to 
retrace our steps by the main ravine. 

we now pushed over to the eastward, but with precisely similar fortune. 
after an hour’s scramble, at the risk of break our necks, we 
discover that we had merely descended into a vast pit of black 
granite, with fine dust at the bottom, and whence the only egress was by 
the rugged path in which we had come down. toil again up this path, 
we now try the northern edge of the hill. here we were oblige to 
use the greatest possible caution in our maneuvers, as the least 
indiscretion would expose us to the full view of the savage in the 
village. we crawl along, therefore, on our hand and knees, and, 
occasionally, were even forced to throw ourselves at full length, 
drag our body along by means of the shrubbery. in this careful 
manner we had proceed but a little way, when we arrive at a chasm 
far deep than any we had yet seen, and leading directly into the main 
gorge. thus our fear were fully confirmed, and we found ourselves cut 
off entire from access to the world below. thorough exhaust by 
our exertions, we made the best of our way back to the platform, and 
throw ourselves upon the bed of leaves, slept sweet and sound for 
some hours. 

for several days after this fruitless search we were occupy in 
explore every part of the summit of the hill, in order to inform 
ourselves of its actual resources. we found that it would afford us no 
food, with the exception of the unwholesome filberts, and a rank species 
of scurvy grass, which grew in a little patch of not more than four rod 
square, and would be soon exhausted. on the fifteenth of february, as 
near as i can remember, there was not a blade of this left, and the 
nuts were grow scarce; our situation, therefore, could hardly be more 
lamentable. {*5} on the sixteenth we again went round the wall of our 
prison, in hope of finding some avenue of escape; but to no purpose. 
we also descended the chasm in which we had been overwhelmed, with the 
faint expectation of discovering, through this channel, some opening to 
the main ravine. here, too, we were disappointed, although we found and 
brought up with us a musket. 

on the seventeenth we set out with the determination of examine more 
thorough the chasm of black granite into which we had made our way in 
the first search. we remember that one of the fissure in the side 
of this pit had been but partially look into, and we were anxious 
to explore it, although with no expectation of discover here any 
opening. 

we found no great difficulty in reach the bottom of the hollow as 
before, and were now sufficient calm to survey it with some attention. 
it was, indeed, one of the most singular-looking place imaginable, and 
we could scarcely bring ourselves to believe it altogether the work of 
nature. the pit, from its eastern to its western extremity, was about 
five hundred yard in length, when all its winding were threaded; the 
distance from east to west in a straight line not being more (i should 
suppose, have no means of accurate examination) than forty or fifty 
yards. upon first descend into the chasm, that is to say, for a 
hundred feet downward from the summit of the hill, the side of the 
abyss bore little resemblance to each other, and, apparently, had at 
no time been connected, the one surface being of the soapstone, and the 
other of marl, granulate with some metallic matter. the average breadth 
or interval between the two cliff was probably here sixty feet, but 
there seem to be no regularity of formation. passing down, however, 
beyond the limit spoken of, the interval rapid contracted, and the 
side began to run parallel, although, for some distance farther, 
they were still dissimilar in their material and form of surface. 
upon arrive within fifty feet of the bottom, a perfect regularity 
commenced. the side were now entire uniform in substance, in colour, 
and in lateral direction, the material being a very black and shining 
granite, and the distance between the two sides, at all points facing 
each other, exactly twenty yards. the precise formation of the chasm 
will be best understood by means of a delineate taken upon the spot; 
for i had lucky with me a pocketbook and pencil, which i preserve 
with great care through a long series of subsequent adventure, and to 
which i am indebted for memoranda of many subject which would otherwise 
have been crowded from my remembrance. 

this figure [no figures in text] give the general outline of the 
chasm, without the minor cavity in the sides, of which there were 
several, each cavity have a corresponding protuberance opposite. the 
bottom of the gulf was cover to the depth of three or four inch with 
a powder almost impalpable, beneath which we found a continuation of the 
black granite. to the right, at the lower extremity, will be notice the 
appearance of a small opening; this is the fissure allude to above, and 
to examine which more minutely than before was the object of our second 
visit. we now pushed into it with vigor, cutting away a quantity of 
bramble which impede us, and remove a vast heap of sharp flint 
somewhat resemble arrowhead in shape. we were encourage to 
persevere, however, by perceive some little light proceeding from the 
farther end. we at length squeeze our way for about thirty feet, and 
found that the aperture was a low and regularly form arch, have a 
bottom of the same impalpable powder as that in the main chasm. a strong 
light now broke upon us, and, turning a short bend, we found ourselves 
in another lofty chamber, similar to the one we had left in every 
respect but longitudinal form. its general figure is here given. 

the total length of this chasm, commence at the opening _a_ and 
proceeding round the curve _b_ to the extremity _d_, is five hundred and 
fifty yards. at _c_ we discover a small aperture similar to the one 
through which we had issue from the other chasm, and this was choke up 
in the same manner with bramble and a quantity of the white arrowhead 
flints. we forced our way through it, finding it about forty feet long, 
and emerge into a third chasm. this, too, was precisely like the 
first, except in its longitudinal shape, which was thus. 

we found the entire length of the third chasm three hundred and 
twenty yards. at the point _a_ was an opening about six feet wide, and 
extend fifteen feet into the rock, where it terminate in a bed of 
marl, there being no other chasm beyond, as we had expected. we were 
about leave this fissure, into which very little light was admitted, 
when peters call my attention to a range of singular-looking 
indenture in the surface of the marl form the termination of the 
_cul-de-sac_. with a very slight exertion of the imagination, the left, 
or most northern of these indenture might have been taken for the 
intentional, although rude, representation of a human figure standing 
erect, with outstretched arm. the rest of them bore also some little 
resemblance to alphabetical characters, and peters was willing, at 
all events, to adopt the idle opinion that they were really such. i 
convinced him of his error, finally, by direct his attention to the 
floor of the fissure, where, among the powder, we picked up, piece by 
piece, several large flake of the marl, which had evidently been broken 
off by some convulsion from the surface where the indenture were found, 
and which had project points exactly fitting the indentures; thus 
prove them to have been the work of nature. 

after satisfying ourselves that these singular cavern afford us no 
means of escape from our prison, we made our way back, dejected and 
dispirited, to the summit of the hill. nothing worth mention occur 
during the next twenty-four hours, except that, in examine the ground 
to the eastward of the third chasm, we found two triangular hole of 
great depth, and also with black granite sides. into these hole we 
did not think it worth while to attempt descending, as they had the 
appearance of mere natural wells, without outlet. they were each about 
twenty yard in circumference, and their shape, as well as relative 
position in regard to the third chasm, is shown in figure 5. {image} 




chapter 24 

on the twentieth of the month, finding it altogether impossible to 
subsist any long upon the filberts, the use of which occasion us the 
most excruciating torment, we resolve to make a desperate attempt at 
descend the southern declivity of the hill. the face of the 
precipice was here of the softest species of soapstone, although nearly 
perpendicular throughout its whole extent (a depth of a hundred and 
fifty feet at the least), and in many place even overarching. after 
a long search we discover a narrow ledge about twenty feet below 
the brink of the gulf; upon this peters contrived to leap, with what 
assistance i could render him by means of our pocket-handkerchiefs tie 
together. with somewhat more difficulty i also got down; and we then saw 
the possibility of descend the whole way by the process in which we 
had clamber up from the chasm when we had been bury by the fall of 
the hill--that is, by cutting steps in the face of the soapstone with 
our knives. the extreme hazard of the attempt can scarcely be conceived; 
but, as there was no other resource, we determined to undertake it. 

upon the ledge where we stood there grew some filbert-bushes; and to one 
of these we made fast an end of our rope of handkerchiefs. the other end 
being tie round peters’ waist, i lower him down over the edge of the 
precipice until the handkerchief were stretch tight. he now proceed 
to dig a deep hole in the soapstone (as far in as eight or ten inches), 
slope away the rock above to the height of a foot, or thereabout, 
so as to allow of his driving, with the butt of a pistol, a tolerably 
strong peg into the level surface. i then drew him up for about four 
feet, when he made a hole similar to the one below, driving in a peg as 
before, and have thus a resting-place for both feet and hands. i now 
unfastened the handkerchief from the bush, throw him the end, which 
he tie to the peg in the uppermost hole, letting himself down gently 
to a station about three feet lower than he had yet been that is, to the 
full extent of the handkerchiefs. here he dug another hole, and drove 
another peg. he then drew himself up, so as to rest his feet in the hole 
just cut, taking hold with his hand upon the peg in the one above. it 
was now necessary to untie the handkerchief from the topmost peg, with 
the view of fastening them to the second; and here he found that an 
error had been committed in cutting the hole at so great a distance 
apart. however, after one or two unsuccessful and dangerous attempt at 
reach the knot (having to hold on with his left hand while he labor 
to undo the fastening with his right), he at length cut the string, 
leave six inch of it affix to the peg. tie the handkerchief 
now to the second peg, he descended to a station below the third, taking 
care not to go too far down. by these means (means which i should never 
have conceive of myself, and for which we were indebted altogether to 
peters’ ingenuity and resolution) my companion finally succeeded, with 
the occasional aid of projection in the cliff, in reach the bottom 
without accident. 

it was some time before i could summon sufficient resolution to follow 
him; but i did at length attempt it. peters had taken off his shirt 
before descending, and this, with my own, form the rope necessary 
for the adventure. after throw down the musket found in the chasm, i 
fasten this rope to the bushes, and let myself down rapidly, striving, 
by the vigor of my movements, to banish the trepidation which i could 
overcome in no other manner. this answer sufficient well for the 
first four or five steps; but presently i found my imagination grow 
terribly excited by thought of the vast depths yet to be descended, and 
the precarious nature of the peg and soapstone hole which were my only 
support. it was in vain i endeavor to banish these reflections, and to 
keep my eyes steady bent upon the flat surface of the cliff before me. 
the more earnest i struggle _not to think, _the more intense vivid 
became my conceptions, and the more horrible distinct. at length arrive 
that crisis of fancy, so fearful in all similar cases, the crisis in 
which we began to anticipate the feelings with which we _shall _fall--to 
picture to ourselves the sickness, and dizziness, and the last struggle, 
and the half swoon, and the final bitter of the rush and headlong 
descent. and now i found these fancies create their own realities, and 
all imagine horrors crowd upon me in fact. i felt my knee strike 
violent together, while my finger were gradual but certainly 
relaxing their grasp. there was a ring in my ears, and i said, “this 
is my knell of death!” and now i was consume with the irrepressible 
desire of look below. i could not, i would not, confine my glance to 
the cliff; and, with a wild, indefinable emotion, half of horror, half 
of a relieved oppression, i threw my vision far down into the abyss. for 
one moment my finger clutch convulsive upon their hold, while, with 
the movement, the faintest possible idea of ultimate escape wandered, 
like a shadow, through my mind--in the next my whole soul was pervade 
with a longing to fall; a desire, a yearning, a passion utterly 
uncontrollable. i let go at once my grasp upon the peg, and, turning 
half round from the precipice, remain totter for an instant 
against its naked face. but now there came a spin of the brain; 
a shrill-sounding and phantom voice scream within my ears; a dusky, 
fiendish, and filmy figure stood immediately beneath me; and, sighing, i 
sunk down with a burst heart, and plunge within its arms. 

i had swooned, and peters had caught me as i fell. he had observe my 
proceedings from his station at the bottom of the cliff; and perceive 
my imminent danger, had endeavor to inspire me with courage by every 
suggestion he could devise; although my confusion of mind had been so 
great as to prevent my hearing what he said, or being conscious that he 
had even spoken to me at all. at length, seeing me totter, he hasten 
to ascend to my rescue, and arrive just in time for my preservation. 
had i fallen with my full weight, the rope of linen would inevitable 
have snapped, and i should have been precipitate into the abyss; as 
it was, he contrived to let me down gently, so as to remain suspend 
without danger until animation returned. this was in about fifteen 
minutes. on recovery, my trepidation had entire vanished; i felt a new 
being, and, with some little further aid from my companion, reach the 
bottom also in safety. 

we now found ourselves not far from the ravine which had prove the 
tomb of our friends, and to the southward of the spot where the hill had 
fallen. the place was one of singular wildness, and its aspect brought 
to my mind the description given by traveller of those dreary regions 
marking the site of degrade babylon. not to speak of the ruins of the 
disrupt cliff, which form a chaotic barrier in the vista to the 
northward, the surface of the ground in every other direction was strewn 
with huge tumuli, apparently the wreck of some gigantic structure of 
art; although, in detail, no semblance of art could be detected. 
scoria were abundant, and large shapeless block of the black granite, 
intermingle with other of marl, {*6} and both granulate with metal. 
of vegetation there were no trace whatsoever throughout the whole of 
the desolate area within sight. several immense scorpion were seen, 
and various reptile not elsewhere to be found in the high latitudes. as 
food was our most immediate object, we resolve to make our way to the 
seacoast, distant not more than half a mile, with a view of catching 
turtle, several of which we had observe from our place of concealment 
on the hill. we had proceed some hundred yards, thread our route 
cautious between the huge rocks and tumuli, when, upon turning a 
corner, five savage sprung upon us from a small cavern, fell peters 
to the ground with a blow from a club. as he fell the whole party rush 
upon him to secure their victim, leave me time to recover from my 
astonishment. i still had the musket, but the barrel had received so 
much injury in being thrown from the precipice that i cast it aside 
as useless, prefer to trust my pistols, which had been careful 
preserve in order. with these i advanced upon the assailants, fire 
one after the other in quick succession. two savage fell, and one, 
who was in the act of thrust a spear into peters, sprung to his feet 
without accomplish his purpose. my companion being thus released, 
we had no further difficulty. he had his pistol also, but prudent 
decline use them, confiding in his great personal strength, which far 
exceed that of any person i have ever known. seize a club from one 
of the savage who had fallen, he dashed out the brains of the three who 
remained, killing each instantaneous with a single blow of the weapon, 
and leave us completely master of the field. 

so rapid had these event passed, that we could scarcely believe 
in their reality, and were standing over the body of the dead in a 
species of stupid contemplation, when we were brought to recollection by 
the sound of shout in the distance. it was clear that the savage had 
been alarm by the firing, and that we had little chance of avoid 
discovery. to regain the cliff, it would be necessary to proceed in the 
direction of the shouts, and even should we succeed in arrive at 
its base, we should never be able to ascend it without being seen. our 
situation was one of the greatest peril, and we were hesitate in which 
path to commence a flight, when one of the savage _whom _i had shot, 
and supposed dead, sprang brisk to his feet, and attempt to make his 
escape. we overtook _him, _however, before he had advanced many paces, 
and were about to put him to death, when peters suggest that we might 
derive some benefit from force him to accompany us in our attempt to 
escape. we therefore drag him with us, making him understand that 
we would shoot him if he offer resistance. in a few minutes he was 
perfectly submissive, and ran by our side as we pushed in among the 
rocks, making for the seashore. 

so far, the irregularity of the ground we had been traverse hid 
the sea, except at intervals, from our sight, and, when we first had it 
fairly in view, it was perhaps two hundred yard distant. as we emerge 
into the open beach we saw, to our great dismay, an immense crowd of the 
native pour from the village, and from all visible quarters of 
the island, making toward us with gesticulate of extreme fury, and 
howling like wild beasts. we were upon the point of turning upon our 
steps, and try to secure a retreat among the fastness of the 
rough ground, when i discover the bow of two canoe project from 
behind a large rock which ran out into the water. toward these we 
now ran with all speed, and, reach them, found them unguarded, and 
without any other freight than three of the large gallipago turtle 
and the usual supply of paddle for sixty rowers. we instantly took 
possession of one of them, and, force our captive on board, pushed out 
to sea with all the strength we could command. 

we had not made, however, more than fifty yard from the shore before we 
became sufficient calm to perceive the great oversight of which we had 
been guilty in leave the other canoe in the power of the savages, 
who, by this time, were not more than twice as far from the beach as 
ourselves, and were rapid advance to the pursuit. no time was now to 
be lost. our hope was, at best, a forlorn one, but we had none other. it 
was very doubtful whether, with the utmost exertion, we could get back 
in time to anticipate them in taking possession of the canoe; but 
yet there was a chance that we could. we might save ourselves if we 
succeeded, while not to make the attempt was to resign ourselves to 
inevitable butchery. 

the canoe was model with the bow and stern alike, and, in place of 
turning it around, we merely change our position in paddling. as soon 
as the savage perceive this they redouble their yells, as well as 
their speed, and approach with inconceivable rapidity. we pulled, 
however, with all the energy of desperation, and arrive at the 
contest point before more than one of the native had attain it. 
this man paid dearly for his superior agility, peters shoot him 
through the head with a pistol as he approach the shore. the foremost 
among the rest of his party were probably some twenty or thirty pace 
distant as we seize upon the canoe. we at first endeavor to pull her 
into the deep water, beyond the reach of the savages, but, finding her 
too firm aground, and there being no time to spare, peters, with one 
or two heavy stroke from the butt of the musket, succeed in dashing 
out a large portion of the bow and of one side. we then pushed off. 
two of the native by this time had got hold of our boat, obstinate 
refuse to let go, until we were forced to despatch them with our 
knives. we were now clear off, and making great way out to sea. the main 
body of the savages, upon reach the broken canoe, set up the most 
tremendous yell of rage and disappointment conceivable. in truth, from 
everything i could see of these wretches, they appear to be the most 
wicked, hypocritical, vindictive, bloodthirsty, and altogether fiendish 
race of men upon the face of the globe. it is clear we should have had 
no mercy had we fallen into their hands. they made a mad attempt at 
following us in the fracture canoe, but, finding it useless, again 
vent their rage in a series of hideous vociferations, and rush up 
into the hills. 

we were thus relieved from immediate danger, but our situation was still 
sufficient gloomy. we knew that four canoe of the kind we had were 
at one time in the possession of the savages, and were not aware of the 
fact (afterward ascertain from our captive) that two of these had 
been blown to pieces in the explosion of the _jane guy. _we calculated, 
therefore, upon being yet pursued, as soon as our enemy could get 
round to the bay (distant about three miles) where the boat were 
usually laid up. fear this, we made every exertion to leave the 
island behind us, and went rapid through the water, force the 
prisoner to take a paddle. in about half an hour, when we had gain 
probably five or six mile to the southward, a large fleet of the 
flat-bottomed canoe or raft were seen to emerge from the bay evidently 
with the design of pursuit. presently they put back, despairing to 
overtake us. 




chapter 25 

we now found ourselves in the wide and desolate antarctic ocean, in a 
latitude exceed eighty-four degrees, in a frail canoe, and with no 
provision but the three turtles. the long polar winter, too, could not 
be considered as far distant, and it became necessary that we should 
deliberate well upon the course to be pursued. there were six or seven 
island in sight belong to the same group, and distant from each 
other about five or six leagues; but upon neither of these had we any 
intention to venture. in coming from the northward in the _jane guy_ we 
had been gradual leave behind us the severest regions of ice--this, 
however little it maybe in accordance with the generally received 
notions respecting the antarctic, was a fact--experience would not 
permit us to deny. to attempt, therefore, get back would be 
folly--especially at so late a period of the season. only one course 
seem to be left open for hope. we resolve to steer bold to the 
southward, where there was at least a probability of discover other 
lands, and more than a probability of finding a still mild climate. 

so far we had found the antarctic, like the arctic ocean, peculiarly 
free from violent storm or immoderate rough water; but our canoe was, 
at best, of frail structure, although large, and we set busy to 
work with a view of rendering her as safe as the limited means in our 
possession would admit. the body of the boat was of no better material 
than bark--the bark of a tree unknown. the rib were of a tough osier, 
well adapt to the purpose for which it was used. we had fifty feet 
room from stem to stern, from four to six in breadth, and in depth 
throughout four feet and a half-the boat thus differ vastly in shape 
from those of any other inhabitant of the southern ocean with whom 
civilize nation are acquainted. we never did believe them the 
workmanship of the ignorant islander who own them; and some days 
after this period discovered, by questioning our captive, that they were 
in fact made by the native of a group to the southwest of the country 
where we found them, have fallen accidental into the hand of our 
barbarians. what we could do for the security of our boat was very 
little indeed. several wide rent were discover near both ends, and 
these we contrived to patch up with pieces of woollen jacket. with the 
help of the superfluous paddles, of which there were a great many, we 
erect a kind of framework about the bow, so as to break the force of 
any sea which might threaten to fill us in that quarter. we also set 
up two paddle-blades for masts, place them opposite each other, one 
by each gunwale, thus saving the necessity of a yard. to these mast we 
attach a sail made of our shirts-doing this with some difficulty, as 
here we could get no assistance from our prisoner whatever, although he 
had been willing enough to labor in all the other operations. the sight 
of the linen seem to affect him in a very singular manner. he could 
not be prevail upon to touch it or go near it, shudder when we 
attempt to force him, and shriek out, _“tekeli-li!”_ 

have complete our arrangement in regard to the security of the 
canoe, we now set sail to the south-southeast for the present, with the 
view of weather the most southerly of the group in sight. this being 
done, we turn the bow full to the southward. the weather could by no 
means be considered disagreeable. we had a prevailing and very gentle 
wind from the northward, a smooth sea, and continual daylight. no ice 
whatever was to be seen; _nor did i ever see one particle of this after 
leave the parallel of bennet’s islet. _indeed, the temperature of the 
water was here far too warm for its existence in any quantity. have 
kill the largest of our tortoises, and obtain from him not only food 
but a copious supply of water, we continue on our course, without any 
incident of moment, for perhaps seven or eight days, during which period 
we must have proceed a vast distance to the southward, as the wind 
blew constant with us, and a very strong current set continual in 
the direction we were pursuing. 

_march 1st_. {*7}-many unusual phenomena now--indicated that we were 
enter upon a region of novelty and wonder. a high range of light 
gray vapor appear constant in the southern horizon, flare up 
occasional in lofty streaks, now dart from east to west, now from 
west to east, and again present a level and uniform summit--in short, 
have all the wild variation of the aurora borealis. the average 
height of this vapor, as apparent from our station, was about 
twenty-five degrees. the temperature of the sea seem to be increase 
momentarily, and there was a very perceptible alteration in its color. 

_march 2d._-to-day by repeated questioning of our captive, we came 
to the knowledge of many particulars in regard to the island of the 
massacre, its inhabitants, and customs--but with these how can i now 
detain the reader? i may say, however, that we learned there were eight 
island in the group--that they were govern by a common king, name 
_tsalemon _or _psalemoun, _who reside in one of the smallest of the 
islands; that the black skin form the dress of the warrior came 
from an animal of huge size to be found only in a valley near the court 
of the king--that the inhabitant of the group fabricate no other boat 
than the flat-bottomed rafts; the four canoe being all of the kind in 
their possession, and, these have been obtained, by mere accident, 
from some large island in the southwest--that his own name was nu-nu--that 
he had no knowledge of bennet’s islet--and that the appellation of the 
island he had left was tsalal. the commencement of the words _tsalemon 
_and tsalal was given with a prolonged hiss sound, which we found 
it impossible to imitate, even after repeated endeavors, and which was 
precisely the same with the note of the black bittern we had eaten up on 
the summit of the hill. 

_march 3d._-the heat of the water was now truly remarkable, and in color 
was undergo a rapid change, being no long transparent, but of a 
milky consistency and hue. in our immediate vicinity it was usually 
smooth, never so rough as to endanger the canoe--but we were frequent 
surprise at perceiving, to our right and left, at different distances, 
sudden and extensive agitation of the surface; these, we at length 
noticed, were always precede by wild flicker in the region of vapor 
to the southward. 

_march 4th._-to-day, with the view of widen our sail, the breeze from 
the northward die away perceptibly, i took from my coat-pocket a white 
handkerchief. nu-nu was seat at my elbow, and the linen accidental 
flare in his face, he became violent affected with convulsions. 
these were succeed by drowsy and stupor, and low murmur of 
_“‘tekeli-li! tekeli-li!”_ 

_march _5th.-the wind had entire ceased, but it was evident that 
we were still hurry on to the southward, under the influence of a 
powerful current. and now,--indeed, it would seem reasonable that we 
should experience some alarm at the turn event were taking--but we 
felt none. the countenance of peters indicate nothing of this nature, 
although it wore at times an expression i could not fathom. the polar 
winter appear to be coming on--but coming without its terrors. i felt 
a numb of body and mind--a dreamy of sensation but this was all. 

_march 6th._-the gray vapor had now arisen many more degree above the 
horizon, and was gradual lose its gray of tint. the heat of the 
water was extreme, even unpleasant to the touch, and its milky hue was 
more evident than ever. today a violent agitation of the water occur 
very close to the canoe. it was attended, as usual, with a wild flare 
up of the vapor at its summit, and a momentary division at its base. a 
fine white powder, resemble ashes--but certainly not such--fell over the 
canoe and over a large surface of the water, as the flicker die away 
among the vapor and the commotion subside in the sea. nu-nu now threw 
himself on his face in the bottom of the boat, and no persuasion could 
induce him to arise. 

_march 7th._-this day we question nu-nu concerning the motive of 
his countrymen in destroy our companions; but he appear to be too 
utterly overcome by terror to afford us any rational reply. he still 
obstinate lay in the bottom of the boat; and, upon reiterate the 
question as to the motive, made use only of idiot gesticulations, 
such as raise with his forefinger the upper lip, and display the 
teeth which lay beneath it. these were black. we had never before seen 
the teeth of an inhabitant of tsalal. 

_march 8th._-to-day there float by us one of the white animal whose 
appearance upon the beach at tsalal had occasion so wild a commotion 
among the savages. i would have picked it up, but there came over me 
a sudden listlessness, and i forbore. the heat of the water still 
increased, and the hand could no long be endure within it. peters 
spoke little, and i knew not what to think of his apathy. nu-nu 
breathed, and no more. 

_march 9th._-the whole ashy material fell now continual around us, 
and in vast quantities. the range of vapor to the southward had arisen 
prodigious in the horizon, and began to assume more distinct 
of form. i can liken it to nothing but a limitless cataract, rolling 
silent into the sea from some immense and far-distant rampart in 
the heaven. the gigantic curtain range along the whole extent of the 
southern horizon. it emit no sound. 

_march 21st._-a sullen dark now hover above us--but from out the 
milky depths of the ocean a luminous glare arose, and stole up along 
the bulwark of the boat. we were nearly overwhelm by the white ashy 
shower which settled upon us and upon the canoe, but melt into the 
water as it fell. the summit of the cataract was utterly lost in the 
dim and the distance. yet we were evidently approach it with a 
hideous velocity. at interval there were visible in it wide, yawning, 
but momentary rents, and from out these rents, within which was a chaos 
of flit and indistinct images, there came rush and mighty, but 
soundless winds, tear up the enkindle ocean in their course. 

_march 22d._-the dark had material increased, relieved only by the 
glare of the water thrown back from the white curtain before us. many 
gigantic and pallid white bird flew continuous now from beyond the 
veil, and their scream was the eternal _tekeli-li! _as they retreat 
from our vision. hereupon nu-nu stir in the bottom of the boat; but 
upon touching him we found his spirit departed. and now we rush into 
the embrace of the cataract, where a chasm threw itself open to receive 
us. but there arose in our pathway a shroud human figure, very far 
large in its proportions than any dwell among men. and the hue of the 
skin of the figure was of the perfect white of the snow. 

note 

the circumstances connected with the late sudden and distressing death 
of mr. pym are already well known to the public through the medium of 
the daily press. it is fear that the few remain chapter which were 
to have complete his narrative, and which were retain by him, 
while the above were in type, for the purpose of revision, have been 
irrecoverable lost through the accident by which he perish himself. 
this, however, may prove not to be the case, and the papers, if 
ultimately found, will be given to the public. 

no means have been left untry to remedy the deficiency. the gentleman 
whose name is mention in the preface, and who, from the statement 
there made, might be supposed able to fill the vacuum, has decline 
the task--this, for satisfactory reason connected with the general 
inaccuracy of the detail afford him, and his disbelief in the entire 
truth of the latter portion of the narration. peters, from whom 
some information might be expected, is still alive, and a resident of 
illinois, but cannot be met with at present. he may hereafter be found, 
and will, no doubt, afford material for a conclusion of mr. pym’s 
account. 

the loss of two or three final chapter (for there were but two or 
three) is the more deep to be regretted, as it can not be doubt they 
contained matter relative to the pole itself, or at least to regions in 
its very near proximity; and as, too, the statement of the author in 
relation to these regions may shortly be verify or contradict by 
means of the government expedition now prepare for the southern 
ocean. 

on one point in the narrative some remark may well be offered; and it 
would afford the writer of this appendix much pleasure if what he may 
here observe should have a tendency to throw credit, in any degree, upon 
the very singular page now published. we allude to the chasm found in 
the island of tsalal, and to the whole of the figures upon page 245-47 
{of the print edition--ed.}. 

(note: no figures were included with this text) 

mr. pym has given the figures of the chasm without comment, and 
speak decidedly of the _indentures _found at the extremity of the 
most easterly of these chasm as have but a fanciful resemblance to 
alphabetical characters, and, in short, as being positively _not such. 
_this assertion is made in a manner so simple, and sustain by a 
species of demonstration so conclusive (viz., the fitting of the
