projection of the fragment found among the dust into the indenture 
upon the wall), that we are forced to believe the writer in earnest; 
and no reasonable reader should suppose otherwise. but as the fact in 
relation to all the figures are most singular (especially when taken in 
connection with statement made in the body of the narrative), it may 
be as well to say a word or two concerning them all--this, too, the more 
especially as the fact in question have, beyond doubt, escape the 
attention of mr. poe. 

figure 1, then, figure 2, figure 3, and figure 5, when conjoin with 
one another in the precise order which the chasm themselves presented, 
and when deprived of the small lateral branch or arch (which, it 
will be remembered, serve only as a means of communication between the 
main chambers, and were of total distinct character), constitute an 
ethiopia verbal root--the root “to be shady,’--whence all the 
inflection of shadow or darkness. 

in regard to the “left or most northwardly” of the indenture in figure 
4, it is more than probable that the opinion of peters was correct, 
and that the hieroglyph appearance was really the work of art, 
and intended as the representation of a human form. the delineate 
is before the reader, and he may, or may not, perceive the resemblance 
suggested; but the rest of the indenture afford strong confirmation 
of peters’ idea. the upper range is evidently the arabic verbal root 
“to be white,” whence all the inflection of brilliancy and 
whiteness. the lower range is not so immediately perspicuous. the 
character are somewhat broken and disjointed; nevertheless, it can not 
be doubt that, in their perfect state, they form the full egypt 
word, “the region of the south.” it should be observe that 
these interpretation confirm the opinion of peters in regard to the 
“most northwardly” of the figures. the arm is outstretched toward the 
south. 

conclusion such as these open a wide field for speculation and exciting 
conjecture. they should be regarded, perhaps, in connection with some 
of the most faint detailed incident of the narrative; although in no 
visible manner is this chain of connection complete. tekeli-li! was the 
cry of the affrighte native of tsalal upon discover the carcase 
of the _white _animal picked up at sea. this also was the shudder 
exclamation of tsalal upon discover the carcass of the _white 
_materials in possession of mr. pym. this also was the shriek of the 
swift-flying, _white, _and gigantic bird which issue from the vapory 
_white _curtain of the south. nothing _white _was to be found at tsalal, 
and nothing otherwise in the subsequent voyage to the region beyond. it 
is not impossible that “tsalal,” the appellation of the island of the 
chasms, may be found, upon minute philological scrutiny, to betray 
either some alliance with the chasm themselves, or some reference to 
the ethiopia character so mysterious written in their windings. 

_“i have graven it within the hills, and my vengeance upon the dust 
within the rock.”_

{*1} whaling vessel are usually fitted with iron oil-tanks--why the 
_grampus_ was not i have never been able to ascertain. 

{*2} the case of the brig _polly_, of boston, is one so much in point, 
and her fate, in many respects, so remarkably similar to our own, that 
i cannot forbear allude to it here. this vessel, of one hundred and 
thirty ton burden, sail from boston, with a cargo of lumber and 
provisions, for santa croix, on the twelfth of december, 1811, under the 
command of captain casneau. there were eight soul on board besides the 
captain--the mate, four seamen, and the cook, together with a mr. hunt, 
and a negro girl belong to him. on the fifteenth, have clear 
the shoal of georges, she sprung a leak in a gale of wind from the 
southeast, and was finally capsized; but, the mast go by the board, 
she afterward righted. they remain in this situation, without fire, 
and with very little provision, for the period of one hundred and 
ninety-one days (from december the fifteenth to june the twentieth), 
when captain casneau and samuel badger, the only survivors, were taken 
off the wreck by the fame, of hull, captain featherstone, bound home 
from rio janeiro. when picked up, they were in latitude 28 degree n., 
longitude 13 degree w., have drift above two thousand miles! on the 
ninth of july the fame fell in with the brig dromero, captain perkins, 
who landed the two sufferer in kennebeck. the narrative from which we 
gather these detail end in the following words: 

“it is natural to inquire how they could float such a vast distance, 
upon the most frequent part of the atlantic, and not be discover all 
this time. they were pass by more than a dozen sail, one of which came 
so nigh them that they could distinct see the people on deck and on 
the rigging look at them; but, to the inexpressible disappointment of 
the starve and freezing men, they stifle the dictate of compassion, 
hoist sail, and cruel abandoned them to their fate.” 

{*3} among the vessel which at various times have professed to meet 
with the aurora may be mention the ship san miguel, in 1769; the ship 
aurora, in 1774; the brig pearl, in 1779; and the ship dolores, in 1790. 
they all agree in give the mean latitude fifty-three degree south. 

{*4} the terms morning and evening, which i have made use of to avoid 
confusion in my narrative, as far as possible, must not, of course, be 
taken in their ordinary sense. for a long time past we had had no night 
at all, the daylight being continual. the dates throughout are according 
to nautical time, and the bearing must be understood as per compass. i 
would also remark, in this place, that i cannot, in the first portion of 
what is here written, pretend to strict accuracy in respect to dates, or 
latitudes and longitudes, have kept no regular journal until after 
the period of which this first portion treats. in many instance i have 
rely altogether upon memory.

ligeia 

and the will therein lieth, which dieth not. who knoweth the mystery 
of the will, with its vigor? for god is but a great will pervade all 
things by nature of its intentness. man doth not yield himself to the 
angels, nor unto death utterly, save only through the weakness of his 
feeble will.--joseph glanvill. 

i cannot, for my soul, remember how, when, or even precisely where, 
i first became acquaint with the lady ligeia. long years have since 
elapsed, and my memory is feeble through much suffering. or, perhaps, i 
cannot now bring these points to mind, because, in truth, the character 
of my beloved, her rare learning, her singular yet placid cast of 
beauty, and the thrill and enthrall eloquence of her low musical 
language, made their way into my heart by pace so steady and 
stealthy progressive that they have been unnoticed and unknown. yet 
i believe that i met her first and most frequent in some large, old, 
decay city near the rhine. of her family--i have surely heard her 
speak. that it is of a remotely ancient date cannot be doubted. ligeia! 
ligeia! in study of a nature more than all else adapt to deaden 
impression of the outward world, it is by that sweet word alone--by 
ligeia--that i bring before mine eyes in fancy the image of her who is 
no more. and now, while i write, a recollection flash upon me that 
i have never known the paternal name of her who was my friend and my 
betrothed, and who became the partner of my studies, and finally the 
wife of my bosom. was it a playful charge on the part of my ligeia? or 
was it a test of my strength of affection, that i should institute 
no inquiry upon this point? or was it rather a caprice of my own--a 
wildly romantic offering on the shrine of the most passionate devotion? 
i but indistinct recall the fact itself--what wonder that i have 
utterly forgotten the circumstances which originate or attend it? 
and, indeed, if ever she, the wan and the misty-winged ashtophet of 
idolatrous egypt, presided, as they tell, over marriage ill-omened, 
then most surely she preside over mine. 

there is one dear topic, however, on which my memory fail me not. it is 
the person of ligeia. in stature she was tall, somewhat slender, and, in 
her latter days, even emaciated. i would in vain attempt to portray 
the majesty, the quiet ease, of her demeanor, or the incomprehensible 
lightness and elasticity of her footfall. she came and departed as a 
shadow. i was never made aware of her entrance into my closed study save 
by the dear music of her low sweet voice, as she placed her marble hand 
upon my shoulder. in beauty of face no maiden ever equal her. it was 
the radiance of an opium-dream--an airy and spirit-lifting vision 
more wildly divine than the phantasy which hover vision about the 
slumber soul of the daughter of delos. yet her features were not of 
that regular mould which we have been false taught to worship in the 
classical labor of the heathen. “there is no exquisite beauty,” say 
bacon, lord verulam, speak truly of all the form and genera of 
beauty, “without some strange in the proportion.” yet, although i saw 
that the features of ligeia were not of a classic regularity--although 
i perceive that her lovely was indeed “exquisite,” and felt that 
there was much of “strangeness” pervade it, yet i have try in vain 
to detect the irregularity and to trace home my own perception of “the 
strange.” i examine the contour of the lofty and pale forehead--it 
was faultless--how cold indeed that word when applied to a majesty so 
divine!--the skin rival the purest ivory, the commanding extent and 
repose, the gentle prominence of the regions above the temples; and 
then the raven-black, the glossy, the luxuriant and naturally-curling 
tresses, setting forth the full force of the homeric epithet, 
“hyacinthine!” i look at the delicate outline of the nose--and 
nowhere but in the graceful medallion of the hebrew had i beheld a 
similar perfection. there were the same luxurious smooth of surface, 
the same scarcely perceptible tendency to the aquiline, the same 
harmonious curve nostril speak the free spirit. i regard the 
sweet mouth. here was indeed the triumph of all things heavenly--the 
magnificent turn of the short upper lip--the soft, voluptuous slumber 
of the under--the dimple which sported, and the color which spoke--the 
teeth glancing back, with a brilliancy almost startling, every ray of 
the holy light which fell upon them in her serene and placid, yet most 
exulting radiant of all smiles. i scrutinize the formation of the 
chin--and here, too, i found the gentle of breadth, the soft 
and the majesty, the full and the spirituality, of the greek--the 
contour which the god apollo reveal but in a dream, to cleomenes, the 
son of the athenian. and then i peer into the large eyes of ligeia. 

for eyes we have no model in the remotely antique. it might have been, 
too, that in these eyes of my beloved lay the secret to which lord 
verulam alludes. they were, i must believe, far large than the ordinary 
eyes of our own race. they were even fuller than the fullest of the 
gazelle eyes of the tribe of the valley of nourjahad. yet it was only 
at intervals--in moments of intense excitement--that this peculiarity 
became more than slightly noticeable in ligeia. and at such moments was 
her beauty--in my heated fancy thus it appear perhaps--the beauty of 
being either above or apart from the earth--the beauty of the fabulous 
houri of the turk. the hue of the orb was the most brilliant of black, 
and, far over them, hung jetty lash of great length. the brows, 
slightly irregular in outline, had the same tint. the “strangeness,” 
however, which i found in the eyes, was of a nature distinct from the 
formation, or the color, or the brilliancy of the features, and must, 
after all, be refer to the expression. ah, word of no meaning! behind 
whose vast latitude of mere sound we intrench our ignorance of so much 
of the spiritual. the expression of the eyes of ligeia! how for long 
hour have i ponder upon it! how have i, through the whole of a 
midsummer night, struggle to fathom it! what was it--that something 
more profound than the well of democritus--which lay far within the 
pupil of my beloved? what was it? i was possessed with a passion to 
discover. those eyes! those large, those shining, those divine orbs! 
they became to me twin stars of leda, and i to them devoutest of 
astrologers. 

there is no point, among the many incomprehensible anomaly of the 
science of mind, more thrilling exciting than the fact--never, i 
believe, notice in the schools--that, in our endeavor to recall to 
memory something long forgotten, we often find ourselves upon the very 
verge of remembrance, without being able, in the end, to remember. and 
thus how frequently, in my intense scrutiny of ligeia’s eyes, have 
i felt approach the full knowledge of their expression--felt it 
approaching--yet not quite be mine--and so at length entire depart! 
and (strange, oh strangest mystery of all!) i found, in the commonest 
object of the universe, a circle of analogy to that expression. i 
mean to say that, subsequent to the period when ligeia’s beauty pass 
into my spirit, there dwelling as in a shrine, i derived, from many 
existence in the material world, a sentiment such as i felt always 
arouse within me by her large and luminous orbs. yet not the more 
could i define that sentiment, or analyze, or even steady view it. 
i recognize it, let me repeat, sometimes in the survey of a 
rapidly-growing vine--in the contemplation of a moth, a butterfly, a 
chrysalis, a stream of running water. i have felt it in the ocean; in 
the fall of a meteor. i have felt it in the glance of unusually age 
people. and there are one or two stars in heaven--(one especially, a 
star of the sixth magnitude, double and changeable, to be found near the 
large star in lyra) in a telescopic scrutiny of which i have been made 
aware of the feeling. i have been fill with it by certain sound from 
string instruments, and not unfrequent by passage from books. among 
innumerable other instances, i well remember something in a volume of 
joseph glanvill, which (perhaps merely from its quaintness--who shall 
say?) never fail to inspire me with the sentiment;--“and the will 
therein lieth, which dieth not. who knoweth the mystery of the will, 
with its vigor? for god is but a great will pervade all things by 
nature of its intentness. man doth not yield him to the angels, nor unto 
death utterly, save only through the weakness of his feeble will.” 

length of years, and subsequent reflection, have enable me to trace, 
indeed, some remote connection between this passage in the english 
moralist and a portion of the character of ligeia. an intensity in 
thought, action, or speech, was possibly, in her, a result, or at least 
an index, of that gigantic volition which, during our long intercourse, 
fail to give other and more immediate evidence of its existence. 
of all the women whom i have ever known, she, the outwardly calm, the 
ever-placid ligeia, was the most violent a prey to the tumultuous 
vulture of stern passion. and of such passion i could form no estimate, 
save by the miraculous expansion of those eyes which at once so 
delight and appall me--by the almost magical melody, modulation, 
distinct and placid of her very low voice--and by the fierce 
energy (rendered doubly effective by contrast with her manner of 
utterance) of the wild words which she habitual uttered. 

i have spoken of the learning of ligeia: it was immense--such as i 
have never known in woman. in the classical tongue was she deep 
proficient, and as far as my own acquaintance extend in regard to the 
modern dialect of europe, i have never known her at fault. indeed upon 
any theme of the most admired, because simply the most abstruse of the 
boast erudite of the academy, have i ever found ligeia at fault? how 
singularly--how thrillingly, this one point in the nature of my wife has 
forced itself, at this late period only, upon my attention! i said her 
knowledge was such as i have never known in woman--but where breathe 
the man who has traversed, and successfully, all the wide area of 
moral, physical, and mathematics science? i saw not then what i now 
clearly perceive, that the acquisition of ligeia were gigantic, were 
astounding; yet i was sufficient aware of her infinite supremacy to 
resign myself, with a child-like confidence, to her guidance through the 
chaotic world of metaphysics investigate at which i was most busy 
occupy during the early years of our marriage. with how vast a 
triumph--with how vivid a delight--with how much of all that is 
ethereal in hope--did i feel, as she bent over me in study but little 
sought--but less known--that delicious vista by slow degree expand 
before me, down whose long, gorgeous, and all untrodden path, i might at 
length pass onward to the goal of a wisdom too divine precious not to 
be forbidden! 

how poignant, then, must have been the grief with which, after some 
years, i beheld my well-grounded expectations take wings to themselves 
and fly away! without ligeia i was but as a child grope benighted. 
her presence, her reading alone, render vivid luminous the many 
mystery of the transcendentalism in which we were immersed. wanting 
the radiant lustre of her eyes, letters, lambent and golden, grew dull 
than saturnian lead. and now those eyes shone less and less frequent 
upon the page over which i pored. ligeia grew ill. the wild eyes blaze 
with a too--too glorious effulgence; the pale finger became of the 
transparent waxen hue of the grave, and the blue vein upon the lofty 
forehead swell and sank impetuous with the tide of the gentle 
emotion. i saw that she must die--and i struggle desperate in spirit 
with the grim azrael. and the struggle of the passionate wife were, to 
my astonishment, even more energetic than my own. there had been much in 
her stern nature to impress me with the belief that, to her, death would 
have come without its terrors;--but not so. words are impotent to convey 
any just idea of the fierce of resistance with which she wrestle 
with the shadow. i groan in anguish at the pitiable spectacle. i would 
have soothed--i would have reasoned; but, in the intensity of her wild 
desire for life,--for life--but for life--solace and reason were 
the uttermost folly. yet not until the last instance, amid the most 
convulsive writhe of her fierce spirit, was shaken the external 
placid of her demeanor. her voice grew more gentle--grew more 
low--yet i would not wish to dwell upon the wild meaning of the quiet 
utter words. my brain reel as i hearken entranced, to a melody 
more than mortal--to assumption and aspiration which mortality had 
never before known. 

that she love me i should not have doubted; and i might have been 
easily aware that, in a bosom such as hers, love would have reign 
no ordinary passion. but in death only, was i fully impress with the 
strength of her affection. for long hours, detain my hand, would she 
pour out before me the overflow of a heart whose more than passionate 
devotion amount to idolatry. how had i deserve to be so blessed by 
such confessions?--how had i deserve to be so cursed with the removal 
of my beloved in the hour of her making them? but upon this subject 
i cannot bear to dilate. let me say only, that in ligeia’s more than 
womanly abandon to a love, alas! all unmerited, all unworthi 
bestowed, i at length recognize the principle of her longing with so 
wildly earnest a desire for the life which was now flee so rapid 
away. it is this wild longing--it is this eager vehemence of desire 
for life--but for life--that i have no power to portray--no utterance 
capable of expressing. 

at high noon of the night in which she departed, beckon me, 
peremptorily, to her side, she bade me repeat certain verse compose by 
herself not many days before. i obey her.--they were these: 

lo! ‘tis a gala night 
within the lonesome latter years! 
an angel throng, bewinged, bedight 
in veils, and drown in tears, 
sit in a theatre, to see 
a play of hope and fears, 
while the orchestra breathe fitful 
the music of the spheres. 

mimes, in the form of god on high, 
mutter and mumble low, 
and hither and thither fly; 
mere puppet they, who come and go 
at bidding of vast formless things 
that shift the scenery to and fro, 
flap from out their condor wings 
invisible wo! 

that motley drama!--oh, be sure 
it shall not be forgot! 
with its phantom chase forever more, 
by a crowd that seize it not, 
through a circle that ever returneth in 
to the self-same spot, 
and much of madness and more of sin 
and horror the soul of the plot. 

but see, amid the mimic rout, 
a crawl shape intrude! 
a blood-red thing that writhe from out 
the scenic solitude! 
it writhes!--it writhes!--with mortal pang 
the mime become its food, 
and the seraph sob at vermin fang 
in human gore imbued. 

out--out are the lights--out all! 
and over each quiver form, 
the curtain, a funeral pall, 
come down with the rush of a storm, 
and the angels, all pallid and wan, 
uprising, unveiling, affirm 
that the play is the tragedy, “man,” 
and its hero the conquer worm. 

“o god!” half shriek ligeia, leap to her feet and extend 
her arms aloft with a spasmodic movement, as i made an end of these 
lines--“o god! o divine father!--shall these things be undeviating 
so?--shall this conquer be not once conquered? are we not part and 
parcel in thee? who--who knoweth the mystery of the will with its 
vigor? man doth not yield him to the angels, nor unto death utterly, 
save only through the weakness of his feeble will.” 

and now, as if exhaust with emotion, she suffer her white arms to 
fall, and return solemn to her bed of death. and as she breathe her 
last sighs, there came mingle with them a low murmur from her lips. i 
bent to them my ear and distinguished, again, the conclude words of 
the passage in glanvill--“man doth not yield him to the angels, nor unto 
death utterly, save only through the weakness of his feeble will.” 

she died;--and i, crush into the very dust with sorrow, could no 
long endure the lonely desolate of my dwelling in the dim and 
decay city by the rhine. i had no lack of what the world call 
wealth. ligeia had brought me far more, very far more than ordinarily 
falls to the lot of mortals. after a few months, therefore, of weary and 
aimless wandering, i purchased, and put in some repair, an abbey, which 
i shall not name, in one of the wildest and least frequent portion of 
fair england. the gloomy and dreary grandeur of the building, the 
almost savage aspect of the domain, the many melancholy and time-honored 
memory connected with both, had much in unison with the feelings of 
utter abandon which had driven me into that remote and unsocial 
region of the country. yet although the external abbey, with its verdant 
decay hanging about it, suffer but little alteration, i gave way, with 
a child-like perversity, and perchance with a faint hope of alleviate 
my sorrows, to a display of more than regal magnificence within.--for 
such follies, even in childhood, i had imbibe a taste and now they came 
back to me as if in the dotage of grief. alas, i feel how much even 
of incipient madness might have been discover in the gorgeous and 
fantastic draperies, in the solemn carving of egypt, in the wild 
cornice and furniture, in the bedlam pattern of the carpet of tuft 
gold! i had become a bounden slave in the trammels of opium, and my 
labor and my orders had taken a coloring from my dreams. but these 
absurd i must not pause to detail. let me speak only of that one 
chamber, ever accursed, whither in a moment of mental alienation, i 
led from the altar as my bride--as the successor of the unforgotten 
ligeia--the fair-haired and blue-eyed lady rowena trevanion, of 
tremaine. 

there is no individual portion of the architecture and decoration of 
that bridal chamber which is not now visibly before me. where were the 
soul of the haughty family of the bride, when, through thirst of gold, 
they permit to pass the threshold of an apartment so bedecked, a 
maiden and a daughter so beloved? i have said that i minutely remember 
the detail of the chamber--yet i am sadly forgetful on topic of deep 
moment--and here there was no system, no keeping, in the fantastic 
display, to take hold upon the memory. the room lay in a high turret of 
the castellated abbey, was pentagon in shape, and of capacious 
size. occupy the whole southern face of the pentagon was the sole 
window--an immense sheet of unbroken glass from venice--a single pane, 
and tint of a leaden hue, so that the ray of either the sun or moon, 
passing through it, fell with a ghastly lustre on the object within. 
over the upper portion of this huge window, extend the trellice-work 
of an age vine, which clamber up the massy wall of the turret. the 
ceiling, of gloomy-looking oak, was excessive lofty, vaulted, and 
elaborate fret with the wildest and most grotesque specimen of a 
semi-gothic, semi-druidical device. from out the most central recess of 
this melancholy vaulting, depended, by a single chain of gold with long 
links, a huge censer of the same metal, saracenic in pattern, and with 
many perforation so contrived that there writhe in and out of them, 
as if endue with a serpent vitality, a continual succession of 
parti-colored fires. 

some few ottoman and golden candelabra, of eastern figure, were in 
various station about--and there was the couch, too--bridal couch--of 
an indian model, and low, and sculpture of solid ebony, with a 
pall-like canopy above. in each of the angle of the chamber stood on 
end a gigantic sarcophagus of black granite, from the tomb of the kings 
over against luxor, with their age lid full of immemorial sculpture. 
but in the drape of the apartment lay, alas! the chief phantasy of 
all. the lofty walls, gigantic in height--even unproportionable 
so--were hung from summit to foot, in vast folds, with a heavy and 
massive-looking tapestry--tapestry of a material which was found alike 
as a carpet on the floor, as a covering for the ottoman and the 
ebony bed, as a canopy for the bed, and as the gorgeous volute of the 
curtains which partially shade the window. the material was the richest 
cloth of gold. it was spotted all over, at irregular intervals, with 
arabesque figures, about a foot in diameter, and wrought upon the cloth 
in pattern of the most jetty black. but these figures partook of the 
true character of the arabesque only when regard from a single point 
of view. by a contrivance now common, and indeed trace to a very 
remote period of antiquity, they were made changeable in aspect. to one 
enter the room, they bore the appearance of simple monstrosities; but 
upon a farther advance, this appearance gradual departed; and step by 
step, as the visitor move his station in the chamber, he saw himself 
surround by an endless succession of the ghastly form which belong to 
the superstition of the norman, or arise in the guilty slumber of the 
monk. the phantasmagoric effect was vastly heighten by the artificial 
introduction of a strong continual current of wind behind the 
draperies--giving a hideous and uneasy animation to the whole. 

in hall such as these--in a bridal chamber such as this--i passed, with 
the lady of tremaine, the unhallowed hour of the first month of our 
marriage--passed them with but little disquietude. that my wife dread 
the fierce moody of my temper--that she shun me and love me but 
little--i could not help perceiving; but it gave me rather pleasure than 
otherwise. i loathe her with a hatred belong more to demon than 
to man. my memory flew back, (oh, with what intensity of regret!) to 
ligeia, the beloved, the august, the beautiful, the entombed. i revel 
in recollection of her purity, of her wisdom, of her lofty, her 
ethereal nature, of her passionate, her idolatrous love. now, then, did 
my spirit fully and freely burn with more than all the fire of her own. 
in the excitement of my opium dream (for i was habitual fetter in 
the shackle of the drug) i would call aloud upon her name, during the 
silence of the night, or among the sheltered recess of the glen 
by day, as if, through the wild eagerness, the solemn passion, the 
consume ardor of my longing for the departed, i could restore her to 
the pathway she had abandoned--ah, could it be forever?--upon the earth. 

about the commencement of the second month of the marriage, the lady 
rowena was attack with sudden illness, from which her recovery was 
slow. the fever which consume her render her nights uneasy; and 
in her perturb state of half-slumber, she spoke of sounds, and of 
motions, in and about the chamber of the turret, which i conclude 
had no origin save in the distemper of her fancy, or perhaps in the 
phantasmagoric influence of the chamber itself. she became at length 
convalescent--finally well. yet but a brief period elapsed, ere a second 
more violent disorder again threw her upon a bed of suffering; and from 
this attack her frame, at all times feeble, never altogether recovered. 
her illness were, after this epoch, of alarm character, and of more 
alarm recurrence, defy alike the knowledge and the great exertion 
of her physicians. with the increase of the chronic disease which 
had thus, apparently, taken too sure hold upon her constitution to 
be eradicate by human means, i could not fail to observe a similar 
increase in the nervous irritation of her temperament, and in her 
excitable by trivial cause of fear. she spoke again, and now more 
frequent and pertinaciously, of the sounds--of the slight sounds--and 
of the unusual motions among the tapestries, to which she had formerly 
alluded. 

one night, near the close in of september, she pressed this 
distressing subject with more than usual emphasis upon my attention. she 
had just awaken from an unquiet slumber, and i had been watching, 
with feelings half of anxiety, half of vague terror, the workings of her 
emaciate countenance. i sat by the side of her ebony bed, upon one of 
the ottoman of india. she partly arose, and spoke, in an earnest low 
whisper, of sound which she then heard, but which i could not hear--of 
motions which she then saw, but which i could not perceive. the wind was 
rush hurried behind the tapestries, and i wish to show her 
(what, let me confess it, i could not all believe) that those almost 
inarticulate breathings, and those very gentle variation of the figures 
upon the wall, were but the natural effects of that customary rush of 
the wind. but a deadly pallor, overspreade her face, had prove to me 
that my exertion to reassure her would be fruitless. she appear to 
be fainting, and no attendant were within call. i remember where 
was deposit a decanter of light wine which had been ordered by her 
physicians, and hasten across the chamber to procure it. but, as 
i step beneath the light of the censer, two circumstances of a 
startle nature attract my attention. i had felt that some palpable 
although invisible object had pass lightly by my person; and i saw 
that there lay upon the golden carpet, in the very middle of the rich 
lustre thrown from the censer, a shadow--a faint, indefinite shadow of 
angel aspect--such as might be fancy for the shadow of a shade. 
but i was wild with the excitement of an immoderate dose of opium, and 
heed these things but little, nor spoke of them to rowena. have 
found the wine, i recross the chamber, and pour out a gobletful, 
which i held to the lip of the faint lady. she had now partially 
recovered, however, and took the vessel herself, while i sank upon an 
ottoman near me, with my eyes fasten upon her person. it was then that 
i became distinct aware of a gentle footfall upon the carpet, and 
near the couch; and in a second thereafter, as rowena was in the act 
of raise the wine to her lips, i saw, or may have dream that i 
saw, fall within the goblet, as if from some invisible spring in the 
atmosphere of the room, three or four large drops of a brilliant and 
ruby colored fluid. if this i saw--not so rowena. she swallow the wine 
unhesitatingly, and i forbore to speak to her of a circumstance which 
must, after all, i considered, have been but the suggestion of a vivid 
imagination, render morbid active by the terror of the lady, by the 
opium, and by the hour. 

yet i cannot conceal it from my own perception that, immediately 
subsequent to the fall of the ruby-drops, a rapid change for the worse 
took place in the disorder of my wife; so that, on the third subsequent 
night, the hand of her menial prepared her for the tomb, and on the 
fourth, i sat alone, with her shroud body, in that fantastic chamber 
which had received her as my bride.--wild visions, opium-engendered, 
flitted, shadow-like, before me. i gaze with unquiet eye upon the 
sarcophagi in the angle of the room, upon the vary figures of the 
drapery, and upon the writhe of the parti-colored fire in the censer 
overhead. my eyes then fell, as i call to mind the circumstances of 
a former night, to the spot beneath the glare of the censer where i had 
seen the faint trace of the shadow. it was there, however, no longer; 
and breathing with greater freedom, i turn my glance to the pallid 
and rigid figure upon the bed. then rush upon me a thousand memory 
of ligeia--and then came back upon my heart, with the turbulent violence 
of a flood, the whole of that unutterable wo with which i had regard 
her thus enshrouded. the night waned; and still, with a bosom full of 
bitter thought of the one only and supreme beloved, i remain gaze 
upon the body of rowena. 

it might have been midnight, or perhaps earlier, or later, for i had 
taken no note of time, when a sob, low, gentle, but very distinct, 
startle me from my revery.--i felt that it came from the bed of 
ebony--the bed of death. i listen in an agony of superstitious 
terror--but there was no repetition of the sound. i strained my vision 
to detect any motion in the corpse--but there was not the slightest 
perceptible. yet i could not have been deceived. i had heard the noise, 
however faint, and my soul was awaken within me. i resolute and 
persevering kept my attention rivet upon the body. many minutes 
elapse before any circumstance occur tend to throw light upon the 
mystery. at length it became evident that a slight, a very feeble, and 
barely noticeable tinge of color had flushed up within the cheeks, 
and along the sunken small vein of the eyelids. through a species of 
unutterable horror and awe, for which the language of mortality has no 
sufficient energetic expression, i felt my heart cease to beat, my 
limb grow rigid where i sat. yet a sense of duty finally operate to 
restore my self-possession. i could no long doubt that we had been 
precipitate in our preparations--that rowena still lived. it was 
necessary that some immediate exertion be made; yet the turret was 
altogether apart from the portion of the abbey tenant by the 
servants--there were none within call--i had no means of summon them 
to my aid without leave the room for many minutes--and this i could 
not venture to do. i therefore struggle alone in my endeavor to call 
back the spirit ill hovering. in a short period it was certain, however, 
that a relapse had taken place; the color disappear from both eyelid 
and cheek, leave a wan even more than that of marble; the lip 
became doubly shrivel and pinched up in the ghastly expression 
of death; a repulsive clammy and cold overspread rapid the 
surface of the body; and all the usual rigorous illness immediately 
supervened. i fell back with a shudder upon the couch from which i had 
been so startling aroused, and again gave myself up to passionate 
waking vision of ligeia. 

an hour thus elapse when (could it be possible?) i was a second 
time aware of some vague sound issue from the region of the bed. i 
listened--in extremity of horror. the sound came again--it was a sigh. 
rush to the corpse, i saw--distinctly saw--a tremor upon the lips. in 
a minute afterward they relaxed, disclose a bright line of the pearly 
teeth. amaze now struggle in my bosom with the profound awe which 
had hitherto reign there alone. i felt that my vision grew dim, that 
my reason wandered; and it was only by a violent effort that i at length 
succeed in nerve myself to the task which duty thus once more had 
pointed out. there was now a partial glow upon the forehead and upon the 
cheek and throat; a perceptible warmth pervade the whole frame; there 
was even a slight pulsation at the heart. the lady lived; and with 
redouble ardor i betook myself to the task of restoration. i chafe 
and bath the temple and the hands, and use every exertion which 
experience, and no little medical reading, could suggest. but in vain. 
suddenly, the color fled, the pulsation ceased, the lip resume the 
expression of the dead, and, in an instant afterward, the whole 
body took upon itself the icy chilliness, the livid hue, the intense 
rigidity, the sunken outline, and all the loathsome peculiarity of 
that which has been, for many days, a tenant of the tomb. 

and again i sunk into vision of ligeia--and again, (what marvel that i 
shudder while i write,) again there reach my ear a low sob from the 
region of the ebony bed. but why shall i minutely detail the unspeakable 
horrors of that night? why shall i pause to relate how, time after 
time, until near the period of the gray dawn, this hideous drama of 
revivify was repeated; how each terrific relapse was only into a 
stern and apparently more irredeemable death; how each agony wore the 
aspect of a struggle with some invisible foe; and how each struggle was 
succeed by i know not what of wild change in the personal appearance 
of the corpse? let me hurry to a conclusion. 

the greater part of the fearful night had worn away, and she who had 
been dead, once again stirred--and now more vigorous than hitherto, 
although arouse from a dissolution more appalling in its utter 
hopeless than any. i had long cease to struggle or to move, and 
remain sitting rigid upon the ottoman, a helpless prey to a whirl of 
violent emotions, of which extreme awe was perhaps the least terrible, 
the least consuming. the corpse, i repeat, stirred, and now more 
vigorous than before. the hue of life flushed up with unwonted energy 
into the countenance--the limb relaxed--and, save that the eyelid were 
yet pressed heavy together, and that the bandage and drapery of the 
grave still impart their charnel character to the figure, i might 
have dream that rowena had indeed shaken off, utterly, the fetter of 
death. but if this idea was not, even then, altogether adopted, i could 
at least doubt no longer, when, arise from the bed, tottering, with 
feeble steps, with closed eyes, and with the manner of one bewilder in 
a dream, the thing that was enshroud advanced bold and palpable into 
the middle of the apartment. 

i tremble not--i stir not--for a crowd of unutterable fancies 
connected with the air, the stature, the demeanor of the figure, rush 
hurried through my brain, had paralyzed--had chill me into stone. i 
stir not--but gaze upon the apparition. there was a mad disorder 
in my thoughts--a tumult unappeasable. could it, indeed, be the 
living rowena who confront me? could it indeed be rowena at all--the 
fair-haired, the blue-eyed lady rowena trevanion of tremaine? why, why 
should i doubt it? the bandage lay heavy about the mouth--but then 
might it not be the mouth of the breathing lady of tremaine? and the 
cheeks--there were the rose as in her noon of life--yes, these might 
indeed be the fair cheek of the living lady of tremaine. and the chin, 
with its dimples, as in health, might it not be hers?--but had she then 
grown tall since her malady? what inexpressible madness seize me with 
that thought? one bound, and i had reach her feet! shrink from my 
touch, she let fall from her head, unloosened, the ghastly cerement 
which had confine it, and there stream forth, into the rush 
atmosphere of the chamber, huge masses of long and dishevelled hair; it 
was black than the raven wings of the midnight! and now slow open 
the eyes of the figure which stood before me. “here then, at least,” 
i shriek aloud, “can i never--can i never be mistaken--these are the 
full, and the black, and the wild eyes--of my lost love--of the lady--of 
the lady ligeia.” 




morella 

itself, by itself, solely, one everlasting, and single. 

plato: sympos. 

with a feeling of deep yet most singular affection i regard my friend 
morella. thrown by accident into her society many years ago, my soul 
from our first meeting, burn with fire it had never before known; but 
the fire were not of eros, and bitter and torment to my spirit was 
the gradual conviction that i could in no manner define their unusual 
meaning or regulate their vague intensity. yet we met; and fate bound us 
together at the altar, and i never spoke of passion nor thought of 
love. she, however, shun society, and, attach herself to me alone 
render me happy. it is a happiness to wonder; it is a happiness to 
dream. 

morella’s erudite was profound. as i hope to live, her talent were of 
no common order--her powers of mind were gigantic. i felt this, and, in 
many matters, became her pupil. i soon, however, found that, perhaps 
on account of her presburg education, she placed before me a number of 
those mystical writings which are usually considered the mere dross of 
the early germany literature. these, for what reason i could not imagine, 
were her favourite and constant study--and that in process of time 
they became my own, should be attribute to the simple but effectual 
influence of habit and example. 

in all this, if i err not, my reason had little to do. my convictions, 
or i forget myself, were in no manner act upon by the ideal, nor was 
any tincture of the mysticism which i read to be discovered, unless i 
am greatly mistaken, either in my deed or in my thoughts. persuade 
of this, i abandoned myself implicit to the guidance of my wife, and 
enter with an unflinching heart into the intricacy of her studies. 
and then--then, when pore over forbidden pages, i felt a forbidden 
spirit enkindle within me--would morella place her cold hand upon my 
own, and rake up from the ashes of a dead philosophy some low, singular 
words, whose strange meaning burn themselves in upon my memory. and 
then, hour after hour, would i linger by her side, and dwell upon the 
music of her voice, until at length its melody was taint with terror, 
and there fell a shadow upon my soul, and i grew pale, and shudder 
inward at those too unearthly tones. and thus, joy sudden fade into 
horror, and the most beautiful became the most hideous, as hinnon became 
ge-henna. 

it is unnecessary to state the exact character of those disquisition 
which, grow out of the volumes i have mentioned, formed, for so 
long a time, almost the sole conversation of morella and myself. by 
the learned in what might be term theology morality they will be 
readily conceived, and by the unlearn they would, at all events, 
be little understood. the wild pantheism of fichte; the modify 
paliggenedia of the pythagoreans; and, above all, the doctrine of 
identity as urge by schelling, were generally the points of discussion 
present the most of beauty to the imaginative morella. that identity 
which is term personal, mr. locke, i think, truly define to consist 
in the sane of rational being. and since by person we understand an 
intelligent essence have reason, and since there is a consciousness 
which always accompany thinking, it is this which make us all to 
be that which we call ourselves, thereby distinguish us from 
other being that think, and give us our personal identity. but the 
principium indivduationis, the notion of that identity which at death 
is or is not lost for ever, was to me, at all times, a consideration of 
intense interest; not more from the perplex and exciting nature of 
its consequences, than from the marked and agitate manner in which 
morella mention them. 

but, indeed, the time had now arrive when the mystery of my wife’s 
manner oppress me as a spell. i could no long bear the touch of her 
wan fingers, nor the low tone of her musical language, nor the lustre 
of her melancholy eyes. and she knew all this, but did not upbraid; she 
seem conscious of my weakness or my folly, and, smiling, call it 
fate. she seem also conscious of a cause, to me unknown, for the 
gradual alienation of my regard; but she gave me no hint or token of 
its nature. yet was she woman, and pine away daily. in time the crimson 
spot settled steady upon the cheek, and the blue vein upon the pale 
forehead became prominent; and one instant my nature melt into pity, 
but in, next i met the glance of her meaning eyes, and then my soul 
sicken and became giddy with the giddy of one who gaze downward 
into some dreary and unfathomable abyss. 

shall i then say that i long with an earnest and consume desire for 
the moment of morella’s decease? i did; but the fragile spirit clung to 
its tenement of clay for many days, for many week and irksome months, 
until my torture nerves obtain the mastery over my mind, and i grew 
furious through delay, and, with the heart of a fiend, cursed the days 
and the hour and the bitter moments, which seem to lengthen and 
lengthen as her gentle life declined, like shadow in the die of the 
day. 

but one autumnal evening, when the wind lay still in heaven, morella 
call me to her bedside. there was a dim mist over all the earth, and 
a warm glow upon the waters, and amid the rich october leaves of the 
forest, a rainbow from the firmament had surely fallen. 

“it is a day of days,” she said, as i approached; “a day of all days 
either to live or die. it is a fair day for the son of earth and 
life--ah, more fair for the daughter of heaven and death!” 

i kiss her forehead, and she continued: 

“i am dying, yet shall i live.” 

“morella!” 

“the days have never been when thou couldst love me--but her whom in 
life thou didst abhor, in death thou shalt adore.” 

“morella!” 

“i repeat i am dying. but within me is a pledge of that affection--ah, 
how little!--which thou didst feel for me, morella. and when my spirit 
depart shall the child live--thy child and mine, morella’s. but thy 
days shall be days of sorrow--that sorrow which is the most lasting of 
impressions, as the cypress is the most enduring of trees. for the hour 
of thy happiness are over and joy is not gather twice in a life, as 
the rose of paestum twice in a year. thou shalt no longer, then, play 
the teian with time, but, being ignorant of the myrtle and the vine, 
thou shalt bear about with thee thy shroud on the earth, as do the 
moslemin at mecca.” 

“morella!” i cried, “morella! how knowest thou this?” but she turn 
away her face upon the pillow and a slight tremor coming over her limbs, 
she thus died, and i heard her voice no more. 

yet, as she had foretold, her child, to which in die she had given 
birth, which breathe not until the mother breathe no more, her child, 
a daughter, lived. and she grew strange in stature and intellect, and 
was the perfect resemblance of her who had departed, and i love her 
with a love more fervent than i had believe it possible to feel for any 
denizen of earth. 

but, ere long the heaven of this pure affection became darkened, and 
gloom, and horror, and grief swept over it in clouds. i said the child 
grew strange in stature and intelligence. strange, indeed, was her 
rapid increase in bodily size, but terrible, oh! terrible were the 
tumultuous thought which crowded upon me while watch the development 
of her mental being. could it be otherwise, when i daily discover 
in the conception of the child the adult powers and faculty of the 
woman? when the lesson of experience fell from the lip of infancy? and 
when the wisdom or the passion of maturity i found hourly gleam from 
its full and speculative eye? when, i say, all this became evident to my 
appall senses, when i could no long hide it from my soul, nor throw 
it off from those perception which tremble to receive it, is it to be 
wonder at that suspicions, of a nature fearful and exciting, crept in 
upon my spirit, or that my thought fell back aghast upon the wild tale 
and thrill theory of the entomb morella? i snatch from the 
scrutiny of the world a being whom destiny compel me to adore, and 
in the rigorous seclusion of my home, watch with an agonizing anxiety 
over all which concerned the beloved. 

and as years roll away, and i gaze day after day upon her holy, and 
mild, and eloquent face, and pour over her mature form, day after 
day did i discover new points of resemblance in the child to her mother, 
the melancholy and the dead. and hourly grew dark these shadow of 
similitude, and more full, and more definite, and more perplexing, and 
more hideous terrible in their aspect. for that her smile was like her 
mother’s i could bear; but then i shudder at its too perfect identity, 
that her eyes were like morella’s i could endure; but then they, too, 
often look down into the depths of my soul with morella’s own intense 
and bewilder meaning. and in the contour of the high forehead, and 
in the ringlet of the silken hair, and in the wan finger which bury 
themselves therein, and in the sad musical tone of her speech, and 
above all--oh, above all, in the phrase and expression of the dead on 
the lip of the love and the living, i found food for consume thought 
and horror, for a worm that would not die. 

thus pass away two lustra of her life, and as yet my daughter 
remain nameless upon the earth. “my child,” and “my love,” were the 
designation usually prompt by a father’s affection, and the rigid 
seclusion of her days preclude all other intercourse. morella’s name 
die with her at her death. of the mother i had never spoken to the 
daughter, it was impossible to speak. indeed, during the brief period of 
her existence, the latter had received no impression from the outward 
world, save such as might have been afford by the narrow limit of her 
privacy. but at length the ceremony of baptism present to my mind, 
in its unnerve and agitate condition, a present deliverance from the 
terror of my destiny. and at the baptism font i hesitate for a name. 
and many title of the wise and beautiful, of old and modern times, of 
my own and foreign lands, came throng to my lips, with many, many 
fair title of the gentle, and the happy, and the good. what prompt 
me then to disturb the memory of the bury dead? what demon urge me to 
breathe that sound, which in its very recollection was wont to make ebb 
the purple blood in torrent from the temple to the heart? what fiend 
spoke from the recess of my soul, when amid those dim aisles, and in 
the silence of the night, i whisper within the ear of the holy man 
the syllables--morella? what more than fiend convulse the features of 
my child, and overspread them with hue of death, as start at that 
scarcely audible sound, she turn her glassy eyes from the earth to 
heaven, and fall prostrate on the black slab of our ancestral vault, 
responded--“i am here!” 

distinct, coldly, calm distinct, fell those few simple sound within 
my ear, and thence like molten lead roll hissing into my brain. 
years--years may pass away, but the memory of that epoch never. nor was 
i indeed ignorant of the flower and the vine--but the hemlock and the 
cypress overshadow me night and day. and i kept no reckoning of time 
or place, and the stars of my fate fade from heaven, and therefore the 
earth grew dark, and its figures pass by me like flit shadows, 
and among them all i beheld only--morella. the wind of the firmament 
breathe but one sound within my ears, and the ripple upon the sea 
murmur evermore--morella. but she died; and with my own hand i bore 
her to the tomb; and i laugh with a long and bitter laugh as i found 
no trace of the first in the channel where i laid the second.--morella. 




a tale of the ragged mountain 

during the fall of the year 1827, while reside near charlottesville, 
virginia, i casual made the acquaintance of mr. augustus bedloe. this 
young gentleman was remarkable in every respect, and excited in me a 
profound interest and curiosity. i found it impossible to comprehend 
him either in his moral or his physical relations. of his family i could 
obtain no satisfactory account. whence he came, i never ascertained. 
even about his age--although i call him a young gentleman--there was 
something which perplexed me in no little degree. he certainly seem 
young--and he made a point of speak about his youth--yet there were 
moments when i should have had little trouble in imagine him a hundred 
years of age. but in no regard was he more peculiar than in his personal 
appearance. he was singularly tall and thin. he stoop much. his limb 
were exceedingly long and emaciated. his forehead was broad and low. his 
complexion was absolutely bloodless. his mouth was large and flexible, 
and his teeth were more wildly uneven, although sound, than i had ever 
before seen teeth in a human head. the expression of his smile, 
however, was by no means unpleasing, as might be supposed; but it had 
no variation whatever. it was one of profound melancholy--of a phaseless 
and uncease gloom. his eyes were abnormal large, and round like 
those of a cat. the pupils, too, upon any accession or diminution of 
light, underwent contraction or dilation, just such as is observe in 
the feline tribe. in moments of excitement the orb grew bright to a 
degree almost inconceivable; seeming to emit luminous rays, not of a 
reflect but of an intrinsic lustre, as do a candle or the sun; yet 
their ordinary condition was so total vapid, filmy, and dull as to 
convey the idea of the eyes of a long-interred corpse. 

these peculiarity of person appear to cause him much annoyance, and 
he was continual allude to them in a sort of half explanatory, 
half apologetic strain, which, when i first heard it, impress me very 
painfully. i soon, however, grew accustomed to it, and my uneasy 
wore off. it seem to be his design rather to insinuate than directly 
to assert that, physically, he had not always been what he was--that 
a long series of neuralgic attack had reduce him from a condition of 
more than usual personal beauty, to that which i saw. for many years 
past he had been attend by a physician, name templeton--an old 
gentleman, perhaps seventy years of age--whom he had first encounter 
at saratoga, and from whose attention, while there, he either received, 
or fancy that he received, great benefit. the result was that bedloe, 
who was wealthy, had made an arrangement with dr. templeton, by 
which the latter, in consideration of a liberal annual allowance, had 
consent to devote his time and medical experience exclusively to the 
care of the invalid. 

doctor templeton had been a traveller in his younger days, and at paris 
had become a convert, in great measure, to the doctrine of mesmer. it 
was altogether by means of magnetic remedy that he had succeed in 
alleviate the acute pains of his patient; and this success had very 
naturally inspired the latter with a certain degree of confidence in the 
opinion from which the remedy had been educed. the doctor, however, 
like all enthusiasts, had struggle hard to make a thorough convert of 
his pupil, and finally so far gain his point as to induce the sufferer 
to submit to numerous experiments. by a frequent repetition of these, a 
result had arisen, which of late days has become so common as to attract 
little or no attention, but which, at the period of which i write, had 
very rarely been known in america. i mean to say, that between doctor 
templeton and bedloe there had grown up, little by little, a very 
distinct and strong marked rapport, or magnetic relation. i am not 
prepared to assert, however, that this rapport extend beyond the 
limit of the simple sleep-producing power, but this power itself had 
attain great intensity. at the first attempt to induce the magnetic 
somnolency, the mesmerist entire failed. in the fifth or sixth he 
succeed very partially, and after long continue effort. only at the 
twelfth was the triumph complete. after this the will of the patient 
succumb rapid to that of the physician, so that, when i first became 
acquaint with the two, sleep was brought about almost instantaneous 
by the mere volition of the operator, even when the invalid was unaware 
of his presence. it is only now, in the year 1845, when similar miracle 
are witness daily by thousands, that i dare venture to record this 
apparent impossible as a matter of serious fact. 

the temperature of bedloe was, in the highest degree sensitive, 
excitable, enthusiastic. his imagination was singularly vigorous and 
creative; and no doubt it derive additional force from the habitual use 
of morphine, which he swallow in great quantity, and without which he 
would have found it impossible to exist. it was his practice to take 
a very large dose of it immediately after breakfast each morning--or, 
rather, immediately after a cup of strong coffee, for he ate nothing in 
the forenoon--and then set forth alone, or attend only by a dog, upon 
a long ramble among the chain of wild and dreary hill that lie westward 
and southward of charlottesville, and are there dignified by the title 
of the ragged mountains. 

upon a dim, warm, misty day, toward the close of november, and during 
the strange interregnum of the season which in america is term the 
indian summer, mr. bedloe departed as usual for the hills. the day 
passed, and still he did not return. 

about eight o’clock at night, have become seriously alarm at his 
protract absence, we were about setting out in search of him, when he 
unexpected made his appearance, in health no worse than usual, and 
in rather more than ordinary spirits. the account which he gave of his 
expedition, and of the event which had detain him, was a singular one 
indeed. 

“you will remember,” said he, “that it was about nine in the morning 
when i left charlottesville. i bent my steps immediately to the 
mountains, and, about ten, enter a gorge which was entire new to 
me. i follow the winding of this pass with much interest. the scenery 
which present itself on all sides, although scarcely entitle to be 
call grand, had about it an indescribable and to me a delicious aspect 
of dreary desolation. the solitude seem absolutely virgin. i could not 
help believe that the green sod and the gray rocks upon which i trod 
had been trodden never before by the foot of a human being. so 
entire secluded, and in fact inaccessible, except through a series 
of accidents, is the entrance of the ravine, that it is by no means 
impossible that i was indeed the first adventurer--the very first and 
sole adventurer who had ever penetrate its recesses. 

“the thick and peculiar mist, or smoke, which distinguish the indian 
summer, and which now hung heavy over all objects, served, no doubt, 
to deepen the vague impression which these object created. so dense 
was this pleasant fog that i could at no time see more than a dozen 
yard of the path before me. this path was excessive sinuous, and 
as the sun could not be seen, i soon lost all idea of the direction 
in which i journeyed. in the meantime the morphine had its customary 
effect--that of endue all the external world with an intensity of 
interest. in the quiver of a leaf--in the hue of a blade of grass--in 
the shape of a trefoil--in the hum of a bee--in the gleam of a 
dew-drop--in the breathing of the wind--in the faint odor that came 
from the forest--there came a whole universe of suggestion--a gay and 
motley train of rhapsody and immethodic thought. 

“busied in this, i walk on for several hours, during which the mist 
deepen around me to so great an extent that at length i was reduce 
to an absolute grope of the way. and now an indescribable uneasy 
possessed me--a species of nervous hesitation and tremor. i fear to 
tread, lest i should be precipitate into some abyss. i remembered, too, 
strange story told about these ragged hills, and of the uncouth and 
fierce races of men who tenant their grove and caverns. a thousand 
vague fancies oppress and disconcert me--fancies the more 
distressing because vague. very sudden my attention was arrest by 
the loud beating of a drum. 

“my amaze was, of course, extreme. a drum in these hill was a thing 
unknown. i could not have been more surprise at the sound of the trump 
of the archangel. but a new and still more astound source of interest 
and perplexity arose. there came a wild rattling or jingle sound, as 
if of a bunch of large keys, and upon the instant a dusky-visaged and 
half-naked man rush past me with a shriek. he came so close to my 
person that i felt his hot breath upon my face. he bore in one hand 
an instrument compose of an assemblage of steel rings, and shook them 
vigorous as he ran. scarcely had he disappear in the mist before, 
pant after him, with open mouth and glaring eyes, there dart a huge 
beast. i could not be mistaken in its character. it was a hyena. 

“the sight of this monster rather relieved than heighten my 
terrors--for i now made sure that i dreamed, and endeavor to arouse 
myself to waking consciousness. i step bold and brisk forward. 
i rub my eyes. i call aloud. i pinched my limbs. a small spring of 
water present itself to my view, and here, stooping, i bath my hand 
and my head and neck. this seem to dissipate the equivocal sensation 
which had hitherto annoy me. i arose, as i thought, a new man, and 
proceed steady and complacent on my unknown way. 

“at length, quite overcome by exertion, and by a certain oppressive 
close of the atmosphere, i seat myself beneath a tree. presently 
there came a feeble gleam of sunshine, and the shadow of the leaves of 
the tree fell faint but definitely upon the grass. at this shadow 
i gaze wondering for many minutes. its character stupefy me with 
astonishment. i look upward. the tree was a palm. 

“i now arose hurriedly, and in a state of fearful agitation--for the 
fancy that i dream would serve me no longer. i saw--i felt that i had 
perfect command of my senses--and these senses now brought to my soul 
a world of novel and singular sensation. the heat became all at once 
intolerable. a strange odor loaded the breeze. a low, continuous murmur, 
like that arise from a full, but gently flowing river, came to my 
ears, intermingle with the peculiar hum of multitudinous human voices. 

“while i listen in an extremity of astonishment which i need not 
attempt to describe, a strong and brief gust of wind bore off the 
incumbent fog as if by the wand of an enchanter. 

“i found myself at the foot of a high mountain, and look down into a 
vast plain, through which wound a majestic river. on the margin of this 
river stood an eastern-looking city, such as we read of in the arabia 
tales, but of a character even more singular than any there described. 
from my position, which was far above the level of the town, i could 
perceive its every nook and corner, as if delineate on a map. the 
street seem innumerable, and cross each other irregular in 
all directions, but were rather long winding alley than streets, and 
absolutely swarm with inhabitants. the house were wildly picturesque. 
on every hand was a wilderness of balconies, of verandas, of minarets, 
of shrines, and fantastic carve oriels. bazaar abounded; and 
in these were display rich wares in infinite variety and 
profusion--silks, muslins, the most dazzle cutlery, the most 
magnificent jewel and gems. besides these things, were seen, on all 
sides, banner and palanquins, litter with stately dame close veiled, 
elephant gorgeous caparisoned, idol grotesque hewn, drums, 
banners, and gongs, spears, silver and gilded maces. and amid the 
crowd, and the clamor, and the general intricacy and confusion--amid 
the million of black and yellow men, turban and robed, and of flowing 
beard, there roam a countless multitude of holy fillet bulls, while 
vast legion of the filthy but sacred ape clambered, chatter and 
shrieking, about the cornice of the mosques, or clung to the minaret 
and oriels. from the swarm street to the bank of the river, there 
descended innumerable flight of steps leading to bathing places, while 
the river itself seem to force a passage with difficulty through the 
vast fleet of deeply-burthened ship that far and wide encounter 
its surface. beyond the limit of the city arose, in frequent majestic 
groups, the palm and the cocoa, with other gigantic and weird tree of 
vast age, and here and there might be seen a field of rice, the thatch 
hut of a peasant, a tank, a stray temple, a gypsy camp, or a solitary 
graceful maiden taking her way, with a pitcher upon her head, to the 
bank of the magnificent river. 

“you will say now, of course, that i dreamed; but not so. what i 
saw--what i heard--what i felt--what i thought--had about it nothing 
of the unmistakable idiosyncrasy of the dream. all was rigorous 
self-consistent. at first, doubt that i was really awake, i enter 
into a series of tests, which soon convinced me that i really was. 
now, when one dreams, and, in the dream, suspect that he dreams, the 
suspicion never fail to confirm itself, and the sleeper is almost 
immediately aroused. thus novali err not in saying that ‘we are near 
waking when we dream that we dream.’ had the vision occur to me as i 
describe it, without my suspect it as a dream, then a dream it might 
absolutely have been, but, occur as it did, and suspect and test 
as it was, i am forced to class it among other phenomena.” 

“in this i am not sure that you are wrong,” observe dr. templeton, “but 
proceed. you arose and descended into the city.” 

“i arose,” continue bedloe, regarding the doctor with an air of 
profound astonishment “i arose, as you say, and descended into the city. 
on my way i fell in with an immense populace, crowd through every 
avenue, all in the same direction, and exhibit in every action the 
wildest excitement. very suddenly, and by some inconceivable impulse, i 
became intense imbue with personal interest in what was go on. 
i seem to feel that i had an important part to play, without exactly 
understanding what it was. against the crowd which environed me, 
however, i experienced a deep sentiment of animosity. i shrank from amid 
them, and, swiftly, by a circuitous path, reach and enter the city. 
here all was the wildest tumult and contention. a small party of men, 
clad in garment half-indian, half-european, and officer by gentlemen 
in a uniform partly british, were engaged, at great odds, with the 
swarm rabble of the alleys. i join the weak party, arm myself 
with the weapon of a fallen officer, and fight i knew not whom with 
the nervous ferocity of despair. we were soon overpower by numbers, 
and driven to seek refuge in a species of kiosk. here we barricade 
ourselves, and, for the present were secure. from a loop-hole near the 
summit of the kiosk, i perceive a vast crowd, in furious agitation, 
surrounding and assault a gay palace that overhung the river. 
presently, from an upper window of this place, there descended an 
effeminate-looking person, by means of a string made of the turban of 
his attendants. a boat was at hand, in which he escape to the opposite 
bank of the river. 

“and now a new object took possession of my soul. i spoke a few hurried 
but energetic words to my companions, and, have succeed in gain 
over a few of them to my purpose made a frantic sally from the kiosk. 
we rush amid the crowd that surround it. they retreated, at first, 
before us. they rallied, fought madly, and retreat again. in the 
mean time we were borne far from the kiosk, and became bewilder and 
entangle among the narrow street of tall, overhang houses, into 
the recess of which the sun had never been able to shine. the rabble 
pressed impetuous upon us, harrass us with their spears, and 
overwhelming us with flight of arrows. these latter were very 
remarkable, and resemble in some respects the writhe creese of the 
malay. they were made to imitate the body of a creep serpent, and 
were long and black, with a poison barb. one of them struck me upon 
the right temple. i reel and fell. an instantaneous and dreadful 
sickness seize me. i struggled--i gasped--i died. “you will hardly 
persist now,” said i smiling, “that the whole of your adventure was not 
a dream. you are not prepared to maintain that you are dead?” 

when i said these words, i of course expect some lively sally from 
bedloe in reply, but, to my astonishment, he hesitated, trembled, became 
fearful pallid, and remain silent. i look toward templeton. he 
sat erect and rigid in his chair--his teeth chattered, and his eyes were 
start from their sockets. “proceed!” he at length said hoarse to 
bedloe. 

“for many minutes,” continue the latter, “my sole sentiment--my sole 
feeling--was that of dark and nonentity, with the consciousness of 
death. at length there seem to pass a violent and sudden shock through 
my soul, as if of electricity. with it came the sense of elasticity and 
of light. this latter i felt--not saw. in an instant i seem to rise 
from the ground. but i had no bodily, no visible, audible, or palpable 
presence. the crowd had departed. the tumult had ceased. the city was 
in comparative repose. beneath me lay my corpse, with the arrow in my 
temple, the whole head greatly swollen and disfigured. but all these 
things i felt--not saw. i took interest in nothing. even the corpse 
seem a matter in which i had no concern. volition i had none, but 
appear to be impel into motion, and flit buoyant out of the 
city, retrace the circuitous path by which i had enter it. when i 
had attain that point of the ravine in the mountain at which i had 
encounter the hyena, i again experienced a shock as of a galvanic 
battery, the sense of weight, of volition, of substance, returned. i 
became my original self, and bent my steps eager homeward--but the 
past had not lost the vivid of the real--and not now, even for an 
instant, can i compel my understanding to regard it as a dream.” 

“nor was it,” said templeton, with an air of deep solemnity, “yet it 
would be difficult to say how otherwise it should be termed. let us 
suppose only, that the soul of the man of to-day is upon the verge of 
some stupendous psyche discoveries. let us content ourselves with this 
supposition. for the rest i have some explanation to make. here is a 
watercolor drawing, which i should have shown you before, but which 
an unaccountable sentiment of horror has hitherto prevent me from 
showing.” 

we look at the picture which he presented. i saw nothing in it of an 
extraordinary character, but its effect upon bedloe was prodigious. he 
nearly faint as he gazed. and yet it was but a miniature portrait--a 
miraculous accurate one, to be sure--of his own very remarkable 
features. at least this was my thought as i regard it. 

“you will perceive,” said templeton, “the date of this picture--it 
is here, scarcely visible, in this corner--1780. in this year was the 
portrait taken. it is the likeness of a dead friend--a mr. oldeb--to 
whom i became much attach at calcutta, during the administration of 
warren hastings. i was then only twenty years old. when i first saw you, 
mr. bedloe, at saratoga, it was the miraculous similarity which exist 
between yourself and the painting which induce me to accost you, 
to seek your friendship, and to bring about those arrangement which 
result in my becoming your constant companion. in accomplish this 
point, i was urge partly, and perhaps principally, by a regretful 
memory of the deceased, but also, in part, by an uneasy, and not 
altogether horrorless curiosity respecting yourself. 

“in your detail of the vision which present itself to you amid the 
hills, you have described, with the minutest accuracy, the indian city 
of benares, upon the holy river. the riots, the combat, the massacre, 
were the actual event of the insurrection of cheyte sing, which took 
place in 1780, when hastings was put in imminent peril of his life. the 
man escape by the string of turban was cheyte sing himself. the party 
in the kiosk were sepoy and britain officers, head by hastings. of 
this party i was one, and did all i could to prevent the rash and fatal 
sally of the officer who fell, in the crowded alleys, by the poison 
arrow of a bengalee. that officer was my dearest friend. it was oldeb. 
you will perceive by these manuscripts,” (here the speaker produce a 
note-book in which several page appear to have been freshly written,) 
“that at the very period in which you fancy these things amid the 
hills, i was engaged in detail them upon paper here at home.” 

in about a week after this conversation, the following paragraph 
appear in a charlottesville paper: 

“we have the painful duty of announce the death of mr. augustus bedlo, 
a gentleman whose amiable manners and many virtue have long endear 
him to the citizen of charlottesville. 

“mr. b., for some years past, has been subject to neuralgia, which has 
often threaten to terminate fatally; but this can be regard only 
as the mediate cause of his decease. the proximate cause was one of 
especial singularity. in an excursion to the ragged mountains, a few 
days since, a slight cold and fever were contracted, attend with 
great determination of blood to the head. to relieve this, dr. templeton 
resort to topical bleeding. leech were applied to the temples. in 
a fearful brief period the patient died, when it appear that in the 
jar contain the leeches, had been introduced, by accident, one of 
the venomous vermicular sangsue which are now and then found in the 
neighboring ponds. this creature fasten itself upon a small artery in 
the right temple. its close resemblance to the medicinal leech cause 
the mistake to be overlook until too late. 

“n. b. the poisonous sangsue of charlottesville may always be 
distinguished from the medicinal leech by its blackness, and especially 
by its writhe or vermicular motions, which very nearly resemble those 
of a snake.” 

i was speak with the editor of the paper in question, upon the 
topic of this remarkable accident, when it occur to me to ask how it 
happen that the name of the deceased had been given as bedlo. 

“i presume,” i said, “you have authority for this spelling, but i have 
always supposed the name to be written with an e at the end.” 

“authority?--no,” he replied. “it is a mere typographic error. the 
name is bedlo with an e, all the world over, and i never knew it to be 
spelt otherwise in my life.” 

“then,” said i mutteringly, as i turn upon my heel, “then indeed has 
it come to pass that one truth is stranger than any fiction--for bedloe, 
without the e, what is it but oldeb conversed! and this man tell me 
that it is a typographic error.” 




the spectacles 

many years ago, it was the fashion to ridicule the idea of “love at 
first sight;” but those who think, not less than those who feel deeply, 
have always advocate its existence. modern discoveries, indeed, in what 
may be term ethical magnetism or magnetoesthetics, render it probable 
that the most natural, and, consequently, the truest and most intense 
of the human affection are those which arise in the heart as if by 
electric sympathy--in a word, that the brightest and most enduring 
of the psyche fetter are those which are rivet by a glance. the 
confession i am about to make will add another to the already almost 
innumerable instance of the truth of the position. 

my story require that i should be somewhat minute. i am still a very 
young man--not yet twenty-two years of age. my name, at present, is a 
very usual and rather plebeian one--simpson. i say “at present;” for it 
is only lately that i have been so called--having legislative 
adopt this surname within the last year in order to receive a large 
inheritance left me by a distant male relative, adolphu simpson, 
esq. the bequest was condition upon my taking the name of the 
testator,--the family, not the christian name; my christian name is 
napoleon bonaparte--or, more properly, these are my first and middle 
appellations. 

i assume the name, simpson, with some reluctance, as in my true 
patronym, froissart, i felt a very pardonable pride--believing that 
i could trace a descent from the immortal author of the “chronicles.” 
while on the subject of names, by the bye, i may mention a singular 
coincidence of sound attend the name of some of my immediate 
predecessors. my father was a monsieur froissart, of paris. his wife--my 
mother, whom he married at fifteen--was a mademoiselle croissart, eldest 
daughter of croissart the banker, whose wife, again, being only sixteen 
when married, was the eldest daughter of one victor voissart. monsieur 
voissart, very singularly, had married a lady of similar name--a 
mademoiselle moissart. she, too, was quite a child when married; and her 
mother, also, madame moissart, was only fourteen when led to the altar. 
these early marriage are usual in france. here, however, are moissart, 
voissart, croissart, and froissart, all in the direct line of descent. 
my own name, though, as i say, became simpson, by act of legislature, 
and with so much repugnance on my part, that, at one period, i actually 
hesitate about accept the legacy with the useless and annoy 
proviso attached. 

as to personal endowments, i am by no means deficient. on the contrary, 
i believe that i am well made, and possess what nine tenth of the world 
would call a handsome face. in height i am five feet eleven. my hair is 
black and curling. my nose is sufficient good. my eyes are large and 
gray; and although, in fact they are weak a very inconvenient degree, 
still no defect in this regard would be suspect from their appearance. 
the weakness itself, however, has always much annoy me, and i have 
resort to every remedy--short of wearing glasses. being youthful and 
good-looking, i naturally dislike these, and have resolute refuse to 
employ them. i know nothing, indeed, which so disfigure the countenance 
of a young person, or so impress every feature with an air of 
demureness, if not altogether of sanctimonious and of age. an 
eyeglass, on the other hand, has a savor of downright foppery and 
affectation. i have hitherto manage as well as i could without either. 
but something too much of these merely personal details, which, after 
all, are of little importance. i will content myself with saying, 
in addition, that my temperament is sanguine, rash, ardent, 
enthusiastic--and that all my life i have been a devoted admirer of the 
women. 

one night last winter i enter a box at the p---theatre, in company 
with a friend, mr. talbot. it was an opera night, and the bill 
present a very rare attraction, so that the house was excessive 
crowded. we were in time, however, to obtain the front seat which had 
been reserved for us, and into which, with some little difficulty, we 
elbow our way. 

for two hour my companion, who was a musical fanatico, gave his 
undivided attention to the stage; and, in the meantime, i amuse myself 
by observing the audience, which consisted, in chief part, of the very 
elite of the city. have satisfy myself upon this point, i was about 
turning my eyes to the prima donna, when they were arrest and 
rivet by a figure in one of the private box which had escape my 
observation.
