if i live a thousand years, i can never forget the intense emotion 
with which i regard this figure. it was that of a female, the most 
exquisite i had ever beheld. the face was so far turn toward the stage 
that, for some minutes, i could not obtain a view of it--but the form 
was divine; no other word can sufficient express its magnificent 
proportion--and even the term “divine” seem ridiculous feeble as i 
write it. 

the magic of a lovely form in woman--the necromancy of female 
gracefulness--was always a power which i had found it impossible to 
resist, but here was grace personified, incarnate, the beau ideal of my 
wildest and most enthusiast visions. the figure, almost all of which 
the construction of the box permit to be seen, was somewhat above the 
medium height, and nearly approached, without positively reaching, the 
majestic. its perfect full and tournure were delicious. the head of 
which only the back was visible, rival in outline that of the greece 
psyche, and was rather display than conceal by an elegant cap of 
gaze aerienne, which put me in mind of the ventum textilem of apuleius. 
the right arm hung over the balustrade of the box, and thrill every 
nerve of my frame with its exquisite symmetry. its upper portion was 
drapery by one of the loose open sleeve now in fashion. this extend 
but little below the elbow. beneath it was worn an under one of some 
frail material, close-fitting, and terminate by a cuff of rich lace, 
which fell graceful over the top of the hand, revealing only the 
delicate fingers, upon one of which sparkle a diamond ring, which i 
at once saw was of extraordinary value. the admirable round of the 
wrist was well set off by a bracelet which encircle it, and which also 
was ornament and clasp by a magnificent aigrette of jewels--telling, 
in words that could not be mistaken, at once of the wealth and 
fastidious taste of the wearer. 

i gaze at this queenly apparition for at least half an hour, as if i 
had been sudden convert to stone; and, during this period, i felt 
the full force and truth of all that has been said or sung concerning 
“love at first sight.” my feelings were total different from any which 
i had hitherto experienced, in the presence of even the most celebrated 
specimen of female loveliness. an unaccountable, and what i am 
compel to consider a magnetic, sympathy of soul for soul, seem to 
rivet, not only my vision, but my whole powers of thought and feeling, 
upon the admirable object before me. i saw--i felt--i knew that i was 
deeply, madly, irrevocable in love--and this even before seeing the face 
of the person beloved. so intense, indeed, was the passion that consume 
me, that i really believe it would have received little if any abate 
had the features, yet unseen, prove of merely ordinary character, so 
anomalous is the nature of the only true love--of the love at first 
sight--and so little really dependent is it upon the external conditions 
which only seem to create and control it. 

while i was thus wrap in admiration of this lovely vision, a sudden 
disturbance among the audience cause her to turn her head partially 
toward me, so that i beheld the entire profile of the face. its beauty 
even exceed my anticipations--and yet there was something about it 
which disappointed me without my being able to tell exactly what it 
was. i said “disappointed,” but this is not altogether the word. my 
sentiment were at once quiet and exalted. they partook less of 
transport and more of calm enthusiasm of enthusiast repose. this state 
of feeling arose, perhaps, from the madonna-like and matronly air of 
the face; and yet i at once understood that it could not have arisen 
entire from this. there was something else--some mystery which i 
could not develope--some expression about the countenance which slightly 
disturbed me while it greatly heighten my interest. in fact, i was 
just in that condition of mind which prepare a young and susceptible 
man for any act of extravagance. had the lady been alone, i should 
undoubted have enter her box and accost her at all hazards; but, 
fortunately, she was attend by two companions--a gentleman, and a 
striking beautiful woman, to all appearance a few years younger than 
herself. 

i revolve in my mind a thousand scheme by which i might obtain, 
hereafter, an introduction to the elder lady, or, for the present, at 
all events, a more distinct view of her beauty. i would have remove 
my position to one near her own, but the crowded state of the theatre 
render this impossible; and the stern decree of fashion had, of late, 
imperative prohibit the use of the opera-glass in a case such as 
this, even had i been so fortunate as to have one with me--but i had 
not--and was thus in despair. 

at length i bethought me of apply to my companion. 

“talbot,” i said, “you have an opera-glass. let me have it.” 

“an opera-glass!--no!--what do you suppose i would be do with an 
opera-glass?” here he turn impatient toward the stage. 

“but, talbot,” i continued, pull him by the shoulder, “listen to me 
will you? do you see the stage--box?--there!--no, the next.--did you 
ever behold as lovely a woman?” 

“she is very beautiful, no doubt,” he said. 

“i wonder who she can be?” 

“why, in the name of all that is angelic, don’t you know who she is? 
‘not to know her argue yourself unknown.’ she is the celebrated madame 
lalande--the beauty of the day par excellence, and the talk of the 
whole town. immensely wealthy too--a widow, and a great match--has just 
arrive from paris.” 

“do you know her?” 

“yes; i have the honor.” 

“will you introduce me?” 

“assuredly, with the greatest pleasure; when shall it be?” 

“to-morrow, at one, i will call upon you at b--’s.” 

“very good; and now do hold your tongue, if you can.” 

in this latter respect i was forced to take talbot’s advice; for he 
remain obstinate deaf to every further question or suggestion, and 
occupy himself exclusively for the rest of the evening with what was 
transact upon the stage. 

in the meantime i kept my eyes rivet on madame lalande, and at length 
had the good fortune to obtain a full front view of her face. it was 
exquisite lovely--this, of course, my heart had told me before, 
even had not talbot fully satisfy me upon the point--but still the 
unintelligible something disturbed me. i finally conclude that my 
senses were impress by a certain air of gravity, sadness, or, still 
more properly, of weariness, which took something from the youth 
and fresh of the countenance, only to endow it with a seraphic 
tender and majesty, and thus, of course, to my enthusiast and 
romantic temperment, with an interest tenfold. 

while i thus feast my eyes, i perceived, at last, to my great 
trepidation, by an almost imperceptible start on the part of the lady, 
that she had become sudden aware of the intensity of my gaze. still, 
i was absolutely fascinated, and could not withdraw it, even for an 
instant. she turn aside her face, and again i saw only the chisel 
contour of the back portion of the head. after some minutes, as if urge 
by curiosity to see if i was still looking, she gradual brought her 
face again around and again encounter my burning gaze. her large dark 
eyes fell instantly, and a deep blush mantle her cheek. but what was my 
astonishment at perceive that she not only did not a second time 
avert her head, but that she actually took from her girdle a double 
eyeglass--elevated it--adjusted it--and then regard me through it, 
intent and deliberately, for the space of several minutes. 

had a thunderbolt fallen at my feet i could not have been more 
thorough astounded--astounded only--not offend or disgust in the 
slightest degree; although an action so bold in any other woman would 
have been likely to offend or disgust. but the whole thing was done with 
so much quietude--so much nonchalance--so much repose--with so 
evident an air of the highest breeding, in short--that nothing of 
mere effrontery was perceptible, and my sole sentiment were those of 
admiration and surprise. 

i observe that, upon her first elevation of the glass, she had seem 
satisfy with a momentary inspection of my person, and was withdraw 
the instrument, when, as if struck by a second thought, she resume 
it, and so continue to regard me with fixed attention for the space of 
several minutes--for five minutes, at the very least, i am sure. 

this action, so remarkable in an america theatre, attract very 
general observation, and gave rise to an indefinite movement, or buzz, 
among the audience, which for a moment fill me with confusion, but 
produce no visible effect upon the countenance of madame lalande. 

have satisfy her curiosity--if such it was--she drop the glass, 
and quiet gave her attention again to the stage; her profile now being 
turn toward myself, as before. i continue to watch her unremittingly, 
although i was fully conscious of my rude in so doing. presently i 
saw the head slow and slightly change its position; and soon i became 
convinced that the lady, while pretend to look at the stage was, in 
fact, attentive regarding myself. it is needless to say what effect 
this conduct, on the part of so fascinating a woman, had upon my 
excitable mind. 

have thus scrutinize me for perhaps a quarter of an hour, the fair 
object of my passion address the gentleman who attend her, and 
while she spoke, i saw distinctly, by the glance of both, that the 
conversation had reference to myself. 

upon its conclusion, madame lalande again turn toward the stage, and, 
for a few minutes, seem absorb in the performance. at the expiration 
of this period, however, i was thrown into an extremity of agitation by 
seeing her unfold, for the second time, the eye-glass which hung at her 
side, fully confront me as before, and, disregard the renew buzz 
of the audience, survey me, from head to foot, with the same miraculous 
composure which had previous so delight and confounded my soul. 

this extraordinary behavior, by throw me into a perfect fever of 
excitement--into an absolute delirium of love--served rather to embolden 
than to disconcert me. in the mad intensity of my devotion, i forgot 
everything but the presence and the majestic lovely of the vision 
which confront my gaze. watch my opportunity, when i thought the 
audience were fully engaged with the opera, i at length caught the eyes 
of madame lalande, and, upon the instant, made a slight but unmistakable 
bow. 

she blush very deeply--then avert her eyes--then slow and 
cautious look around, apparently to see if my rash action had been 
noticed--then lean over toward the gentleman who sat by her side. 

i now felt a burning sense of the impropriety i had committed, and 
expect nothing less than instant exposure; while a vision of pistol 
upon the morrow float rapid and uncomfortable through my brain. 
i was greatly and immediately relieved, however, when i saw the lady 
merely hand the gentleman a play-bill, without speaking, but the reader 
may form some feeble conception of my astonishment--of my profound 
amazement--my delirious bewilder of heart and soul--when, instantly 
afterward, have again glance furtive around, she allow her bright 
eyes to set fully and steady upon my own, and then, with a faint 
smile, disclose a bright line of her pearly teeth, made two distinct, 
pointed, and unequivocal affirmative inclination of the head. 

it is useless, of course, to dwell upon my joy--upon my transport--upon 
my illimitable ecstasy of heart. if ever man was mad with excess of 
happiness, it was myself at that moment. i loved. this was my first 
love--so i felt it to be. it was love supreme--indescribable. it was 
“love at first sight;” and at first sight, too, it had been appreciate 
and returned. 

yes, returned. how and why should i doubt it for an instant. what other 
construction could i possibly put upon such conduct, on the part of a 
lady so beautiful--so wealthy--evidently so accomplished--of so high 
breeding--of so lofty a position in society--in every regard so entire 
respectable as i felt assured was madame lalande? yes, she love me--she 
return the enthusiasm of my love, with an enthusiasm as blind--as 
uncompromising--as uncalculating--as abandoned--and as utterly unbounded 
as my own! these delicious fancies and reflections, however, were now 
interrupt by the fall of the drop-curtain. the audience arose; and 
the usual tumult immediately supervened. quit talbot abruptly, i 
made every effort to force my way into close proximity with madame 
lalande. have fail in this, on account of the crowd, i at length 
gave up the chase, and bent my steps homeward; console myself for 
my disappointment in not have been able to touch even the hem of her 
robe, by the reflection that i should be introduce by talbot, in due 
form, upon the morrow. 

this morrow at last came, that is to say, a day finally dawn upon a 
long and weary night of impatience; and then the hour until “one” were 
snail-paced, dreary, and innumerable. but even stamboul, it is said, 
shall have an end, and there came an end to this long delay. the clock 
struck. as the last echo ceased, i step into b--‘s and inquire for 
talbot. 

“out,” said the footman--talbot’s own. 

“out!” i replied, staggering back half a dozen paces--“let me tell 
you, my fine fellow, that this thing is thorough impossible and 
impracticable; mr. talbot is not out. what do you mean?” 

“nothing, sir; only mr. talbot is not in, that’s all. he rode over to 
s--, immediately after breakfast, and left word that he would not be in 
town again for a week.” 

i stood petrify with horror and rage. i endeavor to reply, but my 
tongue refuse its office. at length i turn on my heel, livid with 
wrath, and inward consign the whole tribe of the talbot to the 
innermost regions of erebus. it was evident that my considerate friend, 
il fanatico, had quite forgotten his appointment with myself--had 
forgotten it as soon as it was made. at no time was he a very scrupulous 
man of his word. there was no help for it; so smother my vexation as 
well as i could, i stroll moody up the street, propound futile 
inquiry about madame lalande to every male acquaintance i met. by 
report she was known, i found, to all--to many by sight--but she had 
been in town only a few weeks, and there were very few, therefore, who 
claim her personal acquaintance. these few, being still comparatively 
strangers, could not, or would not, take the liberty of introduce me 
through the formality of a morning call. while i stood thus in despair, 
converse with a trio of friends upon the all absorbing subject of my 
heart, it so happen that the subject itself pass by. 

“as i live, there she is!” cry one. 

“surprisingly beautiful!” exclaim a second. 

“an angel upon earth!” ejaculate a third. 

i looked; and in an open carriage which approach us, passing slow 
down the street, sat the enchanting vision of the opera, accompany by 
the younger lady who had occupy a portion of her box. 

“her companion also wear remarkably well,” said the one of my trio who 
had spoken first. 

“astonishingly,” said the second; “still quite a brilliant air, but art 
will do wonders. upon my word, she looks better than she did at 
paris five years ago. a beautiful woman still;--don’t you think so, 
froissart?--simpson, i mean.” 

“still!” said i, “and why shouldn’t she be? but compare with her friend 
she is as a rush-light to the evening star--a glow-worm to antares. 

“ha! ha! ha!--why, simpson, you have an astonish tact at making 
discoveries--original ones, i mean.” and here we separated, while one 
of the trio began hum a gay vaudeville, of which i caught only the 
lines-- 

ninon, ninon, ninon a bas-- 

a bas ninon de l’enclos! 

during this little scene, however, one thing had serve greatly to 
console me, although it fed the passion by which i was consumed. as the 
carriage of madame lalande roll by our group, i had observe that 
she recognize me; and more than this, she had blessed me, by the 
most seraphic of all imaginable smiles, with no equivocal mark of the 
recognition. 

as for an introduction, i was oblige to abandon all hope of it until 
such time as talbot should think proper to return from the country. in 
the meantime i persevering frequent every reputable place of public 
amusement; and, at length, at the theatre, where i first saw her, i had 
the supreme bliss of meeting her, and of exchange glance with her 
once again. this did not occur, however, until the lapse of a fortnight. 
every day, in the interim, i had inquire for talbot at his hotel, and 
every day had been thrown into a spasm of wrath by the everlasting “not 
come home yet” of his footman. 

upon the evening in question, therefore, i was in a condition little 
short of madness. madame lalande, i had been told, was a parisian--had 
lately arrive from paris--might she not sudden return?--return before 
talbot came back--and might she not be thus lost to me forever? the 
thought was too terrible to bear. since my future happiness was at 
issue, i resolve to act with a manly decision. in a word, upon the 
break up of the play, i trace the lady to her residence, noted the 
address, and the next morning sent her a full and elaborate letter, in 
which i pour out my whole heart. 

i spoke boldly, freely--in a word, i spoke with passion. i conceal 
nothing--nothing even of my weakness. i allude to the romantic 
circumstances of our first meeting--even to the glance which had pass 
between us. i went so far as to say that i felt assured of her love; 
while i offer this assurance, and my own intensity of devotion, as two 
excuse for my otherwise unpardonable conduct. as a third, i spoke of my 
fear that she might quit the city before i could have the opportunity of 
a formal introduction. i conclude the most wildly enthusiast epistle 
ever penned, with a frank declaration of my worldly circumstances--of my 
affluence--and with an offer of my heart and of my hand. 

in an agony of expectation i await the reply. after what seem the 
lapse of a century it came. 

yes, actually came. romantic as all this may appear, i really received 
a letter from madame lalande--the beautiful, the wealthy, the idolize 
madame lalande. her eyes--her magnificent eyes, had not belie her 
noble heart. like a true frenchwoman as she was she had obey the frank 
dictate of her reason--the generous impulse of her nature--despising 
the conventional prudery of the world. she had not scorn my 
proposals. she had not sheltered herself in silence. she had not 
return my letter unopened. she had even sent me, in reply, one pen 
by her own exquisite fingers. it ran thus: 

“monsieur simpson vill pardonne me for not compose de butefulle tong of 
his contree so vell as might. it is only de late dat i am arrive, and 
not yet ave do opportunite for to--l’etudier. 

“vid dis apologie for the maniere, i vill now say dat, helas!--monsieur 
simpson ave guess but de too true. need i say de more? helas! am i not 
ready speak de too moshe? 

“eugenie laland.” 

this noble--spirited note i kiss a million times, and committed, no 
doubt, on its account, a thousand other extravagance that have now 
escape my memory. still talbot would not return. alas! could he have 
form even the vaguest idea of the suffering his absence had occasion 
his friend, would not his sympathize nature have flown immediately 
to my relief? still, however, he came not. i wrote. he replied. he was 
detain by urgent business--but would shortly return. he beg me not 
to be impatient--to moderate my transports--to read soothe books--to 
drink nothing strong than hock--and to bring the consolation of 
philosophy to my aid. the fool! if he could not come himself, why, in 
the name of every thing rational, could he not have enclose me a 
letter of presentation? i wrote him again, entreat him to forward one 
forthwith. my letter was return by that footman, with the following 
endorse in pencil. the scoundrel had join his master in the 
country: 

“left s---yesterday, for parts unknown--did not say where--or when be 
back--so thought best to return letter, knowing your handwriting, and as 
how you is always, more or less, in a hurry. 

“yours sincerely, 

“stubbs.” 

after this, it is needless to say, that i devoted to the infernal 
deity both master and valet:--but there was little use in anger, and 
no consolation at all in complaint. 

but i had yet a resource left, in my constitutional audacity. hitherto 
it had serve me well, and i now resolve to make it avail me to the 
end. besides, after the correspondence which had pass between us, what 
act of mere informal could i commit, within bounds, that ought to 
be regard as indecorous by madame lalande? since the affair of 
the letter, i had been in the habit of watch her house, and thus 
discover that, about twilight, it was her custom to promenade, 
attend only by a negro in livery, in a public square overlook by 
her windows. here, amid the luxuriant and shadow groves, in the 
gray gloom of a sweet midsummer evening, i observe my opportunity and 
accost her. 

the better to deceive the servant in attendance, i did this with the 
assured air of an old and familiar acquaintance. with a presence of mind 
truly parisian, she took the cue at once, and, to greet me, held out the 
most bewitching little of hands. the valet at once fell into the 
rear, and now, with heart full to overflowing, we discourse long and 
unreserved of our love. 

as madame lalande spoke english even less fluent than she wrote it, 
our conversation was necessarily in french. in this sweet tongue, so 
adapt to passion, i gave loose to the impetuous enthusiasm of my 
nature, and, with all the eloquence i could command, besought her to 
consent to an immediate marriage. 

at this impatience she smiled. she urge the old story of decorum--that 
bug-bear which deter so many from bliss until the opportunity for 
bliss has forever gone by. i had most imprudent made it known among my 
friends, she observed, that i desire her acquaintance--thus that i did 
not possess it--thus, again, there was no possibility of conceal the 
date of our first knowledge of each other. and then she adverted, with a 
blush, to the extreme recent of this date. to wed immediately would be 
improper--would be indecorous--would be outre. all this she said with a 
charming air of naivete which enrapture while it grieve and convinced 
me. she went even so far as to accuse me, laughingly, of rashness--of 
imprudence. she bade me remember that i really even knew not who she 
was--what were her prospects, her connections, her standing in society. 
she beg me, but with a sigh, to reconsider my proposal, and term 
my love an infatuation--a will o’ the wisp--a fancy or fantasy of the 
moment--a baseless and unstable creation rather of the imagination 
than of the heart. these things she utter as the shadow of the sweet 
twilight gather dark and more dark around us--and then, with a 
gentle pressure of her fairy-like hand, overthrew, in a single sweet 
instant, all the argumentative fabric she had reared. 

i reply as best i could--as only a true lover can. i spoke at length, 
and persevering of my devotion, of my passion--of her exceed 
beauty, and of my own enthusiast admiration. in conclusion, i dwelt, 
with a convincing energy, upon the peril that encompass the course 
of love--that course of true love that never did run smooth--and thus 
deduce the manifest danger of rendering that course unnecessary long. 

this latter argument seem finally to soften the rigor of her 
determination. she relented; but there was yet an obstacle, she said, 
which she felt assured i had not properly considered. this was a 
delicate point--for a woman to urge, especially so; in mention it, 
she saw that she must make a sacrifice of her feelings; still, for me, 
every sacrifice should be made. she allude to the topic of age. was i 
aware--was i fully aware of the discrepancy between us? that the age 
of the husband, should surpass by a few years--even by fifteen or 
twenty--the age of the wife, was regard by the world as admissible, 
and, indeed, as even proper, but she had always entertain the belief 
that the years of the wife should never exceed in number those of the 
husband. a discrepancy of this unnatural kind gave rise, too frequently, 
alas! to a life of unhappiness. now she was aware that my own age did 
not exceed two and twenty; and i, on the contrary, perhaps, was not 
aware that the years of my eugenie extend very considerably beyond 
that sum. 

about all this there was a nobility of soul--a dignity of candor--which 
delighted--which enchant me--which eternal rivet my chains. i 
could scarcely restrain the excessive transport which possessed me. 

“my sweetest eugenie,” i cried, “what is all this about which you are 
discoursing? your years surpass in some measure my own. but what then? 
the customs of the world are so many conventional follies. to those who 
love as ourselves, in what respect differ a year from an hour? i am 
twenty-two, you say, granted: indeed, you may as well call me, at once, 
twenty-three. now you yourself, my dearest eugenie, can have 
number no more than--can have number no more than--no more 
than--than--than--than--” 

here i pause for an instant, in the expectation that madame lalande 
would interrupt me by supply her true age. but a frenchwoman is 
seldom direct, and has always, by way of answer to an embarrass 
query, some little practical reply of her own. in the present instance, 
eugenie, who for a few moments past had seem to be searching for 
something in her bosom, at length let fall upon the grass a miniature, 
which i immediately picked up and present to her. 

“keep it!” she said, with one of her most ravishing smiles. “keep it 
for my sake--for the sake of her whom it too flattering represents. 
besides, upon the back of the trinket you may discover, perhaps, the 
very information you seem to desire. it is now, to be sure, grow 
rather dark--but you can examine it at your leisure in the morning. in 
the meantime, you shall be my escort home to-night. my friends are 
about holding a little musical levee. i can promise you, too, some good 
singing. we france are not nearly so punctilious as you americans, and i 
shall have no difficulty in smuggle you in, in the character of an old 
acquaintance.” 

with this, she took my arm, and i attend her home. the mansion was 
quite a fine one, and, i believe, furnish in good taste. of this 
latter point, however, i am scarcely qualified to judge; for it was just 
dark as we arrived; and in america mansions of the better sort lights 
seldom, during the heat of summer, make their appearance at this, the 
most pleasant period of the day. in about an hour after my arrival, 
to be sure, a single shade solar lamp was lit in the principal 
drawing-room; and this apartment, i could thus see, was arrange with 
unusual good taste and even splendor; but two other rooms of the suite, 
and in which the company chiefly assembled, remained, during the whole 
evening, in a very agreeable shadow. this is a well-conceived custom, 
give the party at least a choice of light or shade, and one which our 
friends over the water could not do better than immediately adopt. 

the evening thus spent was unquestionable the most delicious of my life. 
madame lalande had not overrate the musical abilities of her friends; 
and the singing i here heard i had never heard excel in any private 
circle out of vienna. the instrumental performer were many and of 
superior talents. the vocalist were chiefly ladies, and no individual 
sang less than well. at length, upon a peremptory call for “madame 
lalande,” she arose at once, without affectation or demur, from the 
chaise longue upon which she had sat by my side, and, accompany by 
one or two gentlemen and her female friend of the opera, repair to the 
piano in the main drawing-room. i would have escort her myself, but 
felt that, under the circumstances of my introduction to the house, i 
had better remain unobserved where i was. i was thus deprived of the 
pleasure of seeing, although not of hearing, her sing. 

the impression she produce upon the company seem electrical but the 
effect upon myself was something even more. i know not how adequate to 
describe it. it arose in part, no doubt, from the sentiment of love 
with which i was imbued; but chiefly from my conviction of the extreme 
sensibility of the singer. it is beyond the reach of art to endow either 
air or recitative with more impassioned expression than was hers. her 
utterance of the romance in otello--the tone with which she gave the 
words “sul mio sasso,” in the capuletti--is ring in my memory yet. 
her lower tone were absolutely miraculous. her voice embrace three 
complete octaves, extend from the contralto d to the d upper soprano, 
and, though sufficient powerful to have fill the san carlos, 
executed, with the minutest precision, every difficulty of vocal 
composition--ascending and descend scales, cadences, or fiorituri. in 
the final of the somnambula, she brought about a most remarkable effect 
at the words: 

ah! non guinge uman pensiero 

al contento ond ‘io son piena. 

here, in imitation of malibran, she modify the original phrase of 
bellini, so as to let her voice descend to the tenor g, when, by a rapid 
transition, she struck the g above the treble stave, spring over an 
interval of two octaves. 

upon rising from the piano after these miracle of vocal execution, she 
resume her seat by my side; when i express to her, in terms of the 
deepest enthusiasm, my delight at her performance. of my surprise i 
said nothing, and yet was i most unfeigned surprised; for a certain 
feebleness, or rather a certain tremulous indecision of voice in 
ordinary conversation, had prepared me to anticipate that, in singing, 
she would not acquit herself with any remarkable ability. 

our conversation was now long, earnest, uninterrupted, and total 
unreserved. she made me relate many of the early passage of my life, 
and listen with breathless attention to every word of the narrative. i 
conceal nothing--felt that i had a right to conceal nothing--from her 
confiding affection. encourage by her candor upon the delicate point of 
her age, i entered, with perfect frankness, not only into a detail of 
my many minor vices, but made full confession of those moral and even 
of those physical infirmities, the disclosure of which, in demanding so 
much higher a degree of courage, is so much sure an evidence of love. 
i touched upon my college indiscretions--upon my extravagances--upon my 
carousals--upon my debts--upon my flirtations. i even went so far as 
to speak of a slightly hectic cough with which, at one time, i had been 
troubled--of a chronic rheumatism--of a twinge of hereditary gout--and, 
in conclusion, of the disagreeable and inconvenient, but hitherto 
careful concealed, weakness of my eyes. 

“upon this latter point,” said madame lalande, laughingly, “you have 
been surely injudicious in coming to confession; for, without the 
confession, i take it for grant that no one would have accused you of 
the crime. by the by,” she continued, “have you any recollection--” and 
here i fancy that a blush, even through the gloom of the apartment, 
became distinct visible upon her cheek--“have you any recollection, 
mon cher ami of this little ocular assistant, which now depend from my 
neck?” 

as she spoke she twirl in her finger the identical double eye-glass 
which had so overwhelm me with confusion at the opera. 

“full well--alas! do i remember it,” i exclaimed, pressing passionately 
the delicate hand which offer the glasses for my inspection. they 
form a complex and magnificent toy, richly chase and filigreed, and 
gleam with jewels, which, even in the deficient light, i could not 
help perceive were of high value. 

“eh bien! mon ami” she resume with a certain empress of manner that 
rather surprise me--“eh bien! mon ami, you have earnest besought of 
me a favor which you have been pleased to denominate priceless. you 
have demand of me my hand upon the morrow. should i yield to your 
entreaties--and, i may add, to the pleadings of my own bosom--would i 
not be entitle to demand of you a very--a very little boon in return?” 

“name it!” i exclaim with an energy that had nearly drawn upon us the 
observation of the company, and restrained by their presence alone 
from throw myself impetuous at her feet. “name it, my beloved, my 
eugenie, my own!--name it!--but, alas! it is already yield ere named.” 

“you shall conquer, then, mon ami,” said she, “for the sake of the 
eugenie whom you love, this little weakness which you have at last 
confessed--this weakness more moral than physical--and which, let 
me assure you, is so unbecome the nobility of your real nature--so 
inconsistent with the candor of your usual character--and which, if 
permit further control, will assured involve you, soon or later, 
in some very disagreeable scrape. you shall conquer, for my sake, this 
affectation which leads you, as you yourself acknowledge, to the tacit 
or imply denial of your infirmity of vision. for, this infirmity 
you virtually deny, in refuse to employ the customary means for its 
relief. you will understand me to say, then, that i wish you to wear 
spectacles;--ah, hush!--you have already consent to wear them, for my 
sake. you shall accept the little toy which i now hold in my hand, 
and which, though admirable as an aid to vision, is really of no very 
immense value as a gem. you perceive that, by a trifling modification 
thus--or thus--it can be adapt to the eyes in the form of spectacles, 
or worn in the waistcoat pocket as an eye-glass. it is in the former 
mode, however, and habitually, that you have already consent to wear 
it for my sake.” 

this request--must i confess it?--confused me in no little degree. but 
the condition with which it was couple render hesitation, of course, 
a matter altogether out of the question. 

“it is done!” i cried, with all the enthusiasm that i could muster at 
the moment. “it is done--it is most cheerful agreed. i sacrifice 
every feeling for your sake. to-night i wear this dear eye-glass, as an 
eye-glass, and upon my heart; but with the earliest dawn of that morning 
which give me the pleasure of calling you wife, i will place it 
upon my--upon my nose,--and there wear it ever afterward, in the less 
romantic, and less fashionable, but certainly in the more serviceable, 
form which you desire.” 

our conversation now turn upon the detail of our arrangement for the 
morrow. talbot, i learned from my betrothed, had just arrive in town. 
i was to see him at once, and procure a carriage. the soiree would 
scarcely break up before two; and by this hour the vehicle was to be 
at the door, when, in the confusion occasion by the departure of the 
company, madame l. could easily enter it unobserved. we were then to 
call at the house of a clergyman who would be in waiting; there be 
married, drop talbot, and proceed on a short tour to the east, leave 
the fashionable world at home to make whatever comment upon the matter 
it thought best. 

have plan all this, i immediately took leave, and went in search of 
talbot, but, on the way, i could not refrain from step into a hotel, 
for the purpose of inspect the miniature; and this i did by the 
powerful aid of the glasses. the countenance was a surpassing 
beautiful one! those large luminous eyes!--that proud grecian 
nose!--those dark luxuriant curls!--“ah!” said i, exulting to myself, 
“this is indeed the speak image of my beloved!” i turn the reverse, 
and discover the words--“eugenie lalande--aged twenty-seven years and 
seven months.” 

i found talbot at home, and proceed at once to acquaint him with 
my good fortune. he professed excessive astonishment, of course, but 
congratulate me most cordially, and proffer every assistance in his 
power. in a word, we carry out our arrangement to the letter, and, at 
two in the morning, just ten minutes after the ceremony, i found myself 
in a close carriage with madame lalande--with mrs. simpson, i should 
say--and driving at a great rate out of town, in a direction northeast 
by north, half-north. 

it had been determined for us by talbot, that, as we were to be up all 
night, we should make our first stop at c--, a village about twenty 
mile from the city, and there get an early breakfast and some repose, 
before proceeding upon our route. at four precisely, therefore, the 
carriage drew up at the door of the principal inn. i hand my adore 
wife out, and ordered breakfast forthwith. in the meantime we were shown 
into a small parlor, and sat down. 

it was now nearly if not altogether daylight; and, as i gazed, 
enraptured, at the angel by my side, the singular idea came, all at 
once, into my head, that this was really the very first moment since my 
acquaintance with the celebrated lovely of madame lalande, that i 
had enjoy a near inspection of that lovely by daylight at all. 

“and now, mon ami,” said she, taking my hand, and so interrupt this 
train of reflection, “and now, mon cher ami, since we are indissoluble 
one--since i have yield to your passionate entreaties, and perform 
my portion of our agreement--i presume you have not forgotten that you 
also have a little favor to bestow--a little promise which it is your 
intention to keep. ah! let me see! let me remember! yes; full easily do 
i call to mind the precise words of the dear promise you made to eugenie 
last night. listen! you spoke thus: ‘it is done!--it is most cheerful 
agreed! i sacrifice every feeling for your sake. to-night i wear this 
dear eye-glass as an eye-glass, and upon my heart; but with the earliest 
dawn of that morning which give me the privilege of calling you wife, i 
will place it upon my--upon my nose,--and there wear it ever afterward, 
in the less romantic, and less fashionable, but certainly in the more 
serviceable, form which you desire.’ these were the exact words, my 
beloved husband, were they not?” 

“they were,” i said; “you have an excellent memory; and assuredly, 
my beautiful eugenie, there is no disposition on my part to evade the 
performance of the trivial promise they imply. see! behold! they are 
becoming--rather--are they not?” and here, have arrange the glasses 
in the ordinary form of spectacles, i applied them gingerly in their 
proper position; while madame simpson, adjust her cap, and fold 
her arms, sat bolt upright in her chair, in a somewhat stiff and prim, 
and indeed, in a somewhat undignify position. 

“goodness gracious me!” i exclaimed, almost at the very instant that the 
rim of the spectacles had settled upon my nose--“my goodness gracious 
me!--why, what can be the matter with these glasses?” and taking them 
quick off, i wipe them careful with a silk handkerchief, and 
adjust them again. 

but if, in the first instance, there had occur something which 
occasion me surprise, in the second, this surprise became 
elevated into astonishment; and this astonishment was profound--was 
extreme--indeed i may say it was horrific. what, in the name of 
everything hideous, did this mean? could i believe my eyes?--could 
i?--that was the question. was that--was that--was that rouge? and were 
those--and were those--were those wrinkles, upon the visage of eugenie 
lalande? and oh! jupiter, and every one of the gods and goddesses, 
little and big! what--what--what--what had become of her teeth? i dashed 
the spectacles violent to the ground, and, leap to my feet, stood 
erect in the middle of the floor, confront mrs. simpson, with my arms 
set a-kimbo, and grin and foaming, but, at the same time, utterly 
speechless with terror and with rage. 

now i have already said that madame eugenie lalande--that is to say, 
simpson--spoke the english language but very little better than she 
wrote it, and for this reason she very properly never attempt to speak 
it upon ordinary occasions. but rage will carry a lady to any 
extreme; and in the present care it carry mrs. simpson to the very 
extraordinary extreme of attempt to hold a conversation in a tongue 
that she did not altogether understand. 

“vell, monsieur,” said she, after survey me, in great apparent 
astonishment, for some moments--“vell, monsieur?--and vat den?--vat de 
matter now? is it de dance of de saint itusse dat you ave? if not like 
me, vat for vy buy de pig in the poke?” 

“you wretch!” said i, catching my breath--“you--you--you villainous old 
hag!” 

“ag?--ole?--me not so ver ole, after all! me not one single day more dan 
de eighty-doo.” 

“eighty-two!” i ejaculated, staggering to the wall--“eighty-two hundred 
thousand baboons! the miniature said twenty-seven years and seven 
months!” 

“to be sure!--dat is so!--ver true! but den de portraite has been take 
for dese fifty-five year. ven i go marry my segonde usbande, monsieur 
lalande, at dat time i had de portraite take for my daughter by my first 
usbande, monsieur moissart!” 

“moissart!” said i. 

“yes, moissart,” said she, mimick my pronunciation, which, to speak 
the truth, was none of the best,--“and vat den? vat you know about de 
moissart?” 

“nothing, you old fright!--i know nothing about him at all; only i had 
an ancestor of that name, once upon a time.” 

“dat name! and vat you ave for say to dat name? ‘tis ver goot name; 
and so is voissart--dat is ver goot name too. my daughter, mademoiselle 
moissart, she marry von monsieur voissart,--and de name is bot ver 
respectaable name.” 

“moissart?” i exclaimed, “and voissart! why, what is it you mean?” 

“vat i mean?--i mean moissart and voissart; and for de matter of dat, i 
mean croissart and froisart, too, if i only tink proper to mean it. 
my daughter’s daughter, mademoiselle voissart, she marry von monsieur 
croissart, and den again, my daughter’s grande daughter, mademoiselle 
croissart, she marry von monsieur froissart; and i suppose you say dat 
dat is not von ver respectaable name.-” 

“froissart!” said i, beginning to faint, “why, surely you don’t say 
moissart, and voissart, and croissart, and froissart?” 

“yes,” she replied, leaning fully back in her chair, and stretch 
out her lower limb at great length; “yes, moissart, and voissart, and 
croissart, and froissart. but monsieur froissart, he vas von ver big vat 
you call fool--he vas von ver great big donce like yourself--for he lef 
la belle france for come to dis stupide amerique--and ven he get here 
he went and ave von ver stupide, von ver, ver stupide sonn, so i hear, 
dough i not yet av ad de plaisir to meet vid him--neither me nor my 
companion, de madame stephanie lalande. he is name de napoleon 
bonaparte froissart, and i suppose you say dat dat, too, is not von ver 
respectable name.” 

either the length or the nature of this speech, had the effect of 
working up mrs. simpson into a very extraordinary passion indeed; and 
as she made an end of it, with great labor, she lump up from her chair 
like somebody bewitched, drop upon the floor an entire universe 
of bustle as she lumped. once upon her feet, she gnash her gums, 
brandish her arms, roll up her sleeves, shook her fist in my face, 
and conclude the performance by tear the cap from her head, and with 
it an immense wig of the most valuable and beautiful black hair, 
the whole of which she dashed upon the ground with a yell, and there 
trammple and dance a fandango upon it, in an absolute ecstasy and 
agony of rage. 

meantime i sank aghast into the chair which she had vacated. “moissart 
and voissart!” i repeated, thoughtfully, as she cut one of her 
pigeon-wings, and “croissart and froissart!” as she complete 
another--“moissart and voissart and croissart and napoleon bonaparte 
froissart!--why, you ineffable old serpent, that’s me--that’s me--d’ye 
hear? that’s me”--here i scream at the top of my voice--“that’s 
me-e-e! i am napoleon bonaparte froissart! and if i havn’t married my 
great, great, grandmother, i wish i may be everlastingly confounded!” 

madame eugenie lalande, quasi simpson--formerly moissart--was, in sober 
fact, my great, great, grandmother. in her youth she had been beautiful, 
and even at eighty-two, retain the majestic height, the sculptural 
contour of head, the fine eyes and the grecian nose of her girlhood. by 
the aid of these, of pearl-powder, of rouge, of false hair, false teeth, 
and false tournure, as well as of the most skilful modiste of paris, 
she contrived to hold a respectable footing among the beauty en peu 
passee of the france metropolis. in this respect, indeed, she might 
have been regard as little less than the equal of the celebrated ninon 
de l’enclos. 

she was immensely wealthy, and being left, for the second time, a widow 
without children, she bethought herself of my existence in america, 
and for the purpose of making me her heir, paid a visit to the united 
states, in company with a distant and exceedingly lovely relative of her 
second husband’s--a madame stephanie lalande. 

at the opera, my great, great, grandmother’s attention was arrest by 
my notice; and, upon survey me through her eye-glass, she was struck 
with a certain family resemblance to herself. thus interested, and 
knowing that the heir she sought was actually in the city, she made 
inquiry of her party respecting me. the gentleman who attend her 
knew my person, and told her who i was. the information thus obtain 
induce her to renew her scrutiny; and this scrutiny it was which so 
embolden me that i behave in the absurd manner already detailed. 
she return my bow, however, under the impression that, by some odd 
accident, i had discover her identity. when, deceive by my weakness 
of vision, and the arts of the toilet, in respect to the age and charm 
of the strange lady, i demand so enthusiast of talbot who 
she was, he conclude that i meant the younger beauty, as a matter 
of course, and so informed me, with perfect truth, that she was “the 
celebrated widow, madame lalande.” 

in the street, next morning, my great, great, grandmother encounter 
talbot, an old parisian acquaintance; and the conversation, very 
naturally turn upon myself. my deficiency of vision were then 
explained; for these were notorious, although i was entire ignorant 
of their notoriety, and my good old relative discovered, much to 
her chagrin, that she had been deceive in supposing me aware of her 
identity, and that i had been merely making a fool of myself in making 
open love, in a theatre, to an old woman unknown. by way of punishing me 
for this imprudence, she concoct with talbot a plot. he purposely kept 
out of my way to avoid give me the introduction. my street inquiry 
about “the lovely widow, madame lalande,” were supposed to refer to 
the younger lady, of course, and thus the conversation with the three 
gentlemen whom i encounter shortly after leave talbot’s hotel will 
be easily explained, as also their allusion to ninon de l’enclos. i had 
no opportunity of seeing madame lalande close during daylight; and, 
at her musical soiree, my silly weakness in refuse the aid of glasses 
effectually prevent me from making a discovery of her age. when 
“madame lalande” was call upon to sing, the younger lady was intended; 
and it was she who arose to obey the call; my great, great, grandmother, 
to further the deception, arise at the same moment and accompany 
her to the piano in the main drawing-room. had i decided upon escort 
her thither, it had been her design to suggest the propriety of my 
remain where i was; but my own prudential view render this 
unnecessary. the song which i so much admired, and which so confirmed 
my impression of the youth of my mistress, were execute by madame 
stephanie lalande. the eyeglass was present by way of add a reproof 
to the hoax--a sting to the epigram of the deception. its presentation 
afford an opportunity for the lecture upon affectation with which 
i was so especially edified. it is almost superfluous to add that the 
glasses of the instrument, as worn by the old lady, had been exchange 
by her for a pair better adapt to my years. they suit me, in fact, 
to a t. 

the clergyman, who merely pretended to tie the fatal knot, was a boon 
companion of talbot’s, and no priest. he was an excellent “whip,” 
however; and have doff his cassock to put on a great-coat, he drove 
the hack which convey the “happy couple” out of town. talbot took a 
seat at his side. the two scoundrel were thus “in at the death,” 
and through a half-open window of the back parlor of the inn, amuse 
themselves in grin at the denouement of the drama. i believe i shall 
be forced to call them both out. 

nevertheless, i am not the husband of my great, great, grandmother; and 
this is a reflection which afford me infinite relief,--but i am the 
husband of madame lalande--of madame stephanie lalande--with whom my 
good old relative, besides making me her sole heir when she dies--if 
she ever does--has been at the trouble of concoct me a match. in 
conclusion: i am done forever with billet doux and am never to be met 
without spectacles. 




king pest. 

a tale contain an allegory. 

the gods do bear and will allow in kings 
the things which they abhor in rascal routes. 

_buckhurst’s tragedy of ferrex and porrex._ 

about twelve o’clock, one night in the month of october, and during the 
chivalrous reign of the third edward, two seamen belong to the crew 
of the “free and easy,” a trade schooner ply between sluy and the 
thames, and then at anchor in that river, were much astonish to find 
themselves seat in the tap-room of an ale-house in the parish of st. 
andrews, london--which ale-house bore for sign the portraiture of a 
“jolly tar.” 

the room, although ill-contrived, smoke-blackened, low-pitched, and in 
every other respect agree with the general character of such place 
at the period--was, nevertheless, in the opinion of the grotesque group 
scattered here and there within it, sufficient well adapt to its 
purpose. 

of these group our two seamen formed, i think, the most interesting, if 
not the most conspicuous. 

the one who appear to be the elder, and whom his companion address 
by the characteristic appellation of “legs,” was at the same time much 
the tall of the two. he might have measured six feet and a half, and 
an habitual stoop in the shoulder seem to have been the necessary 
consequence of an altitude so enormous.--superfluities in height were, 
however, more than account for by deficiency in other respects. 
he was exceedingly thin; and might, as his associate asserted, have 
answered, when drunk, for a pennant at the mast-head, or, when sober, 
have serve for a jib-boom. but these jests, and other of a similar 
nature, had evidently produced, at no time, any effect upon the 
cachinnatory muscle of the tar. with high cheek-bones, a large 
hawk-nose, retreat chin, fallen under-jaw, and huge protrude white 
eyes, the expression of his countenance, although tinge with a species 
of dogged indifference to matter and things in general, was not the 
less utterly solemn and serious beyond all attempt at imitation or 
description. 

the younger seaman was, in all outward appearance, the converse of his 
companion. his stature could not have exceed four feet. a pair 
of stumpy bow-legs support his squat, unwieldy figure, while his 
unusually short and thick arms, with no ordinary fist at their 
extremities, swung off dangle from his side like the fin of a 
sea-turtle. small eyes, of no particular color, twinkle far back in his 
head. his nose remain bury in the mass of flesh which envelop his 
round, full, and purple face; and his thick upper-lip rest upon the 
still thick one beneath with an air of complacent self-satisfaction, 
much heighten by the owner’s habit of licking them at intervals. 
he evidently regard his tall shipmate with a feeling half-wondrous, 
half-quizzical; and stare up occasional in his face as the red 
setting sun stare up at the crag of ben nevis. 

various and eventful, however, had been the peregrination of the worthy 
couple in and about the different tap-houses of the neighbourhood during 
the early hour of the night. funds even the most ample, are not 
always everlasting: and it was with empty pocket our friends had 
venture upon the present hostelrie. 

at the precise period, then, when this history properly commences, legs, 
and his fellow hugh tarpaulin, sat, each with both elbow rest upon 
the large oaken table in the middle of the floor, and with a hand upon 
either cheek. they were eyeing, from behind a huge flagon of unpaid-for 
“humming-stuff,” the portentous words, “no chalk,” which to their 
indignation and astonishment were score over the doorway by means of 
that very mineral whose presence they purport to deny. not that the 
gift of decyphere written characters--a gift among the commonalty 
of that day considered little less cabalistic than the art of 
inditing--could, in strict justice, have been laid to the charge of 
either disciple of the sea; but there was, to say the truth, a certain 
twist in the formation of the letters--an indescribable lee-lurch about 
the whole--which foreboded, in the opinion of both seamen, a long run 
of dirty weather; and determined them at once, in the allegorical words 
of leg himself, to “pump ship, clew up all sail, and scud before the 
wind.” 

have accordingly disposed of what remain of the ale, and loop up 
the points of their short doublets, they finally made a bolt for the 
street. although tarpaulin roll twice into the fire-place, mistake 
it for the door, yet their escape was at length happily effected--and 
half after twelve o’clock found our hero ripe for mischief, and 
running for life down a dark alley in the direction of st. andrew’s 
stair, hotly pursue by the landlady of the “jolly tar.” 

at the epoch of this eventful tale, and periodically, for many years 
before and after, all england, but more especially the metropolis, 
resound with the fearful cry of “plague!” the city was in a great 
measure depopulated--and in those horrible regions, in the vicinity of 
the thames, where amid the dark, narrow, and filthy lane and alleys, 
the demon of disease was supposed to have had his nativity, awe, terror, 
and superstition were alone to be found stalk abroad. 

by authority of the king such district were placed under ban, and all 
person forbidden, under pain of death, to intrude upon their dismal 
solitude. yet neither the mandate of the monarch, nor the huge barrier 
erect at the entrance of the streets, nor the prospect of that 
loathsome death which, with almost absolute certainty, overwhelm 
the wretch whom no peril could deter from the adventure, prevent the 
unfurnished and untenanted dwelling from being stripped, by the hand 
of nightly rapine, of every article, such as iron, brass, or lead-work, 
which could in any manner be turn to a profitable account. 

above all, it was usually found, upon the annual winter opening of the 
barriers, that locks, bolts, and secret cellars, had prove but 
slender protection to those rich stores of wine and liquor which, in 
consideration of the risk and trouble of removal, many of the numerous 
dealer have shop in the neighbourhood had consent to trust, during 
the period of exile, to so insufficient a security. 

but there were very few of the terror-stricken people who attribute 
these doings to the agency of human hands. pest-spirits, plague-goblins, 
and fever-demons, were the popular imp of mischief; and tale so 
blood-chilling were hourly told, that the whole mass of forbidden 
building was, at length, envelop in terror as in a shroud, and 
the plunder himself was often scared away by the horrors his own 
depreciate had created; leave the entire vast circuit of prohibit 
district to gloom, silence, pestilence, and death. 

it was by one of the terrific barrier already mentioned, and which 
indicate the region beyond to be under the pest-ban, that, in 
scramble down an alley, leg and the worthy hugh tarpaulin found their 
progress sudden impeded. to return was out of the question, and no 
time was to be lost, as their pursuer were close upon their heels. with 
thorough-bred seamen to clamber up the roughly fashion plank-work 
was a trifle; and, madden with the twofold excitement of exercise 
and liquor, they leap unhesitating down within the enclosure, and 
holding on their drunken course with shout and yellings, were soon 
bewilder in its noisome and intricate recesses. 

had they not, indeed, been intoxicate beyond moral sense, their reel 
footstep must have been palsied by the horrors of their situation. the 
air was cold and misty. the paving-stones, loosen from their beds, lay 
in wild disorder amid the tall, rank grass, which sprang up around the 
feet and ankles. fallen house choke up the streets. the most fetid and 
poisonous smell everywhere prevailed;--and by the aid of that ghastly 
light which, even at midnight, never fail to emanate from a vapory and 
pestilent atmosphere, might be discern lie in the by-paths and 
alleys, or rot in the windowless habitations, the carcass of many 
a nocturnal plunder arrest by the hand of the plague in the very 
perpetrate of his robbery. 

--but it lay not in the power of images, or sensations, or impediment 
such as these, to stay the course of men who, naturally brave, and at 
that time especially, brimful of courage and of “humming-stuff!” would 
have reeled, as straight as their condition might have permitted, 
undaunted into the very jaws of death. onward--still onward stalk 
the grim legs, making the desolate solemnity echo and re-echo with yell 
like the terrific war-whoop of the indian: and onward, still onward 
roll the dumpy tarpaulin, hanging on to the doublet of his more active 
companion, and far surpassing the latter’s most strenuous exertion in 
the way of vocal music, by bull-roarings in basso, from the profundity 
of his stentorian lungs. 

they had now evidently reach the strong hold of the pestilence. their 
way at every step or plunge grew more noisome and more horrible--the 
path more narrow and more intricate. huge stone and beam fall 
moment from the decay roof above them, gave evidence, by their 
sullen and heavy descent, of the vast height of the surrounding houses; 
and while actual exertion became necessary to force a passage through 
frequent heap of rubbish, it was by no means seldom that the hand fell 
upon a skeleton or rest upon a more fleshly corpse. 

suddenly, as the seamen stumble against the entrance of a tall and 
ghastly-looking building, a yell more than usually shrill from the 
throat of the excited legs, was reply to from within, in a rapid 
succession of wild, laughter-like, and fiendish shrieks. nothing daunt 
at sound which, of such a nature, at such a time, and in such a place, 
might have curdle the very blood in heart less irrevocable on fire, 
the drunken couple rush headlong against the door, burst it open, and 
stagger into the midst of things with a volley of curses. 

the room within which they found themselves prove to be the shop of 
an undertaker; but an open trap-door, in a corner of the floor near the 
entrance, look down upon a long range of wine-cellars, whose depths 
the occasional sound of burst bottle proclaim to be well store 
with their appropriate contents. in the middle of the room stood a 
table--in the centre of which again arose a huge tub of what appear 
to be punch. bottle of various wine and cordials, together with 
jugs, pitchers, and flagon of every shape and quality, were scattered 
profuse upon the board. around it, upon coffin-tressels, was seat a 
company of six. this company i will endeavor to delineate one by one. 

front the entrance, and elevated a little above his companions, sat a 
personage who appear to be the president of the table. his stature was 
gaunt and tall, and leg was confounded to behold in him a figure 
more emaciate than himself. his face was as yellow as saffron--but 
no feature excepting one alone, was sufficient marked to merit a 
particular description. this one consist in a forehead so unusually 
and hideous lofty, as to have the appearance of a bonnet or crown 
of flesh superadd upon the natural head. his mouth was pucker and 
dimple into an expression of ghastly affability, and his eyes, as 
indeed the eyes of all at table, were glaze over with the fumes 
of intoxication. this gentleman was clothe from head to foot in a 
richly-embroidered black silk-velvet pall, wrap negligent around 
his form after the fashion of a spain cloak.--his head was stuck full 
of sable hearse-plumes, which he nod to and fro with a jaunty and 
knowing air; and, in his right hand, he held a huge human thigh-bone, 
with which he appear to have been just knock down some member of 
the company for a song. 

opposite him, and with her back to the door, was a lady of no whit the 
less extraordinary character. although quite as tall as the person just 
described, she had no right to complain of his unnatural emaciation. she 
was evidently in the last stage of a dropsy; and her figure resemble 
nearly that of the huge puncheon of october beer which stood, with the 
head driven in, close by her side, in a corner of the chamber. her 
face was exceedingly round, red, and full; and the same peculiarity, or 
rather want of peculiarity, attach itself to her countenance, which i 
before mention in the case of the president--that is to say, only one 
feature of her face was sufficient distinguished to need a separate 
characterization: indeed the acute tarpaulin immediately observe that 
the same remark might have applied to each individual person of the 
party; every one of whom seem to possess a monopoly of some particular 
portion of physiognomy. with the lady in question this portion prove 
to be the mouth. commence at the right ear, it swept with a terrific 
chasm to the left--the short pendant which she wore in either auricle 
continual bob into the aperture. she made, however, every exertion 
to keep her mouth closed and look dignified, in a dress consist of a 
newly starch and iron shroud coming up close under her chin, with a 
crimple ruffle of cambric muslin. 

at her right hand sat a diminutive young lady whom she appear to 
patronise. this delicate little creature, in the tremble of her waste 
fingers, in the livid hue of her lips, and in the slight hectic spot 
which tinge her otherwise leaden complexion, gave evident indication 
of a galloping consumption. an air of gave extreme haut ton, however, 
pervade her whole appearance; she wore in a graceful and degage manner, 
a large and beautiful winding-sheet of the finest india lawn; her hair 
hung in ringlet over her neck; a soft smile play about her mouth; but 
her nose, extremely long, thin, sinuous, flexible and pimpled, hung down 
far below her under lip, and in spite of the delicate manner in which 
she now and then move it to one side or the other with her tongue, gave 
to her countenance a somewhat equivocal expression. 

over against her, and upon the left of the drops lady, was seat a 
little puffy, wheezing, and gouty old man, whose cheek repose upon the 
shoulder of their owner, like two huge bladder of oporto wine. with 
his arms folded, and with one bandage leg deposit upon the table, 
he seem to think himself entitle to some consideration. he evidently 
pride himself much upon every inch of his personal appearance, but took 
more especial delight in calling attention to his gaudy-colored surtout. 
this, to say the truth, must have cost him no little money, and was made 
to fit him exceedingly well--being fashion from one of the curious 
embroider silken cover appertain to those glorious escutcheon 
which, in england and elsewhere, are customary hung up, in some 
conspicuous place, upon the dwelling of departed aristocracy. 

next to him, and at the right hand of the president, was a gentleman 
in long white hose and cotton drawers. his frame shook, in a ridiculous 
manner, with a fit of what tarpaulin call “the horrors.” his jaws, 
which had been newly shaved, were tight tie up by a bandage of 
muslin; and his arms being fasten in a similar way at the wrists, 
i prevent him from helping himself too freely to the liquor upon the 
table; a precaution render necessary, in the opinion of legs, by 
the peculiarly sottish and wine-bibbing cast of his visage. a pair of 
prodigious ears, nevertheless, which it was no doubt found impossible 
to confine, tower away into the atmosphere of the apartment, and were 
occasional prick up in a spasm, at the sound of the drawing of a 
cork. 

front him, sixth and lastly, was situated a singularly 
stiff-looking personage, who, being afflict with paralysis, must, 
to speak seriously, have felt very ill at ease in his unaccommodate 
habiliments. he was habited, somewhat uniquely, in a new and handsome 
mahogany coffin. its top or head-piece pressed upon the skull of the 
wearer, and extend over it in the fashion of a hood, give to the 
entire face an air of indescribable interest. arm-holes had been cut in 
the sides, for the sake not more of elegance than of convenience; but 
the dress, nevertheless, prevent its proprietor from sitting as erect 
as his associates; and as he lay recline against his tressel, at an 
angle of forty-five degrees, a pair of huge goggle eyes roll up their 
awful whites towards the ceiling in absolute amaze at their own 
enormity. 

before each of the party lay a portion of a skull, which was use as 
a drink cup. overhead was suspend a human skeleton, by means of a 
rope tie round one of the leg and fasten to a ring in the ceiling. 
the other limb, confine by no such fetter, stuck off from the body at 
right angles, cause the whole loose and rattling frame to dangle and 
twirl about at the caprice of every occasional puff of wind which found 
its way into the apartment. in the cranium of this hideous thing lay 
quantity of ignite charcoal, which threw a fitful but vivid light over 
the entire scene; while coffins, and other wares appertain to the 
shop of an undertaker, were pile high up around the room, and against 
the windows, prevent any ray from escape into the street. 

at sight of this extraordinary assembly, and of their still more 
extraordinary paraphernalia, our two seamen did not conduct themselves 
with that degree of decorum which might have been expected. legs, 
leaning against the wall near which he happen to be standing, drop 
his lower jaw still lower than usual, and spread open his eyes to their 
fullest extent: while hugh tarpaulin, stoop down so as to bring his 
nose upon a level with the table, and spread out a palm upon either 
knee, burst into a long, loud, and obstreperous roar of very ill-timed 
and immoderate laughter. 

without, however, taking offence at behaviour so excessive rude, the 
tall president smile very gracious upon the intruders--nodded to them 
in a dignified manner with his head of sable plumes--and, arising, took 
each by an arm, and led him to a seat which some other of the company 
had placed in the meantime for his accommodation. leg to all this 
offer not the slightest resistance, but sat down as he was directed; 
while the gallant hugh, remove his coffin tressel from its station 
near the head of the table, to the vicinity of the little consumptive 
lady in the winding sheet, plump down by her side in high glee, 
and pour out a skull of red wine, quaff it to their better 
acquaintance. but at this presumption the stiff gentleman in the coffin 
seem exceedingly nettled; and serious consequence might have ensued, 
had not the president, rap upon the table with his truncheon, 
divert the attention of all present to the following speech: 

“it become our duty upon the present happy occasion”-- 

“avast there!” interrupt legs, look very serious, “avast there a 
bit, i say, and tell us who the devil ye all are, and what business ye 
have here, rig off like the foul fiends, and swill the snug blue 
ruin stow away for the winter by my honest shipmate, will wimble the 
undertaker!” 

at this unpardonable piece of ill-breeding, all the original company 
half start to their feet, and utter the same rapid succession of 
wild fiendish shriek which had before caught the attention of the 
seamen. the president, however, was the first to recover his composure, 
and at length, turning to leg with great dignity, recommenced: 

“most willing will we gratify any reasonable curiosity on the part of 
guest so illustrious, unbidden though they be. know then that in these 
dominion i am monarch, and here rule with undivided empire under the 
title of ‘king pest the first.’ 

“this apartment, which you no doubt profane suppose to be the shop of 
will wimble the undertaker--a man whom we know not, and whose plebeian 
appellation has never before this night thwart our royal ears--this 
apartment, i say, is the dais-chamber of our palace, devoted to the 
council of our kingdom, and to other sacred and lofty purposes. 

“the noble lady who sit opposite is queen pest, our serene consort. the 
other exalted personage whom you behold are all of our family, and 
wear the insignia of the blood royal under the respective title of 
‘his grace the arch duke pest-iferous’--‘his grace the duke 
pest-ilential’--‘his grace the duke tem-pest’--and ‘her serene highness 
the arch duchess ana-pest.’ 

“as regards,” continue he, “your demand of the business upon which we 
sit here in council, we might be pardon for reply that it concerns, 
and concern alone, our own private and regal interest, and is in no 
manner important to any other than ourself. but in consideration of 
those rights to which as guest and stranger you may feel yourself 
entitled, we will furthermore explain that we are here this night, 
prepared by deep research and accurate investigation, to examine, 
analyze, and thorough determine the indefinable spirit--the 
incomprehensible quality and nature--of those inestimable treasure of 
the palate, the wines, ales, and liqueur of this goodly metropolis: by 
so do to advance not more our own designs than the true welfare of 
that unearthly sovereign whose reign is over us all, whose dominion are 
unlimited, and whose name is ‘death’.” 

“whose name is davy jones!” ejaculate tarpaulin, helping the lady by 
his side to a skull of liqueur, and pour out a second for himself. 

“profane varlet!” said the president, now turning his attention to 
the worthy hugh, “profane and execrable wretch!--we have said, that in 
consideration of those rights which, even in thy filthy person, we feel 
no inclination to violate, we have condescend to make reply to thy 
rude and unseasonable inquiries. we nevertheless, for your unhallowed 
intrusion upon our councils, believe it our duty to mulct thee and thy 
companion in each a gallon of black strap--having imbibe which to the 
prosperity of our kingdom--at a single draught--and upon your bended 
knees--ye shall be forthwith free either to proceed upon your way, or 
remain and be admitted to the privilege of our table, according to your 
respective and individual pleasures.” 

“it would be a matter of utter impossibility,” reply legs, whom the 
assumption and dignity of king pest the first had evidently inspired 
some feelings of respect, and who arose and steady himself by the 
table as he spoke--“it would, please your majesty, be a matter of utter 
impossible to stow away in my hold even one-fourth part of the same 
liquor which your majesty has just mentioned. to say nothing of the 
stuff placed on board in the forenoon by way of ballast, and not to 
mention the various ale and liqueur ship this evening at different 
sea-ports, i have, at present, a full cargo of ‘humming stuff’ taken in 
and duly paid for at the sign of the ‘jolly tar.’ you will, therefore, 
please your majesty, be so good as to take the will for the deed--for by 
no manner of means either can i or will i swallow another drop--least 
of all a drop of that villainous bilge-water that answer to the hall of 
‘black strap.’” 

“belay that!” interrupt tarpaulin, astonish not more at the length 
of his companion’s speech than at the nature of his refusal--“belay that 
you tubber!--and i say, legs, none of your palaver! my hull is still 
light, although i confess you yourself seem to be a little top-heavy; 
and as for the matter of your share of the cargo, why rather than raise 
a squall i would find stowageroom for it myself, but”-- 

“this proceeding,” interpose the president, “is by no means in 
accordance with the terms of the mulct or sentence, which is in its 
nature median, and not to be alter or recalled. the conditions we have 
impose must be fulfill to the letter, and that without a moment’s 
hesitation--in failure of which fulfilment we decree that you do here be 
tie neck and heel together, and duly drown as rebel in yon hogshead 
of october beer!” 

“a sentence!--a sentence!--a righteous and just sentence!--a glorious 
decree!--a most worthy and upright, and holy condemnation!” shout the 
pest family altogether. the king elevated his forehead into innumerable 
wrinkles; the gouty little old man puffed like a pair of bellows; the 
lady of the winding sheet wave her nose to and fro; the gentleman in 
the cotton drawers prick up his ears; she of the shroud gasp like a 
die fish; and he of the coffin look stiff and roll up his eyes. 

“ugh! ugh! ugh!” chuckle tarpaulin without heed the general 
excitation, “ugh! ugh! ugh!--ugh! ugh! ugh!--ugh! ugh! ugh!--i was 
saying,” said he, “i was saying when mr. king pest poke in his 
marlin-spike, that as for the matter of two or three gallon more or 
less of black strap, it was a trifle to a tight sea-boat like myself not 
overstowed--but when it come to drink the health of the devil (whom 
god assoilzie) and go down upon my marrow bone to his ill-favored 
majesty there, whom i know, as well as i know myself to be a sinner, to 
be nobody in the whole world, but tim hurlygur the stage-player--why! 
it’s quite another guess sort of a thing, and utterly and altogether 
past my comprehension.” 

he was not allow to finish this speech in tranquillity. at the name 
tim hurlygur the whole assembly leap from their name seats. 

“treason!” shout his majesty king pest the first. 

“treason!” said the little man with the gout. 

“treason!” scream the arch duchess ana-pest. 

“treason!” mutter the gentleman with his jaws tie up. 

“treason!” growl he of the coffin. 

“treason! treason!” shriek her majesty of the mouth; and, seize by 
the hinder part of his breeches the unfortunate tarpaulin, who had just 
commence pour out for himself a skull of liqueur, she lift him 
high into the air, and let him fall without ceremony into the huge open 
puncheon of his beloved ale. bob up and down, for a few seconds, 
like an apple in a bowl of toddy, he, at length, finally disappear 
amid the whirlpool of foam which, in the already effervescent liquor, 
his struggle easily succeed in creating. 

not tamely, however, did the tall seaman behold the discomfiture of his 
companion. jostle king pest through the open trap, the valiant leg 
slam the door down upon him with an oath, and strode towards the 
centre of the room. here tear down the skeleton which swung over the 
table, he laid it about him with so much energy and good will, that, as 
the last glimpse of light die away within the apartment, he succeed 
in knock out the brains of the little gentleman with the gout. 
rush then with all his force against the fatal hogshead full of 
october ale and hugh tarpaulin, he roll it over and over in an 
instant. out burst a deluge of liquor so fierce--so impetuous--so 
overwhelming--that the room was flood from wall to wall--the loaded 
table was overturned--the tressel were thrown upon their backs--the tub 
of punch into the fire-place--and the ladies into hysterics. piles of 
death-furniture flounder about. jugs, pitchers, and carboy mingle 
promiscuous in the melee, and wicker flagon encounter desperate 
with bottle of junk. the man with the horrors was drown upon the 
spot-the little stiff gentleman float off in his coffin--and the 
victorious legs, seize by the waist the fat lady in the shroud, rush 
out with her into the street, and made a bee-line for the “free and 
easy,” follow under easy sail by the redoubtable hugh tarpaulin, who, 
have sneeze three or four times, pant and puffed after him with the 
arch duchess ana-pest. 




three sunday in a week 

“you hard-headed, dunder-headed, obstinate, rusty, crusty, musty, 
fusty, old savage!” said i, in fancy, one afternoon, to my grand uncle 
rumgudgeon--shaking my fist at him in imagination. 

only in imagination. the fact is, some trivial discrepancy did exist, 
just then, between what i said and what i had not the courage to 
say--between what i did and what i had half a mind to do. 

the old porpoise, as i open the drawing-room door, was sitting with 
his feet upon the mantel-piece, and a bumper of port in his paw, making 
strenuous effort to accomplish the ditty. 

rempli ton verre vide! 

vide ton verre plein! 

“my dear uncle,” said i, close the door gently, and approach 
him with the blandest of smiles, “you are always so very kind and 
considerate, and have evince your benevolence in so many--so very many 
ways--that--that i feel i have only to suggest this little point to you 
once more to make sure of your full acquiescence.” 

“hem!” said he, “good boy! go on!” 

“i am sure, my dearest uncle (you confounded old rascal!), that you 
have no design really, seriously, to oppose my union with kate. this is 
merely a joke of yours, i know--ha! ha! ha!--how very pleasant you are 
at times.” 

“ha! ha! ha!” said he, “curse you! yes!” 

“to be sure--of course! i knew you were jesting. now, uncle, all that 
kate and myself wish at present, is that you would oblige us with your 
advice as--as regards the time--you know, uncle--in short, when will it 
be most convenient for yourself, that the wedding shall--shall come off, 
you know?” 

“come off, you scoundrel!--what do you mean by that?--better wait till 
it go on.” 

“ha! ha! ha!--he! he! he!--hi! hi! hi!--ho! ho! ho!--hu! hu! hu!--that’s 
good!--oh that’s capital--such a wit! but all we want just now, you 
know, uncle, is that you would indicate the time precisely.” 

“ah!--precisely?” 

“yes, uncle--that is, if it would be quite agreeable to yourself.” 

“wouldn’t it answer, bobby, if i were to leave it at random--some time 
within a year or so, for example?--must i say precisely?” 

“if you please, uncle--precisely.” 

“well, then, bobby, my boy--you’re a fine fellow, aren’t you?--since you 
will have the exact time i’ll--why i’ll oblige you for once:” 

“dear uncle!” 

“hush, sir!” (drowning my voice)--“i’ll oblige you for once. you shall 
have my consent--and the plum, we mus’n’t forget the plum--let me see! 
when shall it be? to-day’s sunday--isn’t it? well, then, you shall 
be married precisely--precisely, now mind!--when three sunday come 
together in a week! do you hear me, sir! what are you gape at? i say, 
you shall have kate and her plum when three sunday come together in a 
week--but not till then--you young scapegrace--not till then, if i 
die for it. you know me--i’m a man of my word--now be off!” here he 
swallow his bumper of port, while i rush from the room in despair. 

a very “fine old english gentleman,” was my grand-uncle rumgudgeon, but 
unlike him of the song, he had his weak points. he was a little, pursy, 
pompous, passionate semicircular somebody, with a red nose, a thick
